Africa News


January 29, 1990


DEATHS MOUNT


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 221 words


Liberian government troops are indiscriminately killing villagers in a crackdown against rebels in Nimba county in the northeast of the country, according to exiled government opponents and refugees who have fled into neighboring countries. The rebels, said to number about 100, entered the country from Cote d'Ivoire on December 24, They, too, have apparently been killing civilians. Some 35,000 Liberians are reported to have fled into Cote d'Ivoire to escape the fighting, and another 13,000 have arrived in Guinea. Amnesty International has called upon the government to stop extra-judicial killings by government forces. Last week, Liberian President Sam Doe warned soldiers they would face severe penalties if they harassed civilians.

The government has responded to the invasion by arresting opposition political figures in the capital, Monrovia, which has been untouched by the fighting. Two members of the Liberian Action Party have been killed.

Nimba County has been a center of opposition to Doe's rule, but the uprising there is not connected to any of the established political movements. The rebel leader is Charles Taylor, who fled Liberia in 1986 amid accusations of embezzlement. But he was detained in the U.S. where he jumped bail while facing extradition proceedings, Reuters reports.

A "DEVASTATED SOCIETY"


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 92 words


"It is difficult to overstate the Somali government's brutality towards its own people," says the human rights group Africa Watch in a report released this month. Based on several months of interviews with Somali refugees, the report calls for "decisive" international intervention to halt the violence that has plagued Somalia under the military rule of Mohamed Siad Barre. Africa Watch says that if world opinion becomes aware of the tragedy in the north-east African nation, it will find "the anguish of its people" impossible to ignore.

Africa News


January 29, 1990


WHO'S NEXT?


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 268 words


There has been a wave of strikes this month in Mozambique, where labor action has been rare. The strikes began on December 28, when more than two dozen journalists walked out at the weekly newsmagazine Tempo. Since then there has been strike actions by dockers, railway, factory and construction workers, city employees and hospital staff. Some of the strikes, like those at steel and textile mills, were resolved in a few hours. But a walk-out and picketing by nurses and support staff at the country's second largest hospital in the port city of Beira caused wards to close.

The actions have been prompted by an economic adjustment program that has devalued the currency, pushed prices up and left employers unable to pay year-end bonuses.

The government of President Joaquim Chissano, which has outlined a draft constitution that will guarantee workers the right to strike, has taken a moderate position on the disruptions. A statement released by government ministries said the workers' demands were just, but it also said that strikes which endanger lives and the national economy are "immoral."

The proposed new constitution, based on recommendations from the ruling Frelimo party's fifth Congress in July, includes a lengthy list of citizens' rights and freedoms and provides for a nationally-elected president who will be limited to three five-year terms. Currently, government officials are elected by a tiered system, beginning with popular votes at the local level, with each elected body choosing representatives to the next level, ending with the national Peoples' Assembly.

January 29, 1990


BANKING ON BUSSIERES


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 197 words


Lusaka: In a move Zambians are regarding with mixed feelings, Canadian banker Jacques Bussieres has taken up his post as governor of the Bank of Zambia. The appointment is widely viewed as a failure by 65-year-old President Kenneth Kaunda to appoint qualified Zambians to the critical post. 

Since Zambia's independence in 1964, each bank governor's term has lasted only about two and a half years. In the past three years, 11 senior officials of the bank have been sacked for corruption or incompetence. Bussieres' predecessor, Francis Nkhoma, has appeared in magistrate's court here charged with five counts of corruption and contravention of foreign exchange regulations.

With debts that total over $ 1,000 per capita and inflation hovering around 130%, Zambia's government needs a massive injection of hard currency. Although Kaunda says the decision to appoint Bussiere was his own, sources here say it was one of the conditions for reaching agreement with the International Monetary Fund for new loans. Zambian officials are due to hold meetings in Paris in March with representatives of the Fund, the World Bank and other international donors and lenders.

January 29, 1990


WHOOPS!


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 159 words


A staff shortage at Africa News, which has meant that writing, editing and proofing is done by too few people on impossible deadlines, has necessitated an alarming number of corrections in recent weeks -- a trend we hope we've now reversed.

Several typographical errors cropped up in the review of V.Y. Mudimbe's book, The Invention of Africa, in the last issue of 1989. One of them, which probably left attentive readers shaking their heads, dropped a phrase in quoting a 1965 reissue of Mary Kingsley's Travels in West Africa. Our sentence has Kingsley making the comment in 1965. Since her African travels took place a century ago, any recent pronouncement by Mary Kingsley would have been bigger news than we made of it.

For those interested in the impressions of a modern traveler who followed Kingsley's footsteps, the London firm Bloomsbury has published One Dry Season, an account of West African travels by Caroline Alexander.

Africa News


January 29, 1990


FOCUS ON AFRICA


LENGTH: 278 words


Devotees of the British Broadcasting Corporation's Africa reporting can now keep up with the coverage, even when sunspots impede the reception. To mark the Africa Service's 50th anniversary this year, the BBC has launched a quarterly magazine named after the Service's 30-year-old flagship program, Focus on Africa. The magazine's editor, Robin White, who also edits the radio program, says he expects regular publication to begin this summer.

Meanwhile, an inaugural issue is giving readers a glimpse of the qualities that have won the Africa Service a loyal listenership over the years: sophisticated design, writing that integrates a lively personal style into the reporting, and a spirited dialogue with the audience. What the printed version of Focus loses in you-are-there sound, it gains in vivid photography. Copies of the special issue are available for $ 3.00 each from the BBC, Bush House, LondonWC 2.

Meanwhile, across the Atlantic, Nigerian writer Chinua Achebe, who is teaching at Dartmouth, is publishing a new monthly called African Commentary. The journal will, Achebe says, invite leading writers and scholars from around the world to "meditate on the condition of Africans and of people of African descent."

Achebe says the magazine's staff is committed both "to reclaiming the rich heritage of Africa" and to pondering "the question of Africa's and the Black's place in the world today." Out of the complex mix of perspectives that will be presented, he says, there will emerge "a sound, clear vision of the way forward."

Subscription information may be obtained from African Commentary, 29 Pray Street, Amherst, MA 01002.

Africa News


January 29, 1990


NIGERIA'S EMBATTLED PRESS


SECTION: MEDIA


LENGTH: 1461 words


Lagos -- Nigeria's feisty press, easily the liveliest in Africa, is a study in paradox. Hemmed in by various restrictive laws, constantly under the watchful eye of the military government and buffeted by a severe newsprint scarcity, the press in Africa's most populous country manages to maintain a reputation for freedom and high quality.

The press tradition is an old one. Newspapers began publishing in Nigeria on December 3, 1859, 100 years before the West African country became independent from Britain. The first paper, Iwe Irohin Yoruba, "Newspaper for the Yoruba" was established by the Reverend Henry Townsend, an Anglican missionary, in Abeokuta, western Nigeria. It sold for 120 cowries -- about $ .01-- and carried news of church activities, trade reports, marriage announcements and post office notices.

Today there is a riot of over 100 newspapers and magazines. The serious dailies include the government owned Daily Times and New Nigerian, and the privately owned Guardian, National Concord, Punch and Vanguard. Privately owned news magazines include Newswatch, The African Guardian and African Concord. Human interest magazines include Quality and muckrakers such as Vintage People and Climax -- African versions of the American National Enquirer. 

This proliferation of publications has spurred the emergence of a new generation of journalists -- better paid and more educated, in response to an increasingly critical readership, and more respected.

But some aspects of the negative image of journalists remain, notably the "brown envelope" phenomenon -- the buying of publicity through cash "gifts" to journalists.

Although the government owns most dailies, an increasingly significant proportion is owned by private individuals and organizations, unlike in most African countries. However, radio and television are still wholly owned and controlled by the government.

Despite long stretches of military dictatorship, the Nigerian press prides itself as being the freest on the continent. Sven Harold Jurchewsky, a Canadian diplomat who recently served in Lagos, says the degree of press freedom in Nigeria is remarkable. "You have a freer press than most places in the world" he says.

Still, that freedom is provisional. "This Government Has AIDS" screamed a recent headline in The New Horizon, a leftist Lagos magazine, quoting Gani Fawehinmi, a leading government critic and human rights lawyer. Perhaps only in Nigeria, of all the military-ruled African countries, could a newspaper carry such a banner. But Fawehinmi was arrested and is being tried under a law which forbids statements that could "jeopardize" the military government's program of transition to civilian rule.

That process is at a delicate stage. Last December, the military government of General Ibrahim Babangida announced that none of the six new political parties which had been formed to contest the elections scheduled for 1992 had "qualified." The government, itself, he said, would organize two national parties, free of the corruption and backroom wheeling and dealing that had characterized previous civilian governments.

While many politicians were dismayed, other observers were impressed with the abrupt move, which they hoped would curb influence buying and make the campaign process more truly democratic. Ray Ekpu, editor-in-chief of Newswatch magazine, which has often been a vocal -- and besieged -- government critic, called Babangida's action a "master stroke."

Is the Nigerian press really free? Ray Ekpu, of Newswatch , says that compared to many other African countries, the Nigerian press is better off. "But then," he says, "Africa can be compared to the community where there is no maiden." In such a community, according to Nigerian folklore, old women are regarded as maidens. "In the context of the African press -- where there are no maidens," Ekpu says, "Nigeria is its maiden."

Various laws, enforced irregularly and selectively, hang like a Sword of Damocles over the Nigerian press.

The Official Secrets Act of 1962 prohibits the reproduction or retention of "classified matter" by, or the transmission of such classified matter to "an unauthorized person." Matters are "classified" and persons are "unauthorized" as defined by the government.

Although rarely invoked, the law discourages investigative journalism concerning government activities.

In 1984, the military government of Major-General Muhammadu Buhari enacted Decree 4, a law allowing jail terms for journalists who publish reports that "embarrass" the government. Two reporters, Nduke Irabor and Tunde Thompson, both of the Guardian, were jailed under the law for prematurely publishing a list of government ambassadorial nominees.

For a time, the Babangida government, which took power in 1985, enjoyed a honeymoon with the press. But the romance had turned sour by April 1987, when Newswatch was proscribed for six months for publishing a secret document on Nigeria's political transition to civilian rule.

The State Security (Detention of Persons) Decree -- popularly known as Decree 2 -- has been the most recent government instrument for muzzling the press. Under the decree, the government can detain anyone indefinitely and without trial for "reasons of state security."

Another law -- The Media Council Decree of 1988 -- has generated such a furor that it has been inoperable. It provides for an 18 member media council whose chairperson and secretary, along with ten other council members, are appointed by the country's president. The Nigerian Union of Journalists will have just four representatives, and the Newspaper Proprietors Association and the Guild of Editors will each have two. The council will have the power to register and de-register journalists.

Predictably, Nigerian journalists have rebelled against the decree, although the government-owned press, which cannot protest as loudly as the private press, has kept out of the fray. Pini Jason, editor of a national newsmonthly, compared the legislation with South Africa's recent abortive attempts to "licence" journalists through registration requirements.

Even when the laws themselves are not employed, journalists who publish what the authorities regard as offensive articles are regularly invited for "chats" -- a euphemism for brief detentions -- with security agents. Thus, many editors tread softly, imposing substantial self-censorship. But if there is worry here about government controls, there is concern, too, about the effect that civilian rule will have on the press. The time-worn cliche, "He who pays the piper dictates the tune" applies to private as well as government-owned newspapers. Editors who wish to retain their jobs avoid running stories that contradict the views of their proprietors, whether they be government or not. With the coming of civilian rule, many journalists are fearful that their papers will become -- as has happened in the past -- mere mouthpieces for the political views of their various owners.

As if the problem of retaining editorial independence is not enough of a headache, Nigeria's press has been adversely affected by the country's economic structural adjustment program. The devaluation of the country's currency, the naira, has made it difficult for the Nigerian Newspaper Manufacturing Company to afford the importation of raw ma-terials, leading to severe shortages.

The little newsprint available is costly. High paper prices have meant expensive newspapers on the streets, leading to reduced demand. Prices have more than doubled, with newspapers selling for N1.00 ($ .15), up from 50 kobo ($ .07) before the economic reform program. Erratic fluctuations in the naira's value, which also affects the price of newsprint, have made rational planning by publishers difficult. Numerous publications have been forced out of business.

Despite the daunting problems, most Nigerian journalists believe that their publications have largely lived up to their role as a watchdog for the people, even while they have maintained an emphasis, common to less industrialized countries, on "development" coverage.

But the price has been high. Journalists have discovered that, all too often, the sword is mightier than the pen. Late in 1986, Dele Giwa, the crusading founding editor of Newswatch was killed by a parcel bomb delivered to his home. Nobody has been charged with the murder, which has cast a pall over the efforts of other investigative journalists, who never know what reaction to expect when they break an important story. As Newswatch wrote in an editorial after its 1987 ban was lifted, practising journalism in Nigeria is like "walking blindfold through a minefield."

January 29, 1990


ZAMBIA: LINKING POPULATION AND ENVIRONMENT


LENGTH: 875 words


Lusaka -- Zambia's annual population growth rate of 3.7% -- one of the highest in Africa -- has become a major source of concern to the government.

At its independence in 1964, Zambia could afford to offer its three million citizens free medical care and education as well as subsidized food. Today, the country has a population of 7.5 million and is unable to provide them with even basic services.

Part of the problem is that Zambia, like many African countries, is heavily reliant on the export of commodities, whose prices fluctuate unpredictably on international markets. Copper, which provides 80% of Zambia's foreign exchange earnings, has dropped precipitously since the mid-1970s, despite a modest recovery last year. 

The result is a severe strain on health, housing and education. Thousands of children are being denied advancement beyond seventh grade because of limited places in secondary schools.

There is little the government can do to control copper prices. But it has begun to take firm measures to stem the population boom. Officials here have been formulating a policy designed to reduce the population growth rate to 3.4% by the year 2000 and to 2.5% by 2015. In its early stages, the program should enhance health and welfare by preventing premature deaths and illnesses, especially among mothers and children.

The government says the population policy will play a central role in national development strategies. The goal is to integrate a consideration of population factors into all development planning and to assure the right of every couple and individual to freely and responsibly decide on the number and spacing of their children.

At the same time, a privately-funded organization, the Environment and Population Center, has been formed to increase awareness of population issues among both policy makers and the general public. The group says it wants to dramatize the adverse effects of unchecked population growth, such as indiscriminate exploitation of the country's natural resources and the degradation of the environment.

Zambian government policy aims to slow population growth by initiating new measures and by improving and sustaining those already in place. Among the components of the new plan are a national program of education and assistance for family planning and an expansion of the country's population data base.

The Environment and Population Center will conduct briefings, seminars and workshops. It plans to produce and distribute information on such topical issues as women's reproductive health, women in development, the place of the child in society, adolescent pregnancy, migration patterns, deforestation and erosion trends, environmental protection and pollution control.

The Center's chair, Willa Mung'omba, a former president of the African Development Bank, says there are good reasons for linking population and environmental questions and for being concerned about both.

"The major one," he says, "is that the economies of the Third World are not growing as fast as their populations." In Zambia, if the current population growth rate is maintained until the year 2010, the country's population will more than double to about 15 million people. "To maintain a decent and satisfactory life," Mung'omba says, "the rate of economic growth will need to grow much faster that it has done since 1964."

"If this does not happen," Mung'omba warns, "we are likely to have a much poorer society than we have today, less educated, malnourished and without adequate health facilities to look after itself."

Mung'omba argues that the success of any population policy lies in the active participation of women. In most societies, women outnumber men, and most of them are poor. Because the poor in most countries have little influence over the course of national events, women are, in effect, powerless, he says.

A country like Zambia, he says, where the biggest sector of women are always poor, powerless and pregnant, cannot hope to have sustained development without drawing women fully into the development process.

On the linkage between environment and population, Mung'omba says that the technological revolution that has produced unprecedented levels of economic growth and prosperity for the industrialized world also has exacted immense and growing costs. The environmental resource base of the planet is being degraded, risking its life support system and, therefore, human health and well being.

"We are literally living off the earth's capital," Mung'omba warns, "and that capital is being seriously depleted. If you are a businessman, you will understand that we cannot continue to run our planet this way any more than we could run a business for long by running down and living off its capital."

At the current rate of exploitation, the Center's director says, the earth is headed for bankruptcy. And just as "a bankrupt company offers no hope for its employees, a bankrupt earth will leave its population lost and hopeless."

Mung'omba hopes that the Center's focus on both environmental and population problems will add a new dimension to the new national effort to curb Zambia's population growth and inform its people.

Africa News


January 29, 1990


DESIGNING DEMOCRACY


LENGTH: 108 words


For the first time in its history, the United Nations is joining a range of non-governmental organizations to host a major international conference. 

The meeting, scheduled for Arusha, Tanzania on February 16-20, is designed to attract grassroots participation from all over Africa to discuss "Popular Participation in Development." The conference schedule of workshops and demonstrations is designed to avoid the formality of most international gatherings and to make the conference itself a participatory event.

Organizers hope that the meeting will add momentum to calls for greater democracy in countries across the continent.

Africa News


January 29, 1990


WORRIES ATTEND NEW INITIATIVES


SECTION: DEVELOPMENT


LENGTH: 1731 words


Only dramatic action can avert the continuing decline of African living standards during the 1990s, according to the latest assessment of Africa's economies by the United Nations Economic Commission for Africa (ECA). But African planners are increasingly fearful that international events will further strain the continent's resources and blunt its ability to implement necessary reforms. The attention and financial resources being directed towards Eastern Europe are, to many Africans, a realization of those fears.

Presenting preliminary figures for Africa's performance during 1989, ECA Executive-Secretary Adebayo Adedeji said the year will be most remembered as the end of a lost decade of development opportunities. In a January 15 statement at ECA headquarters in Addis Ababa, Ethiopia, Adedeji pointed to a rapid deterioration in social welfare and the disintegration of productive capacity and infrastructural facilities during the 1980s.

In 1989, Africa's GDP (its overall output of goods and services) grew by 2.9%. Although that was an improvement on the previous year's growth of 2.4%, per capita incomes continued to decline. 

Because population is rising faster than the rate of economic growth, the average person's income fell by 0.7% in 1988 and dropped 0.3% last year.

The ECA figures portray a continent highly vulnerable to factors beyond its control. Much of last year's improved output is attributed to exceptionally good weather conditions. And because Africa's relatively small industrial sector is heavily reliant on agricultural materials, better agricultural performance contributed to increased manufacturing output.

Among the challenges Africans must confront is the growing economic integration of Europe. Many fear that Africa, faced with a formidable new trading bloc, will become even less able to escape the legacy of colonialism, which left African countries with weak economies oriented towards producing the raw materials which fueled European growth.

A boom in prices for those commodities in the early post-colonial years encouraged African governments to plan -- and lenders to advance the funds -- for ambitious and often ill-conceived development projects. But steady declines in the world commodity market since the mid-1970s has left Africa saddled with debts it is unable to repay.

Part of Africa's economic growth in 1989 is due to an improvement in some commodity prices. Higher international fuel prices boosted export earnings for the region's oil producers and countries like Zambia and Zaire have benefited from a rise in copper earnings.

At the same time, prices for commodities that provide virtually all the foreign exchange earnings for many countries continued a downward plunge. Leading the decline were cocoa and robusta coffee, the principle variety grown in Africa. Cocoa prices dropped nearly 17% last year while coffee fell by 12.5%.

In this environment, Adedeji said, the nations of Africa must look to their own resources and strengths. Echoing the themes of recent ECA and African proposals for economic recovery, Adedeji said African governments must implement a new generation of economic and political reforms.

The primary document laying out the emerging African concensus on economic policies is the African Alternative Framework to Structural Adjustment Programs. AAF-SAP, as it is called, says that African countries must curb spending, diversify exports, and switch expenditures to the most promising sectors for self-sufficiency, such as food production. And they must democratize the development process, including freeing women to participate more fully.

AAF-SAP argues -- and Adedeji in his presentation emphasized -- that regional cooperation within Africa must replace some of the continent's dependence on the industrialized nations.

Africans must rely on themselves, Adedeji said. Donors can "give a helping hand, and no more."

Still, no one argues that Africa can recover -- or even arrest its decline -- on its own. Every plan that has been advanced for Africa, whether from Western financial institutions like the World Bank or Africa's own proposals, calls for increased levels of international assistance.

And that, say the analysts, is unlikely. In fact, total American assistance to sub-Saharan Africa for the last four years has already declined over the levels of the mid-1980s, dropping from just over $ 2 billion in 1985 toa proposed level of slightly more than $ 1 billion for this year (although emergency food aid could push the final total higher).

African leaders have watched with some uneasiness as events in Eastern Europe and Central America have monopolized media coverage of international affairs.

"What worries everybody," says Bertram Collins, who heads the Africa Recovery office at the United Nations, "is that there may be a preoccupation with other parts of the world whose interests are now on the front pages of every newspaper and whose concerns will certainly attract a good deal of the attention of the world's leading policy makers -- and perhaps also a large part of the resources available for rehabilitation, reconstruction and development" in Africa.

The U.S. Congress late last year voted $ 840 million in economic assistance over the next three years

to Poland and Hungary alone. President George Bush pledged that similar aid would flow to other Eastern European countries which adopt economic and political reforms, while talk was heard on Capitol Hill that Panama's needs would top $ 1 billion.

African worries about where those resources would be found were not soothed by public discussion of the issue in the U.S. In a series of interviews and statements, the Acting Director of the U.S. Agency for International Development, Mark Edelman, has pointed out that in the new aid game, somebody has to lose. "Money for Eastern Europe will come out of what would otherwise have gone to Central America, Africa and the Middle East," Edelman said, indicating that as many as a dozen nations that would normally expect to receive U.S. aid this year will now get nothing.

In Kenya, where government officials have been expressing concern about the future of Western aid funds, Nairobi's Daily Nation news-paper quoted U.S. Ambassador

Smith Hempstone as saying that Washington's interest in Eastern Europe will deplete the resources previously allocated to Africa.

"You see the source is still the same," Hempstone told the paper, "but the recipients are increasing."

Other U.S. officials say Africa probably will not be caught in that squeeze, and certainly not this year. One of the reasons is that a significant chunk of assistance to Africa comes from the Development Fund for Africa (DFA) -- a portion of worldwide development aid that Congress set aside for Africa for 1988 and has continued to fund. The DFA designates a pot of funds -- separate from other aid monies -- to programs that deliver development assistance to Africa, so that they can be more flexible and responsive to Africa's special needs. Projected DFA funds for this year are approximately $ 569 million. "There is a conscience-driven momentum for keeping at least that level of assistance going for Africa," says one State Department economics officer.

On the other hand, the official concedes, there are "natural and psychological to say nothing of strategic reasons" for guessing that African aid won't rise. "The Africans have been getting a fair piece of money for a long time, and only recently have a lot of them been trying to use it in ways that we would think were good," the official says. "The Eastern Europeans haven't had the chance, haven't fed at this particular trough -- either the World Bank, the U.S. or the Japanese trough."

Africans see things differently. For some time now, many African economic planners have been arguing that their countries have been making the painful economic adjustments that international financial institutions like the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund have advocated. African nations have been moving towards more open economies and away from government ownership and central control. In addition, some are, even in the midst of the economic dislocations that accompany the reforms, moving towards more participatory political systems. Yet, they say, Western governments have fallen short of the commitments they made to support these difficult processes.

Now, Africans see similar reforms in Eastern Europe generating broad enthusiasm and immediate financial backing.

That's natural, says the State Department official. "In our case, cultural and historic links are much closer to Europe than they are to anywhere else. A lot more Polish-Americans live in the United States than, say, Malian-Americans. And that does make a difference."

The difference a domestic lobby makes can clearly be seen in the case of Israel, which, with a population of only four million, gets $ 3 billion this year. One North African country

does benefit from the Israeli constituency though; Egypt, whose aid from the U.S. is tied to Israel's by a formula agreed upon in the Camp David accords, is getting around $ 2.1 billion in 1989 -- twice the amount going to all of sub-Saharan Africa.

Bertram Collins says that there is an emerging consensus between African countries and international donors about the way ahead for Africa. Despite some continuing differences on strategies, Africans accept the need for far-reaching reforms -- and donors have recognized the danger of quick fixes that focus only on balanced budgets. Collins points to a major World Bank report released late last year (see Africa News, Dec. 25, 1989) as an important document "that admits to certain errors and failures in the past." It calls for increased support for far-sighted programs in such areas as health care and education which have been hardest hit by economic adjustment. "That would have been front page news at another time," says Collins. But developments in the Soviet Union and its allies have pushed Africa out of the story.

"I am concerned that Africa might become increasingly marginalized," Collins says, "and that the good will that exists and the clear vision which is now becoming more and more evident will not bring about the results we all want."

Africa News


January 29, 1990


FAVORED FEW TAP ZAIRE WEALTH


LENGTH: 3821 words


 

This article is part of a year-long research project on the secrets of Zairean state finances. The investigation was begun by Africa News contributor Steve Askin, with follow up by Reed Kramer. An earlier report (Africa News, June 12, 1989) documented the disappearance of funds from the Zaire treasury and apparent diversions of export revenues.

Askin's writings for the Christian Science Monitor and Business Week were the first to explore the role of a senior member of Congress, Rep. Mervyn Dymally (D-CA), in promoting business opportunities in Africa for his associates. Further articles probing Dymally's activities have appeared in recent months in Legal Times, the Journal of Commerce, the Los Angeles Times, and the Washington Post.

Askin's forthcoming book on Zaire under Mobutu explores more more fully the complex web of financial connections between the Zaire government and a number of leading Americans. 

The only extensive recent interview with President Mobutu Sese Seko to appear in the U.S. media was published by Africa News on March 7, 1988. The interview was conducted by Dr. Jeffrey Elliot, a political science professor from North Carolina Central University and Rep. Mervyn Dymally, on his first visit to Zaire. Elliot and Dymally had previously collaborated on an interview with Fidel Castro for Playboy.

Africa News received the full transcript of a several-hour question and answer session with Mobutu, which we edited to be one of a four-part series on Zaire. Elliot and Dymally were paid at our standard per-word rate.

In its April and May, 1988 issues, the French monthly Jeane Afrique Magazine reprinted the interview from Africa News.

Next month, the full text, as well as Elliot/Dymally interviews with seven other Zaireans including two Mobutu opponents, will be released in book form, Voices of Zaire: Rhetoric or Reality, by the Washington Institute Press.

At a time when Africa is actively courting American business, U.S. firms have found their path to one of Africa's largest mining companies blocked by an unorthodox arrangement with a New Jersey food broker.

President Mobutu Sese Seko of Zaire has granted a lucrative contract to a company controlled by one of his long-time American supporters, Grover Connell. Under the agreement, Connell Rice and Sugar Company holds exclusive rights to procure most of the equipment, spare parts and other items obtained in the U.S. by Gecamines, the government-owned mineral giant in Zaire.

To the handful of diplomats, bankers and corporate officials who have known about it, the Connell contract illustrates the extent to which U.S. relations with Zaire revolve around a group of prominent Americans, whose ability to do business there is based on personal ties to Mobutu, and whose financial interests are closely intertwined with efforts to win friends and influence for Zaire and its leader.

Although not a public figure, Grover Connell is a wealthy businessman with good political connections. On Capitol Hill, he is well-known for an unusual $ 84,000-a-year seminar program, under which members of Congress have been receiving honoraria for visiting Connell headquarters in Westfield, New Jersey. According to data compiled by Common Cause, Connell Rice disbursed $ 2,000 each, the legal limit, to 39 House members and three Senators in 1988 and paid out the same amount in 1987.

The program makes Connell the largest corporate donor of Congressional honoraria, a controversial channel for political contributions. (Under the pay-raise legislation adopted by the House of Representatives in December, members are now barred from accepting honoraria, but the Senate still permits the practice.) Only four large trade associations, led by the Tobacco Institute, gave more to members of Congress than Connell did in 1988, the last year for which figures are available.

Earlier this month, four House members who have received Connell honoraria travelled with Grover Connell to Zaire, where his company handles the largest share of U.S. government food aid contracts under the Food for Peace program.

Zaire, a strategically-located central African nation bordering nine other countries, has one of the world's most impoverished populations and one of its most flamboyantly wealthy leaders. Since seizing control of the former Belgian Congo in a 1965 CIA-backed coup, Mobutu has held virtually absolute sovereignty. He also relies heavily on support from Western governments, particularly France and the U.S. -- and sometimes from Belgium, Zaire's former colonial ruler with whom relations have been stormy.

Successive American administrations have backed Mobutu on the premise that he is the only leader capable of holding together the vast nation -- about the size of the U. S. east of the Mississippi River -- and a treasure house of minerals.

But Mobutu's reputation for human rights abuses, lavish spending and corruption has tarnished his public image. "Congress has restricted aid to Zaire partly because it is a plutocracy where resources are diverted from their assigned destinations and have historically been so," Rep. Howard Wolpe, chairman of the House Africa subcommittee said last year. "We have seen, in this subcommittee, evidence that literally hundreds and hundreds of millions of dollars have vanished into the hands or the bank accounts of the president and his collaborators."

According to diplomatic sources, Connell Rice and Sugar has been collecting a 30% commission on Gecamines purchases in the U.S. since the contract was signed in early 1988. The purchases normally average about $ 5 million a month, providing annual earnings for the Connell company of more than $ 15 million. These fees are "far out of line with anything anyone else is charging," says one knowledgeable official. Although he expressed his willingness to talk with Africa News, Connell repeatedly failed to be available for an interview.

There was heavy resistance to the Connell contract at Gecamines, says the official. "They had no desire to have this rice dealer charge them for something they had been doing for themselves," the official says. But Mobutu -- whose photograph appears on the frontispiece of the annual report -- effectively controls the company by appointing its top officials. And he apparently wanted the contract signed.

At the World Bank and International Monetary Fund, where Zaire is seeking assistance to meet payments on its crippling $ 8 billion foreign debt, Connell's lock on procurement and his price tag have caused dismay. Gecamines is by far the country's largest business, producing copper and cobalt worth about $ 800 million to $1.3 billion a year (depending on market conditions) and providing up to three-quarters of Zaire's export earnings.

Because of its vital role in contributing foreign exchange to Zaire's ailing economy, Gecamines is kept under close scrutiny by the international lending institutions, and irregularities have been uncovered throughout the mining company's history.

Although the contract with Connell is seen as "perfectly legal," according to one knowledgeable official, there is concern that the high commissions are reducing revenues that are badly needed for Zaire's overdue loan payments and for budgetary outlays under an IMF economic stabilization program.

Following protests by Zaire's lenders and donors, Gecamines payments to Connell have been reduced, the official says. A number of Gecamines suppliers are now refusing to work through Connell and are making sales directly to the mining company, as they did before the contract was signed, industry sources report.

Still, the contract is regarded as part of a continuing pattern under which Zaire's budget and its mining revenues serve as a "private pool of funds for Mobutu and his friends," in the words of one senior international economist.

"Frankly, I'm not sure what the hell is happening to that money," says a lending institution officer. But many officials are convinced the funds are somehow being rerouted to seek greater support for Zaire in Washington and around the country.

For more than a quarter of a century, Mobutu has assiduously wooed America's leaders. He has been a frequent visitor to the White House since 1963, when, as head of the army but not yet head of state, he was received by John F. Kennedy. He has already managed three separate encounters with President George Bush -- more than any other African head of state -- twice at the White House last year and once at Emperor Hirohito's funeral in Tokyo.

Bush is probably the closest friend Mobutu has had in the Oval Office. By Mobutu's count, the two men have met 15 times since Bush first visited Kinshasa in 1972 while serving as U.S. ambassador to the United Nations. Bush has sailed on Mobutu's luxurious Zaire River yacht, and the Zaire president has been included in a congenial family dinner at the Bush retreat in Kennebunkport, Maine.

Mobutu told interviewers last year that Bush came to "know Zaire's problems by heart" during his year as head of the CIA.

On the Democratic side, Mobutu's most important and longest-standing relationship has been with New York diamond magnate Maurice Tempelsman, a leading political fund-raiser who is also current chair of the African-American Institute. His business involvement in the country is as old as the Mobutu government.

Over the years, Tempelsman has served as an important intermediary with both Democratic and Republican administrations and has negotiated business deals in Zaire worth hundreds of millions of dollars for American, South African and Japanese firms.

More recently, the Zaire leader has developed ties with several members of the Congressional Black Caucus, particularly the immediate past chair, Mervyn Dymally of California. In 1988, at Dymally's request, Mobutu contributed $ 250,000 to a California charity, Coalition for a Free Africa (CFA), founded by Dick Griffey, a friend and campaign contributor to both Dymally and former presidential candidate Jesse Jackson.

Mobutu has also made extensive use of paid lobbyists to plead his case in Washington. Currently, Zaire employs two firms. One of them, van Kloberg and Associates, headed by Edward J. van Kloberg, III, has a history of representing controversial governments, among them Zaire's closest East European ally, Romania. Zaire's other contract is with Black, Manafort, Stone, and Kelly, a public relations company with high-level Republican ties which also represents the Unita rebel movement of Angola, headed by Jonas Savimbi.

Some of Zaire's earlier Washington representatives were drawn from the world of the fabulously wealthy. In the mid-1980s, they included Nael el-Assad, a relative of Saudi Arabian arms merchant Adnan Khashoggi, once one of the richest non-head-of-states in the world; and Robert Maheu, the ex-FBI man who helped the CIA recruit a Mafia hitman to try to kill Fidel Castro and in the 1970s served as chief assistant to that decade's richest American, the late Howard Hughes.

Altogether, Zaire has spent over $ 3 million in the past five years seeking to influence American policy. But the debate in Washington has shifted very little. Each administration has continued supporting Mobutu, seeing him as the only viable option for pro-tecting the interests of Zaire, while his critics in Congress have fought to restrict and reduce foreign aid.

The latest and most serious challenge to the Zaire leader on Capitol Hill is the near-total aid cutoff -- on grounds of "uncontrollable corruption" -- proposed by Rep. Ronald Dellums (D-CA), who chairs the Congressional Black Caucus. When he first introduced the legislation in 1988, Dellums received the backing of most of his Caucus colleagues and many other members as well. But Mobutu has made inroads into that support by aggressively reaching out to the Caucus membership.

At the center of the effort has been Mamadi Diane, a Guinean-born businessman who heads a shipping brokerage firm, Amex International, which is the agent for U.S. government food aid shipments to Zaire.

Diane and Connell have business connections dating back to the mid-1970s, when Diane worked for the St. John International Company. Connell was part owner of an affiliate company, St. John Maritime, which was implicated in the Tongsun Park Koreagate rice-buying scandal.

Park, a U.S.-educated Korean businessman, gave hundreds of thousands of dollars to members of Congress, both to boost support for South Korea's military dictatorship and to win Food for Peace contracts for his business associates. He was convicted of making bribes, but charges against Connell were dismissed in 1979, after Park withdrew his allegations that Connell knew about the scheme.

Today, all three men have close ties to Mobutu, as well as overlapping African involvements. Diane serves as shipping agent for Food for Peace consignments to Zaire, while Connell Rice supplies much of the food.

Park's precise relationship with Mobutu remains a mystery, but he appears to be playing both a political and a business role. When Rep. Dan Burton of Indiana, the ranking Republican on the House Africa subcommittee, stopped off in Zaire after visiting Savimbi in Angola last year, he says he was surprised to encounter Park at Gbadolite, Mobutu's presidential palace. Last June, according to columnist Jack Anderson, Park was present when Paul Manafort met with Mobutu to secure the lobbying contract.

Both Connell and Diane have been active in promoting Congressional visits to Zaire. The four members of the House who accompanied Connell there this month include two liberal Democrats from his state, Robert Torricelli and Robert Mrazek, as well as Mike Espy, a black Democrat from Mississippi, and Bill Lowery, a Republican from California. Torricelli has received $ 6,000 in honoraria from Connell over three years, Lowery $ 4,000 and Mrazek and Espy $ 2,000 each.

Two years ago, Mrazek authored a letter to Mobutu, co-signed by 47 other House members, which expressed disquiet about "persistent reports of widespread government corruption and human rights abuses." Torricelli and Espy were among the co-signers. Mrazek says he is still concerned about political freedoms in Zaire, but he is hopeful of a "more forthcoming attitude on the part of the Zairean government with respect to human rights." Mrazek says his airfare was paid out of Congressional travel funds; while in Zaire the four Congressmen were guests of the Zaire government.

Diane has organized trips to Zaire for Dymally and other black members of Congress, and he has actively lobbied Zaire's cause on Capitol Hill. In 1988, he established the Zaire American Research Institute (ZARI) in Washington, D.C. to "promote friendship, cooperation, and improved relations" and to engage in research and disseminate information, according to incorporation papers filed with the District of Columbia government. The organization's 1988 tax records indicate that initial funding of $ 100,000 was used to open an office on K Street and hire an executive director, Marva Jo Camp, who had been an aide to Dymally. Last year, Camp was replaced by Glenwood Roane, an attorney who has been director in three African countries for the U.S. Agency for International Development, AID.

Funding for the institute has come from Zaire businessmen, Roane said in an interview. The largest contributor has been Bemba Saolona, chairman of Zaire's principal business organization. Bemba is widely regarded as one of Mobutu's closest advisors and business associates. He owns a string of manufacturing, agricultural and distribution companies, as well as SCIBE, the country's second largest airline.

Roane says Bemba contributed about $ 90,000 to ZARI in 1989 -- out of a total budget of some $ 220,000. Another $12,000 was given by the General Motors subsidiary in Zaire, GMAZ. In addition, Bemba provided air transport for a group of black mayors who were taken to Zaire by ZARI in June. In May, ZARI received an $ 80,000 transfer from the Swiss Bank account of Idrissa Toure, a Guinean believed to be associated with Diane in several business ventures.

In the coming year, Roane says, ZARI will concentrate on helping Zaire formulate a "responsible policy" for handling its immense rain forest. "President Mobutu is fully aware of the importance of that resource," Roane says, "and we want to move quickly to assist efforts to preserve it." Whether Zaire can obtain a reduction of its debt by promising to preserve its forests, in the sort of "debt-for-nature" exchange that international lending agencies have been discussing with Brazil, is undetermined. But ZARI hopes to coordinate an international conference to examine all aspects of the rain forest question to be held in Zaire late this year.

While ZARI and Diane have concentrated on the Black Caucus, van Kloberg has focussed on improving Zaire's image among other members of Congress and in the news media. Working under a two-year, $ 300,000 agreement, the firm made most of the arrangements for Mobutu's official trip to Washington last July, which included not only an Oval Office visit but also meetings on Capitol Hill and a session with reporters at Blair House. Van Kloberg also handled a follow-up trip by the vice prime minister, Nimy Mayidika Ngimbi, whom Mobutu has put in charge of "citizen's rights and liberties."

More recently, the firm has put together two issues of a newsletter, Zaire News and plans to establish a Zaire-American friendship committee, composed of prominent figures from both countries.

After his June visit, Mobutu beefed up the PR effort. He signed a one year, $ 1 million contract with Black, Manafort, Stone and Kelly immediately following his departure from the U.S., where he reportedly wasangered by criticisms of his government in news reports and from members of Congress. The Black Manafort firm has managed three successful visits for Jonas Savimbi, whose warm welcome here is something that has eluded Mobutu. Charles Black and Paul Manafort, the firms' principals, played leading

roles in the Bush presidential campaign of 1988, alongside their former partner, Lee Atwater, the Bush campaign manager and current chair of the Republican National Committee.

In the forefront of the campaign against Mobutu over the past two years has been the Rainbow Lobby, a Washington, D.C.-based group affiliated with the New Alliance Party but unconnected to Jesse Jackson's Rainbow Coalition. The Rainbow Lobby has actively promoted the Dellums Bill and has forged links with leading Zaire opposition figures. The group has attacked Dymally for his Zaire-related activities, and Dymally has responded in kind.

Last year, Glenwood Roane of ZARI sued the Lobby for defamation in connection with remarks made by Howard University professor Nzongola Ntalaja at a Rainbow Lobby-sponsored seminar. But Roane has recently dropped the suit. In another counter-attack, a forthcoming article in the magazine American Spectator by David Brock, who is highly critical of Mobutu's Congressional adversaries, alleges that the Lobby "set up" Rep. Gus Savage (D-IL) to keep him from joining the Black Caucus defections from the Dellums bill. Savage has been accused by a Peace Corps volunteer of sexually molesting her during a visit Savage made to Zaire with Diane last year.

Van Kloberg has led the campaign against the Rainbow Lobby and is credited with achieving results on Capitol Hill for his controversial client. Black Manafort's role, on the other hand, remains fuzzy. "We never see them at all," says one administration aide. As with the Connell contract with Gecamines, insiders are asking what the firm is doing to earn its high fee. Black Manafort officials won't comment on their activities.

Dymally's role in obtaining gifts and contracts in Africa for political associates has provoked questions about possible conflicts of interest, including a complaint to the House Ethics Committee from the U.S.- Congo Friendship Committee, a Rainbow Lobby ally.

In June, the Journal of Commerce reported that Dymally had written to Ugandan President Yoweri Museveni, protesting his govern-ment's decision to hire as its agents for food aid shipments "a firm other than Amex International," Diane's company.

Dymally has also intervened with the Angolan government to obtain oil shipping contracts for Dick Griffey, who owns Solar Records and the African Development Public Interest Corporation (ADPIC), a Hollywood trading company. Griffey says during one trip to Luanda, Dymally introduced him to Angolan officials and said "the best way" for the government to resolve its problems with the United States is "to make contact with the African-American community" and "to find a black American and do business with him." Angola has been seeking to convince Washington to end its support for the Unita insurgency and normalize diplomatic relations.

On another occasion, the Washington Post has reported, Dymally used a meeting in Washington with Angolan oil minister Pedro de Castro Van-Dunem Loy to ask that Luanda expedite processing of a $ 12 million shipment already agreedto for Griffey's ADPIC.

In 1988, Griffey's Coalition for a Free Africa paid part of the expenses for part of an African tour which took him to seven nations, and later to Brazil. During his travels, Dymally says he sought to raise additional funding for CFA. So far, the only sizeable donation the coalition has received beyond Mobutu's $ 250,000 is some $300,000 contributed by Griffey, according to Virgil Roberts, Griffey's general counsel and president of ADPIC.

Dymally also sought an oil contract in Angola for Camp, the former ZARI executive director and part-time Dymally aide. He made the request in a letter to Angola's ambassador in New York Manuel Pacavira.

Both Griffey and Dymally adamently dispute charges that they are involved in conflicts of interest or profiteering at Africa's expense. Dymally insists that his efforts are a legitimate and important part of his role as a black political leader. "I see nothing wrong with helping black businessmen get contracts in Zaire or anywhere else," Dymally said in an interview. Criticism of him for these actions "is all racism," he says. White Americans and Europeans have a corner on most business opportunities in Africa," he charges, "and they don't want to share them."

Griffey says he is not getting involved in Africa to make money. The $ 250,000 Mobutu contribution to CFA had "nothing to do," Griffey says, with hisbusiness activities.

"I'm going to do something that helps the people," he says. "I see Africa as the new frontier, the world's richest continent, the world's poorest people."

REMEMBER AFRICA


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 97 words


Pope John Paul II, ended an eight-day five-nation tour of west and central Africa this month with an appeal that the continent and its needs not be forgotten in the international excitement over events in Europe and the Soviet Union.

The appeal carried additional weight because it came from a pontiff who was Polish by birth and surely has a keen interest in the revolutions underway in socialist countries.

The Pope visited Cape Verde, Guinea-Bissau, Mali, Burkina Faso and Chad, all Sahelian countries where the standard of living is among the poorest in the world.

AIDS HOPE FROM KENYA


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 310 words


Researchers from the Kenya Medical Research Institute (Kemri) have formally announced the development of a drug which they say eliminated most of the symptoms of AIDS during clinical trials.

News of the drug's apparent success first was made public in December, when Kemri celebrated its tenth anniversary. Although some local press reports hailed the trials as a breakthrough, with headlines suggesting that a cure for AIDS had been found, the magazine Weekly Review criticized Kemri for announcing the drug before publishing the research in a scientific journal.

"An announcement without professional or academic publishing has little or no standing in scientific circles," wrote Weekly Review editor Hilary Ng'weno.

Still, in a cover story on the drug, called KEO89, the magazine said the new treatment shows genuine promise.

At a press conference in Nairobi last Tuesday, Kemri director Dr. David Keoch stressed that the interferon-based drug offers no cure for AIDS. But he said trials on more than 100 patients at Kenyatta National Hospital had reversed most symptoms within weeks after therapy began, without serious side affects. The only drug currently recognized as an effective AIDS treatment, AZT, shows results only

after being taken for longer periods and its side effects are severe.

Keoch said the clinical results of KEO89, which is being developed in collaboration with Texas and Japanese-based laboratories, will soon be published.

This is not the first time in recent months that Kenyan scientists have shared results of potentially important research. Late last year the International Center for Insect Parasitology and Ecology, located just outside Nairobi, was able to demonstrate that its inexpensive insect traps could virtually eliminate the destructive tsetse fly from test tracts of infested land.

LANGUAGE: English

Africa News


February 12, 1990


EDGING TOWARDS TALKS


LENGTH: 752 words


President F.W. de Klerk's February 2 speech has left all the political actors in South Africa, including his own ruling National Party, scrambling to find new ground on which to stand. The decisions to unban the African National Congress (ANC) and other opposition groups, suspend the death penalty, lift restrictions on political activists and the media, and release Nelson Mandela have required a re-evaluation of both tactics and strategy by all sides.

It will take some time before the positions of the government and the now-legal ANC are formulated sufficiently to make possible real negotiations on power sharing in the country.

The ANC last year set forth its preconditions for negotiations in the Harare Declaration adopted by the Organization of African Unity and the United Nations General Assembly (see Africa News, December 4 and 25, 1989). These include:

* releasing of all political prisoners and detainees unconditionally;

* lifting all bans and restrictions on organizations and persons;

* removing all troops from the townships;

* ending the state of emergency and repealing all legislation, including the Internal Security Act, designed to circumscribe political activity; and 

* ceasing all political trials and political executions.

Similar preconditions are detailed in the U.S. Comprehensive Anti-Apartheid Act of 1986 (for a summary, see Africa News, August 1989). Congress determined that the sanctions against South Africa imposed by the Act would automatically expire if the South African government agrees to:

* free Nelson Mandela and "all persons persecuted for their political beliefs or detained unduly without trial;"

* repeal the state of emergency and release all detainees;

* legalize "democratic political parties;"

* repeal the Groups Areas Act and the Population Registration Act; and

* enter into "good faith negotiations with truly representative members of the black majority without preconditions."

The president also is empowered to lift or modify the sanctions, after giving Congress 30 days notice to disapprove. The South African government must take the first step outlined above, along with three of the other stipulations, and make "substantial progress towards dismantling the system of apartheid and establishing a non-racial democracy."

A comparison of these two sets of criteria with the decisions outlined in de Klerk's speech, indicates that the South African government has now agreed to much -- but not all -- of what has been demanded.

The Human Rights Commission in South Africa says only about 25% of the approximately 2,500 convicted political prisoners now jailed will be released by de Klerk's announcement that Nelson Mandela, and all other persons imprisoned for belonging to previously-proscribed organizations, will be freed.

Although the State of Emergency remains in place, de Klerk removed various regulations restricting education and the media, while retaining the ban on television coverage of unrest. In a statement last week, the Washington, D.C.-based Lawyers' Committee for Civil Rights Under Law said that even if "these draconian provisions of the State of Emergency regulations are repealed, the South African government retains all of those powers and more under the permanent legislation, the Internal Security Act, which remains unaffected by President de Klerk's announced reforms."

On the toleration of dissent, in addition to unbanning the ANC, the government legalized the Pan Africanist Congress and the South African Communist Party, which was outlawed in 1950. Restrictions were also lifted on another 33 anti-apartheid groups.

De Klerk has taken no action to pull soldiers and police from black townships, nor has he asked parliament to repeal the Group Areas Act or the Population Registration Act. The ANC, however, has not made abolition of racially exclusive legislation a pre-condition for talks, leaving this for negotiation.

Although he did not abolish political executions, as the Harare declaration asked, de Klerk went further than most observers expected by completely suspending executions, pending an inquiry into the issue of death sentences.

Government opponents, including the ANC, have called de Klerk's speech an important first step towards creating a "climate for negotiations." Although wide gaps still di-vide the two sides, both appear to be groping towards a common ground that would enable talks about South Africa's future to begin.

LANGUAGE: English

Africa News


February 12, 1990


SOUTH AFRICA: POLICE HATCHET MAN FLED TO ANC


SECTION: DEATH SQUADS


LENGTH: 1179 words


Harare, Zimbabwe -- Former South African policeman Dirk Coetzee buries his head in his hands during a conversation about his role in death squad activities. Asked how he would feel if he came face to face with two children whose father he had murdered in 1981, Coetzee, who once commanded a police counter-insurgency unit, visibly fights to contain his emotions.

Coetzee has admitted that he participated in killing human rights lawyer Griffiths Mxenge, who was a prominent defender of political prisoners, including members of the African National Congress (ANC). Coetzee says he acted under orders fromtop police officials, who told him to make the murder appear to be part of a robbery attempt. Mxenge was hacked to death and his skull bashed in by African policemen under Coetzee's command. Three years later, Mxenge's widow Victoria, also a lawyer, was assassinated in front of her Durban home. 

"I couldn't face the Mxenge children right now, nor any of the women I made widows," says Dirk Coetzee, struggling both with English and with remorse. "Maybe some years from now, after I have testified to everyone I can and exposed my superiors, maybe then they will be able to forgive me."

Coetzee says he was a senior member of a counter-insurgency unit operating from a farm outside Pretoria, South Africa's administrative capital. He describes a number of similar units that functioned as death squads to kill hundreds of anti-apartheid activists inside South Africa, in neighboring countries and in Europe.

Social anthropologist David Webster was the leading researcher into political assassinations until he himself was assassinated last year. Shortly before being gunned down outside his Johannesburg home, Webster wrote that at least 121 anti-apartheid campaigners -- 60 in South Africa and 61 in exile -- had been killed since 1978. Those numbers were given credibility late last year, when another former member of a police anti-terrorist unit, Butana Almond Nofomela, detailed his involvements in numerous killings of suspected or known activists. Nofomela named former Captain Dirk Coetzee as the field commander of his assassination squad (see Africa News, December 4, 1989).

In the wake of the Nofomela allegations, Coetzee fled, first to the Indian Ocean island of Mauritius -- where he told his story to a reporter for the anti-apartheid Afrikaans-language newspaper Vrye Weekblad -- and then to London.

"I realized it was all over," says Coetzee. "I knew I would be charged, I knew my superiors would duck and dive and dump me, and I knew if I told the truth in South Africa they would get me."

After the Coetzee interview was published in November, South African human rights groups pushed for an independent investigation into the charges of both Coetzee and Nofomela. Government officials initially said that police investigators could handle the job. But on January 31, South African Justice Minister Kobie Coetsee said President de Klerk had decided to appoint an independent judicial Commission of Inquiry into the allegations. In response, the Campaign for a Judicial Inquiry Into Hit Squads canceled a protest march scheduled for February 1.

Dirk Coetzee says now that it was the ANC underground inside South Africa, with which he was able to make contact, who helped arrange his escape from the country. When he got to London, he says, he was questioned at length by Jacob Xuma, a member of the National Executive Committee, the ANC's policy-making body. "I spilled my guts out," Coetzee says. "I told him if he didn't believe my story, I would need help for four months to get on my feet, get a job, so my wife and two children could join me."

But the ANC did believe him. The details he gave of his activities coincided with growing revelations from other former policemen and from the ANC's own intelligence.

After three days of conversations in London, Xuma and Coetzee flew to Zimbabwe. Here he has spent his time telling ANC officials about the inner workings of the death squads, implicating scores of men he says are still in the police force, as well as a former Minister of Police, Louis le Grange, now speaker of South Africa's parliament.

In an interview with no ANC members present, Coetzee says he underwent a transformation in the weeks he has been closeted with ANC intelligence operatives. "I discovered the South African government had lied to me all my life," he says. "They told me the ANC were communist terrorists who wanted to push whites into the sea. I hunted them down, I murdered without any conscience. I felt I was justified that I was saving South Africa."

Coetzee says that Jacob Xuma and the other ANC members he has met have convinced him that he himself was a victim of apartheid. "Last September," he says, "I voted for the ¢far-right/ Conservative Party. Now I am with the ANC. That's because I learned the truth. I will serve the ANC in whatever way they decide for the rest of my life." He says he would like other policemen, especially those who are black, to understand that they are "just cannon fodder" who will be dumped when the going gets tough.

Lawyers from South Africa are expected to visit Coetzee at the ANC's exile headquarters in Lusaka, Zambia, to take evidence for the Commission of Inquiry. Coetzee says he would also like an opportunity to share his experiences in Europe and the United States.

A cynical but common view of Coetzee is that he has cleverly saved his life by defecting to the ANC. But those who have heard Coetzee apologize to families of his victims in an anguish-strangled voice find it easy to believe his sincerity.

¢Police officials in South Africa argue that Coetzee was a renegade who had already left the force under a cloud, after a 1983 departmental hearing into various criminal activities, such as drug dealing and pornography, within police ranks. Some have suggested that his fervent embrace of the ANC, like his previous "true believer" work in death squads, may be a sign of a mental instability caused by diabetes, which was the formal reason for his premature retirement from the police./

Whatever Coetzee's original motivation or current state of mind, many of his charges are expected to receive further support. The ANC says that within weeks it will hold a press conference to present about a dozen of Coetzee's death squad colleagues whose names have not yet been released, in addition to two former secret policemen, Heine Human and Martin Dolincheck.

Human, a Swede who left South Africa in December, reportedly has told the ANC that he was involved in arrangements to assassinate the organization's representative in France, Dulcie September, who was killed in 1988.

Dolincheck had been sentenced to death in the Indian Ocean island nation of Seychelles, after a South African-sponsored mercenary group failed in a 1981 attempt to overthrow the government of President Albert Rena. He was reprieved and allowed to return to South Africa a year later, but defected to the ANC in 1986.

HORN OF AFRICA: MILLIONS TO DIE IF FOOD SHUT OFF


SECTION: FAMINE


LENGTH: 804 words


With starvation threatening as many as ten million people in the Horn of Africa, pressure is growing on the military regimes in both Ethiopia and Sudan to allow relief shipments to reach needy populations.

Much of the immediate focus is on the creation of safe zones of passage for relief supplies in the war-ravaged regions of the two countries. Last month, Senators Edward M. Kennedy (D-MA) and Alan K. Simpson (R-WY) issued a joint appeal for the opening of "mercy corridors" for the delivery of food to stricken areas under the auspices of the United Nations.

United Nations Secretary General Javier Perez de Cuellar has also called upon the Ethiopian government and two rebel movements to allow distribution of food to needy civilians, and various church agencies have made similar requests. The UN has been trying to launch Operation Lifeline II in Sudan, modeled on a largely-successful campaign in 1989, to get provisions into strife-torn areas. 

However, John Prendergast, a coordinator of the church-related Coalition for Peace in the Horn of Africa in Washington, D.C., says the "endless negotiations" over safe passage of relief supplies are "ill-fated." During the 1984 famine, he argues, much effort was wasted on attempts to se-cure these transit agreements, when more accessible channels existed for bringing in supplies from neighboring states. The pattern is being repeated in the current crisis, he says.

Cross-border shipments are usually fraught with political complications, which is why the international community has sought to obtain the cooperation of the Ethiopian and Sudanese authorities. But in both countries, with insurgents gaining ground in the fighting, the regimes have refused to allow relief shipments into the areas of the heaviest conflict -- where the need for food is usually the greatest.

Last week, a seven-person delegation from American humanitarian agencies returned with grim news from Eritrea, Ethiopia's northern-most province where the guerrilla movement fighting for independence has the upper hand over the Ethiopian army in large areas of the territory.

"Eritrea has run out of food," says one delegate from InterAction, the 120-member coordinating council for U.S. relief groups. "Farmers are selling their goats, their livelihood," she adds, "because there is no grass for them to eat." Although the Ethiopian government agreed last month to allow church agencies to deliver food into certain rebel-held areas, and over 90 metric tons of food aid have been donated by the international community for Eritrea, relief is "just starting to trickle in," she says.

Altogether, one million people are believed to be facing starvation in Eritrea. An additional three million are said to be at risk in Tigray, south of Eritrea, where another insurgent movement has taken control.

In Sudan, fighting between government forces and the Sudanese Peoples' Liberation Army (SPLA) in the south has been heavy in recent weeks. Relief workers were forced to evacuate Juba, the largest town in the south, after the SPLA took up positions on the outskirts of the city and began shelling in what some observers believe was an effort to close the airport and severely limit supply access to the region. Thousands of civilians fled into the northern regions and into Uganda and Zaire in search of food and safety. Although the shelling appeared to stop last week, residents face immediate and severe food shortages.

Meanwhile, Egyptian President Hosni Mubarak, current head of the Organization of African Unity, began another mediation effort last week, hosting talks in Cairo between the Sudanese government and the SPLA. Discussions involving the Ethiopian government and the Eritrean Liberation Front -- one of several rebel groups operating out of Eritrea -- were reportedly planned for Sanaa, the Yemeni capital. But the prospects for success in any of these efforts appear poor, following the unsuccessful attempt at mediation of both conflicts by former President Jimmy Carter last year.

Kennedy and Simpson, who serve as chair and ranking minority members of the Senate subcommittee on Immigration and Refugee Affairs, have said that heightened international pressure may be required to get the two governments' agreements on safe passage arrangements. The United Nations Security Council stated that they should be convened to debate the issue if the two regimes refuse to allow food deliveries to non-combatants.

John Prendergast says the level of pressure should be even greater. He calls for a cut off in all bilateral and multilateral forms of aid and trade with the Ethiopian and Sudanese governments and for a vigorous effort to "make food, medicine, seeds, and tools available, no matter where the victims of conflict reside."

COHEN ON SOUTH AFRICA


LENGTH: 686 words


AN: What is your reaction to what President de Klerk has done?

Cohen: We welcome the announcement of the legalization of organizations and the impending release of political prisoners because we think it is a giant leap toward the normalization of political life. As long as the anti-apartheid groups can organize and communicate with the masses and develop negotiating strategies we think it's a good basis for beginning the negotiating scenario. But it is only the beginning, it doesn't solve the problem of apartheid. 

AN: How does the current approach of the South African government differ from what you have seen before?

Cohen: Pre-de Klerk, you had what I call the policy of co-option. Now you have a desire to negotiate and compromise and bargain for a new constitution. That is what has turned the situation around.

AN: What do you want to see happen now?

Cohen: I would hope that within the next few months you will see the release of all the political prisoners, including Nelson Mandela, preliminary discussions about how to negotiate a new constitution, and -- hopefully before the end of the year -- the beginning of a full-fledged negotiation on rewriting a consti-tution to bring about a democratic, non-racial, representative society.

AN: What about the ANC's concern about amnesty for its exiled members?

Cohen: This should be resolved in what I call "talks about talks." Before you can get into a real negotiating situation you have to develop a framework, so the question is, who is a political prisoner and who is not? And who abroad should be allowed to come back and who would be subject to prosecution? All this should be discussed between the two sides. And I hope that when Nelson Mandela is released he could play a major role in that.

AN: Has de Klerk taken the steps required for negotiations to begin?

Cohen: We have always agreed with the mass democratic movement that before you can negotiate you have to set up the right climate. And we think a lot of the climate has been created already. There are still a few steps to be taken -- principally the lifting of the state of emergency -- but I am hopeful that they will be taken within the next few months.

AN: The Comprehensive Anti-Apartheid Act sets out five requirements for terminating the U.S. sanctions (see page 10). Is it premature to talk about lifting them?

Cohen: It is not premature. At such a time as four out of five of the requirements of the anti-apartheid act have been fulfilled, we will consult with Congress to discuss the suspension or modification of sanctions as called for by the act.

AN: But doesn't the legislation require government to first enter into "good faith" negotiations?

Cohen: It says the government must "agree to enter into good faith negotiations." And I believe that in his speech F.W. de Klerk effectively agreed to enter into good faith negotiations.

AN: Are you saying he has satisfied the conditions of the law?

Cohen: No, there are two discriminatory laws that must be repealed. There is also a requirement that the government "has made substantial progress" toward dismantling apartheid, which is a very subjective requirement that could be debated.

AN: What role do you see for the U.S.?

Cohen: We are clearly not mediators or facilitators, but since we do have a sanctions program we feel we do have influence. Our policy is to encourage both sides to negotiate in good faith and to criticize either or both sides if they show signs of not negotiating in good faith.

AN: Are you saying U.S. leverage is a result of the sanctions?

Cohen: I think we always had leverage because there was always the threat of sanctions. We also have international political and diplomatic strength.

AN: Why did President Bush invite both de Klerk and Mandela to Washington?

Cohen: The purpose is to encourage a political settlement by talking to them and giving our point of view. The invitation itself is a recognition of what they have been doing in recent months, which we consider to be positive on both sides.
