AID TIED TO DEMOCRACY


SECTION: INTERVIEW: HERMAN J. COHEN


LENGTH: 1026 words


Washington, DC-- Until Africans "get into step with the rest of the world" and move away from one-party rule, "they will find it difficult to maintain the attention of the international community," says Herman J. Cohen, the assistant secretary of State for African Affairs.

Interviewed in his sixth floor office last week after one year as the Bush administration's top Africa policymaker, Cohen called upon African governments to "seriously think" about the implications of the democratic wave now sweeping such areas as eastern Europe. "The single party state may no longer be accepted in the world as inherently democratic."

In the interview, which touched on a broad range of topics and countries:

* Cohen called South African President F.W. de Klerk's February 2 speech legalizing black opposition parties a "giant leap," but said "it doesn't end apartheid." 

* He condemned the Angolan government's current military offensive against the Unita rebel movement and said U.S. support for Unita will be maintained at a "necessary and sufficient" level.

* He said the U.S. is ready to offer military assistance to soon-to-be-independent Namibia for safeguarding its valuable offshore fishing areas.

* He denied that Africa "is being discriminated against" in the new administration foreign aid requests that give the continent a declining percentage of worldwide economic aid.

* He said that while the administration opposes U.S. right-wing support for Renamo insurgents in Mozambique, "we know of no American laws being broken by anybody."

* He defended the administration for "doing everything we can" to prevent massive starvation and encourage an end to the conflicts in the Horn of Africa.

* He said indiscriminate killing of civilians in Liberia in reaction to an invasion in northeastern Nimba county has been halted, with the assistance of two American military advisors sent to the area to work with government soldiers.

Cohen, who served as senior director for Africa on the National Security Council staff during the final 24 months of the Reagan presidency, has won generally good marks from African governments and Congress for his first year's performance. His predecessor, Chester Crocker, who held the same State Department post throughout the previous administration, had a rocky relationship with Congress. But Cohen assumed office with bipartisan backing on Capitol Hill and has managed to keep the confidence of key senators and House members of both parties.

However, he is also regarded here as less influential in shaping policy than was Crocker, who generally set the course of African issues for the Reagan administration. Cohen is not part of Secretary of State James Baker's inner circle of advisors, and at several critical junctures, Cohen has had to give way to his immediate superior, Under Secretary Robert Kimmett, a long-time Baker aide (see Africa News, November 1989).

Africa hands in Congress were disappointed when the new administration aid request arrived on Capital Hill last week with a smaller proportion of foreign aid for Africa than in any year since 1985. And some blamed the assistant secretary for not fighting for a larger share.

The aid budget asks $ 616 million for development assistance and economic support for Africa in fiscal year 1991 -- 10.9% of the worldwide total, compared with 11.1% this year and 13.95% in 1984. In the interview, Cohen denied that the request is a reflection of an "anti-Africa attitude," calling it a result of "our overall budget crisis."

Until reduced defense spending frees funds for foreign aid, the level of assistance for Africa is unlikely to increase, he said. And much depends on what steps Africa takes politically. "As Africa demonstrates more commitment to economic reform and democracy," he said, "I will advocate that we should reflect that in increasing aid levels."

In his first year, Cohen has focussed heavily on events in southern Africa. Last month, he visited South Africa for discussions with President de Klerk, his senior ministers, and other politically-involved leaders.

As a result of de Klerk's actions this month, Cohen says he hopes to see "full-fledged negotiations" before the end of the year. With the release of Nelson Mandela, he said, the government will have taken sufficient steps to warrant consideration by Congress and the administration of the easing of American sanctions against South Africa.

The Comprehensive Anti-Apartheid Act of 1986 lists five actions the South African government must take to terminate the sanctions. It also offers the president the option of easing some of the restrictions if four of the five conditions are met. Cohen suggested that President George Bush may wish to exercise that option soon, if de Klerk follows through on his reforms.

Asked whether Namibia, which is adopting a liberal democratic constitution, can expect to receive U.S. assistance, he said a "modest" program is planned for fiscal year 1991. But immediately upon independence next month, the new government will be offered the kind of military assistance program aimed at fisheries protection that has been tried successfully in several west African countries, he said.

During a stopover in Luanda last month on his way back from South Africa, Cohen encouraged the government of Angola to give up on "trying to win on the battlefield." Until a process of national reconciliation takes place, he reiterated, U.S. aid for Unita will be maintained. In the interview, Cohen said the administration continues to back the mediation efforts of Zairean President Mobutu Sese Seko, who has proposed an unconditional cease-fire and direct talks between the government and Unita.

The assistant secretary offered little prospect for any decisive U.S. role in helping to resolve the conflicts in such places as Mozambique, Liberia, or the Horn of Africa. Asked if the American government might increase the pressure for peaceful solutions by calling greater attention to the troubles, he said "there are only so many press releases we can make and so many messages we can send."

BIG MONEY FOR WASHINGTON VISITS


LENGTH: 735 words


Washington, DC -- Well-paid lobbyists and publicists are doing a booming business with African governments whose leaders are visiting the U.S. With the size of the contracts on the rise, competition for some of the most lucrative deals is intensifying.

Last fall, two lobbyists with long-standing White House ties won contracts to assist in arranging a trip by Congolese President Denis Sassou-Nguesso, who is making the first State visit by an African leader to the Bush White House this week. Thad Garrett, a minority affairs aide to Bush when he was vice president, and Peter Hannaford, a former partner of Michael Deaver and top Reagan White House advisor, are said to be handling different aspects of the three-day stay.

Garrett was also reported to be in the running for the contract to arrange Nigerian President Ibrahim Babangida's visit, which was planned for January and then cancelled by the Nigerian leader. The Nigerians eventually took their business to other firms. Nigerian Ambassador Hamzat Ahmadu signed up van Kloberg and Associates for $ 70,000 to assist with arrangements. Edward van Kloberg, a former American University dean, represents Zaire and Cape Verde and has handled recent visits by the King of Lesotho and President Pierre Buyoya of Burundi. 

But two rival firms went to Lagos to make separate deals. The largest was a one-year, $ 1 million contract to "provide professional services" of a "general nature,"signed in December with Black, Manafort, Stone and Kelly. Known for its representation of the Angolan Unita rebel movement -- and for involvement in the ongoing scandal at the Department of Housing and Urban Development -- the firm had previously signed up another van Kloberg client, the government of Zaire, for a similar amount (see Africa News, January 29, 1990 and November 1989).

For additional trip preparations, Lagos engaged -- with an initial payment of over $ 40,000 -- the services of the Evans Group, composed of four former Republican Congressmen and a former Black Manafort employee, John Aycoth, who has filed suit against his former employer for breach of contract. The four ex-House members include Mark Siljander, who served on the House Africa subcommittee after being elected in 1981 but lost a bid for re-election in 1986 after making an appeal to religious fundamentalists to "break the back of Satan" and support him.

But the money was never put to use. Nigerian President Babangida canceled following a major shake-up in the military command structure and the Armed Forces Ruling Council, which governs the country. According to West Africa magazine, Nigerian and foreign companies spent huge sums on other arrangements and publicity for the visit, including a 12-page color spread in the February issue of Ebony magazine.

Black Manafort also assisted with the private visit this month of Kenyan President Daniel arap Moi. The firm's contract with Kenya, signed last April, is a one year, $ 500,000 deal. Black Manafort handled media contacts during Moi's trip, including a press briefing by Foreign Minister Dr. Robert Ouko. Last year, Black Manafort managed publicity when the Kenyan government lit a bonfire of $ 3 million worth of ivory, publicizing its campaign for a worldwide ban on ivory trade to protect elephants, which are endangered by poaching.

Before Angolan President Jose Eduardo dos Santos' planned visit this month -- which, like Babangida's, was also cancelled -- Luanda engaged the services of the law firm of Laxalt, Washington, Perito and Dubuc, for a reported $ 800,000 a year. Soon after the representation became public, Paul Laxalt resigned from the firm. A conservative Republican from Utah, the former senator -- who chaired Ronald Reagan's presidential campaigns -- came under strong pressure from right-wing Unita backers over the deal.

The overlapping contracts signed by many African governments is evidence that lobbyists are considered essential when doing business with Washington.

But just how useful these often-expensive representations are for impoverished African governments remains unclear to many observers. One administration official involved in the visit argues that Sassou-Nguesso never required the costly help of a high-prestige lobbyist. The trip was already approved -- though not specifically scheduled -- before either of the contracts were signed.

ANGOLA ALARM GREETS CONGO LEADER


SECTION: U.S.-AFRICA


LENGTH: 1559 words


With his scheduled visit to the White House on Monday, Congolese President Denis Sassou-Nguesso became the first African leader to be accorded a State visit during the Bush administration. High on the U.S. agenda for the bilateral discussions is the intense fighting in Angola, Congo's southeastern neighbor.

Though the trip was announced only last week, it has been long in the planning, largely as an outcome of the key role Congo played in the U.S.-brokered 1988 negotiations which resulted in the Angolan and Namibian peace accords. "It's a thank-you," one official said.

Last week, before arriving in the U.S., the Congolese leader met with the presidents of Zaire and Gabon, Mobutu Sese Seko and Omar Bongo, in a renewed attempt to find a solution to the Angolan conflict. Sassou-Nguesso, who has headed the central African nation since 1979, will be the third African president to visit Washington this month. Daniel arap Moi of Kenya and Pierre Buyoya of Burundi attended the National Prayer Breakfast on February 1, where they talked with President George Bush. Scheduled visits last month by two other leaders -- General Ibrahim Babangida of Nigeria and Angolan President Jose Eduardo dos Santos -- were both canceled. 

As a State visitor, the Congolese president is having an arrival ceremony on the White House grounds and a formal State dinner in the evening. For an official visit, such as the one by Buyoya, the pomp is missing and meetings with top level officials are the only formalities on the schedule. Moi came on a private visit, which does not require extensive pre-arrangements with U.S. officialdom.

As Bush prepared to welcome Sassou-Nguesso, who enjoys generally good relations with the Angolan government in Luanda, the subject of Angola is again claiming the attention of U.S. policymakers. Fapla, the Angolan army, is engaged in a

major assault in the southwest corner of the country against the Unita rebel movement led by Jonas Savimbi, which is supported and armed by the U. S. A fierce battle has been raging, with both sides last week claiming to hold the strategic town of Mavinga, only 20 miles from Unita headquarters at Jamba.

After Luanda announced that Fapla had captured Mavinga, the State Department issued a sharp condemnation of the government's "futile offensive" and called for negotiations to end the conflict. Assistant Secretary of State Herman Cohen visited Luanda on January 24 to press for a halt to the action.

The following day, Cuba announced a suspension in the withdrawal of its troops from Angola --

as agreed under the 1988 accords -- after a Unita attack on a southern Angolan village killed four Cubans and three Angolan soldiers.

Havana said it was the second time Cuban troops had been subjected to "terrorist and criminal actions" by Unita.

Cuba, which has stayed ahead of the withdrawal timetable, having removed over half the 50,000 Cubans that were in Angola when the accords were signed, accused the U.S. of "sabotaging" the agreement by its continuing assistance to the rebel movement.

Angola's President dos Santos had planned to precede his Congolese counterpart to Washington. In response to an invitation from 34 members of the House of Representatives, dos Santos had been expected in the capital city on February 6, on a private visit arranged by TransAfrica, the African-American lobby. The trip was canceled after the Bush administration pressured dos Santos not to come, TransAfrica's Executive Director Randall Robinson says.

Administration officials say "no obstacles" were placed in the way of the trip. Rather, they say, Luanda's decision was based partly on advice from the Portuguese government. Portuguese Prime Minister Anibal Cavaco Silva met in Washington last month with senior U.S. officials, including President Bush, who reportedly expressed his dissatisfaction with the Angolan offensive. The Portuguese leader, who had hoped to persuade the Bush administration to improve relations with Luanda as a prelude to resumed negotiations, returned to Lisbon convinced that American backing for Unita remains firm, diplomatic sources report.

Dos Santos' visit was predicated in large measure on the premise that support for Savimbi is eroding in Congress, where the rebel leader has enjoyed broad bipartisan backing. But, the current fighting has prompted renewed expressions of support for Savimbi from his staunchest allies on Capitol Hill. Although there has not been any broad mobilization of pro-Savimbi activity this year in either the Senate or the House, the current government offensive and Luanda's reported refusal to accept new terms for negotiations have hindered efforts to improve Angola's reputation among members of Congress.

At the same time, the peace talks advocated by Angola's neighbors, including the Congo, have been stymied by the level of mistrust dividing the Angolan parties. Savimbi, meanwhile, has sought to boost his backing in Europe. At the end of January, he began what was to be a five-nation tour with a four-day stop in Portugal, Angola's former colonial ruler. Although he met with both Prime Minister Anibal Cavaco Silva and Portuguese President Mario Soares, Silva played down the significance of the encounter, saying he met the Unita leader in his capacity as a party leader, not as the head of government. After Lisbon, though, Savimbi cut short his trip and returned home because of the Mavinga fighting.

President Bush is expected to ask Sassou-Nguesso to persuade Angola to end the fighting and to begin talks with Unita. The Congolese leader, dos Santos, and six other African heads-of-state have scheduled to meet to discuss peace efforts, but the summit has been postponed at least three times since November. Meanwhile, the fighting has steadily increased.

U.S. officials believe the government is intent on handing Unita a major defeat. "They have thrown everything they can into this," one analyst says, "and they are pushing Unita to the limit."

In Congress, Unita's friends are pushing for an increase in covert American assistance for the rebel movement, which is believed to have risen to about $ 50 million this year. On February 1, six conservative Republicans asked Secretary of State James Baker to "ensure that Unita is sufficiently supplied during this critical period." The letter was signed by Senators Malcolm Wallop, Connie Mack, Larry Pressler, Steve Syms, Gordan Humphrey and Jesse Helms. "Given deterioration of the situation from Unita's standpoint," they said, "we must seriously question the adequacy of U.S. support."

In a separate letter, Minority Leader Robert Dole called on Baker to raise the Angolan issue during his February 8-9 meetings with Soviet leaders in Moscow.

Meanwhile, a task force on terrorism and unconventional warfare, set up by the conservative House Republican Research Committee, has been issuing regular briefings on the fighting. "The actual situation in the field seems to be very grave for Unita," last week's report said. "The ceaseless aerial and artillery bombardment, already among the heaviest in Angola's history, is continuously intensifying." A major

government assault is still to be expected, the report said.

In their letter, the six Senators refer to "disturbing rumors" that supplies have been "either non-existent or intermittent" since a CIA cargo plane crashed in November while ferrying weapons and other supplies to Jamba. Sources with access to information on the classified operation say air flights were suspended for several weeks after the accident, which killed four Americans. "A new plane had to be obtained, painted, and put into service," one official said, "and that all took time."

But the flights were resumed by the first of this year, the sources say, and the frequency of the flights has increased in recent weeks. The CIA is sending in fuel, spare parts and ammunition for anti-tank and other heavy weapons, the sources report. Before the December 1988 peace accords, South Africa was supplying most of Unita's fuel, trucking it into Jamba from Namibia. Unita claims to be self-sufficient in food and to be capturing the small arms and ammunition it needs from government forces.

Last June -- at the time President Mobutu emerged as mediator after arranging a brief meeting and handshake between dos Santos and Savimbi during a 19-nation summit at his palace in Gbadolite -- Mobutu halted U.S. shipments to Unita through Zaire. But after a visit to Washington, Mobutu allowed the operation to resume, U.S. officials say.

As the fighting rages, the peace process remains mired in disagreements. Angola charges that Savimbi reneged on promises made at the July summit, including a pledge that Unita would negotiate within the framework of the country's current one-party constitution. Unita blames the Angolan government for rejecting Mobutu's subsequent plea to begin talks immediately.

In an attempt to break the impasse the Congolese, Zairean and Gabonese presidents, in their meeting in Kinshasa last Wednesday, reportedly considered

a new Angolan proposal. It is that discussion that Sassou-Nguesso was expected

to share with President Bush, in the

hope of securing U.S. backing for a

new round of diplomacy to end the fighting.

Africa News


February 12, 1990


REBORN TO HORROR


LENGTH: 2033 words


 

Like most Mozambican children, twelve-year-old Jorge once was surrounded by people who loved him: parents and grandparents, sisters and brothers, aunts and uncles.

Then one day the rebel group Renamo arrived at the village in Inhambane province where Jorge lived. The invaders were searching for porters to transport their supplies and for

people to populate the rural areas under Renamo control and grow food for the soldiers. Jorge and his parents were force-marched to a Renamo base camp, but his baby brother was killed.

"The baby was baggage; he didn't suit Renamo's goals or purposes," says Neil Boothby bitterly. 

Boothby is a Duke University psychologist just back from two years working with Mozambique's children of war. In the process of helping to set up treatment programs, Boothby and his colleagues conducted 210 in-depth interviews and compiled questionnaires from talks with over 1,200 children who had been kidnapped by Renamo. What they learned is staggering.

Among the five million children living in the war-affected areas -- which is virtually all of Mozambique outside ten urban centers -- 75 out of every 100 will see people murdered, often in large numbers. Eighty-eight percent will witness people being tortured by Renamo. Seventy-three percent will witness women being raped. Over half -- 63% -- will be kidnapped and taken from their families and communities. Of those, three-quarters will be forced to be porters and laborers.

But the most tragic stories come from the 28% of kidnap victims between the ages of five and 15 who are forced to participate directly in Renamo's tortures and killings.

Jorge's story is sadly typical. His father was killed and his mother was turned over to a group of soldiers for their sexual satisfaction. He himself was "adopted" by a Renamo chief as a personal servant. He was beaten and eventually was forced to beat others. In all of these situations, he and other children in the camp were warned to show no emotion and were taught not to care. When he was 14, Jorge was made leader of a gang of youths who were sent to attack villages over the course of the next year.

Finally he was able to escape, and what he told the adults who have tried to help him cope with his memories has haunted Neil Boothby.

"I have had two lives," Jorge said. "My first life was with my mother and my father and my family. My second life began when Renamo took me to their base camp. I was reborn in that camp and am not the same as before. What I saw, what I did, those were the things that changed me."

Still, Jorge may be one of the lucky ones. Some children have been forced to kill or to participate in the torture of members of their own families. And many have responded with a numb withdrawal from human interaction. The first goal of therapy for such children is to provide a safe, loving environment. Only after the children feel secure, are they encouraged to communicate their experiences through socio-dramas, re-enactments and drawings.

Neil Boothby's acquaintance with Mozambican children began at an orphanage, where 42 "very tough" boys between the ages of six and 16 were lodged. Mozambique has had limited experience with orphanges, because strong families and community networks have cared for homeless children. But this war has not only overwhelmed the traditional systems; it has produced a breed of children who cannot be cared for in a conventional setting.

"I was scared to death," Boothby says. The boys had made themselves knives and razor blade weapons, and they were totally suspicious of adults. It took two to three months of spending time with them before the team of psychologists, health workers and care givers could break through the protective walls the children had built around themselves. "If there was anything healing about any-thing we did," says Boothby, "it was being there and caring about these kids and providing consistency and love."

All the techniques for helping the children understand and cope with what happened to them have helped, Boothby says. But the key to the children's chances for a normal future, he believes, is the relationships that are formed between the children and the members of the Organization of Mozambican Women, the OMM, which has taken on the task of helping young war victims.

At the orphanage, the OMM women who came daily and who stayed with the boys at night were themselves scared in the beginning. But they were able to work through their fears and become close to their young wards.

Girls in Renamo's custody, although less likely to be forced to torture and kill, endure their own nightmares. "It's virtually impossible not to be raped," says Boothby. "It's virtually impossible not to have children you don't want." And often those children are killed or abandoned, he says, because they don't meet the needs of Renamo. Boothby hopes that treatment programs run by women experienced in dealing with rape victims can be established in Mozambique soon.

During his two years in Mozambique, Boothby helped the Ministry of Health develop and coordinate programs for the thousands of children across the country who have been exposed to or have participated in Renamo's brutality. The results, so far, have been glimmers of hope in an otherwise grim story. Although the children are tormented, most of them, over a period of time, have become able to express both their experiences and their feelings. A prime concern of the adults who work with them is to help them understand that they

may have had little choice, either in the things that happened to them or the things they were made to do.

"They're so vulnerable, so small in comparison to the people that are asking them to do this," Boothby says. "It begins with fear. If they don't do it, they themselves will be killed. And afterwards they're so humiliated that rage takes over."

Therapists working with children who have been forced to commit atrocities believe that the fear and the rage eventually obliterate moral sensibility. The child loses both a sense of self and the link between self and the rest of humanity. Re-establishing those links is the final step in re- storing the kidnapped children to their lives in the Mozambican community.

"The only way you can re-enter that life is through forgiveness," says Neil Boothby. "You have to be able to forgive yourself, but you also -- probably more importantly -- have to be forgiven by the community, often by the very people you have victimized." And that, the therapists found, is often a difficult relationship to negotiate. Much of the early work of the treatment teams was helping the Mozambique government develop amnesty protocols for the children, and working with communities, teachers, government officials, and other children to help all of them see the returnees as victims.

The Ministry of Health is working to identify all displaced children, whether or not they were captured by Renamo. In all, some 200,000 children are thought to be separated from their families -- some because their parents were kidnapped or killed and others because they were lost in the confusion or aftermath of an attack. Using information from the children, Polaroid photos, voice recordings and simple circulars, researchers have been able -- in the last four to five months alone -- to reunite over 3,000 children with a family member.

In a recent effort in Sofala province, over 200 children were documented in a two-week period. Officials now hope that as many as 80% of the displaced children can be returned to a family or community unit.

But the work is difficult, and like all efforts to alleviate the suffering of Mozambicans, it is dangerous. From 70-90% of the country cannot be traveled safely. And it is the social service systems -- schools, churches, day care centers and clinics -- that are Renamo's prime targets. Many, if not most, of the children who are returned to their families risk being kidnapped again.

Those tactics have bemused anyone who has seen their effects. "It is beyond my ability to imagine," says Boothby, who has also worked with children from other war-wracked countries, including Cam-bodia under the Khmer Rouge. "The only thing I can come up with is that for Renamo, people are basically abstractions. They have a political agenda, and everything is oriented towards accomplishing that."

But just what that agenda is, and who the shadowy figures behind it are, has eluded most observers. The Mozambique government has expressed its willingness to negotiate with anyone who can control what it calls the Renamo "bandits." It authorized Mozambican churches to hold secret talks with Renamo representatives, and it has backed an attempt by Kenya's President Daniel Arap Moi and Zimbabwe Prime Minister Robert Mugabe to arrange direct talks between the government and the rebels. So far, nothing has come of those efforts.

Living in Mozambique has left Neil Boothby with his own anger. As a clinician, he says, his focus was on the children with whom he was working and what he could do to help them. Back in the U.S., he has begun to think about the meaning of his experiences. "OK," he says. "You've heard hundreds of children tell you their stories, you've listened to that. What does it mean? What does it mean?"

What it means, he has concluded, is that Renamo -- a group founded by the intelligence services of the former white government of what is now Zimbabwe, and maintained by South Africa after Zimbabwe's independence -- is now supported by money and supplies from West Germany, Portugal, the U.S., Australia and South Africa. Relief workers, he says, constantly see signs of that support, which is believed to come through an international network of right-wing political and religious groups. (see Africa News, November 1989).

"I've personally arrived in at least half a dozen ¢places/," Boothby says, "and seen evidence of various ¢U.S.-based/ churches involved with Renamo. It's something that makes me very sad as an American."

On the other hand, Boothby says, the official U.S. presence in Mozambique is heartening. Ambassador Melissa Wells presides over an active aid program, including the U.S. Agency for International Development-funded project, run by Save the Children, which took Boothby to Mozambique. U.S. support for such programs will rise by a million dollars this year, over the nearly $ 350,000 appropriated in 1989, according to an AID official.

Other government and private projects are part of an international effort for Mozambican children. Unicef, the United Nations Children's Fund, is active, as are European aid programs, particularly from the Scandanavian countries. Canada has long had a strong presence in the country. In the U.S., the Chicago-based Mozambique Support Net-work (MSN) has just launched a campaign to raise funds for an orphanage housing 30 children in Zambezia province, one of the areas hardest hit by Renamo.

But as long as Renamo can conduct its campaigns, there is a limited amount anyone can do to save the children of Mozambique. Renamo's style of building its base camps, where rings of civilians are confined between rings of Renamo soldiers in expanding concentric circles, discourages government troops from attacking -- even when they locate a Renamo stronghold and have the means to dislodge it.

Critics of U.S. policy argue that the Bush administration should be doing more to end the war, including putting pressure on South Africa to block support for Renamo, whether the aid program is covert, as in the past, or is unsanctioned by the current South African administration. "It's important," says MSN's Lisa January, "to identify exactly who is providing the support, both in South Africa and in the United States, and to see that it's stopped."

Meanwhile, the drive to ameliorate the suffering of those children who can be reached is continuing. Neil Boothby is back in Mozambique this week, to brief his replacement, who will join the effort to help the most severely disturbed children reforge their links with the human family.

"If a military regime wants to solve all problems before making way for democracy," says General Olesugun Obasanjo in his new memoir, "democracy will remain a pipe dream."

Not My Will is an account of the general's term as Nigeria's head of state from 1976 to 1979. Like his three previous books, it is sure to be controversial because of its outspoken views on both domestic and international issues. In an interview with the Nigerian magazine Newswatch, Obasanjo allowed himself to be drawn into a discussion of current economic realities in Nigeria, Africa's most populous nation. Obasanjo today is very much an elder statesman, often sought for international commissions and conferences. His latest appeal for democracy, pluralism and popular participation in government will be widely studied.

COME BACK LATER


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 243 words


Presidential elections scheduled for Feb. 18 in the Comoros were put off until this week, following claims of fraud lodged by opposition candidates. A lack of voting materials was cited as the reason for the postponement. 

The elections are the first free balloting in the Indian Ocean nation, which has been ruled for a decade and a half by authoritarian governments that have been backed -- and many believe, controlled -- by white mercenaries. That era appeared to end last November, when President Ahmed Abdallah was assassinated and mercenaries took direct control of the government, before being persuaded to leave three weeks later by France and South Africa. French officers replaced the mercenaries who had commanded the feared "presidential guard," which has been renamed the Comoran Security Forces.

The Comoros consists of three islands that declared independence from France in 1975. Mayotte, a fourth island in the group, retains its French ties. The islands, which have seemed a paradise to thousands of European tourists, are gripped by an economic crisis that has left government workers without pay for over three months.

The election campaign has stirred intense popular interest in the devoutly Islamic country. Everywhere in the islands, crowds came out to greet the field of candidates, who range from a 70-year traditionalist serving as interim head of state to a 42-year-old Marxist recently released from prison.

February 26, 1990


CONGO: BUILDING A STABLE BASE


SECTION: U.S.-AFRICA


LENGTH: 832 words


Washington, DC. -- When Congolese President Denis Sassou-Nguesso arrived here on the day Nelson Mandela was released in South Africa, it was clear that his visit would attract little media attention. But at the end of a three-day stay, which included a meeting and formal dinner with President George Bush at the White House, the 46-year old leader seemed pleased with the welcome he received.

In 1986, while serving as chair of the Organization of African Unity, Sassou-Nguesso visited the U.S. but was denied a meeting with President Ronald Reagan. This time, as the first African leader accorded a state visit since Bush took office last January, the Congolese president literally received red carpet treatment. 

From the formal arrival ceremony on the White House lawn on Feb. 12 until his send-off by helicopter two days later from the Washington Monument grounds -- to the accompaniment of military honor guards and a 21-gun salute -- much of the schedule was occupied by ceremonial activities. It was something the for-mer military man seemed to revel in.

During a breakfast meeting with reporters at Blair House, the official U.S. government guest house, Sassou-Nguesso talked about the prospects for an end to war in Angola, Congo's western neighbor. He said he was looking to another neighbor, President Mobutu Sese Seko of Zaire, who has assumed the task of mediating in Angola, to "take some initiative." In a meeting earlier this month, he said he had encouraged the Zaire leader to become more active in promoting talks between the Angolan government and the Unita rebel movement.

Although he said he had discussed Angola with Bush, Sassou-Nguesso refused to criticize U.S. backing for Unita, despite Congo's good relationship with the Angolan government in Luanda.

Here are highlights of Sassou-Nguesso's remarks about his country's political and economic policies.

Question: Is Congo a Marxist state?

Sassou-Nguesso: We have always said that our long term goal is the building up of socialism in our country. But for the time being we are still laying the foundation of a nation and creating a democratic society. We need to make sure all national forces participate in the development of the affairs of our country. Economically, we favor the coexistence of the state sector, the mixed sector and the private sector, even the cooperative sector.

Question: The title "peoples' republic" conjures up in the West an image of a heavily government-dominated economic system such as has existed in China and eastern Europe, for example. To what extent do you want to abandon socialism and move to free market economics?

Sassou-Nguesso: We don't understand the comparison that is sometimes made between our country and eastern Europe, because we have never sought to imitate what is

going on in other societies. We have always wanted to develop according to the wants of our people, taking into account the realities in our country.

The openness our country has always had to private enterprise and a free market takes into account the situation we face.

Question: Do you think that your country one day may have a multi-party political system?

Sassou-Nguesso: Yes, of course, because we are not systematically opposed to the idea of multi-party systems. For your guidance, I must tell you that in the past we had a multi-party system. At the time of independence, there were many political parties in our country. It is from that experience and the strife which resulted that the people decided to have a one-party system. I think that normal development will lead to creation of multi-party systems in Africa.

Question: To what extent do you think the economic development of your country and of Africa in general is tied to the process of democratization?

Sassou-Nguesso: I think that we have to set free -- to liberate -- productive forces and the initiative taken by people, and I think African leaders understand that very well. We first have to build stable states and create the bases of nations, with the participation of all the people. And I think if these conditions are created, we will move towards economic and political development because everyone will be participating in the process.

Question: What percentage of your gross national product is represented by private enterprise?

Sassou-Nguesso: Our gross national product comes mainly from oil, a sector mostly run by private companies. In the agriculture area, private enterprise accounts for about eighty percent of our production, and it's about the same for forestry.

Question: Do you expect any concrete economic benefits from your visit to the U.S.?

Sassou-Nguesso: We have felt some real interest on the part of several companies. And the government -- President Bush himself told me -- is committed to encouraging American investment. The bilateral investment treaty we have signed will serve as a basis for promoting that activity.

February 26, 1990


LIBERIA: GOVERNMENT ACCUSED OF ABUSES


LENGTH: 866 words


Bodies floating down the river that flows along Liberia's border with Guinea have become a not uncommon sight during the past two months of conflict in the northern Liberian region of Nimba County.

According to accounts gathered by telephone and reports from recent visitors, deaths of civilians in the area are still mounting after two months of violence. A somewhat mysterious rebel incursion has left hundreds of people dead in the region. Eyewitnesses say most of the deaths have occurred at the hands of government troops. 

Refugees fleeing the fighting have streamed into neighboring Guinea to the west and Cote d'Ivoire to the east. A United Press International report last week from Gbe-nda, Cote d'Ivoire said the numbers of

Liberians in that country have grown to 56,000 and are continuing to rise. "More people arrive from Liberia every day, and tell us their villages have been attacked," one refugee was quoted as saying. "They have no choice but to try to escape."

From Gossopah, Guinea, Reuter reported that 80,000 Liberians are now exiled there. They are being fed and housed by the local inhabitants, themselves impoverished, with whom they share ethnic ties. "We like the Liberians," Gossopah's chief Mussah Mamy told Reuter, "they are our own people. But we wish there were a better road out of here so they could leave."

The U.S. has contributed $ 25,000 to the International Committee of the Red Cross to aid the refugees. Other donors are expected to offer assistance following the release of recommendations from a United Nations High Commission for Refugees survey of conditions and needs, which was completed last week.

The rebels are led by Charles Taylor, a former Liberian government official who has said in interviews with the BBC that he is seeking the overthrow of President Samuel K. Doe. But the incursion has not gathered popular support, despite the fact that Doe has many opponents in the country. Taylor's background -- he fled Liberia for the U.S. in 1986 amid accusations of embezzlement -- and the methods adopted by his previously-unknown National Patriotic Forces of Liberia have won him little backing from established political groups or the general citizenry.

The rebels are said to have carried out hit and run attacks on villages and government installations. In retaliation, government troops are said by refugees to have destroyed villages and captured and killed civilians. The troops are also reported to have engaged in sexual assaults on women in the area.

Human rights organizations, including Amnesty International and Africa Watch, last month issued appeals for an end to extrajudicial executions. Earlier this month, Africa Watch called upon the Bush administration to withdraw military advisors recently dispatched to work with Liberian forces in Nimba.

The State Department says the two advisors have accompanied the commander of the Liberian army, Gen. Moses Craig, to Nimba to advise on "matters involved with restoring and maintaining discipline" among government troops. In a recent interview, Assistant Secretary of State for Africa Herman Cohen said the presence of the two Americans has "greatly helped" improve the situation.

But in a letter to Secretary of State James Baker, Africa Watch argued that the stated U.S. objective of minimizing abuses by military personnel has "rarely been achieved" in the places by the introduction of training programs and advisors. "It is unclear whether the administration seriously believes that they can have any effect on an army alleged to have killed hundreds of civilians in the past six weeks," the organization said in a Feb. 2 statement.

In the capital, Monrovia, news of the fighting in Nimba County has been filtering in slowly. But opposition political figures report a growing unease about the government's intentions towards its critics. Doe has already announced that funds designated for the registration of voters in preparation for elections in 1991 have been used to pay for the counter-insurgency effort. Many of Doe's opponents expect him to interfere with the electoral process. He is widely believed to have manipulated the results of the last national election in 1985 to squeak out a narrow victory over the Liberian Action Party.

The pervasive sense of fear was heightened last month by the brutal slaying of Robert Phillips, a former LAP leader who was one of several Doe opponents tried for treason in 1986. In the early hours of Jan. 4, relatives discovered Phillips' badly-beaten body in the bedroom

of his house in the capital. According to accounts provided by the rela-tives, his wrists were slashed to the bone, his fingers broken, and his throat was slit from ear to ear. Neighbors said they had seen four army troops and one civilian at the door before the slaying, the relatives report.

An official inquest into the murder ended abruptly, leading to suspicions of high level interference. A U.S. official, speaking on condition that he not be named, said last week that authorities in Monrovia have assured the American embassy that the investigation is continuing and the results will be made public.

KENYA: OFFICIAL'S DEATH RAISES QUESTIONS


LENGTH: 472 words


Amid student demonstrations charging a police cover-up, two British detectives and a pathologist arrived in Nairobi last week to help their Kenyan counterparts investigate the death of Foreign Minister Robert Ouko.

The popular politician from the Lake Victoria area of western Kenya was found dead near his farm on Feb. 16, less than two weeks after returning from the United States, where he accompanied President Daniel arap Moi on a private visit. At a press conference in Washington,D.C., Ouko displayed the spirited defense of Kenyan policies for which he had become known during a decade as the country's chief foreign affairs official. He disputed charges of political repression, including concerns raised by the human rights group Amnesty International about political detentions. 

President Moi said the circumstances of the foreign minister's death -- Ouko's body was found badly burned and with a gunshot wound -- suggest "foul play," and that every effort would be made to find those responsible. Despite suggestions from some officials that the killing may have been linked to problems within the Ouko family, Kenyan newspaper reports fueled public speculation that the death may have been politically motivated. Comparisons were made to the assassinations of politicians Tom Mboya in 1969 and Josiah Kariuki six years later. However, both those figures were prominent government opponents.

Although official police statements issued when the body was found did not mention suicide as a possibility, several hundred students chanting, "We want the truth," said the police were trying to present the death as a suicide.

Meanwhile, environmentalist Wangari Maathai is trying to assume a low profile after her highly publicized protest against the construction of a 60-story commercial building in Nairobi's downtown Uhuru Park (see Africa News December 25, 1989). In the wake of her opposition to the building, Maathai was castigated by President Moi and other Kenyan officials, and her tree-planting organization, the Greenbelt Movement, was evicted from its offices. In recent weeks,

Maathai has tried to avoid rehashing the issue but finds that it still haunts her. She has replied at some length to a statement by the member of parliament for Mount Elgon, Wilberforce Kisiero, who has banned her from his constituency. Maathai said she feared that the rural women who are the driving force behind the Greenbelt movement will be frightened away by the continuing controversy and that thousands of seedling trees may die for lack of care. While Kenyans were wondering whether a ban against a Kenyan citizen traveling freely within the country could be enforced, the Kenyan government, under pressure from international donors, quietly agreed to postpone the tower complex, "for the time being."

February 26, 1990


THE GAMBIA: SURVIVING AGAINST THE ODDS


SECTION: AROUND THE CONTINENT


LENGTH: 418 words


The Gambia celebrated a quarter century of independence on Feb. 18, in ceremonies marred by the deaths of three people among the 30,000 that were packed into a stadium in the capital, Banjul. Two Gambians and a West German tourist were killed when the gun of a policeman who was jostled by the crowd accidently discharged. In a somber independence message, Gambian President Sir Dawda Jawara said the country had fallen short of its expectations of 25 years ago. Jawara said the Gambia's high rate of childhood deaths, its illiteracy, its lack of economic infrastructure and the gap between the living conditions of urban and rural areas are all signs of the country's weakness. 

The Gambia's improbable boundaries are a graphic reminder of Africa's colonial heritage, which carved the continent into pieces that ignored practical realities. The country is a 190 mile sliver of land -- averaging only 20 miles in width -- that juts into French-speaking Senegal along the Gambia River, one of Africa's most navigable waterways. Several post-independence attempts to unite the two neighbors have faltered, partly due to cultural and linguistic differences stemming from the colonial experience.

The poor economic performance of the Gambia is an illustration that African countries have faced an uphill battle to prosperity, regardless of what path they have followed. At independence, the Gambia attempted to diversify its economy away from a dependence on peanuts, and -- unlike many developing countries -- it opted to concentrate its resources in agricultural production. But the country's promising beginnings floundered, first when drought hit the Sahelian region, and then in the adverse economic climate of the 1980s. The country was set back, President Jawara told West Africa magazine, by a culmination of difficulties: "the drought, the fall in the price of commodities and deteriorating terms of trade, high interest rates in developing countries, currency instability on the international scene, etc."

As a multi-party democracy and champion of human rights both domestically and abroad, the Gambia had hoped for more support from the international community, but, in fact, Jawara says, there has been "a considerable decline in the amount of official development assistance." Smallness, the president says, has meant a lack of power. Still, he says, Gambians can be proud of their sense of unity and of the democratic principles their small country has promoted around the world.

LANGUAGE: English


Copyright 1990 AllAfrica, Inc.


34 of 146 DOCUMENTS


Africa News


February 26, 1990


"HAS SOCIALISM FAILED?"


LENGTH: 2062 words


Socialism is undoubtedly in the throes of a crisis greater than at any time since 1917. The last half of 1989 saw the dramatic collapse of most of the communist party governments of eastern Europe. Their downfall was brought about through massive upsurges which had the support not only of the majority of the working class but also a large slice of the membership of the ruling parties themselves. These were popular revolts against unpopular regimes; if socialists are unable to come to terms with this reality, the future of socialism is indeed bleak.

The mounting chronicle of crimes and distortions in the history of existing socialism, its economic failures and the divide which developed between socialism and democracy, have raised doubts in the minds of many former supporters of the socialist cause as to whether socialism can work at all. Indeed, we must expect that, for a time, many in the affected countries will be easy targets for those aiming to achieve a reversion to capitalism, including an embrace of its external policies.

Shock-waves of very necessary self-examination have also been triggered off among communists both inside and outside the socialist world. For our part, we firmly believe in the future of socialism; and we do not dismiss its whole past as an unmitigated failure. 

(Among other things, statistics recently published in The Economist (UK) show that in the Soviet Union - after only 70 years of socialist endeavour in what was one of the most backward countries in the capitalist world -- there are more graduate engineers than in the U.S., more graduate research scientists than in Japan and more medical doctors per head than in western Europe. It also produces more steel, fuel and energy than any other country.)

Socialism certainly produced a Stalin and a Ceaucescu, but it also produced a Lenin and a Gorbachev. Despite the distortions at the top, the nobility of socialism's basic objectives inspired millions upon millions to devote themselves selflessly to building it on the ground. And, no one can doubt that if humanity is today poised to enter an unprecedented era of peace and civilized international relations, it is in the first place due to the efforts of the socialist world.

But it is more vital than ever to subject the past of existing socialism to an unsparing critique in order to draw the necessary lessons. To do so openly is an assertion of justified confidence in the future of socialism and its inherent moral superiority. And we should not allow ourselves to be inhibited merely because an exposure of failures will inevitably provide ammunition to the traditional enemies of socialism.

The commandist and bureaucratic approaches which took root during Stalin's time affected communist parties throughout the world, including our own. We cannot disclaim our share of the responsibility for the spread of the personality cult and a mechanical embrace of Soviet domestic and foreign policies, some of which discredited the cause of socialism. We kept silent for too

long after the 1956 Krushchev revelations. . . .

The implications for socialism of the Stalinist distortions have not yet been evenly understood throughout our ranks. We need to continue the search for a better balance between advancing party policy as a collective and the toleration of on-going debate and even constructive dissent.

We do not pretend that our party's changing postures in the direction of democratic socialism are the results only of our own independent evolution. Our shift undoubtedly owes a prime debt to the process of perestroika and glasnost which was so courageously unleashed under Gorbachev's inspiration. Closer to home, the democratic spirit which dominated in the re-emerged trade union movement from the early 1970s onwards, also made its impact.

But we can legitimately claim that in certain fundamental respects our indigenous revolutionary practice long ago ceased to be guided by Stalinist concepts. This is the case particularly in relation to the way the party performed its role as a working class vanguard, its relations with fraternal organizations and representatives of other social forces and, above all, its approach to the question of democracy in the post-apartheid state and in a future socialist South Africa.

We have always believed (and we continue to do so) that it is indispensable for the working class to have an independent political instrument which safeguards its role in the democratic revolution and which leads it toward an eventual classless society. But such leadership must be won rather than imposed. Our claim to represent the historic aspirations of the workers does not give us an absolute right to lead them or to exercise control over society as a whole in their name. . . .

This approach to the vanguard concept has not, as we know, always been adhered to in world revolutionary practice and in an earlier period we too were infected by the distortion. But, in our case, the shift which has taken place in our conception of 'vanguard' is by no means a post-Gorbachev phenomenon. The wording on this question in our new program is taken almost verbatim from our Central Committee's 1970 report on organization.

The 1970 document reiterated the need to safeguard, both in the letter and the spirit, the independence of the political expressions of other social forces whether economic or national. It rejected the old purist and domineering concept that all those who do not agree with the party are necessarily enemies of the working class. And it saw no conflict between our understanding of the concept of vanguard and the acceptance of the African National Congress as the head of the liberation alliance.

Despite the inevitable limitations which illegality imposed on our inner-party democratic processes, the principles of accountability and electivity of all higher organs were substantially adhered to. Seven underground Congresses of our party have been held since 1953. The delegates to Congress from the lower organs were elected without lists from above and always constituted a majority. The incoming Central Committees were elected by a secret ballot without any form of direct or indirect 'guidance' to the delegates. In other words, the Leninist concept of democratic centralism has not been abused to entrench authoritarian leadership practices.

Our structures, down to the lowest units, have been increasingly encouraged to assess and question leadership pronouncements in a critical spirit and the views of the membership are invariably canvassed before finalizing basic policy documents. Our 7th Congress, which adopted our new program, "The Path to Power", was a model of democratic consultation and spirited debate. . . .

As we have already noted, one of the most serious casualties in the divide which developed between democracy and socialism was in the one-sided relationship between the ruling parties and the mass organizations. In order to prevent such a distortion in a post-apartheid South Africa we have, for example, set out in our draft Workers' Charter that:

'Trade unions and their federation shall be completely independent and answerable only to the decisions of their members or affiliates, democratically arrived at. No political party, state organ or enterprise, whether public, private or mixed, shall directly or indirectly interfere with such independence.'

The substance of this approach is reflected in the way our party has in fact conducted itself for most of its underground existence. . . .

We do not regard the trade unions or the national movement as mere conduits for our policies. Nor do we attempt to advance our policy positions through intrigue or manipulation. Our relationship with these organizations is based on complete respect for their independence, integrity and inner-democracy. In so far as our influence is felt, it is the result of open submissions of policy positions and the impact of individual communists who win respect as among the most loyal, the most devoted and ideologically clear members of these organizations.

Old habits die hard and among the most pernicious of these is the purist concept that all those who do not agree with the party are necessarily enemies of socialism. This leads to a substitution of name-calling and jargon for healthy debate with non-party activists. As already mentioned, our 7th Congress noted some isolated reversions along these lines and resolved to combat such tendencies.

But, in general, the long-established and appreciable move away from old-style . . . sectarianism has won for our party the admiration and support of a growing number of non-communist revolutionary activists in the broad workers' and national movement. We also consider it appropriate to canvass the views of such activists in the formulation of certain aspects of our policy. For example, we submitted our preliminary conception of the contents of a Workers' Charter for critical discussion not only in our own ranks but throughout the national and trade union movements.

Our party's program holds firmly to a post-apartheid state which will guarantee all citizens the basic rights and freedoms of organization, speech, thought, press, movement, residence, conscience and religion; full trade union rights for all workers including the right to strike, and one person one vote in free and democratic elections. These freedoms constitute the very essence of our national liberation and socialist objectives and they clearly imply political pluralism.

Both for these historical reasons and because experience has shown that an institutionalized one-party state has a strong propensity for authoritarianism, we remain protagonists of multi-party post-apartheid democracy both in the national democratic and socialist phases. . . .

It follows that, in truly democratic conditions, it is perfectly legitimate and desirable for a party claiming to be the political instrument of the working class to attempt to lead its constituency in democratic contest for political power against other parties and groups representing other social forces. And if it wins, it must be constitutionally required, from time to time, to go back to the people for a renewed mandate. The alternative to this is self-perpetuating power with all its implications for corruption and dictatorship.

We dare not underestimate the damage that has been wrought to the cause of socialism by the distortions we have touched upon. We, however, continue to have complete faith that socialism represents the most rational, just and democratic way for human beings to relate to one another. . . .The all-round development of the individual and the creation of opportunities for every person to express his or her talents to the full can only find ultimate expression in a society which dedicates itself to people rather than profit.

The opponents of socialism are very vocal about what they call the failure of socialism in Africa. (They conveniently ignore the fact that most of the countries which tried to create conditions for the building of socialism faced unending civil war, aggression and externally-inspired banditry; a situation in which it is hardly possible to build any kind of stable social formation -- capitalist or socialist.)

But they say little, if anything, about Africa's real failure; the failures of capitalism. Over 90% of our continent's people live out their wretched and repressed lives in stagnating and declining capitalist-oriented economies. International capital, to whom most of these countries are mortgaged, virtually regards cheap bread, free education and full employment as economic crimes. . . .

The way forward for the whole of humanity lies within a socialist framework guided by genuine socialist human-itarianism and not within a capitalist system which entrenches economic and social inequalities as a way of life. So-cialism can undoubtedly be made to work without the negative practices which have distorted many of its key objectives.

But mere faith in the future of socialism is not enough. The lessons of past failures have to be learnt. Above all, we have to ensure that its fundamental tenet -- socialist democracy -- occupies a rightful place in all future practice.

A LONG-BANNED VOICE ENTERS THE DEBATE


LENGTH: 448 words


Regular listeners to the broadcasts of Radio South Africa, have been able to track the fast pace of events in that country through the plethora of new voices on the air. Not only has the government-controlled overseas service been carrying the speeches and interviews of Nelson Mandela and other recent political prisoners, but it also has included in several newscasts the comments of Joe Slovo, the general secretary of the South African Communist Party (SACP).

The SACP spent the last 40 years as an outlawed organization -- a decade more than Mandela's African National Congress (ANC) -- before it was legalized along with other anti-apartheid movements on Feb. 2. Slovo, a lawyer from Johannesburg who has lived in exile since the early 1960s, has long been portrayed by the government and the South African media as a devil figure, a pawn of Moscow deceptively plotting intrigue and violent revolution from outside the country's borders. 

Mandela quickly reaffirmed his admiration for the SACP, commending "its sterling contribution to the struggle for democracy" in his Cape Town speech on the day of his release and calling Slovo "one of our finest patriots." The ANC national executive committee has declared that "the historic alliance" with the SACP will "continue to function with the necessary restructuring," following the unbanning of both organizations.

Although the party has not released membership figures, it has said that the numbers increased by 90% between 1984 and 1989. In a breakdown of members provided to the Johannesburg publication Work in Progress, the party said 70% are African, 16% white, 10% Indian, and 4% mixed-ancestry ("Coloured"). The externally-based members are drawn more heavily from ex-students, professionals and intelligentsia -- all of whom are more likely to seek exile -- than the workers, who comprise 75% of the party's members inside the country, a SACP official stated in written answers to Work in Progress.

The future of the party is now unclear, in the wake not only of its unbanning but also of the demise of Communist Party rule in eastern Europe, where the SACP has enjoyed close relations. Last month, before de Klerk's action, Slovo published a critique entitled "Has Socialism Failed?" It is intended, he has said, to spark discussion and debate about the party's practices and policies, both within and outside the organization. The paper has been widely discussed in anti-apartheid circles in South Africa; major portions were published by the Johannesburg weekly, the New Nation.

AT PRISON GATES, OPPOSING FORCES COOPERATE


LENGTH: 324 words


On the day of Mandela's release, police manned roadblocks along the access road into Victor Verster prison, mainly to control the flow of traffic.

By midday, a 4,000-strong crowd of ecstatic African National Congress supporters and journalists had encircled the gates.

There were two types of organization set up to control the crowds and ensure the occasion went off smoothly. Marshals from the United Democratic Front (UDF) maintained strict control over foreign and local television crews and photographers. A makeshift rope barrier was strung across the verge of the road opposite the entrance and the crews were instructed to remain behind it. 

This prompted some quick improvisation from the media people. Some had brought their own portable ladders. Others simply climbed onto the roofs of taxis and vans. One TV station had the foresight to hire an old municipal crane with a platform to hold the camera crew.

Warning from the marshals that if anyone stepped out of line, Mandela would simply be taken by car straight to Cape Town had the desired effect. None of the photographers broke the agreement.

Other UDF marshals linked arms and formed a human chain to prevent the chanting crowd from blocking the prison entrance.

Inside the ring of marshals, about 100 uniformed police strolled around while their officers maintained radio contact with authorities inside the prison. Sometimes the two systems of law and order cooperated with each other. At other times they engaged in light-hearted competition.

Throughout the day, the "people's police" -- dressed in the khaki uniforms of the ANC -- could be seen talking to the government police in their blue SAP uniforms.

At 4:10 P.mM., the police contingent formed a human barrier between the crowd and the marshalls and signalled that Mandela was about to come out.

"He's walking," yelled a photographer. "He's walking to his freedom." -- E.K.

WHILE THE WORLD WATCHED


LENGTH: 634 words


In a special edition marking the release from prison of Nelson Mandela, the South African newspaper Weekly Mail described the frantic, behind-the scenes effort that greeted the announcement that Mandela would be freed.

The elaborate groundwork and logistics for the reception of Nelson Mandela were planned and executed by members of the Mass Democratic Movement, literally overnight.

Cheryl Carolus, publicity secretary for the United Democratic Front (UDF) in the western Cape said that Dullah Omar, Mandela's attorney, and other anti-apartheid leaders, were notified of the impending release by phone calls from the office of President F.W. de Klerk, just an hour and a half before the president's news conference on Feb. 10.

The regional reception committee in the western Cape had a basic framework in place but no immediate plans to carry out the vast task of organizing for the people of Cape Town to welcome Mandela. 

"So we knew at 3:30 P.M.," Carolus says. "At 5:30 we convened a local reception committee meeting and by

7 P.M. we were able to hold a much larger meeting of senior comrades who volunteered to do the work. Everything had to be arranged from scratch.

Jets had to be chartered to bring members of the Mandela family as well as the Rivonia trialists ¢who were convicted and sentenced along with Mandela 27 years ago/. Accommodation had to be arranged for visiting dignitaries.

Health services had to be provided at both the prison and the rally. Banners needed painting. Marshalls needed to be recruited and briefed. Posters and pamphlets to advertise the event had to be printed.

And then there was the brigade of journalists from the world's richest and most powerful media corporations who were relying on the Mass Democratic Movement to arrange for them to get the first pictures and interviews with Mandela.

Within an hour, Carolus says, sub-committees had been formed, tasks identified and the work set in motion.

"We worked through the night, and at times it seemed that nothing was impossible for us to do," she says. "The Western Cape Traders Association agreed to provide the taxis for transport. The mayor and deputy mayor of Cape Town were amazing. They agreed to throw the city open to Mandela. That's how we got the Grand Parade for the rally and the Cape Town civic center for the press conference."

The work committee decided to print 4,000 posters and 210,000 pamphlets. "We phoned Allied Press in Elsie's River and asked them to be on standby to print," says Carolus. They said, 'We understand. We are all in the trenches together. We'll get the job done.' By 2 A.M. on Sunday the posters and pamphlets were done, free of charge. An army of volunteers was ready to take them into the townships.

And then there were the banners. "In the middle of the night," Carolus says, "we ran out of calico. So someone went and woke up a few businessmen. They agreed to open their factories at three in the morning. They gave us the material for free."

Carolus faced the task of dealing with the army of television crews and photographers who wanted to be at the prison exit to get the first pictures. The reception committee's solution, which was met with some loud grumbling, was to set up a 14-camera pool to take the photos and visuals and share them with other journalists. An infrastructure of first aid services for the day was set up by a volunteer group of doctors and health workers. By 6 a.m. the posters had been put up. The pamphlets were distributed.

Just over two hours later, the first of the Lear jets arrived, with Rivonia trialists and other dignitaries of the resistance movement on board. Food, accommodation, and transport had been arranged. And the banners were being put up on the Grand Parade.

FORGING A FUTURE


LENGTH: 1455 words


 

In the wake of the release of Nelson Mandela, United Democratic Front leader Patrick "Terror" Lekota made a promise to an appreciative group of Americans. South Africans, he said, will establish "the kind of government which will make you and us proud -- which will not make you ashamed to stand before the world tomorrow and say, We supported those people.'"

But despite the dramatic changes of recent weeks, the gulf dividing the government and its opponents is large and the transition to a new order will not be easy. With the first tentative steps underway toward negotiating South Africa's future, the complexities of the process are coming into clearer focus.

The government seems to be approaching the process with a four-year timetable in mind. President F.W. de Klerk's comfortable margin in last year's all-white general elections will enable him to remain in office under the current constitution until late 1994. 

For Mandela's now-legalized African National Congress (ANC) and its allies in what is called the "mass democratic movement" (MDM), there are also time considerations, though of a different nature. They arise from a commitment to a consultative approach that by definition is time consuming.

Key negotiating points will have to be "rigorously and democratically discussed in our structures," says Jay Naidoo, general secretary of Cosatu, the powerful trade union federation that is a key constituent of the MDM.

High level meetings between the government and the recently-unbanned ANC already are being planned, ushering in the phase commentators have labeled 'talks about talks.'

"There's no longer need to kick down the door," de Klerk said in a Feb. 15 interview with Ted Koppel on ABC's Nightline. The ANC and other groups "must walk across the threshold and start to talk," he said. De Klerk announced the unbanning of the ANC and other opposition groups in a Feb. 2 address to Parliament and Mandela was released after 27 years of imprisonment on Feb. 11.

As a first step toward talks, Mandela and the ANC have said the government must remove remaining obstacles to free expression: lifting the state of emergency and releasing all remaining political prisoners. "We insist that they comply with those preconditions," Mandela said in an interview with Charlayne Hunter-Gault on the MacNeil/Lehrer Newshour, "and then there will be room for further progress."

Reaching a compromise on these issues should not prove very difficult, according to Professor Willem de Klerk, a former Afrikaans newspaper editor and elder brother of the president. The government has come to realize that "the ANC is the solution and apartheid is the problem," a recent reversal in thinking that accounts for the sudden turnabout in policy, he said in a telephone interview from his office at Rands Afrikaans University in Johannesburg.

The state of emergency will be lifted as soon as the continuing violence in Natal province can be ended, Willem de Klerk says. He predicts that in return for freeing the remaining political prisoners, the government will ask for the release of white South Africans jailed in neighboring countries for infiltrating ANC ranks.

The ANC has already outlined its terms for meeting a key government demand -- ending the armed struggle. Rejecting calls for a "unilateral moratorium or suspension", the ANC national executive committee in a Feb. 16 statement said a "suspension of hostilities by both sides" should be worked out "upon creation of a climate conducive to negotiations," meaning after the ANC's preconditions for negotiations are met.

While the focus of attention has turned towards talks, there are voices in South Africa still opposed to the process. The Pan Africanist Congress, a rival offshoot of the ANC which was also banned in 1960 and unbanned this month, remains deeply suspicious of de Klerk's intentions. In a statement welcoming Mandela's release, the PAC expressed doubts that talks could bring about the "return of usurped land" and "self determination for the oppressed majority."

The white Conservative Party, which received 30% of the vote in last year's election and is the official opposition in Parliament, is demanding a new election to test de Klerk's popularity.

But from most quarters the emphasis is on what Thabo Mbeki, the director of foreign relations for the ANC, calls "the beginning of a process which hopefully is going to result in a peaceful resolution." Willem de Klerk expects that preconditions can be satisfied and "pre-negotiations" initiated by May or June, with the initial sessions focussing on procedures and an agenda for formal negotiations.

Most observers expect the government to be represented in these early talks by Cabinet ministers who have been meeting with Mandela in prison for the past several years, led by Gerrit Viljoen, minister of constitutional development. The team is likely to include Foreign Minister Roelof 'Pik' Botha, Education Minister Stoffel van de Merwe, and the ministers of justice and police.

The ANC has said its negotiating team will be drawn both from leaders based inside the country and members of the externally-based national executive committee (NEC). Currently, a three-person committee is directing the organization in exile during the illness of President Oliver Tambo -- Secretary General Alfred Nzo, Treasurer Thomas Nkobi and Thabo Mbeki, who, among the three, is the one most likely to participate directly in the first round of meetings. Inside South Africa, the ANC is relying heavily on Walter Sisulu, who has already been designated by the national executive to head an internally-based working group to prepare for the relocation of headquarters from Lusaka, Zambia to Johannesburg.

Another likely participant in the talks is Cyril Ramaphosa, general secretary of the National Union of Mineworkers, who has played a leading part in the national reception committee that has coordinated Mandela's schedule since his release. His central role is a sign of the importance of trade union support for any eventual agreement.

While the issues of the first phase are being resolved between the ANC and the government, Willem de Klerk expects formal negotiations to include many more groups, with the ANC and its affiliates, on the one hand, and the government, on the other, as the main actors. Those talks could get underway by the middle of next year, he says.

For the mass democratic movement, the main goals of negotiations are clear -- the creation of a nonracial, democratic system with equal opportunity for all. But many unanswered questions remain.

None is more important or controversial than economic policy. Mandela's reiteration of a long-standing ANC call for nationalization of the nation's mines and banks was met with a torrent of criticism. "That would kill the goose that lays the golden egg," President de Klerk told Koppel.

The ANC may decide to alter its long-standing nationalization plank, but the problem of a wide disparity in income in South Africa will continue to be of paramount importance to the broad constituencies represented in the MDM. "We have always viewed the struggle for national liberation as part of an uninterrupted struggle for social and economic emancipation," says Cosatu's Jay Naidoo.

In any negotiations, the MDM will be seeking ways to safeguard the interests of all those represented in its structures, he says -- including trade unions, youth groups, women's organizations, and community structures. "There must always be a significant role for these organizations in any post-apartheid society," he says, in an interview published this month in the Johannesburg weekly, the New Nation. "We have to insure that the society is fully democratized."

Vigorous debates about future policies have been taking place within MDM structures for some months. One area of discussion has been about ways to protect "the collective rights of our constituencies," Naidoo says -- in addition to the protections of individual rights that a Bill of Rights in the constitution should offer. Cosatu already is circulating a draft labor charter aimed at safeguarding workers' interests.

The rights and role of women have also been a lively topic of contention, taking up as much as half the discussion time in some of the recent sessions that preceded the Conference for a Democratic Future in December (see Africa News, December, 25, 1989).

That rigorously democratic process is part of "the promise we have made," says the UDF's Lekota, "that if we were supported, we would set up a government that would be worthy of human beings. We intend to live up to that promise."
