NAMIBIA'S COLONIAL HISTORY


LENGTH: 636 words


On the next two pages are some of the key dates in the international process that led to Namibian independence.Protests and Pressure

1884 At the April 24 Berlin Conference that divided Africa among Euro- pean powers, South West Africa becomes a protectorate of Germ- any, beginning a period of genocide in which more than 100,000 Africans are killed by the occupying forces.

1890 Despite fierce resistance by the local population, the region becomes a German colony. 

1915 South Africa, fighting with allied powers during World War I, con-quers South West Africa. German forces surrender.

1920 Following Germany's defeat in World War I, South Africa assumes control of the territory under a League of Nations mandate on December 17.

1933 The League of Nations objects to a South African plan to annex the former German colony.

1960 The South West Africa People's Organization (Swapo) is established. Ethiopia and Liberia apply to the International Court of Justice for a judgement against South Africa for illegally occupying the territory. Two years later, the Court refuses to rule on the application, saying the two countries have no standing in the matter.

1966 Under Resolution 2145, the United Nations General Assembly revokes South Africa's mandate to govern the territory. In August, the first armed con-frontation between South African forces and Swapo fighters is reported.

1967 The United Nations officially recog-nizes the name, Namibia, adopted by the independence movement and derived from the word Namib -- meaning shield or enclosure in the Nama-Damara language. (Namib is also the name of the coastal desert region.)

1969 The UN Security Council endorses the General Assembly's authority in Namibia.

1971 In June, the International Court of Justice declares South African occupation of Namibia illegal. Six months later, Namibian contract workers stage the largest national strike to date. The action effectively curtails production at most of the territory's industries and consolidates the independence movement's position.

1975 In an attempt to find an internal solution to increasing opposition, South Africa convenes the Turnhalle Constitutional Conference with representatives from all Namibia's ethnic groups. Swapo rejects the constitution.

1977 South Africa and Swapo agree to allow the five Western countries of the Security Council -- who come to be known as the Contact Group (U.S., France, West Germany, Canada and Great Britain) -- to mediate nego-tiations on Namibian independence.

1978 In April, the Contact Group introduces a "Proposal for a Settle-ment of the Namibian Situation" -- the forerunner to Resolution 435, which establishes the formula for independence -- to the UN Security Council. The next month, a South African raid on the Kasinga refugee camp in Angola kills over 600 Namibian women and children. In August, Martti Ahtisaari, appointed as the newly-created Special Representative for Namibia to the UN Secretary-General, goes to Namibia to investigate the possibility of elections. The following month, Resolution 435 is adopted by the UN Security Council. South Africa continues to try to solve the conflict internally, by holding December elections, which Swapo boycotts.

1982 In an attempt to persuade South Africa to implement Resolution 435, the UN Security Council adopts a set of constitutional principles. The Reagan administration launches a diplomatic initiative linking Cuban troop withdrawal from Angola with Namibian independence.

1987 Thousands of copper miners from the Tsumeb Corporation in northern Namibia hold a month-long strike. With labor actions occurring throughout the country, the worker's move-ment gains prominence as an important opponent to South African rule.

LANGUAGE: English

Africa News


March 26, 1990


DISENTANGLING FROM SOUTH AFRICA


LENGTH: 1378 words


Windhoek, Namibia -- For several weeks now, officials of the Namibian government that assumed power on March 21 have been trying to determine what funds would be left in the treasury to pay salaries and other ongoing costs of running a country.

Independence means the loss of an annual South African subsidy of $ 200 million to the Namibian budget, even while the Namibian economy remains intertwined with South Africa's. In addition, there is concern that a South African privatization strategy, pursued with increasing vigor in the pre-independence period, has denied the new government control over significant economic resources. The result, some here fear, may be a continuing dependence on outside donors, including South Africa.

Namibian Finance Minister Otto Herrigel has harshly condemned as "plunder" a South African decision last October to privatize assets of employee pension funds. An estimated $ 270 million -- one study puts the amount at nearer$ 400 million -- has been transferred from the formerly state-controlled Statutory Institutions Pension Fund to major South African insurance firms, including Sanlam, Old Mutual and Southern Life. 

The move has stripped the new Namibian government of its major potential source of local investment capital, which officials had planned to use to initiate productive enterprises and generate jobs -- an urgent task in a country with 25-30% unemployment.

Another effect of the transfer, Herrigel says, was to encourage resignations by experienced civil servants entitled to full pension benefits -- many of whom the new administration had hoped to persuade to stay on the job. The finance minister says the government will seek full restitution of the funds from the South African companies.

Privatization has been implemented or attempted in several other areas as well, notably the transportation industry. A reported attempt by conservative church groups to purchase public schools using government loan funds collapsed after angry debate over the issue in the newly-elected Constituent Assembly. Delegates argued that the Namibian government will be forced to build more schools -- not sell off existing ones -- to meet the educational needs of the nation's children, especially in the former war zones of the north.

The strategy of transferring state assets to private hands was developed by an eight-person Privatization Advisory Committee, set up in 1988 with approval from the Cabinet. The committee compiled a priority privatization list for 20 specific economic areas -- culled from more than 100 that were proposed initially. Among the activities targeted were resorts and conservation efforts, posts and telecommunications, hospital and medical services, including laboratory and pharmaceutical entities, and security services.

One of the hardest goals for the new nation of Namibia to achieve will be extricating its economic priorities from those of the South African economy. In fact, increasing numbers of white businessmen and farmers have begun to chaff at the tendency of South Africa to use Namibia as a "captive" market and to limit the access of Namibian goods to overseas markets. So the attempt to expand Namibia's economic opportunities, independent of South African interests, promises to be a cause around which both black and white Namibians can rally.

The challenge is multifaceted. The South African rand has served as Namibia's currency, and is likely to continue to do so for at least the next two years. According to Herrigel, only one of Namibia's seven banks is controlled by local capital. For years, most individual savings or foreign exchange earnings deposited in banks, retirement funds, building societies and insurance companies in Namibia have been transferred routinely to South African firms. A labor researcher has estimated that as much as $ 46 million of such funds are passing from Namibia to South Africa each month -- an annual amount equal to 38.5% of Namibia's gross domestic product.

At the same time, South African banks appear to have been transferring to their Namibian branches large portions of the debt incurred by the previous authorities in Namibia -- a move that may make the inherited debt more difficult for the new government to repudiate. Swapo officials say much of the debt was incurred for South Africa's war effort against the independence movement, as well as for the costly ethnic-based administrations dictated by apartheid policies. Latest figures indicate that the amount owed by the South West African administration may be around $ 192 million -- a sum Swapo says is the responsibility of South Africa, not the Namibian people.

Among the highest priorities of Agriculture Minister Gerhard Hanekom is to make Namibia self-sufficient in food. "It is good and well that we are producing diamonds," Hanekom says. "But we cannot eat diamonds." Hanekom also is concentrating on the urgent need for jobs in agriculture and in rural development. Opportunities for such expansion are seen as particularly ripe in the north, where production has been curtailed by the war.

Hanekom is also responsible for fisheries, water and rural development, a job as imposing as its name. Among the ministry's first moves is extending Namibia's territorial waters to 200 miles offshore, to protect the rich fishing grounds that, until now, have been exploited mostly by South African fleets.

One of the issues perplexing agricultural policymakers is how best to end the exclusion of Ovambo cattle from the national beef market. Since early this century, cattle from Ovamboland were separated from those of southern areas by the "red line" -- a double fence designed to reduce cattle migration. Built in response to a widespread outbreak of diseases that decimated white farmers' herds, the barrier effectively excludes cattle owned by Africans from white-dominated farming and market regions to the south. In the past, cattle vaccination programs were not extended north of the red line.

A proposal currently being considered would move the demarcation line northwards to the Angolan border and extend extension services to black communal farmers as well as white commercial farmers. But any changes must be made cautiously, to avoid Namibian meat being excluded from overseas markets. The European Economic Community, for example, would balk at purchasing meat not guaranteed free of infestation by hoof and mouth disease or other easily-spread cattle ailments.

Getting the beef to market, though, presents another challenge. South Africa retains control of Namibia's largest port, a deepwater facility in the enclave of Walvis Bay which is the terminus of Namibia's rail lines to the outside world.

As the area's only viable commercial port, Walvis Bay handles some 90% of Namibia's imports and exports. Government officials fear that South Africa will now significantly boost port fees, exercising a continuing influence over Namibia's economic development strategy. In addition, Walvis Bay is the site of nearly all the factories that process the Namibian fish catches.

Last week, African leaders attending a special meeting of the Organization of African Unity in Lusaka, Zambia, called on South Africa to give up sovereignty over the enclave and the adjacent islands. There has been speculation that a compromise plan, giving Namibia administrative authority, might be concluded between Pretoria and the new government.

On the favorable side of the economic ledger, officials say, is the increasing interest by foreign companies in Namibia's investment opportunities. The new National Planning Commission, headed by an Oxford-educated Namibian official of the Rossing Uranium Mine, is registering record numbers of companies that want to explore local opportunities.

An element in the growing confidence shown by businesses is the reiteration by Trade and Industry Minister Ben Amathila that the government has no plans to nationalize the mining sector. Mining is the source of 75% of Namibia's foreign exchange earnings and accounts for 25% of the country's tax base.

However, Swapo does want to see a greater share of the profits reinvested inside Namibia, officials say.

Africa News


March 26, 1990


FREEDOM FERVOR IN WAR WRACKED NORTH


LENGTH: 1078 words


Ovamboland, Namibia -- Unexpectedly abundant rains, like a blessing bestowed with independence, have come to northern Namibia, turning a hot, barren terrain into an unexpectedly lush green. Large pools of water look almost like rice paddies, as men and women dot the fields, hoeing and planting.

The landscape, bursting with new life and energy, seems to reflect the jubilant mood of the people here. Bordered by Angola to the north, the area was a stronghold of independence sentiment in the long war against South African rule, and local residents have high expectations for the new government in Windhoek, an eight-hour drive to the south. 

The Casspir armored vehicles of the South African Defense Force -- once a familiar sight on northern roads -- have been heading south for weeks, flanked front and back by United Nations escorts.

A Namibian activist says that the Casspirs are so hated a symbol of South Africa's military occupation and harassment of civilians that they cannot be used in this area, even though the new government is desperately short of transport vehicles.

At the Okatana Mission a few miles from the town of Oshikati, over a hundred Namibian children play lively games in the gathering dusk, racing the wire cars and trucks they have constructed from pieces of scrap. The children are among the more than 40,000 Namibians who returned home after the repatriation of refugees that began last May. Many of their families are still without homes and there are no boarding school places available, so the mission population is likely to remain swollen for some time to come.

Father Hubert, one of the Okatana priests, has been in the area for five years. He regales visitors with stories of the times that South African soldiers tried to search for suspected Swapo members at the mission. Once, he says, soldiers entered church while he was conducting worship. "I was so angry," he recalls. "How dare they interrupt a service to God? They finally got embarrassed and left, but they surrounded the church and waited." (An activist later recounts how the mission sisters, fearing that he was about to be detained and tortured, once helped smuggle him away from the area.)

For Father Hubert, a key sign of peace is the ending of the curfew. People were unable to move around, he says. "And the South Africans would always shoot from several directions during the middle of the night, just to keep people awake and afraid."

Much to the delight of staff members, the numerous benches outside the Human Rights Center in Ondangua sit empty. "They used to be filled to overflowing," a Center worker says.

Since the November elections and the departure of the South African Defense Forces, reports of human rights abuses have dropped to a trickle.

Signs of the November elections still abound -- slogans of the competing parties painted on the sides of buildings, a lonely and abandoned-looking campaign center of the Democratic Turnhalle Alliance, painted in crisp red, white and blue. But here in Ovamboland, which -- with nearby Kavango and Caprivi -- is home to 70% of Namibia's people, the dominant colors are Swapo's red, green and blue. Small postcard-size flags flutter jauntily atop thatch-roofed houses, bars, poles and incongruously-large trees. A young girl sports plastic shoes in Swapo colors. An old woman shelters from the sun under a Swapo umbrella.

The road from Ondangua is a strip of small bars, grocery stores, shops and shantytowns, interspersed with police and military bases that look mostly unused. Untag -- the United Nations Transitional Assistance Group, which monitored the independence process -- maintained a major observation post at Ondangua. The road used to be alive with trucks and vehicles; now, there is only the occasional sentry posted at sand-bagged check points.

Much of the local economy across the north revolved around servicing the consumer needs of South African soldiers and the Namibian units they supervised. The departure of the troops and police has caused a decline in business, although the general sentiment appears to be satisfaction that they are gone. Many exiles have had no work since returning to Namibia, and they are waiting for the new government to take full command of government ministries, hoping that the result will be more jobs.

One of the legacies of the war economy is a throw-away consumer culture, visible in roadside piles of old beer and soda cans. An Untag official says this habit of waste may be one of the hardest effects of the South African presence to overcome. Northern Namibians have become accustomed to discarding items that in many poor countries are carefully hoarded and recycled.

Before independence, Swapo declared a half-day holiday to encourage the clean-up of the unsightly trash piles that also are creating a health hazard. Families could be seen working together in front of their small houses, raking the ground and piling up rubbish for collection.

More difficult to clean up are the damaged schools and clinics and homes that litter the north. The task of reconstruction is immense, as is the challenge of eliminating racial segregation from Namibia's social services and institutions.

At a teachers training college in Ondangua, the Namibian National Student Organization called together students from across the northern region to discuss the restructuring of education and the improvement of learning conditions for students and teachers alike. Members of the newly-formed Namibian Teachers Association, also invited to the meeting, faced hard questions. Teachers, some students said, had not challenged the system of apartheid education strongly enough and had acted, instead, to protect their jobs.

The gathering was a harbinger of the many tough questions that will be asked when the euphoria of independence begins to die down.

Perhaps the biggest task facing the Swapo government is the need to respond quickly to the raised expectations, especially here in the north where the brunt of the war was felt most acutely. Still, it is clear to an outside observer that the new authorities have assumed control with the advantage of massive support from across this region.

Carole Collins visited Namibia at the end of a 4-year assignment as Southern Africa international affairs representative for the American Friends Service Committee. She now resumes her career as a journalist.

Africa News


March 26, 1990


SWAPO FILLS THE RANKS


LENGTH: 861 words


Officials of the new Namibian government are drawn largely from the ranks of Swapo, the independence movement which won last November's election. However, several positions are filled by representatives of other political organizations, and appointees are drawn from most of the country's diverse ethnic groups.

Sam Shafilshuna Nujoma, the president of the country, has been the leader of Swapo since the movement was formally launched in 1960. A man whose deliberateness is sometimes seen as an absence of charisma, Nujoma has won respect and loyalty through his solid, steady leadership, Swapo insider's say. Born on May 12, 1929 in Okahao, in the northern area known as Ovamboland, he attended the Finnish Mission school in the town from 1937 to 1948, before moving to the black township in Windhoek, the capital, to enroll in night school. As Swapo president, Nujoma has travelled throughout the world to lobby for independence. 

Hage Gottfried Geingob, the prime minister, is a member of Swapo's political bureau, the group's top decision-making body, and served as election director during last year's campaign. Known for his charm and his warmth, he was described by one journalist as a "huggy-bear of a man." Born in Otjiwarongo in 1941, Geingob joined the independence movement in 1962, and from 1964 to 1971, he represented Swapo at the UN and in the U.S. He accepted a United Nations post in 1972 and three years later became the first director of the UN Institute for Namibia in Lusaka, Zambia. In anticipation of independence, the institute has provided training in a wide variety of skills to hundreds of Namibians.

Among the other Swapo veterans in the cabinet are Hifikepunye Pohamba, home affairs; Theo-Ben Gurirab, foreign affairs; Peter Mueshihange, defence; Nahas Angula, education, culture and sport; Hidipo Hamutenya, information and broadcasting; Ngarikutuke Tjiriange, justice; Nicky Iyambo, health and social services; Richard Kapelwa, works, transport and communications; Libertine Appolus-Amathila -- the only woman minister -- local government and housing; and her husband, Ben Amathila, trade and industry.

All the Swapo appointees held senior, and in most cases parallel, positions in the exiled leadership of Swapo prior to their return to Namibia for the elections.

A sentimental favorite in the new administration is the minister of mines and industry, Swapo Secretary General Andimba Toivo ya Toivo, who has been called Namibia's "Nelson Mandela." One of the founders of the independence movement, Toivo was arrested with 36 others, convicted of treason by the South African administration in 1967, and imprisoned until 1984. His deputy minister is Helmut Angula, who has been Swapo's permanent representative at the United Nations since 1986, when his predecessor Theo-Ben Gurirab was appointed Swapo foreign secretary.

The government also includes several Swapo leaders who remained inside Namibia throughout the independence struggle. Swapo Vice President Hendrik Witbooi is the minister of labor; Nico Bessinger, a Windhoek architect, is minister of wildlife, conservation, and tourism; Marco Hausiku, president of the teachers union, is minister of lands, resettlement and rehabilitation. Daniel Tjongarero is deputy minister of information, and Buddy Wentworth is education deputy. Hartmut Ruppel, a German-born Windhoek lawyer who jointed Swapo in 1980, is the attorney general.

Swapo officials in other top posts include Richard Tsheehama, minister of state for security, and deputy ministers Jerry Ekandjo, local government; Timothy Hadino Hishongwa, labor; Pendukeni Ithana, wildlife; Nangola Ithete, home affairs; Phillemon Malima, defense; Kaire Mbuende, agriculture; and Marcus Shivute, lands.

The two white cabinet members are both businessmen with no active political histories -- Minister of Finance Otto Herrigel, an economist, and Gerhard Hanekom, minister of agriculture, fisheries, water and rural development. Karl Klaus Dierks, a German-born engineer, is deputy minister of works, transport and communication.

Members of other political parties in deputy ministerial positions include Vekuii Reinhard Rukuro, president of Swanu and of the Namibian National Front, which won one seat in the assembly. Reggie Diergaardt, deputy minister of trade and industry, is head of the Labour Party and national chairman of the United Democratic Front, which holds four seats.

Hans Berker was selected by Nujoma to be chief justice of the Supreme Court, the same position he held prior to independence. In its first sitting, the new National Assembly elected another Swapo veteran, Mose Penaani Tjitendero as speaker. Chosen as deputy speaker was Mburumba Kerina, an important figure in the early history of Swapo, who left in a disagreement with Nujoma and other Swapo leaders in 1963.

Bibliographical information on Namibian officials was drawn partly from the Namibian Handbook and Political Who's Who, by Joe Putz, Heidi Von Egidy, and Perri Caplan (post-election edition, 1990), the Magus Company, P. O. Box 22791, Windhoek 9000, Namibia, Tel: 264-61-37930, Fax: 37908.

March 26, 1990


MISSION ACCOMPLISHED


LENGTH: 229 words


The United Nations Transitional Assistance Group (Untag) winds up a one-year mission overseeing Namibia's independence process on March 31. UN officials are reporting that the job was done with money to spare. 

Before the start of the monitoring effort on April 1, 1989, the UN Security Council engaged in heated debate over the efforts of the permanent members -- led by the United States -- to cut both the size of the force and the proposed outlay. Over African objections, the council approved $ 416 millionin expenditures. The Africa Group feared that without larger UN resources, South Africa, which remained in administrative control of the territory, could not be prevented from influencing the outcome of the elections.

Figures released by the UN show that 7,900 staff, drawn from 109 nations, were part of Untag at its peak. There were 4,300 military personnel and 1,500 police; the rest were civilians.

The Namibian operation was the largest -- and by many accounts the most successful -- peacekeeping effort in the organization's history.

Current projections indicate that $ 367 million will be spent by the end of the mandate on March 31, with a few additional bills still to be paid.

Contingents from several countries, including those of Kenya, have been asked by the new government to stay on until a new Namibian army is trained.

Africa News


March 26, 1990


REGIONAL PEACE HOPES MOUNT AS NAMIBIA CELEBRATES


SECTION: NAMIBIA


LENGTH: 2187 words


Windhoek -- Namibia, Africa's newest nation, this week is turning its attention to the difficult tasks of governance, following five days of celebrations surrounding independence on March 21. "From this day on, our people will have the chance to shape their own future," President Sam Nujoma said in his independence day speech to the nation.

Nujoma was named president by the Constituent Assembly, elected last November in voting monitored by a United Nations observer team. After 23 years of war and a year-long independence process, the country is now face-to-face with new realities -- most seriously, a stark disparity in living standards between the majority and a privileged minority.

Deep divisions resulting from years of bitter conflict clearly remain. Swapo won 41 of the 72 seats in the Constituent Assembly, which is now the country's parliament -- enough to choose the government, but not the two-thirds required to approve a new constitution. Despite continuing tensions, however, the assembly's seven rival parties managed in just 80 days to reach consensus on a document that enshrines broad democratic guarantees. 

Having experienced the possibilities of reconciliation, the new nation marched onto the world stage with a sense of having triumphed. Citizens threw themselves into the continuing festivities, which included sporting events and a national service of thanksgiving. Namibia is, statistically, the world's most Christian nation, and the service at the national stadium attracted church leaders from around the world, such as South African Archbishop Desmond Tutu and Jesse Jackson from the United States. Rock and gospel concerts followed on the weekend.

United Nations Secretary General Javier Perez de Cuellar, speaking at the independence celebrations at midnight on March 20, saluted Namibians for "a remarkable lesson to the whole world in the effectiveness of democratic procedures." President Nujoma said Namibia "has blazed the trail to freedom."

Nujoma thanked the international community for "many years of consistent support for our cause." He said that "the impressive presence" of so many government leaders was evidence that Namibia's independence "is an event of great world importance."

The turnout from all parts of the globe was almost more than the small country could handle. At the airport, where two or three flights come in on a normal day, there were so many pre-independence arrivals that at several points planes had to sit on the pavement waiting a turn at the official receiving gate for their VIP passengers. An appeal was issued for volunteers to bring vehicles to help ferry guests the 27 miles to town.

Official representation from Africa was, not surprisingly, the largest. Leading the list of heads of state were Egyptian President Hosni Mubarek, the current chairman of the Organization of African Unity, and Kenneth Kuanda, chairman of the frontline states in southern Africa, joined by their counterparts from Angola, Botswana, Cape Verde, Congo, Djibouti, Kenya, Mali, Mozambique, Nigeria, Sudan and Tanzania. Other countries sent high-level delegations as well. U.S. Secretary of State James Baker was joined in attendance by the foreign ministers from three other world powers -- the Soviet Union, Britain and West Germany. Palestinian leader Yasser Arafat also was on hand.

Heavy rain, which stopped just before the ceremonies began, contributed to a traffic jam that delayed the arrival of dignitaries and the beginning of the ceremonies. The stadium was filled to near-capacity -- and the VIP section was jammed -- when Nelson Mandela stepped through a doorway at the top of the seats behind the official dais, evoking a thunderous ovation.

Both South African State President F.W. de Klerk and Perez de Cuellar gave short addresses before the South African flag was lowered at 12:17 A.M. and the new blue, red, green, and gold flag was raised. After the banner was hoisted, the UN secretary general administered the oath of office to Nujoma. Seated side-by-side during the proceedings, the three men exchanged friendly greetings and chatted amiably. Following the festivities, which included a 20-minute fireworks display, de Klerk was the first leader to walk down a red-carpeted pathway to congratulate Nujoma, by then seated in a ceremonial chair.

Also present in large numbers were representatives of anti-apartheid organizations that have actively supported the independence campaign. As special guests of the new government, they took part in two state dinners, one given by de Klerk and the other by Nujoma, and two stadium celebrations -- the midnight hand-over and a rally the following afternoon at which the new Cabinet was installed.

In his speech at the rally, Nujoma paid homage to "martyrs of our struggle" and gave thanks to the United Nations for its role in winning Namibia's freedom. He also expressed "our deepest gratitude" to the international organizations and individuals "who for so many years have stood by us in our struggle and lent us valuable material assistance and moral support."

But most of the President's address was forward looking, outlining in broad terms the new government's policies. Nujoma cited as Namibia's biggest challenge the fact that two-thirds of the population "are very poor by our standards and by the standards of the world."

He called mass unemployment "one of the most crippling legacies of colonialism" and appealed to the country's people to continue subsistence farming "for the immediate future," while the government explores new avenues for economic growth. He mentioned the potential of agricultural innovations, including the utilization of irrigation methods that minimize water loss, to create jobs and boost farm output.

Nujoma pledged to promote the development of industries, particularly those related to the minerals and crops already being produced in Namibia. And he called for the involvement of the local business community in the mining sector, which is in the hands of South African and British investors. He said the Namibian government regards the private sector as "an engine for growth and prosperity" and that it will remove laws which have limited business opportunities for black Namibians.

"We are committed to a mixed economy," he said, and the government wants to enter joint ventures with the private sector to promote fishing, large-scale farming and agriculture-related industries.

Although he made no mention of the need for outside aid, Nujoma appealed for foreign investment to help boost employment. He said a new investment code will spell out an "open and inviting" attitude and set forth requirements for commitments by investors to national development goals.

As a country that has had international backing, "Namibia will play an active role in international affairs," the president said. The country will become the 160th member of the United Nations and the 52nd member to the Organization of African Unity.

Namibia may unintentionally have made its first major contribution in this sphere already, as leaders who travelled here for independence took advantage of the opportunity for diplomatic discussions on a wide range of subjects.

One topic on the agenda of several of the officials was the fighting in Angola. By the end of the week of talks and exchanges, the prospects seemed good for agreement on a cease-fire between the Luanda government and the rebel movement Unita, led by Jonas Savimbi. Among the first meetings of the week was one between Angolan President Jose Eduardo dos Santos and Secretary of State Baker, the first between such high-level American and Angolan officials since Angola's independence in 1975. The U.S. has never formally recognized the government in Luanda and is currently Unita's major military backer, supplying an estimated $ 50 million in covert assistance this year.

Both sides characterized the session as productive, and dos Santos held subsequent meetings with de Klerk and several other leaders. The dos Santos/de Klerk session lasted 90 minutes instead of the scheduled 45, contributing to speculation that the two countries were close to concluding an agreement that might involve the establishment of trade missions.

The apparent thaw in relations comes only 15 months after South Africa withdrew its troops from Angola, ending a decade of South African invasions. The peace accord between Angola, Cuba and South Africa also led to the agreement for implementing the Namibian independence process and the withdrawal of Cuban troops from Angola.

De Klerk was cheered when he opened his independence ceremony speech by saying: "I stand here tonight as an advocate for peace. The season of violence has passed for Namibia and for the whole of southern Africa."

Secretary of State Baker also discussed Angola with Perez de Cuellar and with Soviet Foreign Minister Edward Shevardnaze, who visited Angola before arriving in Namibia. Assistant Secretary for Africa Herman Cohen, part of Baker's traveling party, held separate talks with the Angolan Defense Force chief, Gen. Franca Ndalu. Baker -- after traveling to Capetown and Johannesburg -- planned to wind-up his trip with a stop in Kinshasa, for meetings with Savimbi and President Mobutu Sese Seko of Zaire, who is recognized as the accepted mediator by both sides in the Angolan conflict.

Diplomats from many countries believe conditions are favorable for a cease-fire that could prove longer lasting than last June's Gbadolite accord negotiated by Mobutu, which failed to stop the fighting. Since December, government forces have steadily penetrated the southeast of the country, as far as the town of Mavingo. Analysts see the advance as significant for two reasons: Unita forces have been pushed back further than ever before -- to within 200 miles of the Unita headquarters -- and the campaign was waged during the rainy season without the assistance of Cuban troops, who have pulled back to the north of the country in accordance with the 1988 tripartite agreement. (Cuba has removed over 30,000 of the 50,000 soldiers previously stationed in Angola, but last week threatened retaliation against the guerrillas after blaming Unita for the ambush of a Cuban supply column that left one Cuban dead and six others wounded.)

There may be little more that Angolan government forces can achieve militarily, and the effort to hold current positions against Unita guerrilla assaults would likely be costly. So both sides may be unusually ready to settle.

Continued fighting in Angola could present a serious problem for the new Namibian government as well. Angola served as Swapo's exile headquarters for more than a decade, and Swapo's guerrilla army fought alongside Angolan and Cuban troops in some of the most crucial battles against Unita and its South African backers. But it would be difficult, if not impossible, for the Swapo government to make a continuing commitment to the war effort. The cost of helping the Angolan government would be hard for Namibia's strained budget to bear, and opposition parties would be sure to object vigorously.

In addition, the current Angolan offensive against Unita is exacerbating the already tense situation in northern Namibia, where Namibians who served in the paramilitary South West African police are living in an uneasy truce with local civilians and former Swapo guerrillas. There have been numerous reports that Unita rebels fleeing the Angolan advance are responsible for widespread thefts and violence inside Namibia.

Nujoma, speaking on independence night, appealed for reconciliation among Namibians of all races and political views. He said Namibians' "collective security and prosperity depend on our unity of purpose and action" and that unity is needed to achieve peace -- in Namibia, and in the region as a whole.

But despite the challenges the new government faces, the air of euphoria here may not be misplaced. Namibia produces minerals that other nations covet, including diamonds and uranium, and it exports furs, fish and beef. Its constitution is one of the most democratic in the world, with an unusual provision that commits the nation to helping the most disadvantaged of its citizens. Unlike many newly-independent countries, its infrastructure is strong, its businesses are buoyant and its leaders are educated and experienced.

They have also proved themselves as adept at diplomacy at home as they have been in exile. Prime Minister Hage Geingob, when he returned to run Swapo's election campaign, disarmed political rivals by inviting them round to his office for tea.

Nujoma himself, who was preceded by an image as a stern ideologue, has flashed his ready smile at friend and foe alike -- and told them all that the nation needs their energy and their skills.

With less than a million and a half people in a territory twice the size of California, Namibians are few in number. But this month at least, they are relishing their role as a model for the rest of us.

March 26, 1990


A SOBER RETURN


LENGTH: 413 words


I stepped off the plane at Maputo airport and looked towards the airport building. The banner was gone. I was no longer welcomed to the zone of free humanity. In the days that followed my fifth arrival in Mozambique, I found myself reacting sharply to other scenes and changes as well. 

In the two years since my last visit, the deaths and fighting have continued unabated and the International Monetary Fund austerity program has begun to show its impact. Two years ago there were hardly any beggars; earlier there were none. Now there are many, both children and adults, in the market, in front of the stores and hotels.

The average salary for a middle-level civil servant is not enough for a family to survive without some other form of income -- a plot of land to grow food, a service to sell, or access to goods at work or elsewhere that can be sold or traded for necessities. Many a family goes hungry between salary checks.

At the same time, the city has been spruced up. Because of the influx of donor organizations, the housing shortage in the old Portuguese section of the city is acute (as it is in the nearby shantytowns because of the influx of refugees from the countryside). There is hardly a decrepit structure to be seen, and the repairs go beyond paint and replaced roof tiles to artistic and tasteful renovations of some beautiful colonial houses. "Why is Maputo such a well-kept secret?" asked a visiting United Nations official. "This is a little paradise."

The once sparsely-stocked market is packed with an array of fruits, vegetables, eggs and meat and the shop windows are filled with goods. But few Mozambicans can do more than windowshop.

Against this backdrop, a speedy process of class formation is taking place, with some living well on aid contracts and associated opportunities while the urban and rural majority sink deeper into poverty.

The war is still the major reality, and a deep battle fatigue pervades the life of virtually every Mozambican. With health and agricultural facilities among those most disrupted by Renamo attacks, the toll, on both individuals and social structures, is staggering. Diseases are spreading, and nearly eight million Mozambicans are either totally or to a large extent dependent on food aid.

It is little wonder that news of the prospect of negotiations have been greeted with such relief.

Stephanie Urdang is the author of And Still They Dance, a portrait of Mozambican women.

Africa News


March 26, 1990


IS REFORM MUTING THE MOZAMBIQUE REVOLUTION?


SECTION: MOZAMBIQUE


LENGTH: 2571 words


After their meeting in Washington, D.C. this month, President George Bush commended Mozambique's President Joaquim Chissano for "taking steps towards democracy parallel with steps toward economic reform."

In January, as part of an effort to make the political process more accessible to ordinary citizens, Chissano introduced a new draft constitution for public debate. The proposed document provides for direct presidential elections, limits terms of office, and guarantees a broad range of human rights, including freedom of religion and the right to strike.

At the same time, Mozambique is implementing an economic austerity plan, backed by the International Monetary Fund, which has spawned a wave of strikes and protests across the country.

Outside the White House, views on the political and economic changes in Mozambique are more ambivalent than those expressed by President Bush. The Canadian publication Southern Africa Report has devoted portions of recent issues to a round- table debate on the implications of the changes in Mozambique. Last month, Agneta Lind and Anton Johnston -- former aid workers from Sweden and Britain -- wrote of their doubts about the effectiveness of economic reforms. 

"Since the introduction of structural adjustment policies in 1987," they said, Mozambique's deficit has risen -- by 45% since 1988 -- and the country has become increasingly dependent on foreign assistance. Aid, they wrote, "is often overvalued by the donor," and a large proportion is used to purchase goods and consulting services and to support aid bureaucracies in the donor countries. Lind and Johnston also worry that aid money encourages corruption and conspicuous consumption among a Mozambican elite. And they are concerned that the government's move away from socialist policies might signal a waning commitment to economic justice.

Aid workers who have spent time in Mozambique tend to argue that the country has always had a large measure of democratic debate. And the process would have evolved further, they say, except for a 13-year war with the Renamo rebel group that has killed 100,000 people, created a million refugees, and threatens five million with starvation.

Long-time supporters of Mozambique argue, for example, that the abolition of the death penalty in the new constitution is a return to long-held values of the ruling Frelimo party. At its independence in 1975, Mozambique became one of a handful of countries to abolish the death penalty, but reintroduced it in 1979, amid growing atrocities by Renamo. In practice, however, the death penalty has been unused for years.

The Bush administration remains convinced that the economic reform package will bring results. The Mozambique government's moves "to open up the economy to market forces" are directly related to economic development, Bush told Chissano during their meeting. Officials of international financial institutions are hopeful as well. James Edgerly of the Arthur D. Little consulting firm, who has just completed a year's work on Mozambique for the World Bank, says the country's potential in agriculture, fisheries, tourism and mining could make it a wealthy country.

Instead, it is one of the poorest and most miserable -- and will remain so, everyone agrees, until the war ends. For several months, Mozambican church officials, along with the leaders of Kenya and Zimbabwe, have tried unsuccessfully to organize peace talks.

The latest stumbling block was Renamo's insistence that Frelimo negotiate not as a government but as a political party, a condition Mozambique dismissed as unrealistic.

Lately, though, Renamo's position appears to be softening. And observers speculate that a recent series of grisly attacks, including one on Feb. 4 that killed nearly eighty Mozambicans on a train, might be Renamo's way of showing its strength before talks begin.

Africa News asked Jose Oscar Monteiro, minister of state in the Mozambican president's office, about the current situation.

AN: How much does the draft constitution represent a real change in direction, and how much are there threads in the proposed changes that go back to the founding of Frelimo, and are part of long-standing principles?

Monteiro: There is both the factor of an evolution of the internal process of democracy and the factor of change. I would say that Frelimo has had a basic commitment to free and open discussion. What does this mean? It means discussing everything with the population, trying to popularize the decisions, and having the feeling of what were the sentiments of the population about any given problem. This is linked to the very intense popular participation that marked the development of Frelimo before independence, and immediately after independence.

The distinctive mark of Frelimo's action -- more than political principle or completely defined political ideology -- was this popular participation. And I think what we realize now is that we have many more educated people -- 15 years make a big difference, although far from what is needed-- and with the development of society in general, it's normal that people begin having different ways of thinking about problems.

In the beginning it was much easier to reach a consensus; now it takes longer. Therefore, it's an internal need -- the evolution of the society requires more democratization.

I'll give you an example. In the preparation of the party congresses, for a certain period, discussions were held at the level of the party. But the questions relating to the last party congress were discussed by the common citizens in the various neighborhoods. So as the society is changing, there is in fact a transformation. This evolution requires changes. Some are more visible than others; when you make changes in a document like the constitution, it is more dramatic, and calls your attention.

I would say that we remain committed to the objectives of providing always a greater civic participation in the development of the country. Are there changes in the economic policy? Definitely, yes. We have been drawing, not only now, but since '82, '83, the lessons of our experiences. We think now that much more role must be given to the family sector in agriculture. This was said in the past, but in practical terms, we did not manage to do what we were saying.

AN: Why not?

Monteiro: One of the reasons was that the Portuguese network that formed the family sector was disrupted ¢when Portuguese settlers fled at independence/ and we had to take over, without experience. At the same time, the state had to take over big agricultural estates, and all the resources of the state went to these projects. Of course, if you see it in the global picture, it does not represent much. There was not really global collectivization or anything like this -- but even in the limited scale, it was draining all the resources. The trend in our state machinery, in the sector of agriculture, was to concentrate every effort in these sectors. The idea was that at least these sectors could provide the basic solutions to the problems of the people.

As a principle it's not wrong; certain basic things we have to be sure to secure. But finally we realized that a huge state conglomerate does not mean really that it works better, that it is more sensitive to the needs of the people. The state shops ¢a system of government-owned stores/ came as a result of shops that were abandoned ¢by the Portuguese/, but at some point we thought that this was a good system. Then we realized, no, this is not the way; the capacities of the state are limited, and the citizen can do much more. I think the society becomes more flexible, and more free. And the state instead can concentrate on what it must do, for instance, to guarantee education -- this is by itself, in my opinion, the most important task for the future.

AN: What are the most important of the proposed constitutional changes?

Monteiro: Elections will be direct, by universal suffrage, direct and secret ballot at every level, and will no longer be linked to the structures of the party. There will be a limitation of terms of office for the president. I think this is very important, because it is one very concrete way of showing to the people that those who are in a position of command are not there for life. They are there because they have the trust of people, they

are the servants, the representatives of those who have elected them. So this is the first principle of democracy.

The second one is the guarantee of individual rights, where we expect the judiciary to play a more important role than in the past. We have a very young judiciary branch, we don't have many lawyers, but we feel that we have the basis upon which to build an independent judiciary that will guarantee these rights of the citizens.

AN: These changes are taking place in the midst of a great conflict. Why do you think you can afford to do such things as abolish the death penalty now, when it was first instituted because of the attacks by Renamo?

Monteiro: Well, I would say that the death penalty, when we introduced it, was a kind of threat against potential violators. As a threat it might or might not have a certain effect.

But the extent of the attacks made it difficult to use this penalty, which would be excessive, if applied to the hundreds of people who have been committing crimes. We realize these people were mostly led to commit crimes because they were abducted, coerced. I have myself spoken with many of these people who have been ¢kidnapped/ -- very young people 10, 12, 16 years old. Therefore, we think that the purpose of such a penalty would not be served by using it. That's the reason why this penalty was abolished.

In practical terms, I must say that for years the penalty has not been used. What we are doing in the proposed draft of the constitution is to recognize some things that we have been doing already for some years.

AN: Some of your critics say the Mozambican government is standing in the way of peace talks by rejecting Renamo's demand that you come to negotiations, not as a government, but as a political party and without any preconditions. How do you see the prospects for peace -- and the obstacles?

Monteiro: There are different obstacles. First I think that we should do our utmost to advance in this process because the destruction is too big. The second thing is that whether you like the government or not, the government is in place; we cannot go back to the times of the Portuguese and discuss about a new state. But there are many things that can be discussed. You see that the proposal of the constitution is not yet

approved and is open for discussion. Therefore, we have said that Renamo people could come and participate in the discussion of the constitution.

When we say we want to know what we are going to discuss, that is because certain things have to be accepted, not just by the government but by the people. We don't believe that we should make ¢fundamental/ changes without consulting the population, which has fought for this freedom we have. I think the country should not be held at ransom to make changes, just because some people by force are imposing them. So the changes that must come, including the changes in the constitution, should be accepted by all of the population. Renamo are a group of people, they are Mozambicans -- despite the type of actions they use, yes, we accept it, and we accept that they should participate in the process. But they cannot impose their will on the others.

AN: What is it that Renamo wants to put on the agenda for talks?

Monteiro: Actually, we have been trying to know what they want. We say that they can come, openly, and in the process of discussing the constitution, and later on in the process of elections, they can make their own proposals. For instance, if they want a multi-party state, this is a question that -- as you have seen in the constitution -- is not mentioned. It is a problem that we decided to put to popular discussion, because it's an important change, and we have to take into account the political culture of the country, the way people have been thinking, and the need for the people to assess the different positions and take a stand. Even if many people might think that one of the systems has more democracy than the other -- even something which is more "democratic" should not be imposed in an undemocratic way! Because then, again, we will have resistance.

AN: How is Renamo sustained?

Monteiro: I think they still get forces supporting them outside of the country. Renamo was founded in 1976 by the white government in what was then Rhodesia. When Rhodesia became independent Zimbabwe, Renamo's base of support shifted to South Africa.

In December, President de Klerk was in Mozambique, and he was asked by the journalists, "What about South African support?" He said the following: "Well, I have given instructions to the official bodies linked to the state to stop every kind of support. But I cannot guarantee that there are not private interests doing it."

The problem is that ¢supporting Renamo/ was transferred from an official operation into a private operation, in order to leave the government out of the action. Now what is important is to track down the people who are using their positions, their means, their airplanes, to continue providing support. And we know, and everybody knows, that this is going on.

Different types of support continue to pour in and enable Renamo to continue thinking that they should go on fighting. I think this encouragement -- it's more the encouragement that they are getting -- is really hardening their position. I think they should accept the very open conditions that are laid down now, which is basically no conditions. And this suffering and pillaging and raping people and using young people and trans-forming their minds and provoking constant movement of the population and therefore hunger and malnutrition with what it entails for the future generations -- must stop very quickly.

AN: What, from your point of view, is behind Renamo's actions?

Monteiro: You have to look into the history of Renamo. It began as a destabilizing factor -- sheer destruction. The objective was to destroy a government. This government had a popular base, for historical reasons, including the fact that Frelimo is the movement that started the victorious war of independence. We took many measures that were in the interest of the people. With the generosity that was typical of this period, we were distributing everything that we could in what we thought was the most just manner. So the way to destroy this base of popular support was not to mobilize support against Frelimo, but to terrorize people.

I was governor in a province, the province of Gaza, and for some months I was baffled with the kind of actions that the bandits were doing. I said, if they want to mobilize the population, why do they act this way? Only later I realized that you can rule a certain number of people through terror. Not for a long time -- but people accept terror. Experience all over the world, not only Mozambique, shows that. What was done in Mozambique was to use this experience in a very cruel and open manner.

Africa News


March 26, 1990


AFRICAN CALL FOR ACCOUNTABLE GOVERNMENT


LENGTH: 1012 words


It was planned a year and a half ago. But last month's conference on Popular Participation in Development, held in Arusha, Tanzania, coincided with a worldwide wave of democratic fervor that gave the gathering an added sense of urgency.

"For us in Africa," said Adebayo Adedeji, who heads the United Nations-sponsored Economic Commission for Africa (ECA), "popular participation is not a mere slogan. It is a matter of life and death." 

Convened by the ECA along with a host of non-governmental organizations, the meeting attracted 500 participants, many from grassroots groups such as trade unions, youth and women's organizations -- constituencies whose views were evident in the final conference declaration.

The emphasis on people power was visible in the seating arrangements at Arusha's international conference center. After passing through an entrance hall crowded with booths displaying homemade wares and locally-based technologies, governmental and UN representatives were directed to seats in the wings, while delegates from non-governmental organizations were placed front and center under a huge banner that read: "The People."

Over the next five days, participants discussed Africa's development crisis in overtly political terms. The delegates "explicitly identified the lack of participatory processes as the primary cause of Africa's unyielding, decade-long economic catastrophe," says Salim Lone, editor of the UN publication Africa Recovery.

"People kept referring to events in Eastern Europe," says Carol Capps of Church World Service, an ecumenical relief and development agency. "The feeling was, if those entrenched governments can change, why can't African governments?"

The conference adopted a 13-page charter that says African governments must "yield space to the people." But recognizing the reluctance of governments everywhere to cede power voluntarily, the charter calls on African people themselves to press for democracy and "to establish independent people's organizations that are genuinely grassroots, democratically administered and self-reliant."

"I think one result of this," Capps predicts, "will be that non-governmental organizations in Africa will increase both in number and strength."

Conference participants recognized that such a course would not be easy. Mazide N'Diaye, president of an umbrella network of voluntary development organizations known as FAVDO (Forum for African Voluntary Development Organizations), said that non-governmental organizations have learned that most African countries leave little room for people's initiatives. But it is increasingly evident that African governments cannot meet the needs of their people, N'Diaye said, so people must organize themselves to fill the gap.

Reflecting the prominence of discussions about the role of women, the charter says that the attainment of equal social, economic and political rights for women must be a central feature of democratic and participatory development.

Ruth Engo-Tgeja, a conference organizer who works with Unifem, the UN agency for women's affairs, said that women's issues must be viewed in a new way. In the past, she said, such priorities as water pumps and day care centers were seen as matters of women's rights. "The more we think about these issues," she said, "the more we believe getting water for the community is not a woman's right. It is a community right . . . ¢Day care/ should be a mainstream problem and not a women's problem."

Having women in decision-making structures, Engo-Tgeja said, insures a responsiveness to important community concerns. She pointed to the Arusha meeting as a case study of that process. Women on the conference coordinating committee, she said, "were able to bring out issues that could not be perceived by our male colleagues" -- issues that were later adopted as part of the charter.

The conference theme of popular participation ensured that the question of democracy would be firmly linked to the debate on economic development issues. Hassan Sunmonu, Secretary-General of the Organization of African Trade Union Unity, said that structural adjustment programs (SAPs) have been imposed -- by international institutions and by African governments -- without the agreement of Africa's people. Although economic reforms are needed, Sunmonu said, the SAPs that have been mandated by the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank haven't worked, because they put the heaviest burdens on the poorest citizens.

Conference delegates generally appeared to share Sunmonu's view that the economic growth rates and increased exports advocated by SAPs have done little to achieve the goal of alleviating poverty.

Labor unions have played a brave role in exposing the myth of SAPs, Sunmonu said, but governments have responded with arrests and repression. "Our members have paid the price for telling the truth," he said.

The Arusha conference charter condemns the suppression of dissent. It defends the right to strike and calls for a halt to widespread bans on youth and student organizations and the "arbitrary" closure of educational institutions. It cited ten indicators of popular participation, including media freedom, protection of the environment and flourishing grassroots movements. The charter also carries a warning to unrepresentative governments, noting that "the forces of freedom and democracy are contagious."

More than a few delegates expressed uneasiness at the reception they might receive upon returning home. But the response of a Tanzanian government representative, who said the host country would not only endorse the charter but would seek ways to implement it, ended the meeting on a note of cheer. It was left to the ECA's Adedeji to issue a challenge to the delegates themselves. "No one else will solve our problems for us," he told the group. "Achieving a turnaround in our society is our primary responsibility. Let us show the increasingly skeptical world that we can make it and that we are determined to make it."

Africa News


March 26, 1990


A SEASON OF DISCONTENT


LENGTH: 679 words


Laurent Ghagbo, an opposition leader in Cote d'Ivoire, was scheduled to go to Paris this month to appear on a live television program about pro-democracy movements in Africa. But the French Embassy denied him a visa. And the Ivorian government cooperated by placing him under house arrest.

Although the restrictions lasted less than a day, the incident typifies the sensitivity of African governments -- and their Western backers -- to a growing surge of popular pressure for political reforms. 

In neighboring Benin, a national movement of workers and civil servants has forced Brigadier General Mathieu Kerekou to accept an entirely new Cabinet and a new prime minister and to free all political prisoners. Although Kerekou remains head of state, he has agreed to call a national conference to draw up a new democratic constitution.

The military leader of Niger, Ali Saibou, has also had to yield to protests, in this case from students who forced him to sack four Cabinet members regarded as taking a hard line against demonstrators.

Gabon, too, is responding to pressures for change. Earlier this month, the national assembly began discussing political reforms that it says will lead to elections within six months. The debate followed weeks of strikes and protests that led President Omar Bongo to dissolve the ruling Democratic Party on February 23. By mid-March, the embattled leader had promised to come up with pay raises for Gabonese workers by the end of the month -- a pledge he may have trouble keeping.

On Africa's northern tier, Moroccan dissidents have been emboldened by political reforms in Tunisia and in next-door Algeria, where strikes and demonstrations have become a daily affair. As in other African countries, economic crisis has fueled the demands for democratic reforms that are coming from across the political spectrum. "The current economic dead end reflects the dead end of democracy in Morocco," said the Al Alam newspaper, an organ of the rightist Istiqlal party.

After five years of internationally-mandated austerity programs, Morocco's economy has not improved. Last year its trade deficit doubled, and citizens are bracing for a new round of price hikes and wage cuts. In oil-rich Gabon, a sharp drop in buying power spurred the protests. President Bongo blamed the problem on restrictions imposed by the International Monetary Fund (IMF).

"Explosions will be hard to avoid," warns the French magazine Jeune Afrique. "African leaders multiply their SOS calls in the direction of Western countries. But the appeals are forwarded to the IMF and the World Bank who tighten the screws more and more."

Some of the unrest, as in Cote d'Ivoire, where students and civil servants have been engaged in running battles with the police, has been violent. In Kenya, students and police tangled at the funeral of murdered foreign minister Robert Ouko. Their calls for government accountability have coincided with increasingly outspoken appeals for greater democracy from human rights advocates, especially church leaders.

From Ethiopia to Mozambique to Benin, a move away from explicit Marxist policies has been part of the pattern. That trend has encouraged analysts in Western governments, who believe that free market economies are the key to easing Africa's development crisis.

African economists are not so quick to agree. Multi-party democracy doesn't have to mean embracing capitalism, many say, especially on a continent where investment capital is scarce or non-existent. Both governments and protesters are vigorously questioning the impact of the economic reforms that were designed to pull Africa's economies out of their downhill slide.

The problems facing Africa are daunting and the proposed solutions numerous. But more and more, Africans are insisting that recovery is impossible without greater democracy. In this and future issues, Africa News will examine the winds of change sweeping Africa and the response of the outside powers who still, to a great extent, influence Africa's destiny.

From the jubilant independence celebrations in Namibia to the quiet end of one-party rule in Benin, a wave of democratic sentiment is sweeping across Africa. Virtually ignored by the outside world, African students, professionals, trade unionists and clerics are pressing for a greater say in their countries' affairs. This issue takes a look at some of those campaigns and the varying government responses.

SOUTH AFRICA: TOXIC WASTE DUMPING CHARGED


LENGTH: 145 words


A coalition of South African labor and environmental groups in cooperation with Greenpeace in the United States has won an apparent victory in a campaign against a U.S. company which ships toxic waste to South Africa. Mercury waste from the British firm Thor Chemicals in Natal has polluted the Mngeweni River, used by the local people for washing and drinking. The groups also say that workers exposed to the toxins in the Thor plant have become ill with mercury poisoning. American Cyanamid has been exporting over ten tons of the used chemicals a year to the waste recycling company. The campaign included protests at the South African plant and at Cyanamid's New Jersey headquarters, a Cyanamid shareholdes' meeting, and at one of its chemical plants. Amid the public outcry over the waste trade, the South African government shut down the Thor plant.

April 30, 1990


HORN OF AFRICA: FAMINE THREATENS EMBATTLED HORN


SECTION: AROUND THE CONTINENT


LENGTH: 1300 words


Continuing strife in the Horn of Africa has once again plunged large parts of Ethiopia and Sudan into crisis.

In Ethiopia, an estimated five million people are currently at risk of starvation, as the fighting continues between government forces and two major insurgent armies. In neighboring Sudan, four and a half million people are directly affected by the on-going conflict between the military government of General Omar Al-Bashir and the Sudan People's Liberation Army (SPLA).

Although the wars have attracted little international attention, the seriousness of the situation has prompted international pressure on the governments of Ethiopia and Sudan and on the rebel forces in each country. But experts believe there may not be sufficient pressure or enough time to avert wide-scale famine. 

Two Congressional hearings earlier this year attempted to draw U.S. attention to the crisis. In his testimony Assistant Secretary of State for Africa Herman Cohen called for increased donations, a negotiated free passage of food and international airlifts in government and rebel-held areas.

According to Cohen, the Ethiopian conflict may be the largest in the world today in terms of combatants, as well as the most destructive. The Joint Relief Partnership, a coalition of church agencies plus the government relief organ, is distributing food to government-held territories and to areas controlled by the Tigray People's Liberation Front (TPLF) -- a rebel force fighting to overthrow the present Ethiopian regime. While the joint effort signifies a step toward government/rebel cooperation, it falls short of the estimated 635,000 metric tons of relief needed. However, no similar effort has been mounted in the northern area of Eritrea.

The conflict has intensified in that region, where the Eritrean People's Liberation Front (EPLF) has been fighting for independence from Ethiopia for three decades. Throughout this month, the government forces of President Mengistu Haile Mariam have been attacking Massawa, the port town captured by the EPLF in February. The UN Food and Agriculture Organization has said that without the use of Massawa for major relief efforts, mass starvation cannot be avoided.

Although the EPLF has said it would allow the port to be a staging area for relief flights, Ethiopian officials want airlifts to be flown into Asmara, a government-held town. But, according to U.S. Congressional sources, a stockpile of undistributed food already exists there.

So far, the only effective means of getting food to the rebel-held territories in Eritrea, where over 75% of the famine victims are located, is through cross-border efforts from Sudan. The relief arm of the EPLF is now working at capacity but lacks an adequate supply of trucks, personnel and food.

A peaceful settlement between the ruling Workers Party of Ethiopia and the EPLF and other warring factions has thus far been illusive. Negotiations over Eritrea, under the mediation of former President Jimmy Carter, stalled last December on the issue of United Nations participation. As a UN member state, Ethiopia would have to invite the world body to the talks before it would agree to attend. The EPLF has asked the UN to be one of the observers, but Ethiopia refuses to concur. Dayle Powell, the director of the Conflict Resolution Program at the Carter Center in Atlanta, says that the Ethiopian government has agreed to begin substantive peace talks, but that the EPLF will only discuss the relief issue and the UN matter. Because the two sides are so distant, according to Powell, Carter has concluded that talks right now would serve no useful purpose. Former Tanzanian President Julius Nyerere has agreed to co-chair with President Carter any further peace efforts, a development which even some Ethiopian government critics say may enhance the possibilities for peace.

Observers see President Mengistu's hold on power as threatened by several factors, including the recent rebel military gains, a disgruntled Ethiopian army which led an unsuccessful coup attempt last May and whose members are tiring of years of forced conscription and poor treatment, and a drop in economic assistance from long-time supporters East Germany and the Soviet Union.

In March, the President unveiled an economic reform package which allows for private investment, private enterprise and private property. Some Western analysts herald the changes as a major step toward economic liberalization. Others, noting that the program does not mention a reduction in military expenditures, believed to be over 60% of the budget, warn that the announcement may be only a scheme to ensure support from the international donors. A high-level Ethiopian dele-gation, led by foreign policy chief, Kesse Kedebe, visited Washington last month to garner economic sup-port from the U.S., the World Bank and the International Monetary Fund. The delegation included advisors from the Israeli government, now one of Ethiopia's strongest supporters.

In Sudan, international efforts to force the government and the SPLA into an agreement on relief efforts have succeeded. In early April, Operation Lifeline began airlifts into the country's war-torn southern region. Operation Lifeline Sudan, created to assist the nearly 3.5 million displaced persons in the conflict areas, is back in action. About a hundred metric tons of food a day is being airlifted into the southern town of Juba. The needs are great and time will be a crucial factor in the effort's success. The rainy season begins next month, making travel throughout much of the region impossible.

Although military offensives in Juba have subsided, fighting continues, and heavy casualties have been reported on both sides. Many civilians are still being forced to flee their homes in search of food and safety.

Peace negotiations between the SPLA and the ruling Revolutionary Command Council, held in Nairobi last December and hosted by Kenyan President Daniel arap Moi were unsuccessful. More recently, Zairean President Mobutu Seso Seko has agreed to try his hand at mediating the conflict. And according to one State Department official, the U.S. is actively working behind the scenes to seek an end to the war.

Roger Winter, head of the U.S. Committee for Refugees, recently returned from Sudan and is less optimistic. He believes that despite its call for peace, the Sudanese government is actually taking steps that will widen the gulf between it and the SPLA. And Winter says that rebel forces, while agreeing to negotiations, feel that talks would be unproductive.

Since taking over the country in a coup last June, President Al Bashir has been moving his country closer to the fundamentalist positions that the largely-Christian rebel forces have been resisting. Bashir's main internal support comes from the National Islamic Front, a radical fundamentalist party. Iran, Iraq and Libya have increased their military support for Sudan. Sharia, or Islamic law, was instated last December. And last month, the government announced its plan to eventually unite with Libya.

Meanwhile, the governments' human rights record has come under fire. The U.S.-based Africa Watch recently published a report condemning the Sudanese government for alleged use of torture and imprisonment without trial as well as for a lack of attention to the plight of its starving population. Africa Watch calls the famine situation in Sudan "an entirely manmade disaster."

Last week, the Sudanese government reported an attempted take-over by retired army officials, but it appears that Bashir remains firmly in control. To discourage further in-surrection, the president ordered the execution of 28 officers involved in the coup attempt.
