Africa News


April 30, 1990


ZAIRE: ONCE AGAIN, MOBUTU MOVES TO OUTFLANK CRITICS


SECTION: DEMOCRACY


LENGTH: 2591 words


It was 'vintage Mobutu' last week, as one of Africa's longest-serving autocrats yielded to increasing pressures and announced an end to one-party rule. Although Mobutu Sese Seko has held power in Zaire for 25 years by using a deft combination of strong-arm and persuasive political tactics, this latest action is probably his boldest and riskiest.

Mobutu, who seized power in a 1965 coup backed by the CIA, announced he would step down as head of the ruling party, and he named a new prime minister to head the government. But he made clear he was not forfeiting ultimate political authority. "My role will certainly not be to cut the blue ribbon for bridges and roads," he told a press conference following his nationally-broadcast address on April 24. He said he would be "above party politics" and "the arbiter, or better still, the last recourse."

The president also announced an end to a ban on Western-style dress, which has required Zairean men to wear high collar jackets similar to those worn by Chinese rulers. But he said he was retaining the prohibition on use of "Christian" names, saying the employment of "authentic Zairean names is an inestimable cultural acquisition that cannot be put into question." 

Three political parties will be legalized initially, Mobutu said. The ruling Popular Movement for the Revolution, which all Zaireans have been required to join, will be one of them, along with the Union for Democracy and Social Progress (UDPS) and a third as-yet unnamed group. He also said UDPS leader Tshisekedi wa Mulumba would be freed from house arrest and allowed to participate along with other opponents in campaigning for elections next April.

Mobutu's about-face comes at a time of growing international and domestic criticism of his regime. Last year, the Zaire leader attained a measure of immunity from attack when he emerged as the accepted mediator between the government of neighboring Angola and the Angolan rebel movement Unita, led by Jonas Savimbi. But Mobutu's failure to bring the two sides together has eroded that status. Last month, two of his prominent Congressional critics, Rep. Stephen Solarz (D-NY) and Howard Wolpe (D-MI) issued stinging denunciations of the Zaire leader's record and called for an end to all U.S. military aid and a requirement that all economic assistance be channeled through non-governmental organizations. Zaire is currently the largest American aid recipient in sub-Saharan Africa.

Mobutu is widely reported to have amassed a huge fortune -- estimated at as much as $ 5 billion -- at least in part by siphoning off government revenues and aid funds from Western donors. The Zaire leader "certainly should be able to make up any shortfall" in U.S. aid money just from the interest on his wealth, Solarz said in Congressional testimony earlier this month. "Credible reports of corruption and misuse of aid abound, and make a mockery of U.S. efforts to transfer aid through the Zairean bureaucracy," he said.

The World Bank has suspended disbursement of funds to Zaire in a dispute with the government over the level of presidential spending, the Wall Street Journal reports.

At home, Mobutu has also run into an unusual outpouring of complaints. After announcing a "national dialogue" earlier this year to solicit the opinions of Zaireans throughout the country, Mobutu was deluged with written critiques of the nation's problems and his own performance. Although the government has not released the submissions, many have circulated privately.

An unusually-critical statement was issued by the conference of Roman Catholic bishops, calling for major changes in the government and in Zaire society. A part of the clerics' ire is directed at the international financial institutions -- the World Bank and International Monetary Fund -- as well as at Western governments which have been insisting on repayment of Zaire's $ 7.4 billion foreign debt. The borrowed funds benefited only a privileged, corrupt few, the bishops say, and they charge that the international lenders sanctioned the diversions.

The following excerpts from the bishops' statement were translated from the document as published by the Paris-based weekly Jeune Afrique.

We, the members of the permanent committee of the bishops of Zaire gathered in an extraordinary session in accordance with the mandate received by the Bishopric Assembly, want, in the name of the latter and in virtue of our pastoral responsibilities, to join our voices with the voices of all the sons and daughters of Zaire, to put forth the contribution of the Catholic Church to the public debate on the functioning of national institutions and the general situation of the country.

In other places, decisive events and essential reforms have shaken the geopolitical balance and toppled governmental systems, particularly in Eastern Europe and certain African nations.

These substantial reforms force us to question ourselves, not only because of the fall into world disrepute, but mainly because the present political system in our country has borrowed a number of concepts, structures and practices from Eastern European countries who have now abandoned them.

Those are the reasons why a public debate is a necessary national step, so that all the institutions in charge of governing the state and managing the public good are subjected to an evaluation.

Such an evaluation is definitely called for since the Second Republic has just entered its twenty-fifth year of existence. Also, we rejoice at the courageous call by the head of state to all sons and daughters of Zaire, asking that, through critiques, suggestions, and constructive propositions, they each contribute to the search for the path to happiness, for integral development, and for the prosperity to which the whole population aspires. Our conscience as priests compells us to take part in this national debate, because these stakes are of prime importance to the future of our country.

In light of the situation in our country, and after rereading the universal teachings of the church and of our own interior directives, we reaffirm that the principal cause -- if not the root -- of paralysis in our national institutions and the structural crisis of the state resides in the hybrid political system. This system gives us "liberalism," offers the advantages to a small minority of owning private property, and at the same time borrows from "totalitarianism" the methods of conquering and of maintaining power. Zaire has inherited the weaknesses of both systems, from which has arisen a system of absolute and autocratic power.

Amid the growing ambiguities and incoherences of our hybrid political system we find the institutionalization of the Popular Movement for the Revolution, transformed into party/state four years later. This can only have the effect of an expensive and useless duplication of governmental structures as well as the managing of a political party by the state. In addition, we question whether this party is really a party, since all Zaireans are forced to be members.

In this hybrid system, the presidential regime, as it is organized in our country, gives the head of state responsibility for the actions of the party and for the executive powers of government. Such an organization inevitably leads to political monism and a concentration of power that leaves little place for the responsibility of alternative authorities.

This regime risks inhibiting, if not eliminating altogether the freedom to criticize the functioning of institutions.

In such a system, public criticism of institutions made by the head of state and his collaborators is hardly convincing. The criticism gives the impression of the relinquishment of power or of a confession without consequences. In this way, the head of state is placed in a very uncomfortable situation, because -- being both the judge and the party -- he cannot sanction the faults of his subordinates, although they present themselves as his advisors or the executor of his orders.

This is why we believe in the necessity of distinguishing the three powers: the legislative, the executive and the judiciary. Furthermore, we think that in a presidential regime, democracy cannot be guaranteed unless the people exercising public functions are truly accountable, and unless the law provides for a structure of checks and balances of the executive by an entity representing the people.

The National Bank and its various institutions obey political imperatives, and function as the cashier of the party/state. They are at the disposition of certain people, particularly the authorities of the country, who each take what they want as they wish. In addition, the national budget is quantitatively insignificant, under-estimated in revenues, unequally spread in spending, submitted to arbitrary deductions, managed at whim and without reference to a comprehensive financial law.

The functioning of our institutions as described above, is well known and is encouraged by Zaire's foreign partners. In fact, they know those with whom they have signed contracts very well and they know where the money they have credited to Zaire lies.

It is, therefore, abnormal and unjust that those who have established this financial system -- which diverts capital to those who use it to their own advantage, then lend it with interest to the rightful recipients -- demand, in return, budgetary restrictions that bring extreme poverty to populations forced to submit to the weight of debts they did not incur.

The people are, therefore, double victims of the international money-lender system, sustained by countries who proclaim themselves promoters of social justice and human rights.

The considerations put forth show that our country is at a crossroad. We are either engaged in a path of deep reform of the system and the political institutions of the state, or we have condemned our country to suffer even greater pains.

In any case, the reforms of the political system must be designed to promote the true cultural values of our people; to safeguard national unity, national integrity, and the peace acquired at great expense; to guarantee the fundamental rights and liberties inherent in human dignity; to assure the participation of everyone, not only to exploit the riches, but to reap the fruit of common effort; and to build an economy that functions for the profit of the nation rather than to serve small-group and outside interests.

Beyond fundamental political reforms needed to address the difficulties, ambiguities and inadequacies of the ruling Popular Movement for the Revolution, the presidential system and the managing of public finances, we want to stress the necessity of a new constitution that would be developed through a national debate involving the entire nation. This new constitution would be the expression of a national consensus and would apply to all Zaireans without exception.

In this context of fundamental reform, we believe it is an opportune time to suggest changes and establish priorities.

* It should be unequivocably recognized that the party is not the nation, and that political organizations have to be distinct from the people, and judged and accepted by the people. It is imperative that the party renounce its leading role over the people and put an end to the useless duplication of the institutions.

* All Zaireans should enjoy a stable constitution and national laws that will be passed or modified only if the whole nation profits. Procedures established by the law must be respected, and the laws themselves should be ethical and conform to the aspirations of the Zairean people.

* All legal measures should be taken to avoid a situation where a minority or a group of citizens can gain power and impose it on the nation for their own profit. All citizens who are competent to do so have the right to participate without discrimination in the management of public affairs.

* All those who exercise public power should stop complaining about what is going wrong in their jurisdiction. On the contrary, they should fulfill their responsibilities, punish the guilty and reward good and loyal citizens.

* We have to avoid undermining the exercise of authority by giving responsibility to people who are incapable or dishonest.

* All national agencies, at whatever level, should answer to a hierarchical authority that is able to take necessary actions. In particular, governmental bodies should be responsible not only to the head of state but also to an agency representing the people that is empowered to rectify deficiencies.

* Freedom of speech, recognized and guaranteed by the constitution, must become effective, because it is indispensable for the exercise of fundamental liberties as well as for the integral and harmonious development of the nation.

* The managers of institutions and public finances must obey specific and established laws. In particular, no politician, state functionary or private group can substitute for the Zairean people and manage public funds as though they were personal inheritances. All abuses and infractions in this domain must be severely penalized by law.

* To contribute to solving the problem of national debt, we must encourage foreign investors but also utilize our own financial resources. It is imperative that the flight of capital be stopped, and that Zaireans reinvest that capital in their own country.

* One of the primary obligations of the political authorities of any country is to guarantee access to medical care and a quality education to all citizens. This demands the rehabilitation of teaching and medical facilities. Also, it is urgent that we study the ways to improve the social security system, analyze the politics of salaries and stabilize the national currency. Experience already shows that in places far from commercial centers and rarely supervised by superior authorities, the cases of administrative abuses multiply. To better protect the population, it is necessary to pay all functionaries of the state and military personnel adequately.

* For these types of reforms to suc-ceed, each son and daughter of Zaire must possess a great patriotic spirit, strong loyalties, and a true love for the fatherland and its integral development. To this end, it is necessary that the education given to individuals and communities concentrate on honesty and the distinction between personal and communal good.

Our country's suffering is explained by inadequate structures and systems that favor the crumbling of morals and the other evils that we deplore. Faced with this situation, we are tempted to believe that only the change of structures and systems will give us moral help and material prosperity.

This change is, of course, necessary, but what is even more important is the conservation and the renewal of the heart and spirit of the sons and daughters of Zaire, because it is from the heart and spirit of men and women that spring the desires and aspirations that inspire the economic and political systems.

The social and moral environment in which we live has, in a way, contaminated us all. The renewal of Zaire and the necessary changes demand that we become new people. This is why it is necessary to support the actions of moral people, notably the church, who try to mold consciences and incite people to pursue transcendent values.

Africa News


April 30, 1990


VICE PRESIDENT: OUR SYSTEM DEMOCRATIC


LENGTH: 1571 words


AN: How would you rate Kenya's economic performance -- are you satisfied that government policies are working?

Saitoti: For the last five years Kenya's economic growth rate has averaged not less than five percent. Indeed, per capita income in Kenya has also been growing positively. This is within a continent where the per capita income for a number of years in Africa actually has been negative. I think there are very few countries in Africa with a positive growth rate, so that is a major success.

But even more important is that Kenya has been able to feed its own people and has also been able to record surpluses for a number of years, save for the times of very serious droughts. We do have our problems and we do have our challenges. We are sparing no effort in facing up to the challenges. 

AN: To what do you attribute your success?

Saitoti: I have always taken the view that not much economic growth can take place in an atmosphere of political strife. We have had good pragmatic leadership; our president is a very far-sighted person and a person committed to the development of the country. And he has provided political stability. I have no doubt at all that without political stability we would not have been able to achieve what we have achieved.

AN: There's been concern expressed by African leaders and other people interested in Africa that the developments in Eastern Europe will take attention away from Africa; that investment and aid money will flow to Eastern Europe, to the detriment of Africa. In an interview we just published, U.S. Assistant Secretary of State Herman Cohen said that one-party African governments can expect to have difficulty in getting attention and assistance from the United States and other parts of the West. Your government is committed to a one-party system. Are you under pressure to change this system?

Saitoti: I just had a meeting with the new U.S. Agency for International Development administrator, and he was very explicit in his assurance that it's not the intention of the American government to shift various sources from Africa to Eastern Europe. A similar statement has been made by the managing director of the International Monetary Fund. We have received similar assurances from France and from quite a number of other countries.

But having said that we have heard parallels being drawn between Eastern Europe and Africa because the Eastern European countries have all had one-party systems, which have collapsed, and they now are having elections and multi-party structures. I think that is putting things in the wrong perspective. One must go back to history. The one-party system came to Europe immediately after the Second World War along with communism, imposed by Stalin. The people of the European countries were never consulted. The one-party system did not reflect the aspirations of the people.

But the one-party systems in African countries, where they do exist, came about as a result of the people's wish. People formed those parties as nationalist movements, movements which reflected the aspirations of the people, movements which were directed at the total emancipation of the people from colonialism. If you take the case of Kenya, we had more than one party. And during the time when there were these several parties -- there were more than two, finally they developed to only two parties -- each party was just a grouping of certain tribes. And during that time there was a lot of violence of a tribal nature.

It is the people themselves who then decided that the two party system did not serve their interests, especially from the point of view of uniting the country. And unity is so vital for the development of our country. So the party we have in Kenya is a party which has evolved as a result of the wishes of the people.

We say the advocates of the multi-party system do not understand the situation in Africa properly.

AN: Critics say that in Kenya there has been an erosion of democratic protections, of human rights, of the rule of law.

Saitoti: Let me tell you our own party -- Kanu -- although a single party, allows for a very great deal of debate within it, and this is true even during the elections. Our elections are very, very, very heated elections even at the party level. So, we feel that allowing people to talk the way they want in matters which they feel strongly about is provided for within the party. And within our own parliament, you will find that our debates are very lively. The government ministers are very much taken to task -- even myself as the vice president -- I've got to defend the various questions that are put forth to me by the parliamentarians. I myself, would wonder whether you have visited another country where debate is as openly carried out as it is in Kenya. Even in the press.

AN: What about a free press?

Saitoti: We do have a free press. The press does not have to say what the government says.

AN: But the government has banned several publications in recent years.

Saitoti: There have been publications which are very, very, very clearly not Kenyan but are probably being used by external forces. Kenyans have been used as mouthpieces -- but certainly the funding of those papers has come from outside because if you look at the actual circulation, there is no way the circulation can justify the publication of those papers. So, money must be coming from somewhere from outside. And again, if you look at those papers, they are not there for informing people, they are not there for promoting free debate or free press. They are there, basically, to abuse, to create problems; to carry out slander campaigns.

AN: Why did your government introduce the practice of queuing, having people stand in line to vote rather than cast ballots? It is a practice which Kenyan churches and international human rights groups have criticized as undemocratic.

Saitoti: Elections for the Parliament are still being done through so-called secret balloting. The queuing system is used at the primary stage. So many people, maybe twenty people, could be interested in a position -- and what you want to do is reduce that to two or three people. That is why we use that one at the primary stage, and it is there is to make sure that the election is carried out under the full glare of the people. And the people like it; the people want to say that 'look here, we stood behind so and so, and we expressed our own view'. Secret balloting has bred a lot of corruption in many countries.

AN: Recently, there have been new criticisms of the churches by Kenyan officials. Do you regard the churches as hostile to the government, as some kind of an unofficial opposition?

Saitoti: No, no, no, no. The Kenyan Government has always coexisted very well with the church. Our President is a very dedicated Christian. He's a person that attends the church services every Sunday. It is not the church that we have a problem with. If there is any problem, it is with a few individuals who make their own statements. But those are not the statements that represent the church.

AN: So you don't foresee a confrontation between the government and, say, the NCCK ¢National Christian Council of Kenya/? Several assistant ministers in your government have called for a crackdown ...

Saitoti: No, no. I do not think that there's likely to be a problem. As I say, essentially it is a problem of individuals.

AN: How would you characterize relations between Kenya and the U. S. right now?

Saitoti: We consider our relations with the American Government good. We have been working closely with the American Government over a number of years and we have no reason to believe that there is any tension at all. We are receiving assistance from them, we are collaborating with them in various forms. So, there would be no reason for us to think otherwise. And I don't believe that the American Government has any different view; I've not heard that.

AN: But your government has been criticized in Congress for its human rights record.

Saitoti: We know that Congress, like our parliament, is open; individuals will say what they want to say. The important thing is, it should be understood that we are a democratic country. We are a country committed to development. These are things that need to be taken into account.

AN: The death in mid-February of Foreign Minister Robert Ouko has shaken your country. An investigation is underway, and there have been rumors suggesting his death was ordered by rivals within the government.

Saitoti: Well, the Government is not run on the basis of rumors. The late minister for Foreign Affairs

and Cooperation was a very loyal person to the president, to the government, to the party. He was a hard working person. He was a man who was very popular among his own colleagues, and we all feel very aggrieved by his death. Probably the saddest person in the loss is the president himself.

The Kenya Government has enlisted the support of the British Scotland Yard, which is internationally known as a very competent investigation agency. We brought them in to work in cooperation with the Kenyan police and the other investigating arms of the government to insure that no stone will be left untouched in really coming to find out what caused the death. That is our commitment.

'OPPRESSION KNOWS NO COLOR'


LENGTH: 209 words


Archbishop Desmond Tutu, who is no stranger to controversy at home, is encountering it abroad as well. As the current president of the All Africa Conference of Churches, the archbishop is making frequent appearances in African capitals, where his comments are often less than welcomed by local officialdom. 

His remarks in Nairobi late last month provoked angry outcries from at least two government officials. Although Tutu gave a carefully-crafted speech at Nairobi University that tried to avoid being inflammatory, his audience seemed even more determined to draw him out on the issues of the day. And finally, after several diplomatic answers to questions about Kenya, Tutu minced no words when asked about oppression. After centuries during which people often suffered under those of another race, he said "now in many places those who oppress are often people of the same complexion."

The archbishop's call for democratic freedoms drew enthusiastic and prolonged applause. "The greatest stability, the greatest prosperity," he said, "can only come in a situation where people have the freedom to choose freely."

"Tutu could win an election here easily," said one veteran Kenyan journalist. "He is really popular with the masses."

Africa News


April 30, 1990


KENYA DEFENDS ONE PARTY RULE


LENGTH: 1536 words


Nairobi -- When President Daniel arap Moi summoned leaders of the ruling party from around the country to a day-long gathering here earlier this month, there was intense speculation about what would result. The Kenya African National Union, or Kanu -- the only political organization permitted under the country's one-party system -- has been expanding its authority and control into all sectors of Kenyan society.

"Whenever Kanu meets, we have to wonder who is in for trouble," said one government critic, referring to purges and crackdowns which have followed party leadership sessions.

The latest meeting of the Kanu national governing council took place amid signs of renewed tension between the government and top church figures (see Africa News, April 9), leading to conjecture that Moi might be planning a move against the National Christian Council of Kenya (NCCK). It also came just six weeks after the still-unexplained murder of the country's foreign minister, Dr. Robert Ouko, which has spawned a spate of rumors, some of them linking the death to inter-party rivalries or over-zealous Moi supporters. 

Although there is no organized voice in Kenya calling for multi-party rule or challenging Kanu's paramount position, events elsewhere in the world apparently have prompted Kenya's leaders, from Moi down, to rush to the defense of the current system. And when Archbishop Desmond Tutu, the anti-apartheid crusader and Nobel laureate, came to town for a meeting of the All Africa Conference of Churches, his comments on the right of people "to choose between different options in any sphere of life" were warmly cheered by a largely-student audience -- and sharply criticized by several government officials.

The lively interchange here echoes discussions taking place throughout Africa, as questions are raised about the efficacy and desirability of one-party and autocratic systems, particularly in light of the economic difficulties plaguing most of the continent.

"You have strong pressure developing in Africa, among the elites particularly, for loosening up the machinery of government to allow more opinions, if not parties," says Hilary Ng'weno, editor of Kenya's leading independent news magazine, Weekly Review.

But neither the developments in Eastern Europe nor the rumblings at home caused the Kanu council to question the current political structure. "It has definite advantages that far outweigh the costly modern experiments that are strange to the psychology, philosophy and life-style of Kenyans," the council declared following its April 3 session.

President Moi has also spoken out on one-party rule on several recent occasions. Last month he was quoted saying a multi-party arrangement could lead to "chaotic situations which would be difficult to reverse," and more recently he has said it would contribute to "tribal friction."

Kanu has been Kenya's sole functioning party throughout most of the post-independence period, although the single party structure was only added to the constitution in 1982. For a period in the 1960s, the Kenya People's Union (KPU) did mount a challenge to Kanu, until violent clashes between the parties' supporters led to the banning of the KPU and the jailing of its leader, Oginga Odinga, by the country's first president, Jomo Kenyatta.

That experience, says Vice President George Saitoti, has convinced Kenyans that with a two-party setup "there will be tension and fighting, there will be civil wars, and the country will be diverted from development." In an interview, he argued that "the two party system did not serve the interests of the people, especially from the point of view of uniting the country." Saitoti says that within Kanu there is "a very great deal of debate, even during the elections."

But if the interchange in the press and the parliament is spirited, Moi has demonstrated a level of intolerance to dissent that has taken his critics by surprise, especially considering the lack of any formalized opposition. The latest victim of the president's displeasure is Waruru Kanja, whose expulsion from Kanu was the main outcome of the national governing council meeting on April 3.

After a 20-year career in politics -- the last two as minister of information -- Kanja was dismissed from the cabinet by Moi the day before the Kanu council convened. Kanja's trouble stemmed from remarks he made last month in parliament, where he linked the Ouko death to the killings of other prominent Kenyan political figures and suggested a connection between the deaths and unnamed government figures.

According to the Nairobi-based Weekly Review magazine, 29 other Kenyans are currently under expulsion orders from Kanu, including Odinga, who in addition to having lead the only opposition party formed since independence is also a former vice president of both the country and the ruling party. He was ousted from Kanu membership in 1982 when he signalled his intention to set up a new opposition party.

The government's decision that year to institutionalize one-party rule was the first of a series of actions that have eroded the rule of law and protection of individual liberties, says Gibson Kamau Kuria, a prominent Nairobi attorney and human rights advocate. A 1988 constitutional amendment removed the security of tenure from senior judges. "It is a sad state of affairs," he said in an interview. At Kenya's independence from Britain in 1963, an independent judiciary was established, but "there is no longer any court which can enforce human rights in this country."

The preliminary election processes, which require voters to line up behind their preferred candidate rather than cast a secret ballot, has taken the country another step away from democracy, he argues. Kuria says the "queuing" system "which also has been criticized by churches ... is very unpopular."

But Vice President Saitoti says queuing is inherently more democratic, because it gives people the assurance that voting is taking place "under the full glare" of public scrutiny. Western-style secret balloting, he says, "has bred a lot of corruption in many countries."

According to Saitoti, who is also minister of finance, Moi's leadership has led to stability and steady economic growth, averaging at least five percent annually for the past five years. Saitoti and other Moi supporters say the president is leading the country effectively and enjoys broad popularity.

But government critics fear the country may be headed for a more repressive future, especially as the problems of economic inequality in the society create wider divisions between the impoverished majority and the political and economic elite.

Africa Watch, the New York-based human rights groups, warns that the government may ban the Nairobi Law Monthly, a outspoken journal edited by Gitobu Imanyara. "The magazine, which was launched in October 1987, remains among the few voices active in the defense of human rights in Kenya, following the ban of other outspoken magazines and a steady campaign of harassment against critics of government policies," Africa Watch says in a report issued this month. The report appeals to the Kenyan government "to end repression or non-violent dissent and to create an atmosphere within which all Kenyans can freely engage in meaningful public debate."

Three publications have been outlawed in the past two years. Two economic journals, Financial Review and Development Agenda, were banned in 1989, and Beyond, an NCCK magazine was shut down in 1988. But Saitoti argues that Kenya has a free press and that only those publications "being used by external forces" to promote disunity and "slander campaigns" have been banned.

Charges of outside financing and control also have been lodged against Kenya's churches, which form the largest institutional opposition to human rights abuses in the country. The long standing church/state conflict was revived several weeks ago, following the jailing of a Presbyterian minister who was sentenced to six years in prison for making what the government says were "subversive" entries in his personal datebook.

Church leaders deny any involvement in subversion and have criticized government officials who have been calling for a crackdown against religious bodies. "Our role as churches is to further the Kingdom of God," says Sam Kobia, the NCCK general secretary. "We are in no way involved in political activities or actions to destabilize the government." Kobia is hopeful that the recent reopening of a dialogue with government leaders may be helping to reduce distrust and minimize the conflict.

Attorney Kuria, whose out-spokenness and willingness to take on politically-sensitive cases have earned him international attention and government displeasure, says there is reason to be optimistic. "The world community is becoming intolerant of those who do not respect human rights," he believes.

Increasingly, Kuria says, there is a "global feeling" that human rights protection is inextricably linked to representative government. "Although the wind may be blowing slowly in Africa, it is quite clear there will be changes here."

STUDENT DEATHS REPORTED


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 242 words


Security forces allegedly killed 150 university students during anti-government protests two weeks ago in the southern Zaire city of Lubumbashi, according to the Times of Zambia. The newspaper said the reports of the killings reached the Zambian town of Ndola, on the border with Zaire, 130 miles from Lubumbashi. 

Earlier last week, the Belgian newspaper Le Soir reported that police had slit the throats of 50 students involved in the unrest. The Zaire government denied the story and threatened legal action against the Brussels-based newspaper.

The students mounted the protest to demand implementation of the democratic reforms President Mobutu Sese Seko promised in a speech last month, the report says. National guard troops called in to quell the rioting reportedly "went on an orgy of bayoneting," the Times said.

On April 24, Mobutu lifted the ban on opposition political parties and promised democratic elections in a year.

Superpowers Take Steps: U.S. and Soviet officials have stepped up efforts to end Angola's 15-year old civil war. Assistant Secretary of State Herman Cohen visited Zaire, and Yuri Yukalov, head of the Soviet Foreign Ministry's Africa Department, visited Lisbon

for discussions with Portuguese officials who

have been encouraging the warring parties to talk. Representatives of the U.S.-backed Unita rebels and the Soviet-supported government met in southern Portugal on April 24 and 25.

SECTION: BITS AND PIECES

The United Nations has established an identification commission to determine the eligibility of voters for a UN-supervised election in Western Sahara. 

The issue of who could vote has been a contentious one between the Polisario Front independence movement and the government of Morocco, which have been fighting for control of the territory since Spain pulled out as the colonial ruler in 1976. FRANKLIN WILLIAMS DIES


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 247 words


The president of the Phelps-Stokes Fund for two decades, who helped found the Peace Corps and served as the organization's director for Africa, has died of lung cancer at the age of 72.

Franklin Williams was an energetic activist for civil rights, education and African issues, widely admired for his enthusiastic support for equal rights. As special counsel to the NAACP, he won reversals of death sentences for several young blacks, and as Californ-ia's assistant attorney general, he forced the Professional Golfers' Association to admit blacks to tournament play.

But he was not always in the right place at the right time. In 1966, he arrived in Accra as the U.S. ambassador to Ghana just a month before the coup that deposed pan-Africanist leader Kwame Nkrumah. As a result of public speculation that the CIA had backed the Nkrumah ouster, Williams was plagued by allegations that he had orchestrated the coup. "I was so proud of representing the United States as a black ambassador in Africa," Williams said later, when asked about the charges. "I knew nothing about it, but I've never been able to escape the suspicion that I got rid of Nkrumah."

But Williams will be remembered for his constant work at the helm of Phelps-Stokes for disadvantaged American Indians, African-Americans and Africans -- and for the personal assistance he could be counted on to give to countless causes, from the Boys Choir of Harlem to the Bishop Tutu Scholarship Fund.

SQUEEZE ON HOUPHOUET-BOIGNY


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 197 words


A series of strikes is besieging the government of Cote d'Ivoire. Members of the police force and the military have staged work stoppages this month to protest proposed wage cuts and poor working conditions. Dock workers, postal and telecommunications employees and electricians are threatening walkouts if their pay demands are not met. President Felix

Houphouet-Boigny has agreed to halt pay cuts for the police and the military, despite the International Monetary Fund's insistence on the cuts as part of its Structural Adjustment Plan. However, more concessions to striking workers could jeopardize future IMF funding. With a declining economy due to a slump in world cocoa prices -- cocoa is Cote d'Ivoire's main export -- and a foreign debt of close to $ 14 billion, Cote d'Ivoire relies heavily on international lending.

Protests, sometimes violent, calling for multiparty democracy and the resignation of the aging president have been frequent in the country since February. In April, the government bowed to pressure and legalized opposition parties, and Houphouet has announced that he will name his successor at the next party congress later this month.

Africa News


May 29, 1990


PRISONERS PREPARE FOR FREEDOM


LENGTH: 1010 words


A working group established by the South African government and the African National Congress (ANC) has been meeting to resolve several issues which stand in the way of full-scale negotiations about South Africa's future. The group was set up during three days of discussions earlier this month between a ministerial delegation, led by President F.W. de Klerk, and senior ANC officials, headed by Deputy President Nelson Mandela.

The working group is charged with finding "norms and mechanisms" for handling the release of political prisoners and the granting of immunity to persons who took part in illegal activity, such as the guerrilla actions of the ANC.

Both the prisoner release and the announcement of immunity is expected to take place soon. But the two parties still differ on what needs to happen next. The government wants the ANC to renounce the use of violence; it blames the group's ambivalence on the issue for encouraging continuing clashes that are claiming lives in various parts of the country. 

The ANC is committed to a "mutual suspension of violence" and wants the government to put a stop to the killings of black demonstrators and onlookers by police.

Reed Kramer spoke with some of those who took part in the guerrilla campaign and with friends and families of prisoners awaiting release.

Cape Town, South Africa -- It takes 45-minutes on the small government-operated ferry -- and an investment of about half a day -- to visit an inmate on Robben Island, one of the world's best-known prisons. But Yasmena Pandy is still making the trip every week to see her husband, Ashley Forbes, who received a 15-year sentence last year for involvement in the military wing of the African National Congress.

Forbes and more than 300 other political activists who took part in the guerrilla campaigns waged by the ANC and by the smaller Pan Africanist Congress (PAC), are expected to be freed as a result of the meetings early this month between the government and the ANC, although no agreement on their release has been announced.

It has been a long wait for these prisoners and their families since President F. W. de Klerk unbanned the ANC and freed Nelson Mandela in February. "They have been carrying on like they were coming out tomorrow," Pandy says.

The men on the island are mostly top echelon members of Umkonto we Size, or Spear of the Nation, the ANC military wing popularly known as "MK." Forbes was an MK commander in the Cape Town region who is said by the government to have received military training in Angola.

He was a student at the University of the Western Cape when he was captured in 1987, following the detention of a colleague, student leader Peter Jacobs, who confessed to guerrilla activities during interrogation. While questioning Jacobs, using electric shock treatment and other coercive measures to elicit information, the police noticed that Jacobs periodically checked the time. After Jacobs was tricked into thinking it was two hours later than it was, he agreed to lead police to the hide-out which he thought Forbes would have abandoned. But police found Forbes there, still asleep, Pandy says.

Eventually Forbes and 14 others, including Jacobs and Pandy, were put on trial. Five were given lengthy sentences with Forbes. Seven others got shorter terms, and Pandy was acquitted.

Their case was one of many in recent years to provide evidence of the broad scale of ANC underground military activity in many parts of the country. Most of those convicted in those trials are now serving sentences on Robben Island.

One of the top ANC officials imprisoned there is Ebrahim Ismael Ebrahim, who received a 20-year term last year after a trial in which he was accused of masterminding guerrilla attacks from neighboring Swaziland. Ebrahim was abducted from Swaziland in 1986, and although he ended up in police custody in South Africa, the government denied responsibility for the kidnapping. It is the second tour on the island for Ebrahim, who served 15 years after being convicted of sabotage in 1964. After his release in 1979, he plunged right back into ANC activities, his friends say. Ebrahim comes from a politically active Natal family; his brother Gora Ebrahim is a senior PAC official.

Although conditions have been relaxed in recent weeks, Pandy says she has not managed to have much contact with her husband. "We have to talk through the glass -- and the glass is dirty, our vision is blurred by it," she says. "When our time is up they may just close the petition between the glass without any warning."

Pandy says the thoughts of her husband and the others have turned to life after prison, since President de Klerk announced the unbanning of the ANC. "Ashley nags me about whether I am buying furniture and whether I am looking for a house, " she says.

But there has been little time for house-keeping details as ANC members respond to the organizational demands of the new situation.

Pandy says she has been asked to help a group of striking prison wardens from Pollsmore, the institution where Nelson Mandela was held prior to release on Feb. 11. She had a surprise visit a few weeks ago from several of the women guards she had come to know quite well during the 15 months she spent in the women's section of the prison in 1986. The women, members of the recently-formed Police and Prison Civil Rights Union (Popcru), were suspended after going on strike in a protest against discriminatory pay and labor practices in the prison system.

When the prisoners emerge from Robben Island, they will be taking up positions in a very different kind of organization from the one they knew before prison.

The ANC is currently making the transition from a clandestine group with an exiled leadership to the principal aboveground political organization campaigning for democratic change. An ANC office has just been opened in Cape Town, one of 14 being set up to rebuild party structures and establish a presence throughout the country.

May 29, 1990


SOUTH AFRICA: MANDELA TRIP READIED


SECTION: NEGOTIATIONS


LENGTH: 956 words


The U.S. tour next month by Nelson Mandela is expected to give a boost to the American anti-apartheid movement, which has lost some of its focus since South African President F.W. de Klerk launched his reform program earlier this year.

Roger Wilkins, national coordinator of the tour, says "the maintenance of sanctions is a key element" of Mandela's U.S. agenda.

For President George Bush, the visit provides an opportunity to pursue the South African policy adopted last year, aimed at promoting peaceful change in South Africa and averting divisive domestic debate. But it could also produce unforeseen political problems, especially if de Klerk's efforts falter or the current moves toward a negotiated solution in South Africa fail. 

The preparations for Mandela's trip, which will take place between June 20 and 30, have drawn together the diverse coalition which gained passage of a major sanctions package in Congress in 1986 and won approval for numerous divestment and boycott measures in states and localities across the country throughout the 1980s. As requested by Mandela and the African National Congress, American trade unions have a leading role in coordinating the trip, along with churches, civil rights and community organizations, and prominent African-Americans, from the entertainment industry in particular.

"We have to be insane to be trying to do this in a month," Harry Belafonte told a May 11 meeting of over 100 people convened to set up a national coordinating body. Belafonte and TransAfrica Executive Director Randall Robinson, are principal organizers of the visit. Robinson, who led the 1984-85 demonstrations in front of the South African embassy in support of sanctions, traveled to Johannesburg to finalize arrangements with Mandela.

The coordinators have had to field a flurry of requests from people and places seeking to be included on the itinerary. Wilkins was chosen to head the effort because "he has the stature and even temperament needed for the job," one activist said. The George Mason University professor is a former journalist who served in the Kennedy and Johnson administrations and was assistant attorney general of the United States.

Mandela's principal role "is to help spearhead the effort to bring peace," Wilkins told the Detroit Free Press. "He did not break rocks on Robben Island to enhance the reputation of those who want to get their pictures taken with him," he said.

Bush issued the invitation to Mandela -- and one to de Klerk -- in February, immediately after de Klerk announced Mandela's release from prison. It was a natural corollary to previous administration actions, which included White House meetings with a delegation of South African church leaders, including Archbishop Desmond Tutu, and

with a delegation from the leading South African anti-apartheid coalition, the pro-ANC United Democratic Front.

The Bush policy was formally set out in National Security Review (NSR) 15, a secret study drawn up a year ago by an interagency team and signed by the president several month later (see Africa News, May 29 1989). The policy was designed, in the words of Assistant Secretary of State Herman Cohen, "to nudge both sides to the table by a strong diplomatic effort." The policy was also fashioned in a manner to attract bipartisan backing in Congress and avoid the disagreements that led Congress to adopt the 1986 sanctions over President Reagan's veto.

After being overshadowed in Washington by events in Eastern Europe, the Soviet Union and Central America, South Africa is again on the White House agenda. In March, the administration signaled renewed concern about events in the region by sending Secretary of State James Baker to southern Africa. Vice President Dan Quayle had been slated to represent the U.S. at Namibia's independence on March 21. Baker went instead, and visited de Klerk in Cape Town afterwards.

Although Mandela agreed to see Baker in Namibia, he criticized the secretary of state for going to South Africa. The ANC has been arguing for a continuation of pressures on

the South African government to insure that real concessions are made when negotiations finally get underway.

Sanctions supporters have been fearful that a slippage in American pressure on Pretoria is almost inevitable, unless renewed public anti-apartheid expressions can be mobilized. That is what the Mandela trip promises to do.

"We think this comes at a very important time," says Jennifer Davis, executive director of the American Committee on Africa, the New York-based group which has spearheaded divestment actions in various parts of the country. "It can help underscore support in the U.S. for continued sanctions until apartheid is abolished, and it can help attract American backing for the ANC and progressive forces in South Africa." The ANC hopes to raise several million

dollars to support its political activities at home during the Mandela trip, according to Lindiwe Mabuza, the chief ANC representative in the U.S.

For the administration, the visit could create complications that the NSR 15 policy was intended to avoid. The ANC and its supporters have been angered by the decision -- still not formally announced -- to receive de Klerk at the White House a few days prior to Mandela's arrival in this country. Anti-apartheid groups have promised to dog de Klerk with protests wherever he appears.

"We are just going to have to wait this one out," one administration official said this week after the controversy over the timing of the two trips arose. "In the end, I ex-

pect both trips will go ahead as planned," he added. "There would be demonstrations whenever de Klerk comes."

TIGRAY: THE REVOLUTION IS A FAMILY AFFAIR


LENGTH: 1291 words


Although the Tigray People's Liberation Front (TPLF) has been fighting the Soviet-backed Ethiopian government for 13 years, the movement has attracted little attention or support in the United States. When it has been mentioned, it has been described most often as 'hard-line' or Marxist-Leninist in orientation.

That perception is incorrect, according to Berhane Gebre, a TPLF Central Committee member who has been in Washington for talks with U.S. officials, members of Congress and interested groups. He says his movement is a broad front representing the people of Ethiopia's Tigray province and favoring democratic government for all Ethio-pians. Here are excerpts from an Africa News interview with Berhane Gebre last week.

AN: What happened to the peace talks you were having with the government last year and earlier this year? 

Gebre: These were preliminary talks dealing mainly with procedures. In the first two rounds, we agreed on some points, but there were two outstanding issues: the composition of the delegation to the substantive talks and the agenda for the main talks.

These remained to be solved in the third round, but the regime decided to terminate the talks.

AN: The government refused to continue?

Gebre: Yes, they refused. We said that the delegates to the main talks should represent the TPLF and EPDM, the Ethiopian People's Democratic Movement. It should be a joint delegation of these two organizations on one side, and the Mengistu regime on the other side.

The TPLF is not conducting the war by itself. The TPLF has joined in a united front with the EPDM called the Ethiopian People's Revolutionary Democratic Front (EPRDF). This front is conducting war in the provinces of Tigray, Wollo, Gondar and Shoa, where Addis is located.

So, the TPLF cannot deliver peace by itself. Our combatants are fighting together in one trench, so if peace is going to be delivered, it has to be delivered by both. The TPLF said that these two have to come together to the peace negotiations. And the regime said that it wants to discuss only with the TPLF. It doesn't want to involve any other movement.

The other point is the issue of the agenda. The regime said the TPLF could only discuss issues of Tigray, not national matters. But the TPLF is not fighting for secession. The people of Tigray are Ethiopians. We have our rights, and we want to have a democratic Ethiopia, where all the people of Ethiopia have their rights respected. If problems in Tigray are going to be solved, they have to be solved in the context of the country at large.

AN: So you don't think there are any prospects for renewed talks?

Gebre: None at all. The regime is not ready to compromise. The TPLF is flexible. We proposed that we have a fourth round of preliminary talks or that we solve the pending issues in the first phase of the main talks. This was to salvage the peace talks. But the regime decided that it wouldn't continue.

You see if we go to the main talks, the TPLF's proposal for a transitional government will be tabled. The EPRDF, the united front of the TPLF and EPDM, has proposed there should be a transitional government composed of all political forces. And then there should be a constitution drafted and free elections held for a democratic government. The regime does not want to discuss this.

AN: How does your group propose to deal with Eritrea?

Gebre: The Eritrean people should decide their own fate, freely, by referendum. It would be up to the Eritreans to decide whether to be united ¢with Ethiopia/ or to form an independent state.

We do not want to continue this war which has gone on for 28 years. If the Eritreans want to be united with us, fine. If the Eritreans decide to form an independent state, we have to respect their will, and we have to try to work on a good relationship.

AN: The forces of the united front are moving closer and closer to Addis. Do you plan to attack the capital?

Gebre: The aim is to remove the Mengistu regime and to establish a provisional government. If the regime accepts a peaceful resolution, then a transitional government could be established. If this regime continues with its stance, then it has to be removed.

We want to have a smooth and orderly transition. Our main concern at this moment is to broaden our base and bring other opposition forces into the agreement to have a transitional government.

AN: Are the people of Tigray receiving food?

Gebre: There are three alternative routes, three alternative means of reaching the 2.5 million people affected by famine. The cross-border operation, working through the Sudan, by the Relief Society of Tigray -- REST -- is the most effective. But there are problems. There are not adequate trucks. The second means is for donors to go and buy food in the western part of Tigray, where it's fertile and there is some surplus.

The third route is the southern corridor, meaning the safe passage that has been conducted by the Ethiopian church organizations -- from ¢the port of/ Assab to Dessie and from Dessie to Tigray. Their target was to bring in food up to 15,000 metric tons a month, but this is not working effectively. There are a lot of constraints by the Ethiopian regime -- denying them fuel and creating a lot of bureaucratic problems. At the same time, ¢the government/ is not allowing other agencies, especially expatriate NGOs ¢non-governmental organizations/, to work with them.

We can foresee a catastrophe. The famine situation in Tigray is acute. There are no mass deaths at this moment, but there is mass starvation. Mass deaths are contained because the food that's going through the Relief Society of Tigray is distributed to the most needy ones, who are at the edge of death. So they are saved from death, but a lot of people are being starved.

We are worried that the rainy season is coming. When the rainy season starts, transport will be affected, and there won't be much food to be distributed to these people who are in starvation. Food should be transported before the rainy season as much as possible.

AN: Is outside assistance vital, then?

Gebre: Yes, yes, there is real need. The people of Tigray are trying to do all they can by themselves. People are working on terracing, on water and soil conservation. They are not folding their hands and waiting for help. What they need at this critical moment is the small support that would enable them to feed themselves and to produce for the coming years. People are really working in a very impressive way.

AN:Has the TPLF shifted its ideological stance, as has been reported?

Gebre: There is lack of understanding of the nature of the TPLF. It has never claimed to be a Marxist-Leninist organization, never. We call it a revolutionary democratic organization.

The TPLF is a broad front; people from all sectors of the population are involved. In the TPLF you'll find dedicated priests, Marxist-Leninists and people with other political and ideological views as well. In Tigray, there is no one who is not involved in the TPLF. The TPLF is a family affair. Everyone has a son, a daughter, a brother, a sister or a husband in the front. So it's a family affair.

AN: What kind of decision-making structures does the TPLF have?

Gebre: The front calls a congress every four years, a congress that is composed of the fighters of the TPLF and people from all villages and regions of Tigray. And, as well, Tigrean exiles from the United States, Europe, the Sudan, and other countries have mass associations which elect representatives and send them to the congress. The congress adopts general policies and elects a leadership for four years.

MENGISTU: 'THE SOLUTION LIES IN PEACEFUL DIALOGUE'


LENGTH: 1942 words


With his forces suffering repeated setbacks on the battlefield, Ethiopian President Mengistu Haile Mariam is grasping for ways to save his 15-year rule over Africa's oldest nation.

Last week, 12 high-ranking and well-known military officers were unexpectedly executed for their role in an attempted coup against him a year ago. Mengistu, who said recently that he has survived nine attempts on his life, had been expected to grant the men clemency, to avoid further erosion of his popularity in both military and civilian circles.

But the executions are characteristic of a regime that achieved and maintained power through the violent elimination of its opponents. Mengistu was one of a group of army officers who forcibly seized control of the ruling military council, known as the Dergue, in November, 1974. They staged their coup just two months after military leaders deposed Emperor Haile Selassie, ending a half-century of rule by the man known as the "Lion of Judah." 

In 1975, Mengistu emerged from a series of bloody internal feuds as the most influential Dergue member, and he became chairman and head of state in 1977. Under Mengistu's control, the Dergue launched a wholesale assault against the country's major political parties, the largest of which shared the rulers' avowed Marxist-Leninist orientation. After thousands of killings during a period known as the "red terror," organized opposition to military control was crushed. Mengistu now governs under a 1987 constitution which established an 85-seat national legislature and a council of state headed by the president.

With Soviet support diminishing and Eastern European aid ended, Mengistu has indicated an interest in improved relations with the U.S. In March, he promised to allow additional political parties to compete with the ruling Workers' Party of Ethiopia.

Recently, he defended his policies to Charlayne Hunter-Gault of the "MacNeil Lehrer NewsHour," in his first interview on American television. Here are major excerpts from that conversation.

Question: Mr. President, the widely held view is that this war has your government up against the wall. Is that wrong?

President Mengistu: This situation in the north, this fighting, is a situation we inherited. It is not our situation. One should ask, what is the origin of this war; what is its genesis?

Of course, the purpose of the revolution was to provide a solution to the problem in the north, to this long-running struggle. Right away, on the triumph of the revolution, we approached the dissidents in the north. They asked us to recognize their rights to secede from Ethiopia. But how can a government representative of the Ethiopian people allow this demand? To allow the secession of this part of Ethiopia would be, really, to commit national suicide. We do not want to do that.

This is an endemically drought-stricken area. We have been approached by international donor organizations who want to provide food for the people. So we said okay, food may come through the port of Massawa. And precisely as we let ourselves provide food for the hungry, the rebels try to encumber international passage. This is the second time they have captured the port of Massawa, timing it precisely with the drought situation in our country.

Question: But how do you respond to the criticism that both sides are putting military objectives before the consideration of starving people? They, as you say, keep capturing the port and you in turn keep bombing the port, destroying hundreds of tons of food as you attempt to recapture this port.

President Mengistu: It wasn't we who first bombed the city of Massawa. It was the rebels in their effort to capture this port who have destroyed the port facilities and the whole city. That the capture of Massawa came precisely at the very moment when the port was most needed for the supply of food for the hungry is something international public opinion should have condemned.

Why should the international donor community insist on the port of Massawa for the transport of

food when there is an alternative route accepted by the United Nations? We have used it in the past and we are using it now. To insist that this food must go through the port of Massawa is really an effort to legalize the illegal capture of the port of Massawa -- something which we cannot allow.

Question: There are many observers who are saying the situation on the battlefield has deteriorated to the extent that a military solution is close to impossible. Do you disagree with that?

President Mengistu: Our option has never been military. We firmly believe that the solution lies in a peaceful dialogue on the problem in the north. The most anomalous thing in this is that they have begun to harden their position, become more intransigent. The talks that were held got stalled because they did not want to continue.

Question: So you are saying that there are no new elements now in the equation?

President Mengistu: There are no new elements because of their inflexibility.

Question: The overwhelming impression is of a country that is impoverished, people who are starving, and political forces who really don't care. How do you counter that image? Does that worry you?

President Mengistu: The perception the world has of Ethiopia is not really the real perception. We are fighting people who have all the sophisticated weapons and medicine they need.

Where do the supplies come from? Definitely there must be strong forces behind the conflict that are driving it. We are fighting against these forces.

Question: Which country are you specifically referring to?

President Mengistu: Well, some of these countries have far-reaching plans to destabilize some African countries. It is some of the Arab countries of the region; it is not all of them. For example, Libya is spearheading this, and others. Some of them are providing arms and the others are providing funds. Several countries are involved in this hostile act in one way or another.

Question: But in that connection, there is a whole new concern arising with the Soviets pulling out of Ethiopia and ending support for you. There are reports now that Israel is stepping in to fill that breach. Because Israel is stepping in in your country, the Arabs are responding to appeals from the opposition forces.

President Mengistu: This allegation is entirely baseless. There is no truth in that we are trying to replace the Soviet Union with Israel and that we have entered into a military pact with Israel. There is absolutely no grounds for that.

Question: Your recent dramatic announcement of a new economic reform program which moved away from Marxism came as a surprise to many people. What was the objective of the socialist revolution in the first place and what went wrong?

President Mengistu: Ethiopia has been basically a mixed economy. To give you a figure, 70% of the Ethiopian economy is privately owned and 30% of that is owned jointly by the state and cooperative sectors. On the whole, agriculture in our country is very primitive. We thought ¢that/ only by meeting some prerequisites could we introduce a degree of modern agriculture.

The first step was to settle the people, to regroup peasants, in a specific area where we could get things through to them easily. It was a non-ideological approach to introduce modern agriculture and modern technology rapidly in the rural area. Of late we have discovered that this did not help productivity.

Question: But specifically what are you planning to do? People are waiting for the next step because they remain skeptical. You have announced reforms, but the specifics of your plan to implement them are still the source of some confusion.

President Mengistu: In the past there was nothing that we did to promote the private sector, but now we've come out in grand style to provide all types of assistance that the private sector would wish to have in order to develop agriculture and legal protection and so on. As far as cooperatives are concerned, the law will allow peasant farmers who wish to continue as cooperatives to do so and for those who don't, the law would allow them to disband and set up as private peasant farmers.

Question: How would you rewrite this chapter of Ethiopian history -- a chapter which often refers to you as the cruel dictator -- Mengistu, a man who refuses to allow political expression, who brutally suppresses dissent and who is indifferent to human life. How would you rewrite that chapter?

President Mengistu: A man made in this image in the first place would not be qualified to come to power in Ethiopia. My personality, my nature, is judged by the Ethiopian people. What is paramount for me, as it is for the Ethiopian people, is the unity of the country, the sovereignty of the country, and I am not ready to compromise on that. Personally, I do not want to see anything die, not even an insect. But the truth is, I am not ready to compromise on the supreme interest of the Ethiopian people.

Question: How soon do you plan to put your government before the people? Do you plan, as you proceed with the economic reforms, to open up your system so that the people can vote?

President Mengistu: The armed forces elected me to assume power precisely at the moment when the country was deeply in anarchy. It was a time when the country was encircled by enemy forces. Its days before disintegration were numbered. I was elected to take power.

To start off, we did not set up a military junta. As soon as we came to power, we went to the people to find out what they wanted, to get a mandate. We put ballot boxes everywhere for people to give us their views. The people gave us their guidance, they told us what to do. So on the basis of the guidance given us by the people, we set up a provisional military government and saved the country from the danger that was hovering over it at that moment.

Question: So do you expect anytime in the foreseeable future there will be opposition parties able to contest, to participate in the political process?

President Mengistu: What everyone must realize is the unity and the social security of the country is paramount. This is one of the most ancient countries in the world.

In its long past, Ethiopia never knew a party line. The first party in Ethiopian history was set up by us. This party is still in its initial stage; it has a long distance to go to mature. It is this government that introduced a republican form of government, by the will of the people. In the future, if the need arises and situations justify the emergence of multiparties, naturally we are flexible and will allow them to exist.

Question: Finally Mr. President, let me just ask you as you reflect on all the things that we have talked about: the war, the famine, the economic conditions in your country, the perception outside. Do you yourself at this point have any regrets about the past 15 years of your administration, anything you wish you could change?

President Mengistu: We have planned in the past for the social and economic betterment of our people and I must say our performance has been really good and I don't really have regrets. There are no areas where I say our performance was poor so I have no bad conscience at all.

But of course the most lamentable thing that I do regret is that we do not have peace. My grandparents did not know peace, my father did not know peace in this country and I don't know peace and, who knows, my children may also never experience peace.

ETHIOPIA: PRESSURES BUILD ON REGIME


SECTION: CONFLICT


LENGTH: 985 words


The ancient African nation of Ethiopia, whose modern era has been plagued by constant warfare, may be on the verge of dramatic political change. Many observers are predicting the imminent downfall of President Mengistu Haile Mariam, who has been in power since 1974.

Troops from the Tigray People's Liberation Front (TPLF) and an alliance of other Ethiopian opposition movements are advancing on the capital, Addis Ababa. They say their aim is to overthrow the current regime and install an interim administration to oversee free elections. At the same time, the Eritrean People's Liberation Front (EPLF) -- fighting for the independence of the northern territory annexed by Ethiopian Emperor Haile Selassie in 1962 -- is poised to attack Asmara, the capital of the disputed region. 

The three decade-long conflict in Eritrea and the more recent fighting in Tigray have claimed an estimated 400,000 casualties, and deaths continue to mount. U.S. Assistant Secretary of State for Africa Herman Cohen has called the conflict "possibly the largest in terms of combatants and the most destructive in the world today."

As a result of the fighting, more than three million people face starvation, according to estimates by various aid experts, although a major international relief effort has averted the kind of disaster that Ethiopia experienced in 1984-85, when as many as a million people died from starvation. "Response to this need has come from around the world," Cohen said in recent testimony before the Senate Africa subcommittee.

The donated supplies are being delivered to hungry people in Eritrea, Tigray and other famine-afflicted areas "under extremely difficult and dangerous conditions," according to Remer Tyson of the Detroit Free Press, who recently made a 1,000-mile reporting trip through Eritrea. "Food is reaching people in far corners of the parched, barren countryside," Tyson says in a report published last week.

Neil Henry of the Washington Post, who spent 13 days early this month traveling in Tigray, also found evidence that the relief effort is "accomplishing a great deal to stem the human suffering wrought by drought and civil war."

Henry rode with one of the truck convoys that the Relief Society of Tigray operates to transport food from the southeastern Sudanese town of Gedaref to the interior of the province. These "heroic caravans," carrying food donated by the U.S., Canada, and the European Community, travel by night to avoid shelling from Ethiopian fighter jets.

"Promptly at sundown each day, the big green relief-society trucks, each filled with 15 tons of cargo, line up at the outskirts of Gedaref where they start up with a mighty roar and begin to roll," Henry says in a May 17 dispatch.

"It is a tough, bone-jarring, teeth-rattling ride over makeshift dirt roads, pitted with stones and craters," he writes. "Amid the cacophony of whining engines, thick wafts of dust pervade the truck cabins so that by dawn drivers and riders alike are covered with grit."

Pleased by the achievements to date, aid officials are nevertheless concerned that international support for the relief effort not diminish. "The situation is quite precarious," says Andrew Natsios, director of the U.S. Foreign Disaster Assistance Agency. Additional supplies are needed in Eritrea, Tigray and several other regions, aid officials say.

According to Berhane Gebre, a TPLF official currently visiting the U.S., there is still widespread famine in Tigray, even though mass deaths there have been warded off up to now (see page 7). But with the approach of the rainy season, he says, the situation will become much more dangerous, since it will be almost impossible to transport food to many areas.

In Eritrea, the situation for many people "has deteriorated rapidly," David Holdridge of Catholic Relief Services told Congress recently. Since February, when the EPLF captured the port of Massawa and government forces responded by aerial bombing raids on the town, relief supplies which were formerly routed through the port have had to be transported by a southern route from Sudan through Tigray to rebel-held areas of Eritrea.

The EPLF controls most of the province and recently began bombarding the airport outside Asmara, apparently in preparation for a major assault on the Eritrean capital, which is defended by about 100,000 government troops. "Latest reports indicate that over 400,000 people in Asmara are in need of relief assistance," Holdridge said.

The State Department has been working towards an agreement to allow the port to be reopened under United Nations auspices to permit the resumption of much-needed relief shipments. But Mengistu has resisted any arrangement that would "legalize the illegal capture" of the town, he has said (see accompanying interview).

Earlier this month, the House Select Committee on Hunger appealed to President George Bush and President Mikhail Gorbachev to take up the Ethiopian issue at this week's summit in Washington. The Committee called upon the Soviets to stop supplying arms to the Ethiopian government and asked all parties to permit unrestricted passage of relief shipments to civilians.

Already, the Soviets are reported to have reduced their support for the regime by cutting military assistance and withdrawing the bulk of their military advisers. Mengistu has also lost the support he had from East Germany and other Eastern European governments.

In March, the beleaguered president announced an about-face in his economic policies. He said Ethiopia was abandoning Marxist-oriented central planning and allowing greater involvement by private enterprise. He lifted government controls on farming, agricultural marketing and ownership of private property. And he appealed for foreign investment.

But little economic progress can be expected while the war rages.
