May 29, 1990


"WHO ARE THOSE GUYS?"


LENGTH: 721 words


Washington, DC. -- When a Liberian exile named Charles Taylor led about a hundred poorly armed men into Liberia on Christmas eve, few people took the invasion seriously. But the harsh response of government forces has turned what might have been a minor rebellion into a movement that threatens to topple the Doe government.

Many of the rebels, who now number several thousand, are villagers -- including women and young people -- who joined the fight after indiscriminate killing of civilians by government forces. International human rights groups say that the original invasion was itself bloody, and aimed particularly at President Samuel Doe's Krahn ethnic group. But in the months since, under the banner of Taylor's National Patriotic Front (NPF), the rebels have broadened their support. NPF spokesman Tom Woewiyu says the group has backing from every segment of Liberian society. 

So little was known about the invaders when they entered Nimba County that the questions began to sound like a reprise of the Butch Cassidy movie: "Who are those guys?" Many Liberians were reassured when Taylor issued a statement on January 1 saying that the NPF is "a non-sectarian nationalist movement which believes in the right of every Liberian to equal protection and opportunity under the law and which does not subscribe to ethnic and social factionalism or recriminations."

U.S. officials had their own view of the NPF's intentions. In February 21 testimony before a House of Representatives Foreign Affairs subcommittee, Assistant Secretary of State Richard Schifter called the rebellion against Doe "a Libyan-supported effort to overthrow him."

Designed to overthrow Doe -- "yes," says the NPF's Woewiyu; Libyan-sponsored -- "no." The rebellion is, he says, "just a coalition of grassroots citizens." If the group had Libyan backing, he asks, why did it invade the country, armed with only a few hand weapons? -- a fact testified to by refugees' eyewitness accounts.

Woewiyu says the fighters now have an abundance of arms captured from fleeing government forces, but he does not deny that the NPF's success has attracted outside interest and support.

As for 42-year-old Charles Taylor, he, like many educated Liberians, studied in the United States. And like many who now oppose the Doe government, he went home after Doe promised to bring democracy to Liberia with his 1980 coup. When the government charged that he embezzled funds in his job as head of Liberia's General Services Agency, he fled back to the U.S.

While being represented by former U.S. Attorney General Ramsey Clark in an appeal against extradition to Liberia, Taylor escaped from a Massachusetts jail. Stories that he spent at least part of the five years since then getting military training in Libya have circulated widely in Washington.

Taylor's proclaimed beliefs, though, are firmly in favor of American-style democracy. "We are American in terms of our philosophy on government and on economy," says Tom Woewiyu.

But Woewiyu questions why the U.S. administration has been so slow to realize that the Doe era is over. "Doe is dead," Woewiyu said, during a break from meetings on Capitol Hill. "He's just not smart enough to lie down."

Woewiyu promises that the NPF will form a transitional government of "the best and brightest Liberians" to organize national elections.

The goal should be balloting within two years, says the Association for Constitutional Democracy in Liberia, a Washington-based network of Liberian groups and individuals working for "the establishment of a democratic society based on the Liberian constitution."

The association has called for Doe to step down and for both sides to observe a ceasefire. It also says that the "problems of Liberia can only be solved by Liberians themselves," but leaders of the group acknowledge that the United States could play an important role in promoting peace by guaranteeing Doe's safety if he resigns.

One way or another, says Woewiyu, Liberians will get democracy. "It is possible in Africa," he says. "People have got this belief that the good things that happen around the world are not possible in Africa. We know that ¢democracy/ is possible, and we are hoping that in the future it will reign not only in Liberia but all over Africa."

"A UNIQUE RELATIONSHIP"


LENGTH: 181 words


U.S. interests in Liberia are "not well-understood by many Americans," says James Bishop, who has served as Ambassador to Liberia for the last three years. "We've had a uniquely intimate relationship with the Liberians over the course of over a hundred and fifty years now," Bishop says. Liberians have been very receptive to American influence and presence, and as a result, we have several facilities there that are important to our ability to conduct business in Africa."

Among those facilities, Bishop says, are "the principal broadcast station for Africa of the Voice of America," and "a diplomatic communications facility which makes it possible for us to communicate with our embassies in Africa in a fashion which would be impossible without that installation." And, says Bishop, "we have had ready access to the ports and airfield in Liberia for purposes which have served our national interests."

There is also a navigation system -- "rather old but still important," Bishop says -- that helps guide ships and aircraft in international waters and airspace.

May 29, 1990


LIBERIA: A SPECIAL RELATIONSHIP


LENGTH: 952 words


Washington, DC. -- With Liberian rebels poised to attack the capital of Monrovia and end the ten-year rule of President Samuel Doe, representatives sent by both sides for discussions with U.S. officials in Washington agreed on one thing: Liberia's problem is also an American problem.

"The United States has an obligation to help Liberia achieve a political compromise," says Winston Tubman, leader of a government-authorized delegation that has been lobbying members of Congress and State Department planners for the last two weeks.

"Of course, we know we are a small country," Tubman says, "and President Bush is busy ¢with/ Eastern Europe, but the problem of Liberia has an older history than that, and it is a direct responsibility of the United States." 

That claim is based on the special, historical relationship between the two countries, dating from Liberia's founding as an independent republic by freed American slaves in 1847. Its government was modeled on the U.S. constitution; its red, white and blue flag is a variation on the stars and stripes; its capital is named for President James Monroe.

And the ties have remained close. The U.S. is Liberia's main investor and trading partner. Firestone Tire and Rubber Company operates the world's biggest rubber plantation at Harbel, 31 miles east of Monrovia.

During much of the recent past, the West African nation, which is slightly smaller than the state of Virginia, has been the largest per capita recipient of U.S. aid in sub-Saharan Africa -- $ 500 million in the last decade and has hosted 4,500 Peace Corps Volunteers. More than 5,000 Americans live and work in Liberia and the U.S. dollar is legal currency.

In 1987, after mounting complaints by Doe's Congressional critics about the government's fiscal irregularities and its corruption, the Reagan administration dispatched a 17-person team to Liberia to manage the country's finances. A year before its two-year mandate ended, the team returned to the U.S., citing lack of cooperation from Liberian officials.

Rebel spokesman Tom Woewiyu says the U.S. government should force President Doe to step down. "Samuel Doe was given millions and millions of dollars and used it against our people," Woewiyu says.

"When money comes from a democratic country, collected from a democratic-minded people, from their hard-earned tax money," he says, "the U.S. has an obligation to make sure those monies are applied to democratic principles and democratic efforts, not to a dictatorship."

Woewiyu urges Americans to insist that their government not "support dictators just because they are convenient."

The issue of Doe's suppression of opposition forces has been raised frequently by human rights organizations. In a report issued this month, the Washington. D.C.-based Africa Watch says that the Reagan administration "missed many opportunities to promote human rights in Liberia." Accepting the results of the "fraudulent election of 1985" was an example, the report says, of "a willful ignorance of past and ongoing abuses by the regime of Samuel Doe."

Former Finance Minister Ellen Johnson Sirleaf won the largest number of votes of any candidate in that election. But she fled the country after being jailed for treason. She now is vice president of Equator Bank in Washington, D.C.

In February, Sirleaf wrote to Richard Shifter, the Assistant Secretary of State for Human Rights, asking for protection for herself and six other Liberians in the United States who she said had been targeted for assassination by the Doe government. The reply thanked Sirleaf for bringing the matter to State Department attention and suggested she notify appropriate officials about the danger.

Two weeks later, in congressional testimony, Shifter said that the U.S. ambassador to Liberia has been spending "more than half of his time, in terms of contacts with the Liberian government . . . expressing himself on human rights problems."

The ambassador, James Bishop, is now back in Washington, preparing to become the U.S. ambassador to Somalia. Bishop says that "human rights and economic reform are at the head of the agenda for our discussions with Liberia and have been for ten years." As the coordinator of an ad hoc State Department working group on the Liberian crisis, Bishop has been at the center of discussions with the various parties to the fighting, but he will say only that "the United States has offered its good offices in the current conflict."

Tubman says that good offices aren't enough. "If the ¢Liberian/ government position is that they will not talk, and the rebels' position is that they will not talk with the Doe government," he says, "then you have a situation where there has to be some other intervention to make that happen."

When told that such an appeal sounds strange coming from the head of a government delegation, Tubman argues that he -- along with several other members of the bipartisan group, which includes trade union and church representatives -- do not speak for the government, although Doe approved their trip. Tubman himself is a former United Nations ambassador from Liberia whose uncle, William Tubman, was Liberian president from 1944 to 1971. His group did not come, Tubman says, to defend what has happened in Liberia in the past, but to seek U.S. help in stopping the war before it reaches the civilian population of Monrovia.

"I'm not saying they should send American troops," Tubman says. "The U.S. exerts considerable pressure and influence in so many ways. I do not know all of the ways available to the American government, but they are considerable, and, in Liberia, they would be decisive."

CALM DOWN


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 271 words


The government of Gabon has restored a fragile peace in Port Gentil, after government troops laid siege to the city to quell several days of protests and riots by opposition party members. President Omar Bongo says he dispatched the troops to enable Shell and Elf oil companies to resume production at their facilities there. Since oil provides 80% of the country's export revenues, the government said the interruption posed a serious threat to the national economy.

Protests in the capital city of Libreville were put down by enforcement of a curfew.

The protests were sparked

by the suspicious death of Joseph Redjambe, leader of the Gabonese Progress Party (PGP), on May 22.

The PGP claims that Redjambe was murdered by drug injection after entering a hotel with a woman from Cote d'Ivoire, hired by Bongo. Authorities have issued a report saying Redjambe died of natural causes, and that the syringe marks on his stomach were caused by the injection of drugs to treat diabetes.

The opposition party leader was found dead the day after Gabon's National Assembly approved a constitution providing for a multiparty election in September, ending 28 years of one-party rule by Bongo and his Gabonese Democratic Party. The government says the rioting will not stall the process of democratic reform.

The government of France dispatched a parachute regiment to reinforce the 500 French troops already in the country. The paratroopers were sent after PGP members held French Consul General Jean Duffau and eight other French citizens for several hours, demanding that France pressure Bongo to resign.

CHOOSE NOW


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 177 words


The 1990s will be a critical decade for the world to confront the challenges of population growth and distribution, according to a report recently released by the United Nations Population Fund (UNFPA). Choices made in the next ten years will determine the quality of life in the 21st century, UNFPA says. World population stands at $ 5.2 billion and is increasing by over 92 million a year. Over 90% of the growth is occurring in developing countries, which are least able to cope with such increases. Children under the age of 15 comprise over 37% of the population of poor countries; in Africa, children account for 45-50%. In the developing world's mushrooming cities, 40-50% of the inhabitants are living in overcrowded conditions, lacking virtually all services.

To effectively confront population challenges, the report says, the role and participation of women must improve, infant and child mortality rates must be lowered and policies aimed at reducing population growth must be implemented at local, national and international levels.

Africa News


June 11, 1990


REDUCING THE RISK


LENGTH: 418 words


Eve Lackritz and her colleagues from the U.S. Centers for Disease Control (CDC) faced questions at Siaya District Hospital that only medical research could answer. Do the blood transfusions given to severely anemic children really save lives? And if the blood does save lives but, at the same time, has the possibility of transmitting the virus that causes AIDS, how can hospital staff decide when to risk transfusions and when to wait?

So the CDC researchers designed a study to evaluate the association between blood transfusion and survival among children admitted to the hospital with severe anemia. 

After six-months of monitoring the admissions and treatment of 1,250 children, the researchers have been able to make real progress towards answering the questions. They found that among an easily-identified group of children, blood transfusion leads to a significantly improved survival rate.

Children under two years of age, with hemoglobin levels of less than 3.5 grams per deciliter -- levels of 13 are considered normal and below 11 is classified as anemia -- had a better chance of surviving, especially if the transfusion took place soon after admission.

On the other hand, when the hemoglobin at admission is 3.5 or better, transfusion is no longer associated with improved survival. The study also found that discontinuing transfusions to children with hemoglobins over 4 would have reduced the number of transfusions given by the hospital by a third. Since children under two years old accounted for 93% of all admissions with a hemoglobin less than 5 and received 94% of all transfusions, "we have found our target group," Lackritz says.

The results suggest that limiting transfusions to children with hemoglobins under 4 would have little adverse effect on survival while protecting a third of the children admitted to the hospital from exposure to blood that might be infected.

Eve Lackritz cautions that the results are preliminary. Although the study was a complex one designed to take a variety of factors into account, it was impossible to totally control the research process. Nevertheless, Siaya Hospital has already begun to be more conservative about the use of transfusions. "After all, they do read our protocols," Lackritz says.

And the research points the way to developing a model that can be used for risk/benefit analysis in other countries by taking into account local variables such as the percentage of the blood supply that carries HIV.

LANGUAGE: English


Copyright 1990 AllAfrica, Inc.

Africa News


June 11, 1990


KENYA: TREATING MALARIA, CAUSING AIDS


SECTION: HEALTH


LENGTH: 1859 words


Siaya District, Kenya -- East Africa has been hard hit by the AIDS virus. Latest World Health Organization statistics place Uganda, Kenya and Tanzania in the top ten countries for the total number of fatal AIDS cases reported worldwide. Kenya ranks eighth. The disease is already draining national healthcare budgets. And now it is making it more difficult to treat other health problems as well.

At the Siaya District Hospital in western Kenya, the problem begins with a two-month old baby with spindly arms, trying to suck his mother's breast as she sits on a wooden bench. Eve Lackritz, an American researcher from the U.S. Centers for Disease Control who has spent several months at the hospital, says she can diagnose the baby's problem without even doing a blood test.

"There are basically no red blood cells here to give this child any color," she says. "If we look at this two-month old, the palm is white, the tongue is white, he is literally white. There is absolutely no color here at all." 

The baby's color is a result of anemia -- anemia that is itself a result of malaria. Malaria parasites are destroying his red blood cells, and that, in turn, is starving his body for oxygen. That's commonplace for this part of Kenya, where dozens of children with malaria-caused anemia come to this small hospital every day.

"You or I would have to get hit by a truck and lose a huge amount of blood volume to be as anemic as this child is now," Lackritz says.

As the baby squirms and tries to get back to his mother's breast, a hospital technician takes out an old-fashioned barber's razor, without any cream, and begins shaving the child's head. The hospital staff is about to give the baby the same treatment they give more than seven hundred other children each year. They are going to slip an IV needle into the baby's head -- older patients get the needle in their arm -- and give him a blood transfusion.

For decades now, hospitals across Africa have turned to blood to treat patients with diseases from malaria to malnutrition.

"Blood transfusion is widely used, vastly more than in the US," says Kent Campbell of the US Centers for Disease Control (CDC). Based at the CDC's Atlanta headquarters, Campbell roams the globe in an attempt to better understand and control malaria. When he visits Siaya Hospital, where a study of blood transfusion has been underway, he works at a counter in the hospital's small lab with his microscope and blood smears.

In a typical American medical center, only a tiny fraction of the children get a transfusion, far less than one percent. But at hospitals such as Siaya, almost one third of the children get blood. The transfusions here usually don't cure the childrens' illnesses. The childrens' main problem is that they have a chronic disease like malaria, or a respiratory infection, or hookworm, or malnutrition, or maybe a combination of all those diseases, and anemia is just one of the symptoms.

"It isn't anemia, per se, which is the problem," Campbell says. "It is some other disease which has brought the child to the hospital."

A blood transfusion can ease the symptom and make the child stronger for a while -- and sometimes even save the child's life -- but to cure the underlying disease, Campbell says, doctors need to treat the children with drugs or sophisticated medical equipment that many African hospitals don't have.

"When medicines are short, which they can be in many parts of Africa," says Campbell, "blood is more available than drugs, and this is the dilemma. Many times you have so little to offer to a patient. Blood is available, therefore it is offered when nothing else may be around."

But now the AIDS virus is changing all that. Just consider the basic statistics. Thirty percent of blood donors in some regions of Africa are infected with the HIV virus which causes AIDS, and studies show that patients who get blood transfusions that are tainted with the AIDS virus are almost certain to become infected themselves. So even though countries like Kenya have begun screening blood as much as possible, researchers like Campbell say the transfusions are risky.

"There's no question that they are spreading the virus." he says. "That's been documented. HIV is being spread and will continue to be spread by blood transfusion until a much clearer understanding of how to effectively use blood transfusion has been developed."

"When the same blood transfusion that may save a child's life, in the short term, may also transmit an infectious disease," says Campbell, "the doctors in these clinics face a real dilemma. You have to ask yourself, what is a doctor to do?"

So Campbell and his CDC colleagues have made several research trips to Siaya Hospital to help the Kenyans sort it out. They have been focusing on children who have malaria-caused anemia, because it puts more children in the hospital and causes more blood transfusions than any other disease.

In one room of the childrens' ward there are ten beds, like narrow army cots squeezed side by side along the walls. The hospital doesn't have enough space to give each child his or her own cot, so two sick children have to share many of the beds. On one of them lies a skinny little girl, Eve Okol, who was checked into the ward three days ago. The girl lies awake but motionless on a cot -- which also holds her mother plus another patient and his mother.

The ward nurse, Veronica Opia Oroko, stops by to check Eve Okol's chart. "Eve was running fever," Opia Oroko says, "she was vomiting, she had swelling of the legs, and diarrhea, loose stools. This was severe anemia."

As researchers struggle to find a way to avoid giving blood to children, mothers like Eve's are consumed with the struggle to buy blood for their children because doctors have always said the blood will cure them. Until last year in Kenya, most patients like Eve got treated for free. But the government is running out of money, so under a new policy, patients' families now have to pay for at least part of the treatments and for the hospital food. And the hospital won't give Eve a transfusion until her mother scrapes up the cash.

"So the girl is awaiting transfusion," Opia Oroko explains. "But the mother is still unable to purchase the items which have to be used, like a blood pack, blood-giving set and a needle."

Such situations have placed an additional burden on the small hospital's staff. At Siaya, when a child is very ill with anemia, and the family cannot find the money to pay for a transfusion, the nurses have been known to pay for treatment out of their own pockets. "Yes, sometimes we do," admits Opia Oroko. "Sometimes in crisis we do. That child -- I couldn't let it die while there was blood there."

Even when children like Eve get their transfusions and go home, chances are they will come back in a month or two for more transfusions. Blood is only a temporary treatment, and children leave the hospital still suffering from the underlying disease.

Of course, the more transfusions the children get, the greater the risk of getting AIDS. Kent Campbell says that the frustrating irony of all this is that the hospitals could probably cure the vast majority of these children without giving any blood -- if the hospital medicine cabinets had more antibiotics and better anti-malarial drugs, and if researchers can develop an easy test that could identify the few children who really do need a transfusion to survive, rather than the majority who don't.

But to do that, researchers need a lot more information. They need to know the hemogloban levels of all the children, what kind of symptoms they get and what happens to the patients after they leave the hospital. That kind of research is hard to do in a hospital like this.

"One of the things you want ¢to know/," Campbell says, "is what percentage of all the kids coming into the hospital have malaria." In the American system you could probably pretty easily get a sense of that.

But in a Kenyan regional hospital like Siaya, with few resources, crowded wards and an overworked staff, record keeping becomes secondary.

In fact, Eve Lackritz says, the hospital cannot even tell how many children die here on any given day. And it's not just a problem of bad records. When a child dies, the family has to pay almost a hundred dollars to get the body out of the morgue. Because many families cannot afford the fee, they sneak the small corpse out of the hospital before the morticians can count it.

"That is a problem," Lackritz says, "when you are trying to figure out who is your target population and who is dying and where you can make an intervention to keep people from dying. If you don't even know who is dying and how many, it is hard to get a handle on the matter."

So, don't even bother asking for statistics about the issue that created all of this concern in the first place: how many patients who get blood transfusions actually get infected with the virus that causes AIDS? There is not enough information to begin to give a firm answer.

Kenyan health officials like Dan Wendo, chief medical officer at Siaya hospital, say blood transfusions pose a small risk because, according to a national policy, all blood has to be screened for the AIDS virus before its used. In fact, Window says, Siaya hospital has its own AIDS virus testing computer which allows them to screen blood right in their small laboratory.

But despite the testing, the AIDS virus can still be transmitted, according to health professionals. The CDC's Kent Campbell says he cannot talk specifically about Siaya hospital because there are not enough data. But in general, he says, even the most sophisticated laboratories make mistakes when they are screening blood for HIV. Studies suggest that up to one percent of the blood samples that a lab test says are safe are really infected with the AIDS virus. And the risk is higher, Campbell points out, in a situation where blood transfusions are frequent.

"One percent may sound like an awfully small percentage," he says. "But on the other hand, when you multiply one percent by many transfusions, that becomes a regrettably large number."

In addition, Campbell says, many hospital labs in poor countries are not very sophisticated. Where lab technicians are not highly trained in running the complicated AIDS screening test, more infected blood slips through.

Campbell says that until Kenyan hospitals are able to avoid using such large amounts of blood to treat desperately ill patients, an unknown number of children will go to hospitals to cure their malaria-caused anemia -- and they will leave infected with the AIDS virus instead.

The good news is that a growing number of medical centers, like the one in Siaya, are at least trying to screen their blood. The staff at Siaya have covered the walls and doors here with posters that show a big fist scrunching the words: "Let's crush AIDS."

This article is adapted from reporting by Daniel Zwerdling for National Public Radio, where he is a correspondent.

Africa News


June 11, 1990


PROTECTIONS PROPOSED


LENGTH: 629 words


Pretoria, South Africa - If Albie Sachs has his way, South Africa -- arguably among the most polluted lands on earth -- will become the second in the world to protect its ecology in a Bill of Rights.

Sachs, an attorney and university professor who recently returned from exile, is a prominent thinker on constitutional issues for the African National Congress. At a conference in Pretoria organized by Lawyers for Human Rights, Sachs presented a strong appeal for environmental rights to be safeguarded in a post-apartheid constitution. "It is not just playing with metaphors to say that we are fighting to free the land, the waters, the sky as well as the people," says Sachs. 

Sachs acknowledges that resistance movements have compelling concerns that tend to exclude the environment from their agendas.

"But we must take responsibility for our country and its future in all its dimensions and implant the question of conservation firmly within our social and geographic reality," Sachs told the Pretoria conference. "Human rights in the broadest sense are indivisible," he said. "When we breathe the air of freedom, we do not want to choke on hidden fumes."

In a section of his talk called, "Do Trees Have Rights?," Sachs outlined ways that respect for ecosystems could be entrenched in the future constitution of South Africa.

Namibia is the only other country in the world that has included the environment in its constitution. A section in that nation's charter requires the state to actively promote policies that protect the territory's ecosystems and biological diversity. It also stipulates that natural resources be used by industry and communities in a sustainable way.

The preamble for a South African constitution, Sachs said, could define principles upon which South Africans should relate to each other and their natural environment. He suggested the charter could include provisions that would oblige the state to examine the potential environmental impact of any laws that pass through its legislature.

A separate code for the environment, linked to the constitution but more detailed in its provisions, could lay down regulations regarding pollution, the disposal of toxic waste, soil erosion and protection of species.

"Finally," Sachs said, "the constitution could acknowledge that our country is part of the African continent and the question of ecology has both a regional and a continental dimension."

Although concern for the environment in South Africa has, until recently, been subsumed by the more burning issues of race and class, Sachs believes that those who take part in the fight against apartheid are driven by an urge to reconnect with nature.

"When we say 'Mayibuye i'Afrika' -- 'come back Africa,'" he says, "we are calling for the return of legal title but also for the restoration of the land, the forest and the atmosphere. The greening of our country is basic to its healing."

But Sachs warns that constitutional protections will not be worth the paper they are written on if a post-apartheid state does not deal with the problems of the laboring poor.

Although, air pollution and environmental toxins cut across race, and class divisions, he says. "We often forget that the most immediate victims are the workers who are expected to spray trees or handle toxic materials without themselves being protected. The guarantee of a safe and clean working environment becomes a key question for any trade union and integrates itself into the question of a safe and clean South Africa."

Sachs insists that a serious program to protect and mend the country's ecosystems has to deal with the question of poverty and the need to provide all citizens with basic social and welfare facilities.

Africa News


June 11, 1990


SOUTH AFRICA: POLLUTION TIED TO POLITICAL SYSTEM


SECTION: ENVIRONMENT


LENGTH: 1126 words


White South Africans are responsible for more of the carbon dioxide emissions that lead to acid rain and a possible "greenhouse effect" than any other population group in the world, says a report published by the Worldwatch Institute, an environmental research group. The document says the statistics for carbon emissions are part of a larger picture of environmental deterioration that is linked to South Africa's apartheid policies and that political reforms could begin to ease many of the problems.

"On a per person basis," says Apartheid's Environmental Impact, "white South Africans are the world's worst greenhouse offenders."

The report, written by Worldwatch senior researcher Alan B. Durning, estimates that per capita carbon emissions for white South Africans was more than 9 tons in 1987, compared to 5 tons for each American and an average of one ton per person worldwide. Durning based his South African estimates on the white's share of national income, since energy consumption patterns closely follow income distribution. 

Although the prosperity of white South Africans would have led to higher energy use in any case, Durning says that the extraordinarily large usage is part of a complex pattern resulting from the political and economic realities of white domination. "Apartheid," he says, "has distorted the nation's pattern of energy use as dramatically as its pattern of land ownership."

South Africa's drive for energy self-sufficiency in the face of economic sanctions, and its isolation from its neighbors in the region, has made it dependent on coal for 85% of its commercial energy -- a ratio exceeded only in North Korea.

In addition, low wages for the 85,000 black miners who dig the coal has kept electricity prices among the lowest in the world and encouraged wasteful usage. And the country's pioneering of synthetic fuels production -- using coal to make fuel oils -- is another energy-intensive project. Overall, South Africa uses twice as much energy to produce a dollar of economic product as the United States and four times as much as Japan, two other nations high on the world scale of energy consumption.

Coal is not the only mineral South Africa has in abundance. The country is the world's major producer of gold, chromite and platinum and the second largest producer of manganese. It is third in production of uranium, fourth in antimony, fifth in diamonds, sixth in asbestos, seventh in nickel, eighth in iron ore, ninth in coal and eleventh in silver.

But mineral riches can devastate the environment. "For every ton of metal that leaves a mining operation's mill," the Worldwatch report says, "about one hundred tons of wastes are left in a heap topside, where they can blow away on the winds, run off into rivers, or leach into groundwater." Apartheid has exacerbated the environmental destruction of mining, the report says. The high cost of maintaining apartheid has made the government dependent on large mining revenues and reluctant to police mining practices. The result has been massive pollution of soil, water and air.

In the short term, Durning says, blacks bear the major brunt of the hazards, including average deaths of two underground gold miners per day, asbestos-related diseases among both miners and their families, and the health effects of living downstream and downwind of the mines.

"South African mining and energy policies contribute to global environmental threats on a scale completely out of proportion to the size of the nation's population and economy," says the report.

Another environmental impact of apartheid identified by the report is the population density in South Africa's ten ethnic reserves. About half of South Africa's 28 million Africans are crowded into the rural reserves, whose fragile ecosystems make them more suited to rangeland than to subsistence agriculture. "During World War I," Durning says, "a government commission surveyed the agricultural carrying capacity of the small reserve of QwaQwa and estimated that the area was already overcrowded at 5000 inhabitants. Today, QwaQwa is home to more than 500,000 people."

As a result, nearly 90% of the graz-ing and cropland in the reserves are suffering "unsustainable rates of soil loss," at the same time that hunger and malnutrition have mounted.

Durning acknowledges that South Africa's ecological problems are hardly unique, and that modern history is full of societies that are squandering their land and poisoning their people. But he argues that political change could begin to reverse the damages that are a direct legacy of apartheid.

And he holds out some hope that a future South African government could combine a commitment to economic growth with a sensitivity to the environment. Already, environmental concerns have been raised by labor unions (see Africa News, April 30, 1990) and by community groups like the Soweto-based National Environmental Awareness Campaign.

At the national level, Max Sisulu, chief of economics and planning for the African National Congress and son of ANC leader Walter Sisulu, has pledged the ANC's commitment to sustaining the environment. In a policy statement last November, Sisulu said that "environmental reconstruction constitutes a major task of a free and democratic post-apartheid South Africa." Among other measures he proposed, Sisulu called for the elimination of nuclear power, the promotion of solar energy, and a halt to the destruction of elephants and other wildlife.

Durning warns that "bold words are easy for those out of power," and that a democratic South Africa will face the same immense challenges that other nations are confronting in the struggle to protect the earth. But by dismantling apartheid, he says, South Africans "will automatically put an end to many of the sources of environmental degradation -- overcrowding in the homelands, over-reliance on coal, production of synthetic fuels, and scorched earth warfare against neighboring countries."

Those countries, too, will feel an early impact from changes in South Africa. Already, the ten nations of the Southern Africa Development Coordination Conference (SADCC) are discussing future relationships in the region. Among the possibilities are hydroelectric projects on rivers in SADCC states that could provide "clean" energy for South Africa plus revenues and energy for the producers.

Durning finds an additional sign of hope in the "one quiet theme that cannot be suppressed" despite the violence and bitterness of South African history -- the shared love of both black and white South Africans for the land itself. If thoughts of a greener future are optimistic, he concludes, neither are they idle speculation.

June 11, 1990


ZAIRE: DYMALLY QUITS AFRICA COALITION AND SCHOLARSHIP FUND


SECTION: U.S.-AFRICA


LENGTH: 955 words


When Rep. Mervyn Dymally (D-CA) convinced President Mobutu Sese Seko of Zaire to donate $ 250,000 to the Coalition for a Free Africa (CFA) more than two yearsago, the congressman says he was trying "to do something positive to help Africa." (see Africa News, January 29, 1990) But his association with the Coalition has resulted in a spate of negative publicity, and last week Dymally gave up his seat on CFA's board of advisors.

"I just didn't think it was worth the beating I was taking in the press to continue," Dymally told the Los Angeles Times. He says he and other black political figures have been unfairly targeted for criticism by white-owned media.

Dymally also resigned as chair of the Dymally Scholarship Fund, following newspaper accounts suggesting his positions in the two organizations represented a conflict of interest. 

The Coalition for a Free Africa was established in 1986 by Richard Griffey, the president of Solar Records in Hollywood, "to provide relief for victims of apartheid and African famine," according to the articles of incorporation filed with the California secretary of state. A year ago, CFA held a telethon to raise funds for South African children. The event was more successful in generating publicity than revenue, according to Virgil Roberts, Solar's general counsel. Roberts says Mobutu's $ 250,000 is the only major funding CFA has received, aside from some $ 300,000 donated by Griffey.

Dymally has acknowledged helping Griffey, a friend and campaign contributor, to obtain a $ 12 million oil contract with the Angolan government. He also tried to persuade the Uganda government to do business with another friend, Mamadi Diane, a Washington, D.C.-based food broker who arranged the congressman's first trip to Zaire in 1988. Dymally's expenses for the trip -- which included stops in Angola, Gabon, Cote d'Ivoire and Ghana -- were paid by CFA and the Zaire government. CFA also paid "out-of-pocket" expenses for a subsequent Dymally trip to Brazil to accept an award for Nelson Mandela on behalf of the Congressional Black Caucus.

Diane heads the Zaire-American Research Institute, which promotes that country's cause in the U.S. and has received most of its funding from Zaire businessmen with close ties to Mobutu.

Accounts of various aspects of Dymally's activities have appeared over the past year in Africa News, Business Week, the Christian Science Monitor, Journal of Commerce, Legal Times, the Wall Street Journal, and the Washington Post. But the congressman has faced constant criticism from the newspaper of the New Alliance, a left-leaning political party which unsuccessfully fielded a candidate against Dymally in last week's California primary.

The Alliance, which has been waging a vigorous campaign against Mobutu, has excoriated Dymally for his support of the Zaire leader. Charges of misconduct against the congressman have been raised repeatedly in the newspaper. And the U.S.-Congo Friendship Committee, which has close ties to National Alliance, filed an ethics complaint against Dymally with the Committee on Official Standards of Conduct of the House of Representatives.

In Washington, another organization closely associated with the Alliance, the Rainbow Lobby, has mobilized pressure on Congress to cut off aid to Zaire. The Lobby has attacked Dymally for refusing to join in the criticisms of Mobutu that have been raised by other Black Caucus members and by liberal Democrats Stephen Solarz and David Obey, as well as Howard Wolpe, who chairs the House Africa subcommittee.

Dymally's resignation from the scholarship fund follows a Washington Post report in January. The article suggested that the congressman had withdrawn his opposition to diamond sanctions against South Africa after the fund received a $ 34,200 contribution from Maurice Tempelsman, a New York diamond merchant who lobbied Dymally on the bill. The scholarship fund finances study trips to Puerto Rico for minority high school students from Dymally's Congressional district.

Both Tempelsman and Dymally denied that the contribution was related to the sanctions deliberations.

Dymally says his shift in position on diamonds came as a result of a plea from the South African diamond-producing country of Botswana and was supported by other sanctions supporters, including Wolpe. The original measure, which was part of the proposed 1988 sanctions bill that would have cut all trade links with South Africa, would have banned the import of diamonds of unidentifiable origin, as well as those designated as coming from South Africa.

Tempelsman, who says he has supported South African sanctions, told Africa News that "a blanket inclusion of diamonds in sanctions legislation -- because ¢diamonds/ normally arrive indistinguishable as to national origin -- would unfairly penalize not only U.S. importers but also such non-South African producers as Botswana." The government of Botswana, in a letter to Dymally, said the proposed ban "would have the potential to destroy" its economy "without producing any noticeable effect on South Africa."

After separate meetings with Botswana officials and with Tempelsman, Dymally agreed to remove the diamond ban and replace it with a provision calling for a study of the diamond industry that is intended to pressure diamond traders into segregating diamonds by country of origin.

Wolpe says the suggestion that Tempelsman's intervention was the reason for the change in language "is simply untrue." The bill as now drafted bans the import of South African diamonds and "expresses clearly the intent of Congress that effective and rigorus enforcement measures would be required," Wolpe says.

Africa News


June 11, 1990


NIGERIA CREDIT FOR ALL


SECTION: DEVELOPMENT


LENGTH: 1113 words


Lagos -- The alluring white paint of the one-story building seems out of place in its surroundings in Ajegunle, a sprawling slum suburb on the outskirts of Lagos. Emblazoned on its entrance are the words "People's Bank of Nigeria. "

In front of the building, Mustapha Sanusi, a carpenter, jostles for a place on the queue with hundreds of other artisans and small scale traders waiting to collect application forms for loans from the bank. "I need the N500 (500 naira - about $ 60) to buy equipment to complete a job I'm doing," says Sanusi, who cannot read or write. He could not get a loan from a commercial bank because he could provide no collateral and besides, the money he needed was "too small." 

So Sanusi turned to the People's Bank, a grassroots financial institution owned by the government and targeted at the poorest Nigerians who live below the poverty line. Ibrahim Babangida, the country's military ruler, says the bank is an indirect scheme "for income generation and income redistribution" among the country's poor, many of whom cannot get credit from commercial banks for reasons similar to Sanusi's. The People's Bank gives out minimum loans of $ 6 (N50), and the maximum it can lend is $255 (N2,000). No collateral is needed. Apart from a service fee of five percent, no interest is charged. Payments begin two weeks after the loan is taken, and the total repayment period is one year.

So far, the People's Bank has lent $ 2 million to 9,000 poor artisans, small scale farmers and entrepreneurs. Nigeria's poorest citizens, who are the Bank's clientele, have borne the heaviest burden of the economic Structural Adjustment Program (SAP) started by Babangida's government in 1986.

The stringent reform measures have weakened the purchasing power of the country's currency, and led to deteriorating living standards. The naira has been devalued by 80%. Subsidies on fuel and utilities have been withdrawn, prompting price increases in all sectors of the economy. But the minimum wage still remains at its pre-SAP level of $ 6 (N125) a month.

Against this backdrop, the People's Bank -- which started last October and already has branches in most of Nigeria's 21 states -- is seen as a godsend by poor Nigerians. "I can't believe this," says a smiling Cecelia Idong, a tailor and mother of five children who borrowed $ 25 (N1,000). "The loan has given my business the boost it so badly needed."

Tai Solarin, a leftist government critic who has been appointed the bank's chairman, says he is happy that "the government has realized that structures operating in the country have not served the purpose of the ordinary people."

Banks that exclusively serve the poor have been successful in India, Malaysia, Pakistan and Bangladesh. Nigeria's People's Bank seems to be inspired by the success of Bangladesh's Grameen (Rural) Bank, which operates in over 7,000 Bangladeshi villages and serves the banking needs of about 300,000 households in the country's rural areas.

But despite the rave reviews that have welcomed the "poor man's bank" here, the scheme is not without problems, and it has its critics. "How do you make it self-sustaining, or is it supposed to be a Father Christmas?" asks Claude Esekody, a Shell Oil Company management trainee. Most analysts here do not think the bank should be run as a charity. But they stress that by its very nature, the new financial institution has to be unorthodox in its methods. "Banking for the poor is 'social banking.' The profit motive is not the driving force," says O.B.A. Obanla, an economic analyst in Lagos. Ade Ojo, dean of the business school at the University of Lagos, says the People's Bank is just a "gimmick" to pacify poor Nigerians who are feeling the pinch of the SAP. Ojo says there is no "authoritative" proof of the bank's viability.

A major criticism of the concept is that the minimum and maximum lending range of $ 6 to $255 is too small to help many small scale businesses. Government officials say they hope to extend the lending ceiling in the coming years and that the small sums being disbursed have clearly helped many poor individuals.

Another fear has been that repayment defaults by loan recipients would make the bank unviable and lead to its collapse. But Bola Ajibola, Attorney-General and Minister of Justice disagrees. "The poor people are more honest than the rich," he says. "The percentage of loan repayment will be higher than in our normal banks."

So far, the figures support Ajibola. An excellent repayment rate of 113 percent has been recorded by the banks. Bank officials report that although $ 25,000 (N200,000) was due to be repaid within the first month of its existence, the beneficiaries repaid $ 31,000 (N250,000). "It's amazing," says Maria Sokenu, the bank's national coordinator. "The rate of repayment is fantastic." Sokenu says many loan beneficiaries don't take the allotted year for full repayment "because they know that as soon as they pay back, they become eligible for a bigger loan."

The low default rate is due partly to the People's Bank's lending procedures. To get a loan, applicants must belong to a trade or professional group of fifteen persons. It is the group that recommends to the bank which of its members should get a loan first. The group is an informal guarantor because the rest of them will not receive any loans until the beneficiary starts paying back on schedule.

Impressed with the bank's initial success, Babangida has approved an extra $ 25 million for its activities. The bank plans to establish 170 new bran-ches this year alone. Its success has attracted the attention of the African Development Bank (ADB), which plans to inject funds into it soon.

In addition to expanding its support for the People's Bank, the Nigerian government is planning to set up "community banks" in rural areas. It will provide initial capital for the bank's start-up, but unlike the People's Bank, the equity capital for community banks will come mostly from villagers who will be required, as they are by the People's Bank, to form themselves into groups for securing loans. Together with a rural banking scheme which the Central Bank of Nigeria started in 1977, the People's Bank and the community banks are designed to take care of the credit needs of Nigeria's rural and urban poor.

Economists here say that banking for the poor is "an economic imperative" for a developing country such as Nigeria, where most of the population live below the poverty line. The essence of the People's Bank, says analyst Obanla, is the recognition that economic opportunities for the poor must be expanded.

Africa News


June 11, 1990


REPORTER'S NOTEBOOK:CAMPUSES IN CRISIS


LENGTH: 1023 words


The National University of Zaire at Lubumbashi (UNZL), where an unknown number of students were massacred by government troops, is a once-thriving institution that now has neither toilets nor textbooks.

Although NZL is one of three institutions at the top of Zaire's educational system, physical conditions are so abysmal that it resembles a war zone, especially since no one has yet removed the scars of the last war fought in this spot, 30 years ago.

The seven-story administration building, constructed as a whites-only hospital in the era of Belgian colonialism, served as headquarters for United Nations peacekeeping troops during the civil strife of the post-independence period. The structure still bears the traces of those bullets, and the subsequent decades of decay have littered the grounds with the wreckage of vehicles for which there are no spare parts. 

The dormitories, whose 5000 residents are packed into a space built for 1900, could be a refugee center, where the half-starved students live on one bowl of porridge a day.

Students and teachers suffer together through classes under the blazing heat of the central African sun, because soldiers from the three military barracks that surround the university have stolen many of the classrooms' corrugated tin roofs.

Still, there's a bright side to the missing roofs. Although the classrooms become unusable during the rains, in dry weather the rooflessness makes for better lighting, compensating for the lack of electric fixtures and the power outages that plunge the campus into periodic darkness.

Similarly, power failures are less serious for studies than they might be elsewhere, since students do little reading at night -- textbooks are rare and the library contains almost nothing published in the last 20 years. A Rockefeller Foundation grant to replenish the collection was stolen before it could be used. Zaire, says a foundation official, is "one of the rare places where we have felt compelled to declare failure."

The struggle to eat preoccupies both students and teachers, constantly impeding their tenacious efforts to study and to teach. Everyone would starve without resorting to some sort of supplemental income. Students sometimes steal light bulbs and desks for resale. Teachers hold other jobs, and some -- apparently a disreputable minority -- extort money from students.

State neglect has made the educational decline inevitable. Although UNZL is in Zaire's southeastern Shaba province, one of Africa's most mineral-rich regions, the school's operating budget, outside of salaries and scholarships, was only $ 16,000 a year in 1987 -- less than the price of one of the chauffeured Mercedes-Benzes that whisk top government officials through the streets of the town.

Faculty and staff salaries are paid separately by the central government, but the money is often late.

The university's poverty is both a product and a symptom of the underfinancing of the entire educational system. Since UNZL was founded in 1955 as a school for the white colonial elite, physical conditions have deteriorated dramatically. "We ate three meals a day in the dining halls and maids washed our clothes and cleaned our rooms," says one of the first black students to study there in 1959. Even in the 1970s, the campus was clean and there was a good dining hall.

People familiar with the school say things began to change after the Mobutu government nationalized the universities in 1971, and that the decline accelerated a few years later when the price of copper -- Zaire's major revenue earner -- collapsed on the world market.

Today the dining halls are closed and malnutrition is increasingly widespread. Rest is elusive for students packed four or five to a tiny room. Teachers say tired, hungry students often fall asleep in class.

Conditions are so grim that those who knew UNZL in better years are amazed that it still exists at all. "They should simply shut down until the government gets serious about education and is prepared to spend some money on reconstruction," says a 1979 graduate.

Despite the difficulties, education and scholarship somehow survive and sometimes thrive. The estimated one out of six students who survive the five year course of study can "match the best at Harvard and Yale," insists a professor who studied in the United States. "If a Zairean tells you he's an architect," says a 1986 architecture graduate from the university in Kinshasa, "you know he's a very strong man who survived an impossible ordeal."

A surprising number of faculty members have given up comfortable positions abroad to teach in Zaire. And while they haven't been able to criticize President Mobutu by name, university-based scholars have produced many of the most sophisticated studies on the roots of hunger, unemployment and agricultural stagnation. Graduate students and university teachers have developed strategies for "participatory research," working directly with peasants and the urban poor to develop ways to found cooperatives, increase food production and meet other basic needs.

Teachers are closely watched, though, whether Zairean or foreign. Last year a Belgian university lecturer was expelled from the country for putting two studies of an opposition party in a university faculty library.

One of the few Zaireans who will be quoted is UNZL economic historian Tshibangu Kabel Musasa. "Simply teaching the truth," he says, "is enough to get in trouble." Trouble is something he knows well. Arrested three times during the 1980s, he nearly lost an eye during severe police beatings.

Students have proved the hardest critics to silence, keeping alive a tradition of spontaneous protest, if not organized opposition. But even complaints about poor living and study conditions is to risk imprisonment or death. Still, each new student generation has included some who are daring enough to test the limits of dissent.

This piece is adapted from a forthcoming book by Carole Collins and Steve Askin, partly based on reporting by Askin for the Chronicle of Higher Education.

Africa News


June 11, 1990


MOBUTU UNDER FIRE


LENGTH: 1054 words


Washington, DC. -- In the wake of an apparent massacre of student protestors, President Mobutu Sese Seko of Zaire is facing a crisis of confidence among the international supporters that have kept him in power since 1965.

Protests are spreading in several parts of the country, fueled by reports that government soldiers murdered between 50 and 100 university students in Lubumbashi last month.

Strikes have reportedly shut down Gecamines, the state-owned mining company that is Zaire's major employer and largest export earner. Protests have also closed schools across the country. Student demonstrations were reported in major towns from the capital Kinshasa to Mbanza-Ngungu in the west to Mbandaka in the north and Mbuji-Mayi in the central region. Among the others reported to be on strike or staging slow downs are doctors in Kinshasa and other cities, who last week defied a government order to return to work, Reuters reported. 

News of the killings, which have made headlines in the European press, sparked a call for an international inquiry from the 12-nation European Community. The EC appeal was made at the request of Belgium, Zaire's former colonial ruler, which retains an extensive stake in the country's economy. Brussels has suspended new loans and aid programs to Zaire.

France, which in the past has sent troops to prop up the Zaire government, announced the indefinite postponement of a scheduled cabinet-level visit to the country by officials planning next year's Francophone summit, scheduled to meet in Kinshasa.

In the United States, where Mobutu was welcomed a year ago as the mediator of Angola's 13-year old civil war, new questions are being raised about his reliability as an ally.

Zaire is currently the largest recipient of American aid in sub-Saharan Africa. But the reports of student slayings are expected to accelerate Congressional attempts to cut the military aid and restrict economic assistance (see Africa News, April 30, 1990).

Any cut in U.S. assistance would be important. Washington has been Mobutu's chief supporter since he seized control in a CIA-backed coup 25 years ago. Administration officials privately acknowledge his continuing usefulness as a funnel for covert U.S. aid to rebels fighting the Marxist Angolan government.

The Zaire unrest comes in the aftermath of Mobutu's April 24 announcement of political reforms, including a promise to end one-party control. But a widespread crackdown on dissenters since the announcement seems to have propelled the current protests to new heights. The level of nationwide activism reported in recent days is unprecedented in a country where independent trade unions and student movements have been banned since the late 1960s.

"Mobutu seems to have unleashed the deadliest of his security forces against his people," says Makau wa Mutua, an attorney with the New York-based Lawyers Committee for Human Rights, who has made two recent visits to the country and is preparing a report on the human rights situation there. "No one can remember such a concentrated period of repression in Zaire in recent times," he says.

Despite the crackdown, the opposition has been emboldened, Mutua says. He recounts a June 3 incident where one leader of the opposition Union for Democracy and Social Progress fired a pistol in the air when security police tried to break up a meeting at his home. The oppositionist was subsequently arrested. Mutua says it seems that people formerly cowed by Mobutu's security apparatus are saying, "To hell with it, we're just going to speak."

One measure of the unusual nature of recent events in Zaire is the account broadcast on May 28 -- the day the Gecamines strikes began -- by the local, government-run radio station in Lubumbashi, where the mining company's operations are centered. With unaccustomed frankness, the radio that Gecamines employees "did not go to work this morning." The station also said that in Likasi, another key mining center, "shops, public and private firms, and ¢government/ departments did not open their doors" and "markets closed shortly after having opened." The newscast reported that schools failed to reopen as scheduled after a holiday break, despite repeated requests "by the urban authorities in a communique broadcast many times on our radio this morning."

The first reports of the Lubumbashi killings appeared in the Brussels daily, Le Soir. Accounts have also been carried in Libre Belgique and the French newspaper Le Monde. The stories say commandos from the Special Presidential Division -- an elite unit recruited from Mobutu's home region and trained by Israeli advisors -- were dispatched from Kinshasa to the Lubumbashi campus, bearing lists of suspected anti-government activists. On the night of May 11, the accounts say, the troops sealed off the university, cut electric lines, assaulted students with knives and bayonets, and set fire to dormitory rooms. A statement issued May 30 by nine Zairean opposition parties and organizations charged that at least 63 people died during the police action. The raid was believed to be a reprisal for student attacks on suspected campus police agents. Lubumbashi students have charged that the agents were responsible for the disappearance and presumed death of 23 of their fellow students in recent years.

The siege of the campus follows a year of escalating challenges to the Mobutu regime -- often led by students -- accompanied by reprisals against the dissidents. Soldiers and police last year killed an unknown number of young people - estimates range from four to more than three dozen - during protests in Lubumbashi and in Kinshasa.

The U.S. State Department formally noted the reported killings "with concern" and called for an independent investigation. Assistant Secretary of State for African Affairs Herman Cohen was in Kinshasa late last month after the incident occurred, but before it received much notice in the foreign press. A State Department official declined to say whether Cohen discussed the incident with Zairean officials. The Department also says it can confirm no reports to counter the Zaire government claim that only one student died in the violence, which it blamed on "faction fighting."

-- with files from Steve Askin

June 18, 1990


NEW STANDARDS


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 920 words


While developing countries as a whole have made significant progress towards human development, as measured by improved literacy rates, lower infant mortality rates and access to primary health care, African poverty is on the increase. The United Nations Development Program's recently released Human Development Report says that any international effort to tackle global social problems must give priority to Africa, where almost two-thirds of the people are below the poverty line. Long-term adjustment strategies, says the UNDP, must be adopted to rebuild African economies, and an overwhelming share of the international community's concessional resources must be earmarked for Africa.

In addition, the report concludes that the practice of measuring development by per capita wealth fails to reveal true human advancement. A nation may be experiencing economic growth, but have social indicators below the average, as in the case of Senegal. Likewise, some nations with low economic performance such as Tanzania, do better than others in providing health and education to their people, the report says. 

To adjust for these discrepancies, the report calls for new standards. In particular, military expenditures, which in some countries are three times that of health and education spending combined, should be substantially reduced, and governments should balance economic considerations more equally with social ones, UNDP says.

WORKERS RIGHTS

Sudan is among four countries cited by Americas Watch, a human rights monitor, for violating international labor rights. The other countries are El Salvador, Guatemala and Colombia.

The rights organization has criticized the U.S. government for continuing preferential trade with the four countries. It also has submitted petitions to the U.S. Trade Representative calling for a suspension of benefits to the countries named until workers rights improve significantly.

Following last year's military coup, the Sudanese government banned all independent trade unions and arrested at least 200 workers, some of whom say they were tortured while in detention.

AIDS HOPE

The anti-AIDS drug Kemron, developed in Kenya, has shown promising results in a recent study conducted by the World Health Organization (WHO).

Researchers in five African countries Kenya, Cote d'Ivoire, Congo, Zimbabwe and Cameroon took part in the study. WHO plans to expand the study to include an additional six African countries.

Earlier this year, the Kenya Medical Research Institute announced that it had developed a drug capable of reversing some of the symptoms of the lethal disease. Kenyan President Daniel arap Moi has announced that Kemron will be distributed locally and internationally as early as this August.

ALL THAT GLITTERS

Namibia officially opened its first gold mine since the coun-try's independence last April.

The Navachab gold mine, whose major shareholder is South Africa's Anglo-American Corporation, is thought to be the first significant open-cast gold mine in southern Africa and is expected to produce two tons of gold a year, equivalent to $ 25 million at today's rates.

President Sam Nujoma, speaking at the opening ceremony, reaffirmed his government's commitment to a mixed economy saying that the aim is to ensure reasonable returns on investment and adequate revenue for the state.

DESERT DIALOGUE

The United Nations Secretary General Javier Perez de Cuellar plans to call a Security Council meeting for later this month to discuss the peace process in Western Sahara, Africa News has learned.

The UN has set up an Identification Commission to determine who is eligible to vote in a referendum to decide the fate of the disputed territory. In early June, the commission called a meeting in Geneva of 38 Saharan dignitaries to discuss the poll. Nineteen of the leaders reside in camps along the Algerian border run by Polisario, the Saharan independence movement, and the others live in Moroccan-held territory. At the Geneva meeting, mediated by the Secretary General's special representative for the Western Sahara, Johannes J. Manz, it was agreed that a 1974 Spanish census will provide a guideline for allot-ting voting rights, but that others, omitted from that census, must be included.

TRIALS AND TALKS

Four Liberian government soldiers were brought to trial last week for the killings of several prominent civilians in a suburb of the capital, Monrovia. The government of President Samuel Doe encouraged foreign journalists to attend the court-martial, attempting to deflect criticism that the government has tolerated or even encouraged the recent spate of civilian murders.

While one army source confirmed that the government wants a "speedy trial" to make an example of the defendants, presiding judge Col. Isaac Nyeplu grudgingly stalled the trial to honor the defendants' request for civilian lawyers. Human rights groups have condemned the Doe government in the past for trial violations and summary executions.

As the trial began, fighting between the government and the rebel National Patriotic Front dwindled, while the two sides concluded initial peace talks in neighboring Sierra Leone. The atmosphere at the talks was "courteous and understanding," said a spokesperson for the Liberian Council of Churches, which mediated the sessions. Although the two sides are deadlocked over the rebel demand that President Doe resign, they have promised to meet again on June 25.

Africa News


June 18, 1990


WHEN THE VOTES ARE COUNTED


LENGTH: 556 words


With both sides in the Angolan conflict talking about elections, the question becomes: who can attract the votes to emerge a winner?

Many of Jonas Savimbi's American supporters believe he could easily win any fairly-conducted poll. But there are no accurate measurements of the support enjoyed by the ruling MPLA or Savimbi's Unita. 

A pluralist situation would be a new experience for both Angolan parties, accustomed as they are to tight control over information and intolerance of public criticism. Savimbi's supporters believe his reputed charisma would give him the edge over the more low-key Angolan president, Jose Eduardo dos Santos.

But Savimbi has vulnerabilities that are not often examined in the generally-positive press coverage he receives. One recent indication was the charges of human rights abuses raised by former followers, charges given extra weight when they were reported by a British journalist, Fred Bridgland, who earlier authored an uncritical biography of the Unita leader.

There may be other chinks in Savimbi's armor as well, according to a recent study by William Minter, an American sociologist whose books include King Solomon's Mines Revisited and Operation Timber: Pages from the Savimbi Dossier, a critical examination of the Unita leader's alleged cooperation with colonial Portugal's secret police.

Minter says Unita's recruitment methods for the past decade have involved the use of both conscription and the abduction of youths in battle.

It is widely recognized that government conscription may have created discontent in many parts of the country. "But it is often assumed that an insurgent movement like Unita operates principally by mobilizing volunteers," Minter writes. "In the 1980s, this was clearly not the case."

Minter bases his findings on six weeks of interviews last year with 16 ex-participants in Unita and on a compilation of first-hand accounts by visitors to Unita areas -- part of a larger research project on insurgency in post-colonial Angola and Mozambique. He has described his conclusions in a 20-page report and in an article in the current issue of Africa Report, published by the African-American Institute in New York.

Minter says Unita has provided "rudimentary health and education services" to the populations in areas they control, but has "aimed at making life physically impossible for civilians who decide to stay in government areas."

A 1989 Africa Watch report accuses Unita of mining fields and roads to reduce food output in government-held regions. The Angolan population is said to have the highest number of amputees per capita in the world.

But MPLA could expect to face electoral dissatisfaction over failed economic policies, since not all the hardships can be blamed on the lengthy war.

The government might also be hampered by divisions within its leadership ranks. Differences over key policy questions, including the terms of an acceptable settlement with Savimbi, are believed by some analysts to be the reason why dos Santos has twice postponed a meeting of the central committee, now scheduled for June 27. These analysts believe the president is hoping to conclude a cease-fire agreement before that session, to avoid being shackled with new preconditions that might make an agreement more difficult.
