Africa News


June 18, 1990


READ THIS PAGE!


SECTION: SPECIAL REPORT: CAPTURING THE CONTINENT: U.S. MEDIA COVERAGE OF AFRICA


LENGTH: 1556 words


 

Africa News Service is a non-profit news agency. We are professionals, adhering as closely as possible to that central premise of American journalism that news should be reported accurately and objectively without regard to the personal opinions of the reporter.

And yet, we are not naive enough to believe that information is ever neutral. The existence of organizations like Accuracy in Media and FAIR (Fairness and Accuracy in Reporting), which hurl darts at press coverage from opposing viewpoints, testifies to the fact that one person's objectivity is another's bias.

In fact, the very existence of Africa News is an implicit assertion that values and opinions intrude upon news judgements. If we thought Africa was getting an appropriate share of media attention that was objective and informed, we wouldn't have spent close to two decades in the struggle to keep Africa News Service afloat financially. 

Conversely, because the continent we cover is not regarded as particularly newsworthy by media decision-makers, we are vulnerable to charges that our reports represent "special interests" rather than impartial news judgements.

Our hard-won reputation for accuracy and professionalism was carved out year by year, bit by bit, against the odds, and it's the most valuable asset we have.

A foundation official recently told us that the only criticism she'd encountered about Africa News is that we occasionally stray across the line that divides reporting on Africa from advocacy for Africa. That perception, we told her, is an occupational hazard we will always face. All we can do to counter it is to wait it out, confident that time will vindicate our work.

When we did a front-page series for the Washington Post on the war in Western Sahara in 1977, there were charges that the articles were pro-Polisario, since the conventional wisdom of the time was that the independence group was a sham and would soon disappear into oblivion. If we said otherwise, the reasoning went, we must be partisan.

Today, the war has brought Morocco's economy to the verge of collapse, and the United Nations, flush from its Namibian success, is sponsoring a peace process. Our reports that Polisario was an indigenous movement with a capacity to fight indefinitely have proved to be nothing more than good, solid journalism.

It may be difficult to recall now that only a short time ago, the African National Congress (ANC) was almost never mentioned in reporting from South Africa. To say that the group would be a major force in South Africa's future invited being labeled not just "advocates" but left-wing extremists. So when we wrote about the growing campaign for Nelson Mandela's release and the ground swell of popular support for the ANC, as we did in 1980, we took care to qualify anything we couldn't prove. When we quoted newspaper editor Percy Qoboza saying Mandela would win any free South African election, we acknowledged that his claim was "difficult to assess in an atmosphere where support of the ANC is a crime of treason."

The balancing act between forthrightness and credibility is easier now. Our reputation is secure, and anyone who doubts our credentials can look at the record of our 17 years of print and broadcast reporting.

But people who knew Africa have always known they could turn to us for the straight story that nobody else had -- both the breaking news and the background to make sense of it. We produced dramatic radio reports from the front lines when Ugandan exiles and Tanzanian troops overthrew Idi Amin. We chronicled the crumbling of imperial Ethiopia and the brutality of the regime that replaced it. We interviewed American mercenaries in Zaire on their way to a CIA-backed takeover attempt in Angola. We brought to light the internal Africa policy deliberations of five successive U.S. administrations.

Such reporting is not just lively and interesting. It is essential information for a democratic society. If the public can be kept ignor-ant or misled by policy-makers who themselves are poorly informed or self-deluding, we're all in trouble.

In February of 1981, ten months after Samuel Doe took power, we began an article on Liberia by saying that "the real direction of Liberia's military government remains uncertain and observers are wondering whether the admin-istration's early populist tendencies have been abandoned." Already, the new government was squandering a popular mandate to reform and democratize the country, and the seeds of 1990 were planted. The revolt against Doe was no surprise; the surprise was that it was so long in coming. The tragedy is that change had to come the way it did.

There are other "Liberia's" in the making. And other African issues that demand sober realism. Neither the people of Africa nor the taxpayers of America are served by the pretense that African countries can pay off their mounting foreign debts without a restructuring of the economic ground rules.

Marianne Spiegel, a former Senate aide and State Department policy planner, once wrote an endorsement of Africa News that we have quoted often because it articulates a goal towards which we constantly strive in our reporting:

Africa News profoundly influences policy -- not by advocating positions or slanting its stories, but by intelligent, informed, reliable reporting. I don't know what we would do without it.

We haven't begun, though, to do the reporting job that's needed. Yes, we occasionally write for the major newspapers and produce reports for National Public Radio (NPR). From time to time we produce syndicated programs for commercial radio. The biweekly publication Africa News reaches an influential readership. In the last year, barely a day has passed that we haven't briefed a reporter heading for Africa, faxed information from our library files to a network news show, helped interviewers formulate questions to ask African guests, or provided our materials to Africa-interest groups.

And we've done it with the help of some of the best in the business. We proudly claim to have spotted journalistic talents that have become recognized in their field: NPR's Celeste Wesson and Mike Schuster, the Wall Street Journal's Joe Davidson, the New York Times's Howard French and National Geographic's Charlie Cobb all once reported for Africa News.

But we can't expect to keep such talents very often. Anyone qualified to do the work that is demanded at Africa News could work anywhere. So the people here know that they are bypassing chances for fame and fortune elsewhere for a job where their next paycheck is uncertain. And they can anticipate working through the night to meet the next deadline, knowing that the pressure won't ease after the deadline is met.

Anyone who has tried to run a small business can imagine what it is like to have to find the time, constantly, to train new business and circulation managers because the last ones needed more financial security and less strain.

What all that means is missed opportunities on a daily basis. It means not finding the time to report on Liberia for "All Things Considered" until the fifth month of the fighting (it was NPR's first report on the topic); it means not being able to pursue a film on Zimbabwe, ten years after independence, for PBS distribution; it means not accepting offers to write articles for major magazines; it means delaying radio syndications because we don't have the time to produce the demo tapes that will be used to market the series to stations; and it means not being able to raise the money for the investment in increased circulation that will make Africa News a self-sustaining publication.

Regular readers know that this editorial note is a departure from our practice of reporting the news and letting it speak for itself -- and saving stories about the importance of our work for fundraising efforts. There are two reasons for the shift.

First, this issue is uniquely editorial in its premise and content. We thought it deserved an explanation. Second, we're making a pitch.

As we prepared this special report, and the half-hour radio documentary that accompanies it -- interviewing dozens of the top reporters, editors and producers in the print and broadcast industries -- one thing became clear. The reasons for the poor coverage of Africa in the major media are deeply ingrained in historical perceptions and present practices, and they won't change quickly.

Meanwhile, the best chance for improving that coverage -- as well as for maintaining an independent source of African news -- is Africa News Service. We have the access; we have the expertise; we have the necessary determination. But we don't have the resources.

Yes, we admit it, we're advocates -- not of biased reporting and not of concealing uncomfortable truths, but of the belief that the opportunity to pursue life, liberty and happiness is as fundamental a right for African people as it is for the people of Poland or Rumania or Nicaragua -- or ourselves.

In the next three years we will be trying to build an Africa News Service that can effectively tell the tales of that pursuit. And we'll need help -- a great deal of help -- from every person who cares about Africa.

Get ready.--Tami Hultman, Executive Editor

AIDS TESTS WIDENED:


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 722 words


By the end of this month, the government of Kenya plans to begin marketing the AIDS drug Kemron, first tested at the Kenya Medical Re-search Institute (Kemri) last year.

Representatives of two companies that helped Kemri initiate testing of Kemron -- Amarillo Cell Culture (ACC) of Amarillo, Texas and Hayashibara Biochemical Laboratories of Okayama, Japan -- will travel to Nairobi to attend Kemron's launching ceremonies.

The Kemron saga began in 1989, when ACC founder Dr. Joseph Cummins ran initial tests in Texas using a single sub-species of the protein alpha interferon, administered to AIDS patients in low doses. After test results looked promising, Cummins presented data to Kemri Director Dr. Davy Koech. Koech immediately offered to help expand Cummins' research, simply because "nothing in the world was known to be working" to alleviate AIDS, according to Dr. Arthur Obel, Koech's colleague at Kemri. 

Cummins, who has been experimenting with low-dosage alpha inteferon for much of his career, jumped at the offer. "We've been trying for twelve years to get people to pay attention to us," Cummins says. "I applaud the courage of the ¢Kenyans/ to try this new tech-nology when no one else would."

Koech suggested using an alpha interferon formula produced by Hayashibara that contained fourteen, rather than one, sub-species of the protein. Using the Hayashibara alpha interferon, Koech and Obel set up initial tests at Kemri.

To minimize the impact of psychological factors, most studies of new drugs include a control group that receives a placebo, an inert substance that does not contain the drug being tested. But the first tests at Kemri did not include a placebo control group because there was so little data on the drug. "We were starting from scratch," Obel says. "We had no way of comparing Kemron against anything."

But the results were astonishing, the researchers say. After days of treatment, symptoms such as lack of appetite, swollen lymph nodes, skin disorders, diarrhea, fever and fatigue were significantly reduced. After four weeks on the drug, patients' white blood cells -- the body's disease-fighting army -- had increased by hundreds. And in the most startling finding, Koech reported that 10% of his patients had "sero-converted," testing negative for the HIV antibody after treatment.

Results of follow-up testing under World Health Organization supervision in Kenya, Cameroon, Congo, Cote d'Ivoire and Zimbabwe, available in early July appeared to confirm that Kemron can reduce AIDS-related symptoms without discernible side effects. However, no patients in the new tests sero-converted.

The initial reports on the Kenyan experiments in February were met with skepticism or ignored by many scientists, who found it hard to believe that small doses of alpha interferon taken orally could produce results when large doses by injection did not.

Testing outside Africa has been delayed because of the difficulty in finding appropriate subjects. Patients often have a history of using other drugs that affect test results. "The good thing about the African study is that to our knowledge no patient over there had access to AZT," says Dr. Alan Richards, director of research at ACC in Amarillo.

Richards says studies will probably begin "in the near future" in Britain, the Philippines and Thailand. "We're waiting for a signature here, some papers there," he says.

In the United States, ACC has licensed Interferon Sciences (IS) of New Brunswick, New Jersey to test alpha interferon in collaboration with Dr. Joseph Hassett at Mount Sinai Medical Center in New York, according to Dr. Stanley Schutzbank, IS executive vice-president.

But because Kemron is now presumed to have positive effects on AIDS patients, testing it has become more complicated. "By the time most patients reach our hands they are in very advanced stages of the disease," Obel says. "Putting those patients on a placebo is in effect advancing them towards death."

Since the Mount Sinai tests involve giving placebos to a control group, Hassett is accepting only patients with AIDS-related complex (ARC), rather than patients with AIDS in advanced stages, because "ARC patients have more time," Schutzbank says. Results of the study should be available in September, he says.

SLAVERY AND SLAUGHTER:


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 188 words


Although slavery has been officially abolished in Mauritania three times in the country's history, there are still at least 100,000 black slaves serving Arab masters, according to a report issued by the human rights group Africa Watch.

The report claims that successive regimes have passed anti-slavery legislation to appease the international community, while pursuing policies to encourage slavery. The government has not released results of the last two censuses, which are suspected to indicate that the Arab ruling group now makes up less than 30 percent of the total population.

In Somalia, President Mohamed Siad Barre has released 45 political prisoners detained for allegedly distributing a manifesto calling for his removal. Barre also announced that multi-party elections will be held in February. Criticism of the government's human rights record intensified earlier this month after Somali security forces opened fire in a soccer stadium in Mogadishu, the capital, when crowds jeered the president. At least 100 people were shot dead or trampled in the resulting panic, according to Africa Watch.

PEACE PLAN:


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 230 words


With much of the work of identifying eligible voters completed, based on a 1974 Spanish census, preparations for a referendum on the future of Western Sahara appear to be moving forward. A United Nations technical mission led by Issa Diallo, special assistant to United Nations Secretary-General Javier Perez de Cuellar, is scheduled to visit the region in early August. The mission is part of the Secretary-General's attempt to establish a peace process that will end 15 years of war between Morocco and the Polisario Front, which is fighting for the independence of the former Spanish colony.

In a June 18th report to the Security Council, Perez de Cuellar outlined in detail the steps he believes must precede a free and fair election, including a cease-fire, completion of the census, the release of Saharan political prisoners and the return of Saharan refugees from neighboring countries. The report proposes civilian, military and security units for a United Nations Mission for the Referendum in Western Sahara, to be called Minurso. "It is clear," the report says, "that the United Nations operation in Western Sahara will be large and complicated."

Polisario has welcomed the Secretary-General's efforts to promote a settlement, while Moroccan Foreign Minister Abdellatif Filali has promised that his country will "seriously examine" the UN proposals.

McDougall Honored For Africa Work


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 127 words


Attorney Gay McDougall, director of the Southern Africa Project of the Lawyers' Committee for Civil Rights Under Law, last week won the Candace Award for international affairs from the National Coalition of 100 Black Women. Television journalist Charlayne Hunter-Gault of the MacNeil/Lehrer Newshour and South African musician Hugh Masekela made the presentation at the eighth annual Candace Awards ceremony in New York sponsored by AT&T. "Candace" is an ancient Ethiopian title meaning empress or queen.

One of ten winners in various fields, McDougall was cited for having founded the Commission on Independence for Namibia, which pressed for and monitored the peace process that resulted in Namibia's independence from South Africa on March 21st.

Africa News


July 23, 1990


FRAGILE UPTURN BESET BY PROBLEMS


LENGTH: 3408 words


When Togolese President Gnassingbe Eyadma visits the United States later this month to see President George Bush and to encourage U.S. investment in his country, he is certain to encounter some difficult questions.

A few weeks ago, Eyadma's ruling party, known as the Rally of the Togolese People (RPT), reaffirmed support for the current single-party political structure and warned that multi-party pressure could produce "unpredictable consequences."

In an interview last month in West Africa magazine, Eyadma said his government has been taking steps to democratize political life. In parliamentary elections in March, 230 candidates stood for 79 positions and several non-party members won seats. "This means democracy has taken root in our political life," the president said. 

Since taking power in a 1967 coup, Eyadma has survived at least three attempts to oust him from power by force. Though he faces no visible organized opposition, he may not be immune from the problems now confronting several of Africa's other long-term leaders. Christian Science Monitor correspondent Robert Press, one of the journalists visiting Togo with a UN-organized group, reported that "a growing number of Togolese are privately saying that stability has come at a high price."

A small west African nation of about three million residents, Togo is currently struggling to shore up its economy and establish a more attractive financial climate for investors.

During his visit to Washington, Eyadma will seek to keep the focus on Togo's economic potential. With a sound infrastructure, a modern airport and shipping port, modern transportation, banking and communication, the country hopes to interest U.S. businesses in making the infusion of capital needed to boost development.

Below, Ernest Harsch looks at Togo's economic prospects.

LOME--Often hailed as a forerunner in privatization and other market-oriented strategies for economic recovery, Togo is still striving to build a sound foundation for sustainable growth and development.

Togolese officials express satisfaction over their country's recent economic performance. "Since our country has implemented a policy of structural adjustment," says Planning Minister Barry Moussa Barque, "there has been real growth."

Yet this satisfaction has been tempered by recognition that the economic improvements have been fragile and uneven and that production and investment levels have not responded to Togo's economic reform program as quickly as expected. Moreover, worrying social costs have been incurred, marked by some deterioration in health, nutrition, education and employment.

Despite fulsome praise from its aid partners and despite repeated debt reschedulings, Togo has also found few improvements in its external economic environment. World market conditions remain unfavorable and international financial support has been less than expected. "The adjustment programs run so far have failed to generate an influx of foreign funds in keeping with our growth targets or our debt," says President Gnassingbe Eyadma.

Togo may be better endowed in terms of agricultural and mineral resources and certain infrastructure (such as a well-developed highway network) than a number of other African Least Developed Countries (LDCs). Its per capita Gross Domestic Product (GDP) in 1987 was $ 290 and its adult literacy rate is about 40%; both figures are somewhat above the LDC average. But the structural weaknesses of Togo's economy and its limited capacity for overcoming them are fundamentally little different. Manufacturing's contribution to total output -- one of the key LDC indicators -- is a mere 8.5%.

The tough economic reforms to which the government has committed itself aim first of all at overcoming the most immediate imbalances in its economic performance. But they also are designed to prepare the basis on which the country may eventually raise itself out of the "least developed" category.

Most analyses of Togo's slide into economic crisis see the beginnings, paradoxically, in the phosphate boom of 1974.

Before the first phosphate exports in 1961, the country had relied primarily on the sale of cocoa and coffee for its foreign exchange earnings. The development of phosphate mining provided an important addition to this limited export mix and soon became Togo's principal source of growth and revenue, accounting for 61% of export earnings by the early 1970s.

In late 1974, world phosphate prices quadrupled. Buoyed by a consequent doubling of its budgetary revenues the following year, the government embarked on an ambitious industrialization drive, sharply stepping up public investments and creating numerous state-owned enterprises, ranging from manufacturing to trade, marketing and banking institutions.

Phosphate prices soon plunged back down, but a boom in cocoa and coffee prices in 1975-76 kept the investment momentum rolling. The government also borrowed heavily, much of it on commercial terms.

This public investment drive soon proved unsustainable, in large part because export earnings fell back to pre-boom levels -- and then continued downward. With receipts well below anticipated figures, Togo began to incur large payments arrears on its external debt. Its current account balance, which had been slightly positive in 1970, was negative to the tune of $ 234 million by1978. Reflecting the commercial terms of much of Togo's borrowing, debt servicing costs rose sharply. Togo was obliged to seek repeated debt reschedulings.

Compounding these difficulties was the disappointing return on the large investments of the mid-to-late 1970s. Some projects had been launched hastily, without serious advance study of their costs or potential markets. Many were weighed down by high personnel costs or crippled by "bad management and corruption," President Eyadma later acknowledged.

Not all state enterprises were inefficient or became a drain on government resources. But Minister for Interior and State Enterprises Koffi Djondo emphasizes that many functioned poorly enough to lead to a "hemorrhage of our public finances."

Aarmed by the deteriorating situation, the Togolese government, as early as 1979, embarked on an initial series of stabilization measures. In 1982-83 it adopted a more rigorous structural adjustment program supported by the International Monetary Fund (IMF) and World Bank. With a fourth structural adjustment loan now under negotiation between Togo and the Bank, Togo's is one of the longer running adjustment processes in Africa.

Like other countries that use the CFA franc, which is pegged to the French franc, Togo was not in a position to employ one of the more common instruments of adjustment, currency devaluation. This meant that it had to rely to a greater extent on other adjustment measures.

Non-profitable state enterprises have been privatized or liquidated. The civil service was reduced (largely through attrition and early retirement) by about 2,000 employees. A salary freeze was in effect from 1983 to 1986, and then again from 1988 to 1989. On top of this, a 5% "solidarity" tax has been levied on all public and private sector employees since 1983. Non-productive sectors saw their budgetary allocations contract: between 1982 and 1985, education's share of total government expenditure fell from 22.9% to 11.7%, while health went from 6.1% to 3.6%.

There has been an increasing emphasis on diversification away from phosphates and on strengthening those sectors perceived as having the greatest potential for growth --agriculture and light industry. In the 1988-89 public investment program, the bulk of allocations were directed toward agriculture (35%) and infrastructure (45%).

Other measures were designed to strengthen the economy's traditional role as a regional warehouse. This included the expansion of facilities for goods transhipped through Togo from neighboring states, as well as a further liberalization of trade and banking regulations.

In terms of production levels and other macro-economic indicators, the results have been encouraging, though not spectacular. GDP has grown by an average 3.3% a year since 1985, though some government projections point to a disappointing rate of 2.5% for 1990.

The budget deficit was brought down modestly, from a ratio of 7.7% of GDP in 1983 to 6.3% last year (despite a sharp rise in 1987 and 1988). Thanks to increased economic activity, private consumption was able to rise an average of 7.6% a year between 1984 and 1989. Inflation has been reduced from an average of 13.2% in 1980-82 to an estimated 2.1% in 1989.

One of the most prominent features of Togo's adjustment process has been its emphasis on privatization.

Thus far 29 enterprises have been affected by the privatization program, which is one of the most ambitious on the continent, encompassing more than a third of all public-sector operations. The ultimate aim is to reduce state involvement to a minimum in most "non-strategic" economic activities.

Of the 29 enterprises, ten were simply liquidated, fourteen were fully or partially privatized, and agreements are yet to be concluded on five others. Although Togolese entrepreneurs have taken shares in a few of them, external investors have tended to play the dominant role.

Some of the privatized firms, after retooling, staff reductions and rationalization of their production lines, have fared quite well. The privatized steel enterprise, now called the Societe Togolaise de Siderurgie (STS), began turning a profit within a year of its opening in 1984 and has done so every year since -- although behind the protection of a 41% tariff. "I'm for tariffs," says STS's chief executive, the U.S. entrepreneur John Moore. "Industries in small countries need protection."

Some aid officials argue that the initial divestitures gave excessive advantages to the newly privatized firms and were not clearly prepared with a view to maximizing benefits to the Togolese economy as a whole. The immediate proceeds from the sale of assets were in many cases enough to cover only the severance pay of dismissed workers.

Togo's manufacturing sector consists of about 100 enterprises, but employs only 1% of the economically active population. Concentrated in the Lome area, it produces mainly for the domestic market -- a very small market, since Togo's population is only 3.4 million. Productivity is low and manufacturing activity is poorly integrated with the rest of the economy. About 90% of inputs are imported.

On the positive side, Togo has good transport and telecommunications links. Its physical infrastructure is to be strengthened further over the next few years through new investments in roads, electrical power and water supply.

Togo also has a relatively well-developed banking system. Nevertheless, Togolese entrepreneurs often complain about the high cost of credit, while small-scale operators find access to bank financing difficult.

Concern for small businesses is not limited to the formal sector. An Artisan Division has been created within the Ministry of Industry and State Enterprises to assist Togo's significant informal sector. "In many African countries the attitude is to just let the informal artisans go along on their own," says Mathey-Apossan Dossvi, head of the Artisan Division. "But they're a very big force, and we need to help them."

At the other end of the spectrum, the government is offering special incentives to induce foreign investors to set up new manufacturing operations in Togo. In late 1989, a law was passed defining the legal status for the establishment of Export Processing Zones. For firms exporting at least three-quarters of their output and reserving 80% of their jobs for Togolese, the plan offers a 10-year tax holiday, reduced electricity and telecommunications rates and exemption from import and export duties. The project is backed by the U.S. Overseas Private Investment Corporation.

One of the first companies to line up for the new export zones is John Moore's STS. He believes such generous incentives and reduced red tape are vital if Togo is to attract significant amounts of foreign investment. "Africa has got to sell itself," he says. "The world is not going to beat a path to its door."

Potentially the strongest and most dynamic sector of the Togolese economy is agriculture. It accounts for 30% of the GDP, 30% of export earnings, and three-quarters of the economically active population. Togo has escaped the severe climatic and ecological shock that have afflicted the Sahelian states. Nevertheless, agricultural output remains highly vulnerable to fluctuations in rainfall, given the general absence of irrigation and the low level of productivity.

The government's single most important step towards boosting production was to increase significantly the prices paid to producers for cocoa, coffee and cotton. Between the 1983-84 and 1986-87 crop years, producer prices for cocoa were increased by 31%, and for cotton by 50%.

The most spectacular response was in cotton output, with seed cotton production more than tripling between 1983 and 1989. Nearly half of Togo's approximately 260,000 smallholders are now cultivating cotton on at least part of their farms.

Coffee output has also increased, from 10,000 tons in 1985 to 14,700 tons in 1989. The cocoa sector, however, has not fared as well, falling from 16,500 tons in 1984 to 8,000 in 1989, despite the significant increase in the producer price over most of that period. This is partly due to the age of most cocoa trees. Output figures, however, are hard to interpret because a significant portion of cocoa and coffee marketed in Togo is believed to actually originate from Ghana through illicit cross-border trade.

The higher producer prices and greater marketed output for cotton are believed to have had a significant impact on the incomes of a sizeable portion of the rural population. The World Bank estimates that export farmers benefited from an average 97% increase in incomes (in real terms) between 1982 and 1986.

This progress is now being undermined, however, by the continuing collapse of world cocoa and coffee prices.

The Office des Produits Agricoles du Togo (OPAT), the country's main agricultural export agency, has been obliged to reduce producer prices over the past two seasons by 37.5% for cocoa and 56.25% for coffee. "We hope this situation changes soon," remarks OPAT Director Katanga Tcha. "If not, the peasants will abandon these products."

Overall, Togo's food situation is somewhat more favorable than in many other African countries. In non-drought years, Togolese farmers are able to meet the bulk of domestic food needs.

There is little room for complacency, however. Food production has been uneven, both geographically and by crop. The southern regions, where maize is more dominant, can often boast of surpluses. But in the north -- where climatic conditions are more unfavorable and where the production of staples like cassava and yams has stagnated -- deficits frequently occur.

Moreover, the food sector has not benefited from the kind of incentives accorded to export crops. Between 1985-88, investments in export-crop projects were twice as high as for integrated rural development projects. Nutritional studies have shown a particular decline in food production in the cotton-growing regions, as farmers shift toward the more profitable crop.

As part of the increased concerns over the social consequences of adjustment, new policies are being formulated to bolster food production. These are to include improvements in irrigation, the expansion of cultivated land areas, the development of animal traction, the extension of roads and marketing systems in the more isolated regions and support for peasant co-operatives and local community initiatives.

A difficult external environment adds to Togo's domestic problems. In recent years, Togo has been confronted with the all-too-familiar predicament of a rising debt burden and falling terms of trade. Earlier debt restructurings led to a bunching of repayments in the latter part of the 1980s, the same years when world prices for cocoa and coffee plunged most precipitously. In 1988 alone, the country suffered $ 40 million in losses as a result of lower commodity prices.

Although the trade situation has remained bleak, the past year brought a slight improvement on the financial front. The French government wrote off $ 145 million of bilateral debt. In June 1989, Togo was able to benefit from thenew debt rescheduling terms approved by the Toronto summit, when the Paris Club of official creditors agreed to a package that helped to reduce Togo's overall debt servicing burden from $ 155 million to $100 million. As a result, Togo's actual debt repayments in 1989 were some 27% of estimated export earnings, an improvement from the 39.1% ratio of two years earlier. Nevertheless, debt servicing was again set to rise significantly in 1990, to $ 124 million.

Projections by the Ministry of Planning and Mines suggest that Togo will need $ 556 million in new borrowing over the period 1990-95 to finance its investment plans. But even this level may be too low, since it assumes a significant increase in export receipts based in part on higher prices for phosphates, cocoa and coffee -- the same commodities that have been faring so poorly.

While a number of Togo's key economic and financial indicators have shown some improvement in recent years, and while certain groups have benefited from higher incomes -- among them export crop producers -- the upturn has yet to be felt in the living standards of major sectors of the Togolese population.

Most troubling, many of those with the lowest incomes and least access to social services and benefits have seen a further deterioration in their position as a result of Togo's economic difficulties and of the stringent measures adopted to address them.

This has spurred Togolese planners and international funding agencies to place a greater accent on measures to safeguard the more vulnerable layers of the population.

In education, Togo was able to achieve major gains in the 1970s, including an increase in the primary school enrollment rate from 40% to 72%. But between 1981 and 1985, as a result of the cuts in educational spending, the rate slipped back down to 55% of the school-age population before rising to 58% by 1987.

Health presents a similar picture. Following significant improvements in the 1970s, the crisis years of 1980-83 brought a sharp cut in real per capita health expenditures by 30%. Some cuts were subsequently restored, but by 1986, real spending per person was still one-fourth less than in 1980. Typically, rural areas suffered the greatest deterioration. Supplies of medicines and medical equipment fell drastically in many health clinics.

During the first half of the 1980s, unemployment had increased to an estimated 20-25% of those active in the formal sector. Government estimates indicate that by 1989, in addition to 2,000 positions trimmed from the civil service, 6,000 Togolese had lost jobs in state-owned enterprises, while another 10,000 were made jobless in the private sector. Few of the newly unemployed received any benefits or assistance in finding new jobs. Several new investment agreements, especially in the textile industry, promise to create thousands of additional jobs in the next few years. Yet overall, new job creation has proved slower than expected.

In the view of Planning Minister Barry Moussa Barque, Togo would have fared worse had it had not opted for the rigors of structural adjustment: "This liberalism will create more jobs than the state interventionism that we had before," Barque says

Nevertheless, concern is growing that further deterioration in Togolese living standards may spur social upheaval, as it has in several neighboring countries. "We needn't be surprised if in Africa we have social problems," remarks President Eyadma. "If people don't find food to eat, they will go out and demonstrate."

Seeking to address such concerns, the next structural adjustment program, Togo's fourth, is expected to place a major emphasis on the protection and strengthening of the social sector.

INTERNAL MISTAKES, INTERNAL CONSTRAINTS


LENGTH: 2189 words


Tanzania's economic reform program is threatened, says its president Ali Hassan Mwinyi, by an "unequal partnership in world trade."

During the 1970's and early 1980's, the east African country's terms of trade -- the value of its exports compared to its imports -- declined 30%. World market prices for Tanzania's traditional export crops, primarily coffee and tea, plummeted, while the cost of essential imports such as oil rose sharply. In addition, the volume as well as the value of Tanzanian exports fell -- 25% in just over a decade -- reflecting lower farm productivity and bottlenecks in transportation and marketing.

As a result of those combined factors, Tanzania's foreign earnings were slashed by more than half, while the cost of servicing the national debt rose to 50% of all export revenues. 

The financial crisis has meant a reversal in several areas where Tanzanians were proud of their progress. Although one of the world's poorest countries at independence in 1961, Tanzania managed to achieve growth rates averaging over 4% during the next decade and a half. It established schools and health clinics within the reach of almost every citizen, and adult literacy approached an extraordinary 90%.

Now, Tanzanians complain of schools without books or paper, clinics without medicines, village water taps that are dry, electrical systems that don't work and roads that have become impassable.

The problems have deepened, despite seven years of stringent economic reforms -- the first three under a Tanzanian-designed program and more recently under an Economic Recovery Program (ERP) mandated by the International Monetary Fund.

Tanzanian officials admit to missteps on the path to economic development. "We agree we mismanaged the economy," says Finance Minister Stephen Kibona.

One of the faults, according to government officials, was lip service to rural development. Despite much talk of agricultural production goals and building rural infrastructure, national budgets devoted a disproportionate share of resources to industrial development. "The biggest mistake we made," says Agriculture Minister Stephen Wassira, "was the disinvestment in agriculture."

Another problem was the growth of state-run enterprises, despite a lack of skilled administrators.

But those difficulties would have been far less serious, say Tanzanians, if the external economic environment had not become hostile towards poor, commodity-dependent countries.

In addition, Tanzania was strained by two international problems closer to home. In 1977, the breakup of the East African Community -- an economic union of Tanzania, Kenya and Uganda -- forced Tanzania to suddenly develop, at great expense, its own postal, telecommunications and transportation systems. The following year, Idi Amin in neighboring Uganda decided to annex a portion of western Tanzania, saying the border between the two countries should be the "natural boundary" formed by the Kagera River. Tanzania went to war to win back the territory.

Then came the world drop in commodity prices and the escalation of oil prices which left Tanzania spending a full 60% of its foreign earnings just to purchase fuel.

Tanzania's current crisis "is not so much a question of internal policy," insists President Ali Hassan Mwinyi. "It is the result of the unfavorable economic order."

"We produce cotton, tea, coffee, cashew nuts," Mwinyi says. "But the prices of these things are fixed by other people, not by us." At the same time, Mwinyi says, Tanzania has to accept whatever prices are demanded for the imports it buys.

In a conversation with journalists that covered a number of questions, Mwinyi said Tanzanians are free to adopt a multi-party system, although he characterized Tanzania's one party as inclusive and democratic. Asked whether Tanzanians will abandon socialism in light of the developments in Eastern Europe, the president said Tanzanian socialism is the concept called "ujamaa" in Swahili. It means communal living, he said, "which is translated as socialism for want of a better translation." In Tanzania, Mwinyi said, 95% of farming activity -- including commercial farming -- is in private hands, as is 80% of the transportation sector.

In his conversation with visiting journalists, President Ali Hassan Mwinyi was most concerned to explain Tanzania's views on economic recovery. Here are excerpts from what he said.

PRESIDENT ALI HASSAN MWINYI: I thought I might use this opportunity to emphasize some of our preoccupations in this country. Our main preoccupation, understandably, is to revitalize our economy and promote sustained development. Some of you, I'm sure, will recall that in the late '70s to mid-'80s, our economy was characterized by acute shortages of food, consumer goods, fuel, and foreign exchange, because of the declining production in both the agricultural sector and the industrial sector. Our GDP (Gross Domestic Product) had reached a negative growth rate of minus 1.6%.

As a result of that trend in our economy, we decided to enter into negotiations with the IMF. And in 1986 we reached an agreement on a three-year Economic Recovery Program, or ERP. The implementation of that Program has yielded some results, good ones too.

First, since then food production has increased remarkably. We have now moved from acute food shortages to self-sufficiency in our major foodstuffs, such as maize, beans, and, to a certain extent, rice. These are the major foodstuffs consumed in our country.

Second, our cash crop production has also increased substantially. Our major obstacle now is the persistent decline in the world prices of our export commodities. For example, the price of coffee dropped from L3,000 a ton just two or three years ago, to just about L600. You could work out the percentage. It is an enormous percentage. The price of cloves, which are produced in the island part of Tanzania, dropped from $ 9,000 per ton in 1982 to just $1,700 perton. The price of cotton has been fluctuating during the three years of our ERP period. Therefore the collapse of world commodity prices frustrates our efforts to increase our foreign exchange earnings.

Three, our industrial capacity utilization has improved considerably because of the increased availability of raw materials and spares. Industrial output increased by 4.2% in 1987 and by 5.4% in 1988. This increased production has reduced the shortage of industrial consumer goods in the country.

Four, the rate of inflation declined from 33% before the implementation of the Economic Recovery Program to an average of 23% in 1989.

Five, in view of the increased production in the agricultural and industrial sectors, our GDP has improved from its negative trend to an encouraging, positive rate of growth. In the first year of the ERP, our GDP growth was 3.6%. In the second it rose to 3.9%. And last year it grew to 4.1%. So there is an obvious upward trend. This is a remarkable improvement, expecially when one considers that the rate of population growth in Tanzania is 2.8%.

But I acknowledge that great challenges still lie ahead of us and we still have enormous economic problems, despite these successes.

During the first phase of the ERP, we had to put great emphasis on increased production in order to reduce the acute shortages and foreign exchange. It was not possible at emphasis on the rehabilitation of the basic social services, because of our limited capability.

As a result of that, the essential social services in the country sharply deteriorated. We have schools without adequate numbers of desks. We have dispensaries and health centers and even hospitals without adequate provision of drugs. Only 50% of the population enjoy clean water. We had planned to do more.

So we have adopted a second phase of the Economic Recovery Program. This one is called the Economic and Social Action Program, which puts greater emphasis on the rehabilitation and improvement of essential social services such as education, health, and clean water. In this respect, we hope to receive ample support from our friends in our determination to improve the economic and social welfare of our people.

Having talked about the success aspect of our efforts in economic recovery, we should also mention something about the after-effects of our efforts to improve the economy.

Some of the IMF conditionalities have not been yielding good results. And the effect has been somewhat counterproductive.

For example, one major conditionality of the IMF is that a country should devalue its currency massively, not just a little, but massively. In our case, we devalued from 17 shillings to the dollar in 1986 to now nearly 200 shillings to the dollar. This means we have devalued to the tune of 900%.

In a country like ours, a developing country, which gets all her needs from imports, and in this case where ¢the prices of/ imports have increased by 900%, the result, as I said, can be somewhat counterproductive.

Because what happened? The prices of fertilizers became beyond the reach of our people. The prices of tractors, which are essential for farming, for production, have also become beyond the reach of our people. The price of imported trucks have also become unaffordable to our people. And these are the most essential tools for production.

And if improvement in the economy needs production, then production here is being stifled by prices which we cannot afford because of this massive devaluation, which was one of the conditions necessary so that the IMF would listen to us.

As you know, production requires transportation. You have to transport what you produce. About 80% of the transport in our country is in the hands of private people. These people used to buy a truck, a new truck, for 500,000 Tanzanian shillings. Now the same truck costs 6 million shillings. This is beyond the reach of our ordinary transporters. We are obviously heading toward a standstill. There will come a time when these trucks and all these means of transport will not be repairable, and so they fall by the wayside.

So whilst our production is increasing, our capability for transport is decreasing. And this is one of the aftereffects of the massive devaluation insisted on by the IMF.

Also, devaluation endangers the real incomes of our people. They sharply reduce purchasing power. And as a result of this you have the usual social effects of embezzlement, corruption, theft, simply to make ends meet. Not necessarily that these people are bad people, but their monthly earnings have been so much eroded that they cannot meet their basic needs for 30 days. So they spend their salary in six days, seven days, eight days, ten days. Then they have got to do something to make sure they survive until the end of the month when they get another.

Another ill-effect of the conditionalities of the IMF is the insistence on high interest rates, in our case of 30%. The idea was that we have inflation in the country because there's a lot of money floating in the hands of the population -- so do something to suck in this money. One way of doing it is to give an incentive, raise the interest rate to 30% so that people will think, "Well, if I can get 30% with my money why should I bother to find other activities?"

Well, our experience is that this is very well in Europe, this is true in developed countries. But in our country, this does not work. It does not work to the interests of the country, for various reasons.

In the first place, the banks are far between. We have banks in Dar es Salaam. The next place you get banks is in Morogoro, 120 miles away. After Morogoro, you find banks in Dodoma, 300 kilometers away. Which peasant in his senses would risk to walk miles to the nearest bank, carrying his money with him, to deposit it in order to get 30% interest?

This condition was all right in 1945 Germany, during the Marshall Plan. Germany had everything. All infrastructure was there. But here in Tanzania this is not the case. The interest rates are high, and yet very few people deposit their money in the banks because the banks are not there.

At the same time, we encourage private people, cooperatives, and certain crop-purchasing authorities to buy all the crops that we have asked our population to produce. We say that nobody should produce and then find themselves at a loss to find a market. But these people, these crop-buying authorities, have no money of their own. They have to go to the bank to borrow, at 30%, in order to buy the crops from the farmers. But when they have bought these crops, as I said earlier, transport is difficult. They keep hanging on to what they have bought, while the interest clock, if I can put it that way, ticks. So this man holds on to what he has bought for three, four, five, six months, with the money he has borrowed at 30%.

These are some of the problems that we have encountered in the course of the implementation of the ERP Program, that we have had with our friend, the IMF.

A TALE OF TWO COUNTRIES


SECTION: ECONOMY


LENGTH: 844 words


The former British colony of Tanzania on Africa's Indian Ocean coast and the former French colony of Togo on the Atlantic side of the continent are more than 2,000 miles apart. Their histories, cultures and political systems are radically different. But in the 1990s they face similar -- and daunting -- challenges. Both are classified by the United Nations as Least Developed Countries (LDCs), a roster of the world's poorest nations. Tanzania has been on the list since its independence in 1961, while Togo toppled onto it in 1982 -- one of several African countries to be reclassified during the last decade.

Like most of their African neighbors, Togo and Tanzania have economies that were built around the production of a limited number of export crops. The practice was introduced by colonial powers and was intensified after independence by African governments themselves. Relying on the advice of international financial experts, African countries borrowed money for ambitious development schemes based on the anticipated earnings of their commodity exports. 

When commodity prices plummeted in the mid-1970s while interest rates rose, many countries entered a cycle of debt and economic crisis from which they have been unable to escape.

For several years now, both Tanzania and Togo have been implementing economic austerity and reform programs supported by the International Monetary Fund (IMF) and the World Bank. The international financial institutions believe that their formulas offer Africa its only hope for economic recovery and development. The IMF wants African countries to trim bloated bureaucracies, discourage costly imports and stimulate the private sector. At the same time, though, on the theory of "comparative advantage," the international institutions are encouraging the continuing expansion of commodity production as the best method of earning foreign exchange for most countries.

Growing numbers of African economists, on the other hand, say that African countries must devise reform schemes that emphasize feeding their people over export earnings and that diversify production away from a few primary commodities. They also argue for plans that devote a larger percentage of resources to social programs and that measure progress by improvements in the quality of life as well as by such statistical indicators as the rate of economic growth. In addition, they say, there must be relief from the crushing burden of debt.

The World Bank has given some of its critics hope that it has heard their pleas for increased focus on the effects of reform policies on the poor. On July 16, the Bank issued a study called Poverty (see publications list, page 13, for ordering information). The study says donors should allocate aid based on the extent to which borrowing governments convincingly demonstrate a commitment to reducing poverty.

On Africa, though, the report is grim. In an introduction, World Bank President Barber Conable says that reducing the number of the world's poor by 400 million people is a feasible goal for this decade but predicts that poverty in sub-Saharan Africa will continue to increase. For the African region, the goal is "containing the number of poor."

John Sewell, president of the Overseas Development Council, welcomed the report as "putting poverty back on the political agenda." More cautiously, the Friends Committee on National Legislation, a Quaker group, joined the aid agency CARE to issue a statement warning that the lending arms of the Bank and the IMF might ignore the new emphasis. The groups say the Bank still "appears blind to the need to boost self-reliance in developing countries." Still, the two groups say the report offers a "glimmer of hope."

But poor countries are increasingly fearful that their problems will get less and less attention in a world whose attention is turning towards Europe. Those fears appeared justified earlier this month at the economic summit of Western nations and Japan in Houston, Texas, where Third World issues got scant attention.

In response, development specialists are making various efforts to insure that those issues do get considered. In Houston, a conference called The Other Economic Summit gathered economists and development workers from around the world in an attempt to call attention to a broader range of issues than the heads of government were considering. The participants planned a series of follow-up activities.

In the continuing debate, the question of whether or not existing structural adjustment programs are working has become a critical one for both supporters and critics of IMF-style reforms. A group of international journalists recently explored that question on a visit to Tanzania and Togo organized by the United Nations. Following are excerpts from an interview the journalists had with the Tanzanian president and from an article on Togo that appears in the current issue of the United Nations publication Africa Recovery by staff writer Ernest Harsch, who was on the trip.

NAMIBIA'S FREEDOMS ARE SECURE


LENGTH: 813 words


Excerpts from an Africa News interview with President Sam Nujoma.

Q: Namibia is said by many constitutional scholars to have some of the best protections for civil liberties of any country. Can you imagine any circumstances under which you might feel the need to suspend these rights?

A: The President is required to take such actions if there is a threat to freedom of the people and the territorial integrity of Namibia -- being invaded by a foreign power. But at the present moment I do not foresee such a threat at all. 

We suffered under colonial occupation for the last 106 years. As we were struggling, we believed that the people must have the right to self-determination. They must have freedom of speech, freedom of assembly, freedom to determine whom they would like to elect as their leaders. This we leave to the people. This is precisely why the people of Namibia went into war, in order to restore their legitimate rights.

Q: Are you willing to have an independent press which can closely scrutinize the government?

A: We welcome the press to report objectively, and it is the right of Namibian citizens to know what is happening in the country. The press is there to report to the people.

Q: How do you feel about the provision that limits a president to serving two terms?

A: We feel that other Namibian citizens should be given the opportunity to participate and to contribute to the country -- and not just one man for a long time.

Q: How do you plan to implement the constitutional mandate for equality of the sexes?

A: First of all we have to ensure that women attend schools right from childhood up to the university level -- and also ensure that there will be equal pay for equal jobs and equal treatment in all spheres of life. And we have to ensure that women are attracted to participate in all the activities and the organs of the state, as well as in public life.

Q: Have you dealt adequately with the issue of the detainees Swapo held during the war?

A: Well, yes. The opposition claimed that we have detainees in foreign countries. We do not. Swapo has no detainees any longer. We have students -- particularly the children under the age of 18 who could not participate in the elections -- in our schools in Zambia, and we have foreigners who are teachers and doctors at those hospitals, but we have no more detainees. And I believe we will discuss this as we discuss other issues that are affecting our nation.

Q: But many people in this country still want to know why Swapo detained its members and apparently tortured some of them.

A: During the war, the South African government recruited our own people into its war machinery -- into Koevoet ¢a paramilitary police unit known for its brutality/ and SWATF ¢the South West African Territorial Force, a military unit set up by the South African army/. As a result of the actions of our own people who were recruited by the enemy, thousands of Namibians were massacred. In the massacre at Kassinga in 1978, more than a thousand people were killed and maimed.

So we were bound to take action in order to protect our people. Had the enemy agents succeeded, there could have been no freedom and independence in Namibia today.

Now, we have created a unified national army, composed of ex-Koevoet, ex-SWATF -- despite the fact that they have committed crimes. Some of our people are very angry that Swapo is "selling out" by integrating these elements who have been killing the people during the war.

Q: In light of those emotions, how are you progressing towards your goal of "national reconciliation"?

A: It's going very well. With the difficulties that are there -- the bitterness -- the wounds are still fresh. As you are aware, there are many who lost dear ones. Some villages have been completely wiped out by the army.

But our policy is to close and leave behind this sad history and to start a new page of history, where we have to forge ahead with immediate burning issues -- to build schools and hospitals, to embark upon economic projects such as agriculture in order to produce adequate food for the population. We can't be looking backwards, we have to move forwards.

Q: Namibia already has an economically privileged white population. Are you at all worried that you might develop, as other nations have, a privileged elite alongside a poor majority?

A: Precisely. We are aware of that. That's why our priority in economic development is agriculture. The main aim is to produce food for our population. And the secondary is to provide clean water for the population, as well as to provide education and health and housing. We have learned in the struggle that our responsibility is not to enjoy the fruit of independence, but our duty and responsibility is to build a stronger foundation for the future generations.

BUSH TO NUJOMA: 'WE LIKE YOUR DEMOCRACY'


LENGTH: 1767 words


NEW YORK--Three months after their country's long-awaited independence, Namibians are facing the same problem they confronted during decades of campaigning for freedom -- getting attention on a world stage dominated by better known actors.

When President Sam Nujoma, Sam;ujoma's first American visit as head of state was announced last month, many U.S. friends of Namibia were fearful that it would be completely overshadowed by Nelson Mandela, Nelson;andela's U.S. tour.

But Nujoma's one-week journey netted Namibia tangible results despite a paucity of press coverage. The 61-year-old leader held what he termed a "very fruitful" meeting with President George Bush, George;.i.Bush, George;ush, signed an important bilateral agreement and met with many of the top policy and opinion makers in Washington. And he witnessed governments from around the world make commitments of more than $ 200 million to Namibia's development needsduring a two-day donors' pledging conference at the United Nations. 

In an Oval Office meeting with President George Bush and other U.S. officials, Nujoma praised American support for the United Nations-sponsored independence process. Nujoma says he briefed Bush "on how busy we are establishing a democratic society."

Bush responded by congratulating the Namibian president on his "pragmatic" political and economic policies, according to Herman Co.i.Cohen, Herman Cohen, Herman;hen, the assistant secretary of State for Africa, who took part in the half-hour talk. Secretary of State James Baker, who led the American delegation to the independence celebrations, also attended the meeting, as did national security advisor Brent Scowcroft, his deputy, Robert Gates, and several other State Department officials.

The high-level presence -- unusual for such a session -- was a measure of the kind of interest that has been sparked by Namibia's moves towards democratization. "Around here Nujoma was regarded as a somewhat inept leader of a rag-tag army," one U.S. official said. "There were plenty of people predicting that Namibia would turn out to be just another failed African leftist dictatorship." Now that Nujoma has emerged as a statesman representing democracy and economic pluralism, the official said, "they just wanted to get a look at the guy."

On the day of the meeting, the White House announced the selection of the first ambassador to Namibia, Genta Hawkins Holmes, Genta Hawkins, currently the second ranking U.S. diplomat in South Africa. Nujoma announced that Namibia will open an embassy in Washington as soon as arrangements can be completed.

Throughout the U.S. trip, Nujoma stressed his theme that Namibians are determined to work hard and to sacrifice --"even to eat only once a day"-- to insure that future generations "do not have to suffer the same consequences we suffered under South African colonial domination." Because of the colonial experience, he said, Namibia is among the least developed countries in the world.

"While we seek international assistance, we do not want to become perpetually dependent on foreign aid," he said in several appearances. Namibia is particularly eager, Nujoma said, to gain self-sufficiency in food production.

"The central features of my government's policy are the consolidation of independence, national reconciliation, and the fostering of peace, unity, economic and social well-being for all our people," he said.

Nujoma was upbeat during his visit, and he was clearly pleased by the response to his appeal for assistance and foreign investment. "We are leaving prepared to go and embark on agricultural projects, water development, education and health, and better houses," he said in an interview near the end of his stay.

After 30 years at the helm of the independence movement Swapo (South West Africa People's Organization), Nujoma has grown accustomed to making a case for Namibia's significance. On his most recent previous U.S. visit -- a four-city tour in mid-1988 -- he issued an appeal that Americans "don't forget Namibia."

This time, his reiterated request not to overlook his country was bolstered by his position as head of new democracy. His appeal also got a boost from Mandela, who on several occasions cited Namibia as an example of a working non-racial successor to an apartheid regime.

On Capitol Hill, Nujoma held separate meetings with House and Senate members. He thanked legislators who "championed the cause" of Namibian independence and expressed appreciation for recent efforts to boost foreign aid to his country. The U.S., which had only planned to give Namibia about $ 500,000in assistance this year, has now committed $ 10 million as a result of Congressional action taken prior to Nujoma's arrival.

Nujoma attracted a good turnout at all of his Washington appearances. The auditorium at the Carnegie Endowment for International Peace was filled to near-capacity for his off-the-record talk, and there were more people than places for the dinner and question session which followed.

At the Center for Strategic and International Studies (CSIS), another large gathering of African specialists from government, journalism, and academia asked Nujoma and his delegation about a wide range of issues -- from the economy to the ecology of the country.

Questioners at the two Washington think-tank sessions zeroed in on various provisions of the Namibian constitution which offer guarantees for a range of personal freedoms similar to the American Bill of Rights. At Carnegie, Nujoma promised that civil liberties will not be abridged in Namibia as they have in many other countries. At CSIS, he said the clause granting equal rights to women will result in opening opportunities for women "in all aspects of national development."

One participant in the CSIS meeting, Laurie Marker-Kraus from the Smithsonian Institution's National Zoological Park, presented Nujoma with a silver cheetah pin in recognition of Namibia's constitutional protections for wildlife. Namibia has the largest population of cheetahs in the world and holds the key to their survival as a species, she said.

The president was repeatedly queried about his economic policies and specifically about nationalization. "Our constitution does not provide for wholesale nationalization," he said. But if the parliament at some point decided some takeovers were required, he said, "adequate compensation will be paid."

He emphasized Namibia's commitment to a "dynamic role" for the private sector, which he called "the engine for growth and prosperity." The government's role will be limited to involvement in a few key sectors -- agriculture, education, health and housing, he said.

While in Washington, Nujoma signed an agreement with the Overseas Private Investment Corporation (OPIC), a U.S. government agency which gives backing to American firms doing business abroad. OPIC provides direct loans, loan guarantees and offers insurance against the risk of political violence. "We don't assure investors that they will earn a profit," said James D. Berg, acting president of the agency, "but in partnership with national governments, OPIC assures that risks be minimized." Nujoma said he hoped the agreement would "consolidate the friendship" between the two countries.

In an interview, Nujoma said he was particularly encouraged by the outcome of the two-day UN donors' pledging conference, which he termed "a resounding success." With over $ 200 million committed from governments and international agencies, Namibia will be able to begin many of its development schemes, he said.

According to Finance Minister Otto Herrigel, the pledges were "more than we expected." It is "extremely encouraging," he said, "to see the solidarity shown by many of our friends and even by some who haven't been friends before."

As a group, the Nordic countries (Denmark, Finland, Iceland, Norway, and Sweden) made the largest pledge at the UN conference -- about $ 60 million for this year, all on a grant basis. Two other European governments made substantial three-year commitments. The West Germans pledged about $ 60 million,while the British pledged about $ 17 million. (However, the German contribution may turn out to be substantially less, since it includes a large loan, and Namibia is seeking to avoid additional indebtedness.)

South Africa offered $ 10 million, while Nigeria and Egypt said they would contribute $ 1 million each.

The Namibian team presented to the donors conference a general policy statement on the reconstruction and development of the country. According to National Planning Commission Director General Zedekia Ngavirue, Namibia needs about $ 200 million a year to implement the development plan, plus some $70 million annually to offset a budget deficit.

The pledges received represent "an impressive beginning" towards that $ 810 million three-year figure, Foreign Minister Theo-Ben Gurirab said in an interview. "We did not really expect that we would emerge at the end of this pledging conference with the whole amount that we will need for the next three years, but we are greatly encouraged by the outcome," he said.

Nujoma appealed to private investors as well as governments while in the U.S. He announced plans to hold a conference for foreign business interests in Windhoek in the next few months.

In an interview on Cable News Network, the president said he hoped that Namibia's rich mineral wealth and prospects for oil would interest American investors. "The Namibian government is creating an environment conducive for private investment," he said in another public appearance.

According to one prominent American businessman, Maurice Tempelsman, Namibia has already gone a long way towards creating "a good atmosphere." Although Tempelsman said it was "a bit early" to expect specific plans to emerge, "everything that has been done up to the present time has enhanced the environment in which the private sector likes to operate." He cited the new investment code now being drawn up by the government and the conclusion of the OPIC treaty.

Tempelsman, a diamond merchant with extensive business involvement in Africa, is chairman of the African-American Institute in New York. He hosted a dinner for the Namibian president with members of two large business organizations to discuss the country's economic plans. Both Templesman's firm and Rossing Uranium Company, a British-owned mining concern, provided logistical assistance to the Namibians for the president's visit.

WHEN THE GOING GETS TOUGH..

Liberia and Kenya, both traditional American allies currently suffering image problems, have engaged Washington consultants who are assisting with their current crises. At the end of May, Liberia signed an $ 800,000, 19-month contract with van Kloberg and Associates, which also represents Zaire and Nigeria. The firm received an up-front payment of $ 150,000 and is scheduled to receive a like amount on October 1, plus $ 500,000 in two installments next year.
