Africa News


July 23, 1990


DOE RETRENCHES, MOI FADES


LENGTH: 1726 words


Although African heads of state at this month's annual meeting of the Organization of African Unity acknowledged the need for accountable government, pro-democracy advocates are facing tough going in many African nations. In Kenya, this month's arrest of government critics is the largest crackdown on opponents since President Daniel arap Moi came to power in 1979.

Across the continent in Liberia, six months of fighting have failed to dislodge strongman president Samuel K. Doe. But last week, plans for a meeting of all Liberia's leading political forces gave rise to hopes that an interim government might be established -- if Doe can be persuaded or forced to relinquish control.

The effort was spearheaded by a group of about 100 Liberians exiled in neighboring Sierra Leone who called for a meeting of representatives of "all existing parties" and of Liberians now living in various foreign countries. The ultimate aim, the statement says, is "the speedy establishment of multiparty democracy through free and fair elections." In the meantime, the exiles say, the representatives should establish an interim government to oversee repatriation of displaced Liberians and organize the voting. 

The statement, issued in the Sierra Leone capital Freetown, where negotiations aimed at ending the Liberian conflict have been taking place, offers an alternative to a direct assumption of power by the rebel forces headed by Charles Taylor, which have been fighting to oust Doe since late last December.

The Freetown statement, co-signed by the leaders of several Liberian political groups and quickly endorsed by senior religious figures, was distributed during an apparent stalemate in the talks between the government and Taylor's National Patriotic Front, whose forces are in control of large parts of Liberia and are poised on the outskirts of the capital, Monrovia.

The Sierra Leone group calls upon Doe "to make good his promise to the Liberian people that he would step aside should they ask him to do so." The interim government would be headed by the Patriotic Front, it says, but would include members of every other party and exile group.

"This is something on which we can now build," says Ellen Johnson-Sirleaf, a prominent exiled Liberian leader and former Cabinet minister. "If this gets off the ground, it will put pressure on Doe to resign," she suggested in an interview.

The plan for an all-party meeting is the first indication of possible movement in the current stand-off, which has seen Taylor's forces march to the capital with little resistance, only to stall at the city's edge.

Whether the rebels have been unable or unwilling to make a final assault against Doe is unclear. There are reports of a split in the ranks, and there have been suggestions that Taylor fears a reaction from the United States if his forces invade the capital. Taylor is said by some associates to believe that Washington might stage a Panama-type action, using Marines now stationed on U.S. naval vessels that have been deployed off Liberia's coast for a potential evacuation of Americans.

Liberia is host for several strategic U.S. installations, which handle diplomatic and intelligence traffic throughout Africa and guide U.S. shipping in the Atlantic. There is also a key Voice of America transmitter outside Monrovia.

A lull in the fighting early last week led to a large-scale exodus from Monrovia, where an increasing but undetermined number of residents have been injured or killed by government soldiers. But most Monrovians remain trapped in the city, with water and food in short supply, electrical service erratic, and telephones out of commission.

Suffering has also been on the rise in several refugee settlements as food and water have been exhausted, according to accounts from relief workers in Guinea, where many of the uprooted Liberians have fled.

The Freetown statement appeals to the Patriotic Front "to strictly observe due respect for human rights and civil liberty" and to avoid acts of vengeance and summary executions. A similar plea has been broadcast by Liberian religious leaders, who have called on both the government and the rebels to stop the bloodshed. Many Liberians are worried that a rebel takeover could lead to the installation of another authoritarian regime, although the Patriotic Front has promised to hold elections after taking control of the country.

An interfaith committee of both Christians and Muslims has been trying to negotiate an end to the conflict and to insure the beginning of a democratic process. Mediation efforts by the committee and by the Economic Community of West African States (Ecowas) faltered after Doe refused to give up power. The Patriotic Front is holding to the demand for his ouster as a precondition for peace.

The apparent breakthrough came last Wednesday when Tom Woewiyu, the Front's representative in the peace talks, returned to Sierra Leone from Liberia with word that rebel leader Taylor had accepted the terms of the Freetown statement, which also won the endorsement of religious leaders.

If the parties can agree on an interim administration whose representatives are drawn from all major sectors of society, Liberian exiles say, the chances for a peaceful resolution will increase.

Meanwhile, in Kenya, President Moi is coming under increasing pressure to open up the political process.

Anglican, Presbyterian and Methodist church leaders have asked the president "as a matter of extreme urgency" to create a "mechanism through which Kenyans from all walks of life will discuss, consult, listen to one another." The appeal came in a July 14 statement that also condemned the recent violence and promised to "uphold the president with ceaseless prayers."

The following day, sermons throughout the country addressed the current crisis, and one prominent Anglican bishop, Henry Okullu, said it was time for Kenyans "to elect a new and proper government." Moi responded with renewed threats against church leaders.

Under Moi's administration, the ruling Kenya African National Union (Kanu) has grown increasingly powerful -- and since 1982 has been the only political party legally allowed to operate.

Public protests against continued one-party rule resulted in 20 deaths earlier this month, according to the official toll. At least 28 government opponents have been detained, including two former Cabinet ministers, Kenneth Matiba and Charles Rubia.

Moi has blamed the current troubles on disgruntled Kikuyus -- members of the country's largest ethnic group which dominated the government under Moi's predecessor, Jomo Kenyatta. But protests against the government have taken place in many parts of the country, and several non-Kikuyu's are among the detainees.

Continued resistance to pluralism is likely to cost Kenya economically. The U.S. has criticized recent government actions, and a joint declaration by the Nordic countries condemned what they called the "brutal" government response to its critics. The five countries -- Denmark, Finland, Iceland, Norway, and Sweden -- said they will "reconsider their economic assistance" of about $ 80 million a year "if the present political situation continues."

In Washington, several members of Congress have asked the Bush administration to "immediately freeze current assistance" and to "reconsider" the request for fiscal year 1991. U.S. aid this years totals $ 46 million, including $ 10 million in military assistance.

The Congressional appeal was made in a July 12 letter signed by five Democrats -- House Majority Whip William Gray, House Foreign Affairs Chairman Dante Fascell, the House and Senate Africa subcommittee chairmen, Howard Wolpe and Paul Simon, and Gus Yatron, who chairs the House subcommittee on human rights.

Although most of this year's aid money already has been dispensed to Kenya, the letter says there is $ 8 million in unobligated development funds and$ 5 million in recently released military aid that should be held up "until there is a marked improvement in the human rights situation."

An earlier letter from Senate Foreign Relations Committee Chairman Claiborne Pell asked Secretary of State James Baker III to make clear that relations between Kenya and the United States "will deteriorate unless significant steps are taken to reinstitute respect for basic human rights."

The pressure to cut off American assistance was boosted by the arrival in Washington last week of Gibson Kamau Kuria, a prominent Kenyan human rights lawyer who was allowed to leave the country after seeking refuge in the American embassy to avoid arrest. In meetings on Capitol Hill, Kuria said that outsiders currently hold the key to change in Kenya, because the government has steadily eroded constitutional protections of dissent for its own citizens.

In addition to the multiparty campaigning by prominent Kenyan politicians, the government faces a less tangible threat from an underground movement that has waged a grass roots anti-Moi crusade.

In May, a group called Mwakenya -- the major underground force -- distributed leaflets throughout the country calling for increased pressure on Moi to resign. This month, a seven-page Mwakenya statement on the current situation was released in London by the well-known Kenyan novelist and playwright, Ngugi wa Thiong'o. "Moi and Kanu must go," the statement says. The group wants to see parliament dissolved, a new constitution written, and multiparty elections held.

Although a long-time government critic, Ngugi only recently became publicly identified with Mwakenya, whose other members remain clandestine -- most of them inside Kenya, he said in an interview. With himself as spokesperson, the movement can speak out more forcefully -- and defend itself against "Moi's accusations and lies," said the writer, who teaches part time at Yale University and lives in exile in London.

The time is right, Ngugi said, to build a broad coalition with church leaders, lawyers, intellectuals, and Kenyans from all backgrounds to force Moi from office.

Most analysts expect Moi to hold on to his position for the foreseeable future. He is said to be considering several reforms, including a restructuring of Kanu to allow greater participation and debate.

SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 96 words


African nations are beginning to add up the bill for higher oil prices, as the crisis in the Persian Gulf continues. In Zimbabwe, fuel products have already risen in price by as much as 65%. On Africa's northern tier, the conflict has split Morocco, which has sent troops to Saudi Arabia, from its partners in the Maghreb Arab Union -- Tunisia, Algeria, Mauritania and Libya -- who have not. Senegal is the first sub-Saharan nation to contribute troops to the anti-Iraq forces.

BLESS THIS HOUSE


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 209 words


After months of controversy among his advisors over whether or not he should do it, Pope John Paul last week consecrated Our Lady of Peace Basilica in Yamoussoukro, Cote d'Ivoire. About 10,000 people were inside the lavish church for the dedication service, while another 80,000 were in the courtyard, built to resemble St. Peter's Square in Rome. The structure, with its marble floors and columns, hand-made stained-glass windows and mahogany pews cost an estimated $ 200 million.

The Polish pope thanked Cote d'Ivoire President Felix Houphouet-Boigny, who is said to have used his own funds to build the church, and the pontiff said he would return to bless the hospital that is to be built in the Basilica complex. Vatican officials said earlier, privately, that the Pope had decided to dedicate the church, only after extracting Houphouet-Boigny's pledge to build both a hospital and a social services center on the grounds.

Security for the ceremonies was tight, as the government feared the dedication might become a focus for the pro-democracy protests that have marked life in Cote d'Ivoire since last spring. Critics had said such expenditure on a church was scandalous in a poor country that is experiencing an economic crisis.

LUANDA LINK-UP


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 205 words


Angolan President Jose Eduardo dos Santos had an unusual meeting with members of Congress last Thursday -- a two-way video conference via satellite. The teleconference was hosted by two House Democrats, Ronald Dellums (CA) who chairs the Congressional Black Caucus and Howard Wolpe (MI) who chairs the subcommittee on Africa, along with Republican John Kasich of Ohio. 

The session, characterized as a "frank exchange of views" -- a description that usually means a heated disagreement -- was arranged by the law firm of Washington, Perito, and Dubuc, which represents the Angolan government in Washington, D.C. The U.S. government has withheld recognition from Luanda since Angola became independent from Portugal in 1975. The Reagan and Bush administrations have backed the Angolan rebel movement Unita with officially covert, but widely acknowledged, military aid.

Last Wednesday, the House Intelligence Committee, in secret session, is reported to have defeated by two votes an attempt by several Democrats to reduce the covert aid package for fiscal year 1991 or to attach conditions to it. The administration's proposed aid increase for Unita is now expected to be debated by the full House later this year.

DEBATING DEMOCRACY


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 359 words


Ghana's district assemblies are responding angrily to criticism leveled at them from the Movement for Freedom and Justice (MFJ), a group that was unveiled at an August 1st press conference. Headed by historian Adu Boahen, the MFJ says its goal is a multi-party democracy to replace the nine-year-old government of the Provisional National Defense Council (PNDC). The MFJ charges that public discussions are impossible in Ghana because of an "atmosphere of fear, suspicion and intimidation" caused by curbs on free expression. And the group says Ghana's district assemblies, a third of whose members are government appointed, have failed to fulfill their promise as representatives of popular opinion. 

The Accra assembly called the MFJ statement "arrogant" and the Bawku West assembly said the district structures were "the most democratic in Ghana's history."

The assemblies were elected last year as part of a PNDC plan to reintroduce democratic structures in Ghana at local and regional levels before holding national elections. Ghana's head of state, Jerry Rawlings, whose popularly-supported 1981 coup overthrew an ineffective and unpopular elected government, says the country's negative experiences at the ballot box shows the need for strong grassroots structures.

But in the last year, doubts about the PNDC commitment to democracy have been growing, as has impatience with the slow pace of change. Although the leadership of the Trade Union Congress supports PNDC policies, at its last congress in 1988, the union federation called for a multi-party system and for repeal of laws allowing arbitrary government action against critics. Ghana's Catholic bishops have recently called for "free, frank and open discussion" on national issues and demanded the unbanning of the church newspaper, the Standard, which was closed six years ago. And after the MFJ announced its existence, officials of the National Union of Ghana Students told Africa News they will press the PNDC to develop a specific program to restore a multi-party system and to effect its own retirement from the scene. --with files from Kwame Karikari in Accra

SINKING LIFELINE


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 195 words


Operation Lifeline Sudan, a United Nations-sponsored agency formed to get food to starving Sudanese, is on the brink of collapse, warns Roger Winter, director of the United States Committee for Refugees. Lifeline was mandated to secure the agreement of both the Sudanese government of Lieutenant General Omar Hassan al-Bashir and the Sudan People's Liberation Movement (SPLM) that is fighting his rule to allow neutral humanitarian aid into all areas of the country. 

But Winter, just back from the Horn of Africa, says Lifeline's work has been compromised by government attempts to deny food to rebel areas -- and by the acquiescence of Lifeline officials in what Winter calls government "manipulation." Winter cites recent Lifeline efforts to curtail the work of relief organizations that operate outside the Lifeline umbrella.

The SPLM is on the verge, Winter says, of withdrawing its cooperation from Lifeline efforts, although there is hope that a newly-appointed Lifeline coordinator, Per Janvid of Sweden, can re-establish good relations. SPLM leader John Garang has already accepted an invitation from Janvid to meet to discuss the situation.

Africa News


September 17, 1990


BEST FOOT FORWARD


LENGTH: 719 words


Despite the latest critique of his human rights policies, President Mobutu Sese Seko of Zaire has shown he hasn't lost his diplomatic touch.

Long known for keeping opponents off balance with unexpected moves -- such as naming Nguza Karl-i-Bond, a critic who had been convicted of treason and sentenced to death, to be Zaire's ambassador to Washington in 1986 -- Mobutu has come up with another surprise. This month he has managed to play host to both Nelson Mandela and the South African Navy.

Mobutu received the African National Congress leader in the provincial city of Goma on September 8th, a day after a South African military spokesperson in Cape Town confirmed that three ships from the nearby Simonstown naval base were on an "assistance operation" with the Zaire navy. The mission, the statement said, was to help locate and remove sunken ships from the harbor of the Atlantic seacoast town of Banana. 

The joint operation was acknowledged after complaints from Angolan officials, who called in Zaire's ambassador to Luanda to demand an explanation for the South African presence just off Angola's coast. Angop, the Angolan news agency, said the South African ships had berthed at the Zairean port of Matadi, about 100 miles inland on the Zaire River on the Angolan-Zaire border. Matadi, Angop pointed out, is a major transshipment point for U.S. covert military assistance to the Angolan rebel movement Unita.

Pretoria said the operation in Zaire is consistent with its policy of helping "those African countries where diplomatic contacts have opened the way for further contact."

Nelson Mandela visited Zaire after attending a meeting of the Organization of African Unity in neighboring Uganda. On September 9th, the South African addressed a rally of about 10,000 people in Kinshasa, the Zaire capital, where he thanked Mobutu for having supported the "release Mandela" campaign.

In the past year, Mobutu has tried to mute both domestic and international criticism by pledging sweeping reforms in Zaire's autocratic political system. Five months ago he announced a one-year transition from single party rule to a three-party democracy.

Last month in New York, the Lawyer's Committee for Human Rights released a 231-page report saying that despite the promised changes, Mobutu's security forces still "have unrestrained authority to arrest, abuse and detain the population," that the government "routinely carries out arbitrary arrest and detention without charge," and that "physical abuse and mistreatment of prisoners are common."

Makau wa Mutua and Peter Rosenblum, who co-authored the report after two fact-finding visits to Zaire, were attacked on their latest trip by what they believe were security forces. The Zairean government denies any role in the incident, but concedes that it closely monitored the men's work in Zaire.

One of the incidents the report chronicles is the killings of an unknown number of students at the University of Lubumbashi in southeast Zaire by Mobutu's special forces in May.

Africa News has obtained eyewitness accounts of several of the killings in which students describe a night of terror. "I saw the masked military men decapitate and cut into pieces the bodies so that they were unrecognizible," said a student who jumped out a window and hid in the woods. "What was left was put on a truck and thrown into a ditch. I saw with my own eyes at least 20 dead students."

Another student says he hid under the bed of his next door neighbor and heard him being beaten to death.

Furor over the killings caused Belgium to suspend aid to Zaire and French officials cancelled a scheduled meeting in the Zaire capital.

In the past, Mobutu could count on his pro-Western policies to net him continued aid and support from Europe and the U.S., despite growing complaints about both his government's legendary corruption and its human rights record. His critics hope that in the post-Cold War world, Mobutu's dictatorial policies may get closer scrutiny when aid monies are disbursed.

And some former critics-turned-colleagues think they see the writing on the wall. Nguza Karl-I-Bond, who returned from Washington to be foreign minister, set up his own opposition party in May -- a move that brings him full circle.

Africa News


September 17, 1990


MARCHING TOWARDS CIVILIAN GOVERNMENT


SECTION: AROUND THE CONTINENT


LENGTH: 1151 words


Nigeria has begun what President Ibrahim Babangida calls the "last lap" before his military regime's promised handover of power to a democratically elected civilian administration in October of 1992.

This month, for the first time since the military's seizure of power in 1983, officially sanctioned political parties are operating freely in the country. Following national conventions in August to select party leaders, the country's two approved parties are busily campaigning for December's local elections. Under a new constitution resembling the American federal model, the government has established two parties - the Social Democratic Party, slightly to the left, and the National Republican Party, to the right of center. 

Meanwhile, President Babangida has reshuffled his administration in a move he says is intended to "demilitarize the process of politics and governance." He has stressed that he is barred by the new constitution from candidacy in the 1992 election.

But having lived under military rule for 19 of Nigeria's 30 years as an independent country, some Nigerians remain skeptical that Babangida will keep to his handover pledge. There is speculation that he may instead try to re-emerge as a civilian head-of-state. "General Ibrahim Babangida will hand over to Alhaji Ibrahim Babangida," says Yusuf Bela Usman, associate professor of history at Ahmadou Belo University.

In a grim reminder of continuing competition for control of Africa's most populous country, there was the jolting news on July 27th that 42 soldiers had been executed for their role in the latest coup attempt last April. The government announcement, made at 7:00 p.m. -- two hours after the men were sent to a firing squad -- followed a lengthy meeting of the Armed Forces Ruling Council, which reviewed the decisions handed down by a military tribunal. The announcement said 764 persons had been acquitted, 9 sentenced to prison, and 31 sent back for retrial.

The bloody April coup attempt and its aftermath seemed to shake the nation's confidence. Previous coups have been purely military affairs, but this time the government says there was significant civilian participation. The insurrection was allegedly financed by a businessman, Great Ogburu, who fled the country and is now on the government's wanted list along with several high-ranking military officers.

Ogburu went first to London, where, according to the Nigerian weekly magazine Newswatch, he penned a letter to the government saying he "was hoodwinked into the plot" by one of the coup leaders. Ogburu is now believed to be in hiding in Texas, Newswatch reports.

The April uprising was also the first in Nigeria where non-commissioned officers played a major role. Only nine of the 42 men executed were of officer rank.

Major Gideon Orka, the coup leader, went on the radio as the takeover began, saying he was acting "on behalf of the middle belt and southern parts of this country" -- a reflection of the resentment felt by many Nigerians about the perceived domination of the country's polity by the Muslim North. He also announced the expulsion of five states that are predominately Muslim from the Nigerian federation.

Ethnic divisions, combined with the religious differences, remain a potent force in Nigerian politics. Babangida stirred animosity in 1986 when he enrolled Nigeria in the Organization of Islamic Countries without clearing the action with the Armed Forces Ruling Council.

The tensions were evident in the reactions to the cabinet and military reshuffle Babangida carried out last December. Defense Minister Lieutenant General Domkat Bali -- from Lantang in Plateau State in the "middle belt" -- and several other senior military figures were replaced with Muslim officers. There were protests from Christian groups, but the government said the fact that the new appointees are Muslim was purely coincidental.

Nevertheless, Bali's removal generated so much dissatisfaction that Babangida decided to postpone a scheduled state visit to Washington in January.

There was some evidence of a sympathetic reaction among non-Northerners to at least some aspects of the coup attempt. "For the first time someone has touched on the real issues causing problems in this country," said university student Eunice Orie. But others reacted differently, seeing stability and the need for democracy as the priorities of the moment. General Bali, whose humiliating dismissal was one of Orka's stated reasons for attempting the coup, called the action "childish and immature."

"Babangida's five years in office have been marked by a steady decline in the people's standard of living, the depletion of the middle class and the widening of the gulf between the extremely rich and the extremely poor," says Newswatch. Still, the magazine said, as a "consummate wielder of power," Babangida has restored confidence and stability in the political system -- emerging in the process "as the most powerful man Nigeria has ever known."

The president's latest move, just over two weeks ago, was a major shake-up of senior aides, a tactic that has become his modus operandi. The changes, announced after a meeting of the Armed Forces Ruling Council, included the appointment of nine new Cabinet ministers, the reassignment of four others, and the establishment -- for the first time in a Nigerian military regime -- of the post of vice president.

Named to the new post is Augustus Aikhomu, a vice admiral who has been Babangida's top aide since October 1986 when he assumed the now abolished post of chief of general staff. Aikhomu is the highest ranking Christian in the predominately Muslim government.

The new post of chief of staff, to which Babangida named Major General Salihu Ibrahim, who had been commander of the 82nd Airborne Division, is intended to be purely military and non-political, in accordance with the new constitution.

Babangida also turned to trusted aides for two other key positions. Major General Ike Nwachukwu was reappointed foreign minister only eight months after leaving that job to command the 1st Mechanized Division of the Nigerian army. Nwachukwu, who was chairman of the military tribunal that tried the coup plotters, is a Christian who replaced Rilwanu Lukman, a Moslem whose ouster had been sought by some Christian critics of the government.

Alhaji Abubakar Alhaji, an experienced negotiator on economic matters, was moved from budget and planning to the finance ministry, where he will have responsibility for dealing with the country's creditors. The appointments were followed by the resignation of all Cabinet ministers from their military positions.

Babangida also named 10 new governors and appointed civilian deputy governors for all 21 Nigerian states. Two of the new deputies are women.

-- with files from Kingsley Chiedu Moghalu

Africa News


September 17, 1990


CREDIT WHERE IT'S DUE


LENGTH: 559 words


A decade ago, Esther Ocloo of Ghana and 14 other women founded an international financial institution to serve women entrepreneurs. This year, in an ongoing tenth anniversary celebration called "Give Women Credit," Women's World Banking (WWB) is taking stock of its accomplishments. Among other things, WWB has created a network of over 90 local groups, extended 56,000 loans of which less than 1.5 per cent were not paid back, and established 25 new loan programs for women around the world. 

On September 27th, that record will be highlighted when Esther Ocloo receives this year's Africa Prize for Leadership for the Sustainable End of Hunger, an annual award presented in New York City by the Hunger Project. Ocloo's co-winner is General Olesegun Obasanjo, a former Nigerian ruler who has emerged as an elder statesman of Africa, campaigning for democracy and agricultural self-sufficiency.

Women's World Banking President Michaela Walsh says the premise that women, even in the poorest communities, can manage their own financial affairs has been proven. The WWB system requires that local groups put up a quarter of the capital for a loan fund, with a cooperating bank providing another quarter and WWB itself providing the remaining half. Each contributor shares the risks proportionately.

The money has funded everything from a bicycle manufacturing business to an electronics firm called Solar Shamba that provides solar-powered rural electrification.

Esther Ocloo says that access to credit was the first essential step to improving the lives of poor women and their families. The next step, Ocloo hopes, is the establishment of community banks to act as the lending agencies. When women have their own banks, she says, the interest that is now being paid to commercial banks "becomes our profit."

The roots of Women's World Banking are in Ghana, where a 1974 meeting of women preparing for an upcoming United Nations Decade for Women conference decided that access to credit was women's most important need. The group included small-scale entrepreneurs such as local market women as well as leaders of women's organizations. Before education or health care or family planning, the group said, women needed money in their pockets. If they had the resources, they could make their own decisions and take care of their other necessities themselves.

Ghanaian women, including Ocloo and former Supreme Court Justice Annie Jiagge, took their notion to the UN conference in Mexico City in 1975, where it found an eager acceptance. But it was five years before the formal launch of WWB at a meeting in Amsterdam. Esther Ocloo was elected to chair the first Board of Directors.

Ocloo says that the women of Ghana, both as women and as people of a nation that had been colonized, must go through a period of painful change. "Our affairs were managed for us," she says. "But now we are managing our own affairs, and like any other new thing, ¢there are/ teething troubles."

But Ocloo is convinced that independence and self-sufficiency are worth the suffering required to attain them, and that people who achieve some control over their own lives discover a tremendous new store of energy. "All of a sudden," she says, "ingenuity is being expressed in all places and at all levels. Everybody is trying to do something!"

September 17, 1990


BOTSWANA: AID AND COMFORT


SECTION: WOMEN:


LENGTH: 1125 words


Earlier this year, consultant Anne Mize of Seattle, Washington was hired by the California-based development organization Tecnica to help a women's cooperative operate a shoe factory in the Botswana town of Kanye. Her experience raised many of the major questions being debated hotly among the development aid community, including the role of foreign experts, the relationship with local counterparts, and the ability of local communities to sustain projects they did not initiate. Here are excerpts from the journal Mize kept.

What I was sent here to do at first glance appears to be impossible. This small shoemaking factory was owned and operated by a white male expatriate. He decided about a year ago to leave the country and sell the business. The women workers refused to leave their jobs, and initiated a worker takeover with the help of Corde, an organization that supports Botswana's cooperative movement. 

Since then, however, there have been a lot of problems. Without their "boss" and enemy no longer around, the women apparently are working less well together than before the enterprise became a cooperative. They have requested that Corde help them establish a collective management system and teach them business skills.

What is needed is better communication and, in American terminology, "team-building skills."

I was hoping to have a month for this work. Because of travel restrictions and the time spent making housing arrangements, I'll only have two and a half weeks. Then the next volunteer comes from the U.S. to teach small business skills. The women will not be open to his training, I fear, unless a psychologically healthier atmosphere is created at the workplace. . .

Personally, my morale is also a bit low. I am presently in Gaborone ¢the Botswana capital/, still waiting to meet with the Corde staff to discuss the project. Thus far they have been helpful but disorganized. This stems from what appears to be overcommitment to helping too many projects. Tomorrow I hope to go back to Kanye and begin interviewing each of the women, with the help of a translator.

On a positive note, I am immediately attracted to Botswana. The landscape bears some resemblance to the southwestern U.S. I grew up in Kansas. Because of my love and need for open space, my heart sings when traveling through this country.

I look forward to getting to know the people better. Some of the folks at the cooperative training session are impressively intelligent and articulate. They do not want Botswana to go the capitalist route; they fear the materialism of South Africa. Learning to establish successful small businesses in the form of cooperatives is important to them. . .

I finally started my job in earnest with the Malekwa cooperative. Simon, the Corde staff member who will work with me, is a Tswana who has worked for several weeks with this group. He's been discouraged with the lack of progress in productivity and morale. As this was his first major assignment with Corde, I think he feels that my coming to work here is both a slap in his face and a relief.

We began with all the workers sitting around the table where they cut and glue shoe leather. Simon talked to them about my consulting and the purpose of the work and of the volunteers who will follow me. He made it clear that this is the last effort Corde will make to help the co-op improve its production.

I explained that I wanted to begin by getting to know them each individually. They looked a little nervous; as it turned out, they were afraid that whatever they said would be used against them. As the interviews began, it was clear that I had to emphasize the confidentiality of our talks.

There were some heart-rending stories. One woman is working to support the children of her daughter, a previous co-op member who had recently died. Another has a child dying in a hospital nearly 100 kilometers away. Another is putting her children through school after her husband walked out. She is 44 -- my age -- and I had mistaken her for 60! The lives of these women are very harsh. The minimal wages they earn for their work barely keeps them alive. Working with African women, I am so often brought to tears. . .

So many whites from around the world are taking positions in the Botswana government. This contributes to my feelings of reservation about doing this job. The dependency this group has on outside help is already great, from marketing to raw materials to organizing. Strategies ¢for improving production/ have not worked thus far, I think, because self-management is difficult for them to internalize. They have been managed for the majority of their working lives. In some ways, I'm coming in to manage them yet again.

Africans must begin to solve their own problems. They must be allowed to have their own land back. My feeling on this trip, after many to southern Africa, is that I may not come back as a worker any longer, but as a visitor and friend. . .

I'm spending time trying to adapt the procedures I've used for team building in the U.S. for this group. Translating everything means the whole process takes twice as long. And part of "First World" team training is bringing conflicts out into the open, followed by a process of resolution. Simon tells me this may be too threatening for these women. It's a dilemma for me; a delicate line to walk.

Can co-ops be a success in Botswana? Is self-management possible for the Malekwa co-op? Though the work we are doing seems to be encouraging the workers, the problems they are facing seem almost insurmountable. They have received no wages for four months. They have thousands of dollars in debts to pay. It may be many more months before their ledger shows a profit. How can they hold out?. . .

After a morning of role-playing conflicts and problems and differing ways to resolve them, we broke for lunch. But none of the women were eating. They said they had no money for food as it was the end of the month. That's when it finally hit me -- the survival level at which they're operating.

Their faces haunt me at night: the dances we dance together, the songs we sing, the tremendous courage of the three crippled ladies, the laughter when I try to dance with them. . .

My last day. Each of the women comes to meet me under the pine tree. They ask me not to forget them. They say that what they've learned would affect all with whom they come in contact. They give me a pair of sandals they've made. They ask me to bring back 15 American husbands!

I leave in a flurry of songs and dances.

"Listen, listen, listen to my heart's song. I will never forget you, I will never forsake you," we chant to each other.

NINE MONTHS OF LIBERIAN CONFLICT


LENGTH: 1050 words


December, 1989 24: Former Liberian government official Charles Taylor invades Liberia's Nimba County with a group of armed dissidents and exiles known as the National Patriotic Front of Liberia.

January, 1990 4: Liberian President Samuel Doe threatens to send troops to stop "dissident incursions" from neighboring Cote d'Ivoire, where up to 10,000 Liberians have fled since fighting began. The murdered and mutilated body of Robert Phillips, a prominent Liberian government critic, is discovered in Monrovia.

9: Doe issues a shoot-to-kill policy after claiming rebel forces killed 200 people, including 16 government officials, in Kahnple and Boutuo. 

15: Amnesty International estimates that 20,000 Liberian refugees are in Cote d'Ivoire and Guinea.

March 28: Bahn, a major trading town in northeast Liberia, falls to the rebels. British national June Jackson and her American husband Tom Jackson, a missionary, are found shot dead after the fighting, attracting international attention to the Liberian war.

29: American and British embassies advise their nationals to leave the country.

April 3: The World Food Program suspends food relief because of continued fighting in Liberia.

15: Government forces say they have crushed the rebellion after government soldiers kill 100 rebels and claim to have surrounded Taylor in Sanniquellie.

May 14: Reporters confirm that rebels control Nimba and Bassa counties, including Yekepa, Liberia's second-largest city.

22: The Doe government admits that the port city of Buchanan has fallen to rebels. Fears of ethnic retributions arise as four mutilated bodies of Gio and Mano people are found in the capital Monrovia. The two groups live in the area where the rebellion began.

25: Doe claims his soldiers have recaptured Buchanan and offers to hold elections to halt the fighting.

29: Liberian legislators reject Doe's call for early elections.

30: West African countries call for an end to hostilities.

31: The United Nations announces a pullout of its personnel after government soldiers attack a UN refugee compound, killing 4 refugees and abducting 40.

June 1: The United States and the United Kingdom station ships off the Liberian coast to evacuate foreign nationals if the fighting intensifies. As rebels approach Monrovia, Doe promises not to run for re-election.

3: Taylor's rebels capture the country's third-largest town, Gbarnga, 100 miles northeast of Monrovia.

4: Killings with ethnic overtones increase as the advancing rebels shut down Robertsfield International Airport near Owen's Grove, 30 miles southeast of the capital.

5: Rebels take over the U.S.-managed, Japanese-owned Firestone rubber plantation near Owen's Grove, killing hundreds from Doe's ethnic group, the Krahn, and the allied Mandingo group.

8: The government announces it has accepted an invitation from religious leaders to participate in peace talks. Government troops recapture the rubber plantation and the airport.

10: Three hundred sixty Americans are airlifted from Liberia into neighboring Cote d'Ivoire.

11: An interfaith mediation committee convenes peace talks in Sierra Leone.

14: In a church-sponsored march on the U.S. Embassy in Monrovia, 1,000 Liberians appeal for American help in ending the war.

16: Peace talks break up without reaching any agreements.

19: Doe declares an amnesty for political exiles, lifts the ban on three political parties and says he will restructure his cabinet.

21: In an effort to isolate the capital, rebels capture Careysburg, near a key Voice of America transmitter.

30: Rebels cut off water and electric power to Monrovia.

July 3: Doe offers to step down if he and the Krahn people are guaranteed immunity.

20: The besieged president barricades himself in the executive mansion as rebel forces capture all parts of the capital except central Monrovia.

27: After invading the center of town, Taylor declares himself president of a new "reconstruction" government.

29: Prince Y. Johnson, leader of a breakaway rebel force, leads an assault on the center of Monrovia, wresting control from government troops.

30: Government troops massacre at least 600 people taking refuge on the grounds of a Lutheran church in Monrovia.

August 5: U.S. marines land in Monrovia to protect the U.S. embassy.

6: Johnson takes a dozen foreigners hostage in an attempt to provoke international intervention to halt the fighting. Liberian refugees in Guinea, Cote d'Ivoire and Sierra Leone now number 400,000.

7: A mediation committee established by Ecowas, the Economic Community of West African States, adopts a framework for a cease-fire and the establishment of an interim government in Liberia.

8: Johnson releases his foreign hostages.

9: Taylor's forces launch another assault on Doe's stronghold in Monrovia.

12: Doe announces his intention of remaining in office another year.

15: Taylor blocks the evacuation of citizens of the West African countries supporting the Ecowas peacekeeping effort.

18: Johnson and a top Doe aide agree on a cease-fire between their respective forces. U.S. marine helicopters evacuate 825 foreigners from Monrovia.

23: In the wake of the failed peace talks, Ecowas sends ground and naval forces to Liberia, with forces drawn from five member states--Nigeria, Ghana, Guinea, Sierra Leone and The Gambia.

30: Ecowas convenes a "Liberian National Conference," in Banjul, The Gambia, where an interim government to be headed by scholar Amos Sawyer is named to govern the country until elections can take place.

31: Taylor's rebels kill about 200 civilian nationals of countries involved in the Ecowas peacekeeping force.

September 1: The Ecowas intervention force takes control of central Monrovia.

8: Taylor's troops attack the port of Monrovia with shells and gunfire as Western relief workers fly into the city.

9: Johnson's rebels capture and wound Doe during a shoot-out. Johnson proclaims himself Liberia's interim president.

10: Doe dies in captivity at Johnson's base camp outside Monrovia.

11: The interim government of Amos Sawyer makes plans to send envoys to Monrovia to prepare for taking over the country.

September 17, 1990


LIBERIAN AGONY CONTINUES


LENGTH: 847 words


Like most residents of the Liberian capital, Monrovia, Bishop Arthur F. Kulah has spent the last two months trying to stay alive through the relentless fighting in the city's streets.

As leader of the United Methodist Church, Liberia's largest denomination, Kulah was a spokesman for church concerns about corruption and human rights abuses under the regime of President Samuel K. Doe, who was finally captured by rebels last week. In early July, when the Bishop's residence was ransacked by angry government troops, Kulah went into hiding.

Along with about two dozen other people, the bishop took refuge in a house in the crowded suburb known as Congotown, where in recent weeks each man, woman and child has subsisted on a few spoonfuls of rice a day, cooked in a little oil. Congotown, like most of Monrovia, has been without electricity, water or telephone service for more than two months, and food is scarce everywhere. Cholera has been reported in scattered parts of the city, and last week the disease appeared to be spreading in an area jammed with 2,000 refugees. 

Already, the death toll from the fighting and from starvation is in the thousands and a quarter of Liberia's 2.3 million people have fled the country.

Among them is J. Lamark Cox, Sr., a businessman and church lay leader who calls himself one of the lucky few. When fighting forced him from his central Monrovia home on July 17th, he found shelter in the same Congotown house as Bishop Kulah. "We were fortunate," Cox says, "that the household had its small store of rice. Thousands and thousands of Liberians cannot get anything to eat now."

The two men had planned to stay in Monrovia to try to maintain a church presence among the desperate population, Cox says. But after the latest peace talks broke down at the end of August, they decided to try to leave the country to seek outside help. Making their way overland to neighboring Cote d'Ivoire, they caught a plane to Freetown, Sierra Leone, where there are large concentrations of Liberian refugees. Bishop Kulah remained in West Africa to focus on getting relief supplies to refugees and into Liberia. Cox continued to the United States, where his wife and children awaited him, to try to stir public concern about the Liberian plight.

Since rebel forces led by Charles Taylor entered Liberia last Christmas, Cox says, government forces have been responsible for most of "the human massacre of large numbers of our people." Doe's forces targeted members of the Gio and Mano ethnic groups whose home area is in the northeast where the rebellion began. As Taylor's forces and a breakaway group led by Prince Johnson gathered support and advanced on the capital, members of Doe's ethnic group, the Krahn, and the closely allied Mandingo group began to be victims of reprisals.

Liberia has "never in our history" experienced the sort of ethnic-based strife that has now spread throughout the country, Cox says. "We never expected it to take place, but it is now something we have to be resolute enough to repair." Cox says Liberia's religious communities, whose members span the various ethnic groups, can play the most important role in reconciliation.

For now, though, the focus is on ending the fighting and getting food, clothing and medicine to the people.

Early last month, a seven-nation mediation committee convened by Ecowas -- the Economic Community of West African States -- hammered out a framework for a cease-fire and sponsored a meeting of Liberian exiles called the Liberian National Conference. The conference selected an interim government, headed by political scientist Amos Sawyer, to rule until elections can be held.

On August 23, after the last round of peace talks failed to get off the ground, Ecowas sent a ground and naval force into the besieged nation to try to impose a cease-fire. Ecowas officials said the West African Community was forced to act after the United States, traditionally Liberia's closest ally, rejected repeated appeals for its intervention.

Last week, Ecowas Executive Secretary Abbas Bundu said that Prince Johnson has promised to cooperate with the Sawyer government, despite the fact that Johnson had declared himself Liberia's president after capturing Doe. Bundu said that the commander of Doe's forces at the executive mansion in Monrovia has also agreed to cooperate with the interim government.

After Doe's reported death while in Johnson's custody, the immediate problem facing the Ecowas troops was how to contain Taylor's forces, who after eight months of fighting, feel they have won the war and don't want to lose it at the peace table. A complicating factor is that Taylor has the support of Ecowas member Burkina Faso, whose government is believed to have supplied Taylor with new weapons.

Meanwhile, Liberians are making urgent appeals for humanitarian assistance. On September 8th, a four-person U.S. government disaster and relief team arrived in Monrovia to assess the situation. But little can be done by anyone until the fighting ends.

October 22, 1990


GUNS STILL MEAN NO BUTTER:


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 252 words


Military buildup in the world continues to be the main obstacle to overcoming hunger, according to the Washington, DC-based Bread for the World Institute on Hunger and Development.

While the arms race in developed countries may have cooled, it is accelerating rapidly in developing countries, whose share of the world arms market increased from 7 to 19 percent between 1960 and 1987, says a report issued by the group on October 16th to coincide with World Food Day. Military conflicts inevitably disrupt food production and distribution, lead to environmental degradation, create refugees and therefore cause extensive hunger, the Hunger 1990 study states.

In the Horn of Africa, the report also says, arms supplied by the United States and Soviet Union feed greater militarization where famine, declining per capita food production, debt and collapsing social services are already overwhelming problems.

The report points up five other major causes of world hunger which can, it says, be addressed effectively by support for food relief agencies and by lobbying for change in government policy. These include overlooking the needs and skills of women in development projects, environmental destruction linked to rapid population growth, emphasis on economic growth without attention to human resource development, exclusion of the poor from projects designed to benefit them, and donor country policies on development aid that can actually undermine any benefits for the developing country.

WHO ARE THOSE MASKED MEN?


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 493 words


Despite a Mali government blackout on news coverage of the conflict, a rebellion by Touareg nomads appears to be continuing. Peace talks October 10-12 in Algeria failed to find a solution to the conflict, Reuters reports from Bamako, the Mali capital.

The rebels say they are fighting for the right "to work, to be educated, to participate in economic and political debate and the right to live." The government of President Moussa Traore identifies its opponents as tools of Libya. 

The current troubles broke out late last June with an attack by well-armed Touareg on a police station in Menaka in the northern Malian desert, followed by raids on other military and administrative targets. The government has declared a state of emergency in the Gao and Timbuktu regions, resulting in what the human rights group Amnesty International says have been over 50 public executions of suspected rebels. In addition, more than a hundred civilians have been reported killed, including dozens of men, women and children in a nomad encampment, while a similar number of deaths are estimated on the government side. Among the numerous people under detention or house arrest, Amnesty says, is Sikaye ag Ekawel, director of a government-sponsored food aid project.

The French Secretary of State for Humanitarian Action, Bernard Kouchner, who visited northern Mali as well as neighboring Niger to assess the situation, calls the conflict "extremely dangerous." The traditional hostility between the region's governments and the nomads who roam across national boundaries could easily be kindled into massive retribution against the Touareg, Kouchner warns. Last month, the European Parliament passed a resolution saying that if the fighting continues, "an entire people will be threatened with annihilation."

The Touareg are a fiercely independent people sometimes called the "blue men," because the indigo dye on the veils worn by Touareg men rubs off on the skin. The rebels call themselves the Movement for the Liberation of Mali (MLM). A recently-released MLM document calls on all Malians who are victims of "injustice and poverty" to join the effort to replace Traore's "dictatorial military regime" with a democratic system.

Although a Touareg-inspired uprising may be improbable, due to ancient animosities between the nomads and the settled peoples of the region, recent years have seen growing sympathy among the various groups in desert societies, all of them hard pressed by drought as well as by the economic crises in their countries. And the call for democracy is becoming increasingly prevalent. President Traore, visiting Washington, D.C. this month, reiterated an August promise to consider political pluralism. He may be forced to think fast. Last week an estimated 100 demonstrators marched through the Malian capital, Bamako demanding multi-party democracy. It was the first public protest against Traore in his 22-year rule.

October 22, 1990


Robert Rodale, 1930-1990


SECTION: EDITORIAL


LENGTH: 417 words


Robert Rodale served as a member of the Board of Directors of Africa News Service from 1988 until he was killed in a car accident in the Soviet Union on September 20, 1990.

Being on our board wasn't something he had time to do. But he did it anyway, because he cared about Africa and he knew that his skills and experience could help us build an effective, financially stable news agency. 

As head of Rodale Press, begun by his father in the 1940s, Robert Rodale supervised an empire that publishes the magazines Prevention, Organic Gardening, Bicycling and several others, as well as half a dozen newsletters and 35 to 40 books a year. The company also operates three book clubs. Other Rodale enterprises include an agricultural research facility and a non-profit organization, the Rodale Institute, devoted to "making agriculture more profitable and biologically sound."

At the time of his death, Rodale was in Moscow to launch a Russian-language edition of the magazine The New Farmer, which encourages "sustainable agriculture" and a reduced reliance on chemicals.

It was his soft-spoken humor combined with passion that set Robert Rodale apart from many crusaders. Indeed, his mild-mannered, almost diffident demeanor made him an unlikely titan of publishing, but he was a marketing genius as well as a visionary -- and he told stories with a wry wit that continued to evoke smiles long after the recounting.

We first met Robert Rodale in 1984, while reporting on his company's role in a resource-efficient farming project in Tanzania. "Never underestimate the power of a book," he told us, relating a tale first told by then-U.S. Ambassador to Tanzania David Miller. The project started, it seems, with Ambassador Miller's wife Mollie, whose copy of The Rodale Book of Composting found its way to Tanzanian President Julius Nyerere.

Before long, a Rodale team was in Tanzania working with agricultural extension officers to revive organic farming techniques that were once common in Africa, but were abandoned after colonialism brought chemical-intensive farming to the continent. "In the Third World you have a shortage of food," Rodale said. "In the U.S. we have an overabundance. In both places the waste of nutrients is the same. So the idea of resource efficiency is of universal application."

Robert Rodale's conviction that everyone can make a difference was contagious. Like many others, we will miss his enthusiasm, his encouragement and his inspiration.

Africa News


October 22, 1990


Talks But No Peace: Election Plans Proceed


LENGTH: 962 words


 

Despite the breakdown of talks between the government of Mozambique and the rebel movement Renamo, the ruling Frelimo party is going ahead with plans for multi-party elections under a new constitution. Since August, a constitutional commission has been discussing proposals for the changeover from a one-party system.

While procedures for the elections, expected to take place next July, are being developed, there is also a vigorous debate on a range of social and economic policies. There are strongly divergent views, within Frelimo as well as outside it, on how far the move towards a free-market system should go and on how to address the growing economic inequalities of recent years. But all the plans and discussions are overshadowed by the dominant issue of peace. There is general agreement that solutions to any of Mozambique's problems depend on ending the conflict that has claimed an estimated 900,000 lives, due both to direct casualties and to war-induced famine. 

A high-level Mozambican government delegation first met Renamo representatives for direct talks in July, followed by a second session in August, both held in Rome. Renamo boycotted a scheduled third meeting in September, and prospects for a renewed round of talks remain uncertain.

Mozambique agreed to the direct negotiations early this year after South African President F.W. de Klerk pledged that his government was no longer backing Renamo. But talks were delayed for months in a dispute over location. When the sessions finally got underway -- led by a top Frelimo leader, Transport Minister Armando Guebuza and Raul Domingos, head of Renamo's Department of External Relations -- they were inconclusive.

In 1989, Mozambique's President Joaquim Chissano had asked Kenyan President Daniel arap Moi and President Robert Mugabe of Zimbab-we to mediate the conflict and pave the way for negotiations. At the second Rome session, according to reporting by Karl Maier for the London newspaper, the Independent, the Renamo delegation refused to discuss substantive issues, demanding instead that Kenya again be invited to mediate. Kenya's permanent secretary for foreign affairs Bethuel Kiplagat reportedly lobbied to transfer the talks to Nairobi.

The Mozambique delegation regarded the quest for new Kenyan involvement as a delaying tactic and remains suspicious of the role of Kiplagat, whom Mozambique sees as a Renamo partisan. On August 11th, the Mozambique Information Agency reported that Kenya is now involved in military training of Renamo troops. The agency said a Kenyan-trained battalion infiltrated Tete province through neighboring Malawi and that Mozambican forces had captured an air shipment of ammunition from Kenya in Manica province.

The Mozambican army, aided by troops from Zim-babwe, appears to have made headway against Renamo in recent months in areas such as the densely populated Zambezia province, as well as in Renamo's traditional base area south of the Zambezi River. Renamo cited the government advances as a reason for withdrawing from the peace talks in September. Mozambique's President Chissano responded that the military operations were normal defensive measures within the country that could stop only with a mutuallyagreed cease-fire -- the objective, he said, of the talks.

Mozambique officials say that despite battlefield successes for the government, there is no hope of a military resolution to the conflict, since making the entire country secure from continuing guerrilla attacks is infeasible.

On the political front, however, the government is pressing forward, leaving little apparent room for new Renamo demands. The group has an open invitation to end the war and register as a political party to contest next year's elections.

Although Mozambique's formal announcement of the shift to a multi-party system made international headlines in July and August, the change was prefigured by release of a draft constitution in January. Under its provisions, presidential candidates must be proposed by 5,000 voters, of whom there must be at least 200 in each province -- an attempt to prevent new parties from encouraging ethnic or regional divisions. The draft also limits a president's tenure to two five-year terms and makes no mention of a Frelimo role in either assembly or presidential elections. The proposals were widely debated in public meetings across the country from April through June.

Summarizing the debate as it related to multiple parties, Chissano said that a majority of the Mozambicans participating, particularly in rural areas, had favored continuation of the one-party system. But the president said that because a substantial minority had argued for political pluralism, it would not be right to block those who wish to do so from forming other parties. A new electoral law is expected to lay down procedures through which new parties may be registered, and will likely incorporate provisions similar to those for presidential candidates, requiring geographical distribution among a potential party's supporters.

Chissano himself, though he seems to enjoy strong personal popularity, told a Tanzanian journalist in late August that he has "no reason to remain in power if the people of Mozambique do not want me." And Frelimo officials acknowledge that their party suffers the disadvantages of incumbency during years of war and economic crisis. But Renamo, with its reputation for brutality against civilians, seems an unlikely heir to the mantle of "people's power" with which Frelimo began its rule at independence in 1975. It remains to be seen whether other forces will emerge to take advantage at the ballot box of the widespread war-weariness and disillusionment.

Africa News


October 22, 1990


Nigerians Take Charge


SECTION: AROUND THE CONTINENT


LENGTH: 774 words


 

Beefed up by some 5,000 additional Nigerian soldiers, the West African peacekeeping force in Liberia is slowly gaining control over the battle-torn Liberian capital Monrovia. The force has been increased in size this month from 4,500 to more than 9,000 and has been reorganized under the command of Major General Joshua Dogonyaro, a member of Nigeria's ruling military council.

Five members of the Economic Community of West African States (Ecowas) contributed troops to the force with the intent of imposing a cease fire on the three warring Liberian factions -- the Liberian army, the main rebel movement led by Charles Taylor, and a breakaway group led by Prince Johnson. The Ecowas Monitoring Group, called Ecomog, includes soldiers from Ghana, Nigeria, Sierre Leone, Guinea and the Gambia. 

"We wanted the outfit to create an atmosphere which would allow an interim government to operate," said Nigerian Vice President Augustus Aikhomu in an interview in New York late last month. "Unfortunately, it didn't work out that way," he said.

Taylor refused to cooperate with the Ecowas effort, which he regards as a maneuver to keep him from seizing power. His National Patriotic Front is believed to control most of the country outside the capital. Last month, Ecowas was embarrassed when Liberian President Samuel K. Doe was captured at Ecomog headquarters, where he apparently went to negotiate his departure from the country. Following a shoot-out between Doe loyalists and Johnson's forces which left 60 dead, Doe was taken to Johnson's camp, tortured and killed.

Following that incident and with fighting in Monrovia continuing, the Nigerians decided to increase their presence. As the largest country in the region, Nigeria was unwilling "to stand idly by and allow such wanton killing to take place," Aikhomu said.

Initially, Nigeria's participation in Ecomog was limited, in an attempt to ease Taylor's suspicions that the force was a Nigerian-inspired effort to rescue Doe's regime, with which the Nigerian military government had had relatively good relations in recent years. Ghanaian General Arnold Quainoo was named to lead Ecomog.

Quainoo remains in command, Aikhomu said -- in charge of the joint military force, under Nigeria's Dogonyaro, who has overall authority over the Ecowas presence in Liberia. A civilian component of Ecomog is being created this month to coordinate relief and refugee operations, the Nigerian vice president said.

Last week, Ecomog established control over central Monrovia and began moving into the suburbs, according to reports from Reuters and United Press International (UPI) correspondents on the scene. Taylor's forces were reportedly forced to pull back. Johnson agreed to withdraw from the city center earlier this month.

Ecowas members have been divided over the Liberian intervention, and the differences have deepened since Doe's death. At the urging of Taylor, the government of Cote D'Ivoire tried to convene an Ecowas summit on October 15th. But the effort failed when several key leaders refused to attend including Gambian President Sir Dawda Jawara, the current Ecowas chaiman. Jawara has now announced plans for a summit on October 29th. Cote D'ivoire and Burkina Faso are backing Taylor, and most other French-speaking states in the region have withheld support for Ecomog.

Brigadier General David Nimley, leader of the remaining Doe loyalists, has rejected Ecomog demands to surrender. "We have nowhere else to go," he told UPI. "This republic of Liberia is ours." He said he would remain in the executive mansion until an interim government arrives to take control.

In August, Liberia's major political parties and religious organizations established an interim administration, led by Amos Sawyer, a political scientist and former university dean. But Sawyer's team has been forced by the fighting to remain outside the country.

Since taking control of the city, Ecomog has uncovered gruesome evidence of the results of months of fighting. The Reuters and UPI reporters have been taken to view two sites where hundreds of bodies have been left to rot. "Half-clothed, decomposed heaps of flesh" remain strewn across the pews, floors and balcony of St. Peter's Lutheran Church in downtown Monrovia, scene of a July 29 massacre by Doe's troops of 600 men, women and children who had taken refuge there from the fighting.

At the end of a runway at Spriggs-Payne airfield, hundreds of skulls and bones were found where they were left by government soldiers after looting and killing sprees in the city, eyewitnesses told the reporters.

Africa News


October 22, 1990


Unrest Could Ignite Widespread Violence


LENGTH: 1162 words


Civil war in the east African nation of Rwanda, which could prove as intractable as many of the other disturbances around the continent, also threatens to rekindle animosities that have resulted in killing on a mammoth scale.

"When communal violence gets started in this part of the world, it can lead to massacres in humongous numbers," says Roger Winter, executive director of the U.S. Committee for Refugees who is just back from a visit to the area. In the last thirty years, fighting, primarily between the region's Tutsi and Hutu groups, has claimed tens of thousands of lives.

"For most of their history, the people of Rwanda have lived together in relative tranquility," says University of North Carolina political scientist Catherine Newbury, author of a study of Rwanda from 1860 to 1960. "The Tutsi and Hutu share a common language and culture and can in no way be described as tribes," she says. But the two groups are distinguished by differences, including the Tutsi tradition of governance by a system of royal chieftaincies. 

The Tutsi have historically held a dominant position over their more numerous neighbors. The German and Belgian colonizers concentrated power and wealth in Tutsi hands and introduced a segregated education system that blocked Hutu mobility and advancement.

In Rwanda, the Hutu -- comprising 85% of the population -- rose up against Tutsi supremacy in 1959, and most of the Tutsi chiefs fled the country. After independence in 1962, an effort by the exiles to return to power was suppressed by the Hutu-run government, resulting in widespread killing of Tutsi. (In next-door Burundi, where the ethnic composition is similar, the governing Tutsi slaughtered an estimated 60,000 Hutu during the most recent outbreak of killing in 1988.)

The longstanding tensions are exacerbated by the modern competition for resources. Rwanda has one of the fastest growing populations in the world -- 3.8 percent per year. And it is densely populated -- seven million people occupying 10,169 square miles, or nearly 690 people per square mile. It's as if Nebraska, with a farming population like Rwanda's but ten times the land area, had 70 million inhabitants instead of 845,000.

Rwandan President Juvenal Habyarimana says the current conflict is a Tutsi effort to regain power.

Fighting broke out at the beginning of October with an invasion from neighboring Uganda by a force of Ugandan army soldiers of Rwandan descent.

Although the insurgents appear to be mostly Tutsi, a six-page political program issued in the name of the Rwandese Patriotic Front (RPF) says the opposition to Habyarimana comes from all sectors of the populace. Rwandans have "outgrown the ill-motivated divisive politics of the last three decades" and have learned to live harmoniously "like the Flemish and Walloons" of Belgium, the document says.

The rebels accuse Habyarimana of undemocratic and corrupt practices and say his regime reserves most educational and vocational opportunities for a small clique from the north, the president's home region. This has alienated not only the minority Tutsi -- 14% of the population -- and the Twa, with one percent, but also Hutu from the south as well as a section of the northern Hutu, the rebels say.

"Rwanda is the only country outside South Africa that issues a passbook indicating the bearer's ethnicity," the RPF document says. "This segregative, apartheid-like policy serves only to deter the progress and development of Rwanda."

In an eight-point program, the front promises the abolition of sectarianism and corruption, "free and fair elections," and the "unconditional" repatriation of Rwandan refugees.

It was the refugee problem that sparked the uprising. Habyarimana has contended that his country cannot absorb all the Rwandans in Uganda, Tanzania and Zaire -- estimated to total between one and two million -- and has asked these countries to accept the exiles as immigrants. Uganda, where most of them are living, has refused.

In January, negotiations between Uganda and Rwanda led to an agreement that the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees would survey the Rwandan exiles to determine whether they want to return home or remain where they are. According to Ugandan President Yoweri Museveni, the accord represented "a positive step," since it included Habyarimana's first acknowledgement of responsibility for the problem. "That's why I am upset by this action," Museveni said of the invasion, in an interview during a visit to Washington earlier this month.

The leader of the RPF invasion force is Fred Rwigyema, a former deputy commander of the Ugandan National Resistance Army (NRA), which fought its way to power in a decade-long campaign against Idi Amin and several successive governments.

Winter, who has known Rwigyema for several years and met with him recently in Uganda, says he sympathizes with the refugees' plight. "But when you consider the risk of massive violence, I have a hard time understanding why these guys went in," he says. The reprisals that followed the attempted Tutsi takeover attempt in 1963 offer a worrisome precedent.

In spite of the history of conflict, Newbury believes that a bloodbath is not inevitable. Habyarimana has succeeded in reducing ethnic tensions, which were very high when he took power in a 1973 coup, she says.

Well-trained and battle-seasoned, the rebel troops quickly seized several towns in the north and reportedly captured a large stockpile of weapons. With an initial force of about 10,000, the invaders have grown to a reported 20,000 or more.

Adding to their strength is a smaller contingent of refugee fighters from Tanzania, led by Colonel Alex Kanyarengwe, who -- like many of his soldiers -- is a Hutu.

President Habyarimana quickly appealed for outside help. Belgium and France sent troops, as did President Mobutu Sese Seko of Zaire, Rwanda's huge western neighbor. With these reinforcements, Rwanda's 4,000-man army was able to recapture the strategic garrison town of Gabiro, 40 miles northeast of the capital. Hundreds of suspected Patriotic Front sympathizers were rounded up in the Rwandan capital, Kigali.

Last week, though, the government suffered military reversals. Gabiro was retaken by Rwigyema's forces, and Zaire abruptly withdrew its forces, officially numbered at 500. With no combat experience and only limited outside backing, government troops may have difficulty containing rebel advances from both the north and south of the country.

Faced by the prospect of protracted conflict, Habyarimana turned to diplomacy to shore up his position. He appealed for United Nations action, met with Museveni in Tanzania, and flew to Europe for consultations with the French and Belgians. The Rwandan, Ugandan and Tanzanian presidents jointly called for a cease-fire and negotiations.

"I don't think this is going to be over quickly," Winter says.

Africa News


October 22, 1990


Museveni Sees Opportunity Ahead


SECTION: INTERVIEW


LENGTH: 885 words


 

At a time when African governments are struggling to reverse the economic decline of the past decade, Ugandan President Yoweri Museveni is highly critical of a "dependency syndrome" which he says afflicts his own planning officials and inhibits his country's economic prospects.

Educated and articulate, Museveni at 46 years of age personifies the new generation of African leaders and potential leaders who are struggling to surmount both the economic and political dislocations of the colonial period and what they regard as the misguided policies of post-colonial African governments. In July, Museveni was elected chairman of the Organization of African Unity -- a position that rotates among the heads of state of the 51-member organization. A former guerrilla leader, Museveni came to power in 1986, after a long military campaign -- first to overthrow the regime of Idi Amin and then to win power from a succession of weak or authoritarian governments that followed Amin's ouster. 

"They beg for loans from outside and fail to see the huge opportunities inside our country," he said of his planning ministry and other economic decision makers, during an interview in Washington earlier this month. "Uganda is one of the richest countries in the whole world." It has abundant natural resources and a geographical location on the equator, "which means we can produce all the year round," he says.

But the country still is dependent on one crop, coffee, for over 90% of its export earnings. The situation has worsened since independence in 1962, when coffee accounted for about half the foreign exchange revenues, the Ugandan leader says.

Museveni is committed to an integrated approach to economic growth and is promoting the processing of raw materials, such as sugar, which previously was exported unprocessed. There is also a concerted effort to establish light industries, including textiles and cement manufacturing.

Uganda is actively courting foreign investors. As long as an investment will save foreign exchange or earn foreign exchange, it will be welcomed, the president says.

The government's top priority is education, something it has been able to do little about since coming to power. "The tax base was very narrow, and there was no way initially we could have tackled education in a comprehensive way," he says.

Addressing another issue that is growing in importance across the continent, Museveni says that Africa's governments must become "accountable" to their citizens. "There must be free access to competition for political power," he says, although Western forms of democracy may not be appropriate in Africa's "pre-industrial societies."

Like many of his counterparts, Museveni is doubtful that introducing multiple parties would lead to real democratic reforms. Politics in Africa, he says, has revolved around competition for access to power -- "for handouts from the state, for civil service appointments," using sectarian appeals -- ethnic or religious. These appeals succeed in agrarian states, Museveni argues, because people do not have a well-developed national identity. The challenge in Africa is ensuring democracy while avoiding sectarianism, he says.

In Uganda, political allegiances have paralleled religious affiliations. Museveni says he is trying to replace the established order with a new arrangement that unites the country. Although he has not banned the old parties, he has suspended their activities and seems intent on destroying them. His own power base is the National Resistance Army, now consisting of an estimated 75,000 soldiers, and its political wing, the National Resistance Movement.

The movement, he says, incorporates many ideologies and provides access to political decisionmaking to all "within a united framework." He argues that the steps taken to create "mass nonpartisan democracy" will prevent the emergence of another Idi Amin-style dictatorship.

Museveni has not faced an election since assuming power nearly five years ago. His term was recently extended to 1995, when he plans national elections for Parliament and the presidency. He has not said whether more than one candidate will be allowed to compete for the highest office. Museveni says he himself would be willing to step down if he fails to win re-election. "Actually I'm looking forward to the rest," he said.

The president expects to see the eventual emergence in Uganda of political parties representing different interests -- not ethnic or other sectarian groups -- "but it hasn't happened yet."

Should pro-democracy forces in countries ruled by dictators take up arms, as Ugandans did, Museveni was asked. "Certainly, if they are not just opportunists looking for a job," he says.

However, the Uganda leader is critical of the rebels who have launched an invasion of neighboring Rwanda from Ugandan territory. "They didn't listen to advice from their friends," Museveni said.

A solution to the problem will come only when Rwanda agrees to restore the rights of the refugees to return home, Museveni insisted. "They should be free to live there, to vote and be voted for."

The taped interview on which this article is based was conducted by Rob Wright, senior editor for the weekly newsletter World Bank Watch.
