Africa News


October 22, 1990


Poorest of the Poor


LENGTH: 562 words


 

Two-thirds of the world's "least developed countries" are in Africa, according to the latest list prepared by the United Nations. Known collectively as LDCs, the "least developed" nations are those low-income countries that are thought to be most vulnerable to continuing economic crises for a variety of structural reasons. Although the African LDCs' per capita GDP (gross domestic product) of $ 237 a year is just above the overall LDC average of $227, African countries fare the worst on most indicators. Life expectancy in the African LDCs is 47 years, three years less than the total LDC average. The infant mortality rate is 121 per 1,000 live births for the African LDCs, compared to 106 for other LDCs. 

The gap between the poorest Africans and the citizens of industrialized nations is illustrated graphically by two countries that sandwich the African continent -- Mali, in the northwest, where 168 babies die for every 1,000 born, and Mozambique, in the southeast, whose death rate per thousand is 172. In contrast, the rate for Japan is five, for Sweden is six, for Hong Kong is eight, for Singapore is nine and for the United States is 10.

A risk factor shared by African LDCs is their extreme dependence on a limited number of export commodities, at a time when commodity prices are continuing to decline on the world market. In 1987, 23 African LDCs depended on just three exports for an average 79 percent of their total earnings. That's a worsening of dependency since 1980, when the top three crops accounted for 76.9 percent of earnings.

Equally troubling is the fact that between 1980 and 1988, the terms of trade of 19 African LDCs declined by 11 percent. Terms of trade represent the difference between the earnings from what countries sell and the cost of what they must buy. This year, the higher oil prices sparked by the Persian Gulf crisis will dramatically worsen the terms of trade for Africa, with the possible exception of the oil-producing countries -- Algeria, Angola, Egypt, Gabon, Libya and Nigeria.

Last month in Paris, the UN convened the Second Conference on Least Developed Countries -- a successor to the first conference on the topic, held in 1981. In an opening address, French President Francois Mitterrand pledged to boost France's development aid to 0.2 percent of his country's GDP, up from 0.14 percent in 1988.

Some countries, including the Netherlands, Saudi Arabia and Libya already exceed the 0.2 percent mark -- and Norway more than doubles that amount at 0.42 percent -- but most wealthy countries fall short of the United Nations target for donors of 0.15 percent of their respective GDPs. In 1988, Japanese development assistance was 0.07 percent, while U.S. aid amounted to just 0.04 percent.

The Paris conference stressed the inevitability of continued decline in LDC living standards unless there is substantially increased help from donors. At the same time, a growing chorus of voices in poorer countries is saying that, without internal reforms -- including democratization -- more aid will mean little. So the economic crisis of the eighties may prove an added impetus to political revolutions of the nineties.

A fact-crammed, 12-page briefing paper on African LDCs is available from the UN Africa Recovery Programme, telephone (212) 963-6857, telex 232422, fax (212) 486-8792.

Africa News


October 22, 1990


The Children of Africa: Who Is Responsible for Killing a Continent's Youth?


SECTION: CHILDREN


LENGTH: 1617 words


Neil Henry, East Africa correspondent for the Washington Post, reflected on this month's World Summit for Children in an article for Outlook, the paper's Sunday opinion section. It is reprinted here with the Post's permission.

NAIROBI - Of all the Liberian war refugees who had crammed into St. Peter's Lutheran Church in Monrovia, few appeared less fearful than a little girl named Patience Nya. She seemed a picture of calm in a sea of human terror.

Patience was about 7 years old, a skinny child in a worn red dress who interrupted an interview I was conducting with her mother to ask if I knew Michael Jackson, the American pop star who remains something close to a deity in Africa. 

Amid the stifling heat and noise on that June day at St. Peter's, where more than 3,000 civilians had sought safety from the marauding thugs of the Liberian army, the girl's guileless question provided a moment of welcome relief and a much-needed perspective on a savage civil conflict.

In a nation where human life seemed to be losing value with each passing day, the children were a vivid reminder that the lives of Liberians need not be fraught with such pervasive evil, anarchy and war. As terrified adults beseeched foreign strangers for help, Patience -- and indeed many other refugee children at the church -- were going on with their lives as if little had changed, playing pickup games of tag and soccer in the crowded St. Peter's compound. Clearly, they were simply too young to understand the depth or meaning of their parents' fear.

I told Patience that, no, I didn't know Michael Jackson.

Undeterred, she breathlessly named a few of her favorite songs as she skipped after me with several giggling friends during my rounds of interviews with other refugees.

One night a few weeks later, a squad of men thought to be Liberian soldiers broke into the church. Armed with machetes, knives, cutlasses and M-16 rifles, they shot and butchered more than 600 refugees in an orgy of violence. The killers left bodies scattered throughout the church, many of them slumped over walls and window sills. Among the dead were a number of young mothers with babies still strapped to their backs.

To this day, I don't know if Patience, her mother Lydia Nya or her friends survived the horror of that July night.

But I do know that no matter who wins this sickening war, which has killed thousands of innocents and produced an estimated half million refugees so far, Liberia likely will need a very long time to recover.

Indeed, it's difficult to imagine the process of recovery even starting until the nation's deeply tormented and traumatized civilians feel reasonably secure under new and more humane leaders and no longer need to fear their own countrymen.

More than 80 heads of state, including President Bush, recently gathered at the United Nations to discuss issues indirectly related to the suffering in Monrovia. The two-day conference, organized by the United Nations Children's Fund, was to focus on "universal problems affecting the world's children," including war, malnutrition, education, drugs, homelessness and the impact of such evils as AIDS, child labor and child exploitation.

The conference came at a time when nearly 40 countries, including a dozen in Africa, have ratified a treaty recognizing children's human rights, the first of its kind in the world.

In many ways, the Unicef summit could not be timelier -- particularly for sub-Saharan Africa, the poorest and least-developed region on earth. The world's children continue to die by the millions each year of malnutrition and preventable or treatable diseases and illnesses such as measles and diarrhea. These problems are especially severe in Africa, where the crisis is exacerbated by heavy debt burdens and by foreign lenders' demands for sharp government cutbacks in spending on health, education and other social programs.

The statistics are depressing.

Africa suffers from more civil conflicts than any other region in the world and holds by far the most refugees -- 30 million and growing. More than 45% of African children never gain access to formal education. Africa's infant mortality is the world's worst.

For the African babies who do survive, the future is not promising: Africa's average annual economic growth rate of 1.4 percent between 1970 and 1985 was barely half the annual rise in population, a shortfall that continues virtually unabated.

Such problems certainly merit the attention of world leaders. Thoughtlessness, incompetence and willful brutality were not listed as agenda items at the World Summit, but these human failings -- regularly exhibited by many of Africa's leaders -- certainly play profound roles in damaging the lives of millions of children and their parents.

Among the 19 African heads of state who attended or promised to come to the New York conference are a couple of the continent's more notable dictators, whose political decisions have caused extreme divisiveness and arguably as much suffering in their countries as any disease.

General Omar el Bashir, president of Sudan, was one. More than a year after seizing power in a military coup, the repressive Sudanese ruler is leading a charge to create a fundamentalist Islamic state in his multi-religious East African nation, thus increasing tensions and inflaming ethnic rivalries in Sudan's five-year-old civil war between the northern government and southern rebels. The human rights group Africa Watch estimates that 500,000 civilian Sudanese have died of starvation, warfare and tribal killings since 1986.

Mohamed Siad Barre, the ailing, octogenarian leader of Somalia, had planned to attend the summit but did not. Among the more notorious decisions of his 20-year rule was a 1988 order for his air force to bomb the northern Somali city of Hargeisa, heartland of the opposition Isaak clan. The widespread bombing of houses, schools and markets destroyed the once-prosperous city and killed thousands of civilians.

In a reign of terror by the Somali army, hundreds of suspected rebel sympathizers were executed. With no end in sight, the war has claimed 60,000 civilian lives and spawned 500,000 refugees.

Similarly, it was not measles, malnutrition or poverty that caused the suffering and deaths of an unknown number of civilian men, women and children in the little town of Ghinda in the rebellious northern Ethiopian region of Eritrea earlier this year. Last year at this time, Ghinda was home to 20,000 Eritreans who lived in makeshift mud huts and tents at a camp for refugees displaced by the 30-year civil war.

Last fall, children by the hundreds swarmed and cheered a group of UN workers and foreign reporters who visited the camp. But in February, yet another round of mass killing broke out between the rebels and government forces, and thousands of additional lives have been lost. The main front between the two armies -- and the focus of great destruction -- was Ghinda.

While the leaders of some of Africa's poorest nations -- Benin, the Central African Republic, the Gambia, Guinea-Bissau, Sierra Leone, Mali, Rwanda, and Uganda attended the New York summit, no one was listed to speak on behalf of the children who died at Ghinda or the ones who once played in the compound at St. Peter's church in Monrovia.

Soviets at the conference didn't point to the $ 1 billion worth of arms Moscow bestowed on the harsh regime of Ethiopia's Mengistu Haile Mariam during the last decade, just as President Bush couldn't be expected to dwell on the $ 1 billion in military aid that Washington provided the irresponsible governments of Somalia, Sudan and Liberia.

To fully assess the grim results of gun-peddling and foreign competition on the continent, one need not look farther than Angola and Mozambique, where millions of children remain displaced, crippled and orphaned after years of civil war, aided and abetted by Americans, Soviets, Cubans and South Africans. Finally, it was highly doubtful that the leaders at the Unicef summit would have cared to address the extent to which half-witted economic policies and official corruption in Africa retard human development. Certainly, many thousands of oral-rehydration kits to treat diarrhea could be bought for the hundreds of millions of dollars in emeralds, diamonds and other rare minerals annually smuggled -- often with official sanction -- out of Zambia, Zaire and Sierra Leone and sold on world markets, off the books, with precious little material benefit to the people of those countries.

One also wonders how much more corn and grain could have been grown for the children of Ethiopia and Tanzania if their leaders hadn't pursued reckless agricultural policies and traumatic peasant relocation schemes that provided no incentives to farmers and inexorably led to a sharp decline in farm production throughout the 1980s.

In some ways, the silence about these issues at the New York summit may resound louder than all the speeches.

One world leader who had been expected to attend the summit was Samuel K. Doe, the president of Liberia who was mortally wounded by rebel troops during a frenzy of killing in Monrovia last month. It was Doe's 10-year reign of corruption, his ruthless murders of opponents and his politics of tribal divisiveness that eventually led to the whirlwind the children of his country are now reaping.

His absence in New York -- indeed, the absence of any shred of government in Liberia -- should represent a timely lesson of moral failure for many of Africa's leaders, those who attended the summit as well as those who stayed at home.

Africa News


October 22, 1990


AFRICA : A TURNING POINT?


LENGTH: 1090 words


As the last decade of the century gets underway, Africa's prospects for the year 2000 appear bleak. But amid the gloomy projections of increased famine, disease and conflict, new voices are pressing the continent's leaders -- and their foreign funders -- towards political and economic reforms.

"We want our governments to re-prioritize -- to spendmore for the social programs that benefit children and less on wars and weapons," said Loatile Seleka, a 20-year old youth leader from Botswana, speaking at the World Summit for Children, held at the United Nations on September 29th and 30th in New York. "We intend to pressurize them in every way we can." 

But African governments face international pressures that compete with the demands of their citizens for increased expenditures on schools, health care and other services to improve living standards. The demand from foreign aid donors is for budget cuts and austerity.

The 20% decline in average income during the 1980s has eroded living standards in many parts of the continent to levels that existed when most of the countries became independent in the 1960s. Part of the debate now taking place is whether the economic reforms being implemented in over 30 countries under the auspices of the International Monetary Fund and the World Bank can reverse the downward trend and nudge the continent onto the path of growth.

"The 1990s will either see Africa further marginalized from the global economy, sliding further down the development scales, or the new decade will see Africa slowly reviving, taking actions needed to bring about recovery and receiving the assistance necessary to sustain it," says Edward V. K. Jaycox, World Bank vice president for Africa.

As figures presented to the World Summit for Children indicate, Africa's problems could readily worsen, if the resources are not found to attack the causes and if the population explosion continues.

Of the 28 children who die around the world each minute, seven are in Africa. As in the rest of the world, two thirds of the 10,000 African children dying daily perish from preventable causes -- diarrhea, measles, tetanus and acute respiratory infections.

The 71 leaders at the World Summit endorsed a plan of action committing their governments to a range of programs for "the survival, protection, and development of children in the 1990s." One model is a United Nations immunization campaign launched in 1985, with a goal of reaching 80% of the world's children by the end of this year. James Grant, executive director of Unicef (United Nations Children's Fund), told a summit briefing the goal is likely to be reached.

Worldwide expenditure of $ 2.5 billion a year throughout this decade could prevent 50 million child deaths, Unicef says. Unicef puts the amount needed in context by pointing out that $ 2.5 billion is what American tobacco companies spend on advertising annually and what Soviets spend on vodka each month -- and what the world puts into military expenditures every day.

According to World Bank President Barber Conable, there are signs that "donors are willing to make special efforts to increase concessional assistance and debt relief to support Africa's economic reforms." Speaking to African finance ministers and central bank governors during last month's World Bank and International Monetary Fund (IMF) annual meeting, he said Africa's moves towards economic reforms and the response by donors is cause for "guarded optimism" about Africa's outlook.

But there continues to be widespread concern about the social impact of IMF-inspired reform policies, particularly on the most powerless sectors of the population, especially children. Structural adjustment, as these reforms are known, generally entails reduced expenditure on health, education, and water resources. Unicef has led a campaign to give structural adjustment "a human face" by incorporating protections for the poor and vulnerable into the reforms. Unicef also insists that "human indicators" be considered alongside economic performance measurements in evaluating whether structural adjustment is succeeding or failing. "Many studies have shown that investment in human resources is in fact the surest way to achieve and sustain long-term economic growth," Unicef says in a presentation prepared for the Summit.

Unicef advocates debt relief as a principal means of easing pressures on adjusting countries and of improving living standards for the poor. "It would take only a little more than $ 6 billion spent on health, nutrition, water, and sanitation to save the lives of 18 billion African children by the year 2000," says Stephen Lewis, a Canadian diplomat who is special advisor on Africa to United Nations Secretary General Javier Perez de Cuellar.

With Africa's finances already stretched to the limit, where can $ 6 billion be found? "You can't say it will come from trade or investment -- they're going down," Lewis says. "You can't say it will come from foreign aid -- it's stagnating. You can't say it will come from exports -- commodity prices are plummeting." The only possible source, he argues, is debt relief. Sub-Saharan African countries are expected to spend an estimated $ 9 billion each year throughout this decade on debt payments.

But the World Bank's Jaycox says Africa's debt problem is being "overblown." Since the debt payment obligations are not now being met by many countries, he argues, debt relief will not free new funds. And he concedes that there are not any other untapped sources for Africa either. "There are no more doors to open for Africa," he says.

That makes the newest added pressure -- rising oil prices -- a clear danger. With most of Africa already engaged in austerity reform programs, there is not "a lot of room for further adjustment on their part," Jaycox says. However, the Bank and the international donor community have "virtually guaranteed" that countries implementing reforms "won't be underfinanced," he says. So he expects Africa's oil import countries to receive some additional assistance.

The Bank has also promised "a significant expansion" in support for improvements in education, health, nutrition and family planning, President Conable said in a letter to Canadian Prime Minister Brian Mulroney, on the eve of the Summit for Children. Mulroney, one of the initiators of the meeting, had appealed to the heads of numerous international agencies to pledge increased backing for programs to aid children. n

"REPRESSION CAN WORK":


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 495 words


The human rights group Africa Watch is calling for a cut in aid to Malawi because of human rights violations in the southern African nation. An Africa Watch report, "Where Silence Rules: The Suppression of Dissent in Malawi," describes dozens of political imprisonments, killings and torture during the 26-year rule of Life-President Hastings Kamuzu Banda.

Up to 20 people were killed in Lilongwe last March, the group says, when police opened fire on a crowd of protesters. Since the Banda government tightly controls domestic news outlets and restricts foreign journalists, the slaughter went largely unnoticed. "Malawi is proof that repression can work," Africa Watch says. 

The March protest, sparked by the discovery that a businessman had bribed a senior official in order to avoid arrest for killing an employee, shows the "level of popular dissatisfaction" in Malawi, according to the report.

Although Africa Watch was denied entry to Malawi, the report says interviews with dozens of Malawian exiles indicate that the government arrests dissidents arbitrarily and detains them without charge or trial, often in squalid conditions. Torture by starvation and beating is routine. One political prisoner, Martin Machipisa Munthali, has been in prison for 25 years, though his nine-year sentence expired in 1974. Other prisoners include Jack Mapanje, one of Africa's most renowned poets, who was arrested upon publication of his book Of Cameleons and Gods in 1987, and Orton and Vera Chirwa, lawyers who founded the only known opposition group, the Malawi Freedom Movement.

During Banda's rule, several exiled dissidents have died under mysterious circumstances. The Africa Watch report is dedicated to Malawian journalist Mkwapatira Mhango, who was killed by a bomb in neighboring Zambia along with members of his family. Africa Watch says the killing was intended to intimidate exiles into silence.

Although groups such as Amnesty International have periodically accused Malawi of human rights violations, the country enjoys good relations with Western countries. But it appears to be gaining critics in the United States. Late last month, seven members of Congress, including Ronald Dellums (D-CA), William Green (D-NY) and Howard Wolpe (D-MI), sent a letter to Malawian Ambassador Robert Mbaya, commending Malawi for ratifying the African Charter on Human and People's Rights but pressing for the release of political prisoners that the Charter requires. "We urge you to convey to Life-President Banda our hope that [those] held without charge might be released immediately and unconditionally unless recognizable and appropriate charges are levied before a court of law," the letter says.

Although the U.S. government acknowledges human rights abuses in Malawi, it has recommended continuing the current level of aid for 1991. This year Malawi received $ 22 million in U.S. assistance, including more than $2 million in military aid.

Felix Houphouet-Boigny has won what he says will be his last term as president of Cote d'Ivoire. The 85-year-old leader of the west African nation made the pledge at the start of last month's campaign. Opposition leader Laurent Gbagbo, who won about 20% of the votes in the official count, has accused Houghouet's ruling party of massive fraud. By Gbagbo's count, he received just under 50%, compared with just over half for the aging president.

Pro-democracy advocates in Mali have formed two new groups to work for a multiparty system in the Sahelian nation. CLOSED DOWN:


SECTION: BITS AND PIECES


LENGTH: 208 words


A five-day general strike brought normal life to a stop last week in Niger, the world's fourth largest uranium producer. The country's 60,000-member trade union movement launched the strike to press demands for multiparty democracy and an easing of economic austerity measures. The action closed the airport in the capital, Niamey, shut down schools, hospitals and government offices, and halted the mining of uranium, the country's main export.

Last Tuesday, a number of pro-democracy demonstrators were injured when they marched on a police station in the southern town of Maradi, reportedly throwing stones and setting fire to police vehicles.

Government sources told the Reuter news agency that the government of President Ali Saibou, who ran unopposed last year in the first elections since independence from France three decades ago, has rejected strikers' demands for a multiparty system and for a national conference to discuss the northwest African country's economic and political situation.

The strike follows nearly a year of growing social unrest. In February, several demonstrators were killed by government troops who fired on university students protesting cuts in spending on education and other social needs.

Africa News


November 12, 1990


SISTERHOOD, SADNESS IN A JOHANNESBURG HOSTEL


LENGTH: 1538 words


ALEXANDRA, South Africa-- A large woman strides down a gloomy concrete corridor in the fortress-like women's hostel in Alexandra township outside Johannesburg. Emerging into the daylight, she looks at the small grass quadrangle in the center of the five-story buildings, encircled with barbed wire to prevent the destruction of the only patch of green.

"We know why the fighting happened in the hostels," says the formidable woman called "Queen Phakati."

"It is because men are living singly. There are no women and children to calm the situation, and they forget -- when they fight -- that they will lose them all." 

Back inside the concrete maze, she pauses for a moment from her analysis of the recent violence to bellow cheerful greetings to other residents down the unlit corridor. "Hey girl -- how are you?"

"I am fine. And how are you my baby?"

This is a far cry from the war-ravaged men's hostels, where tired and despondent men sit in drafty rooms thinking of home.

The women's hostel is abuzz with the activity of over 2,800 women going about their daily chores. There is a feeling of sisterhood. Women stop to chat, or to complain, pausing in their washing, cooking and cleaning. They're strangely at home in the huge impersonal building, none of whose cheerfully decorated tiny rooms opens onto outdoor space.

The women pay the equivalent of about $ 10 a month for their meager space. There is no maintenance to speak of, and squabbles break out over basic facilities like water and lights.

The women are without their husbands, lovers or children. They have each other and, whether they like it or not, their interaction is just about as intimate as marriage. Four women share a bedroom of no more than about five square feet.

"I've always lived in the same room with another woman," says Elizabeth Mabiletsa, a 51-year-old company driver who has been at the hostel for fifteen years. "The two of us are friends but sometimes we don't talk. Sometimes she doesn't want to say good morning. When she's cross I just listen to my radio loud."

Mabiletsa is divorced. She has children in Rustenburg. Every week she goes home, taking money and food to them. "I am not happy here but I have no other place to go. My children cannot visit me here. I am tired now. I want to go home."

She sits with other women around a steel table outside. A man from one of the two men's hostels in Alexandra is fixing their sewing machines.

"We have the same problems in the men's hostel," says Simon Phala, listening to the women arguing about the rent, about whether the "block ladies" do anything to help them, about whether they put pressure on the town council to lower the rent or to maintain the dilapidated building.

Popular opinion has it that the "block ladies," who are supposedly elected from each block of 144 women, have become redundant. Some see them as stooges, unable to spur the council into action -- to fix the blocked drains and broken taps, to stop the stench in the blocked toilets, to deal with the piles of garbage that accumulate in dark corners, the broken windows, unreplaced corridor lights, dirty walls and lack of hot water.

Rows and rows of colorful washing flap in the wind. Women scurry from one room to another along the draughty corridors. Possibly because it is a public holiday, an air of relaxation pervades the place, and most women are ready to stop and recount at length their problems with the hostel, which they chose to name after the well-known white anti-apartheid activist Helen Joseph.

Despite the well-kept rooms, the warm smell of cooking and the feeling that somehow things are softer here than in the men's hostels, the place is clouded in depression. The women, many of whom are jobless and resort to informal sector industries like sewing and hawking food, are unhappy.

In the vast kitchens which resemble makeshift science labs, there are rows of gas burners, the tiny lockers are crammed with food and kitchenware lines the walls. Hunched over cups of tea or busy preparing food for themselves, they speak of their dissatisfaction.

"People pay rent from the first to the seventh of every month. If you pay a day later, they charge you double, " says factory worker Ma Ambe. She has lived at the hostel for ten years and is a dedicated member of the Alexandra Civic Organization (ACO), whose members include residents of the hostels and the neighboring black township.

Discontent with the conditions is boosting support for a four-month-long rent boycott initiated by ACO. More than half the residents have stopped paying rent in an attempt to force the local authorities to maintain the building or, alternatively, to convert the blocks into family units.

Since the boycott began, the situation has deteriorated. "Before we used to have water -- even if it was cold water. Now we have nothing," Ma Ambe says. The women must take their buckets to the township for water supplies.

While these women would jump at the chance to live elsewhere, there are queues of others waiting to come in. Not all the women have families or husbands; some would prefer to live in bachelor quarters. But many of the older women are reluctant to stop paying rent and risk eviction.

The civic organization is pressuring the council to make public a financial accounting of how the hostel dwellers' rent is used. So far, the council has refused. But executive committee chairman Dennis Tau says the council is experiencing serious financial difficulties. "The area where the residence is situated is currently being upgraded," he said, explaining the frequent interruptions in the water and electrical service.

He said negotiations are underway to privatize the hostel. "It is the council's wish that the residences should be converted into housing units as we are presently experiencing a housing crisis."

Meanwhile, hostel residents face an array of difficulties. Despite an advanced prison-like security system where electronic gates seal off blocks at the switch of a button from the supervisor's office, most women say they never feel safe. Men are prohibited from entering the building, but few security guards are in evidence. According to the women, one or two rapes occur almost every weekend. "We are no longer safe because the police don't watch by the gate," says resident Violet Motobo. "Many of us knock off very late. The police go to sleep and there is no security at night."

When a woman is attacked, she shouts "Indoda!" (man) and other women armed with sticks and brooms rush to her aid.

"Then we hit him. Some men have been killed in here," another women says. "But no one gets arrested because they don't know who killed him."

Motobo goes off to find Jane Mogotso who recently found a man hiding in her room. Despite the vastness of the building and endless corridors lined with identical rooms, the communications network is swift and efficient. Motobo reappears minutes later with wizened-looking Mogotso, a cleaner at the family planning center in the hostel complex, which serves hostel-dwellers and the township. Mogotso recounts how she returned from her early-morning washing to find a man wedged behind her cupboard.

The friendly support structure acts as a buffer from the harshness of the impersonal living quarters. The residents contribute small amounts of money when someone dies. When a woman gives birth or falls ill she is rushed to the hospital in one of the few cars owned by the luckier hostel-dwellers, or an ambulance is called from a hostel resident's private telephone. Few have their own telephones, and the communal phones never work.

But limited resources, cramped living conditions and a lack of recreation resources undercut the sisterhood, fueling petty rivalries, tribal differences and jealousy. "There is often fighting in the rooms, sometimes even physical fighting," Mabiletsa says.

There are sometimes squabbles over things like the scarce water supply, the lack of space and -- given language differences -- which radio stations to play. Sometimes residents accuse each other of stealing washing, keeping the light on too late or waking everyone in the room when returning from night shift.

Bribery and corruption are other sore points. Some women get rooms quickly because they know town councilors. And some "favorite" men are allowed to visit the police ladies, the security guards employed by the council.

"We used to have a big room where we used to sew together," sighs Ma Ambe. "Now the councilors' wives use this room for their sewing business. Why should we pay rent for these ladies to do their business?"

There are no electric outlets in the room and the more resourceful women run their radios, hi-fi's and fridges from the single light bulb hanging from the ceiling. Such "unfair advantages" are the source of more resentment and daily bickering.

"The reason we fight is because of our situation," says Motobo. "There is no privacy. If we had our own rooms it would be better."

Somehow the women remain stoic, even cheerful, about their unenviable lot. One sums up the situation: "Yes, we are laughing but, really, it is terrible."

Africa News


November 12, 1990


LOCAL COUNCILS FACE MOUNTING PROTESTS


SECTION: UNRAVELING APARTHEID


LENGTH: 877 words


Before the changes which led to the opening of talks between South African President F. W. de Klerk and African National Congress (ANC) leader Nelson Mandela, rent boycotts and other protests by residents of hundreds of black townships helped build pressure for ending apartheid.

Today, with those talks moving unevenly ahead, many of the rent boycotts continue. And the ANC has stepped up efforts to end support for the system of segregated local governments.

"We call on white municipalities to dissolve and replace the existing structures with local, non-racial constituent assemblies that will negotiate the restructuring of the cities," Thozamile Botha, an ANC national executive committee member, told a consultative conference on local government convened by the ANC in Johannesburg last month. 

A coalition of 38 anti-apartheid civic organizations in the Johannesburg area has begun a campaign of boycotts and protest marches to put pressure on white and black municipal council members.

The pressures have prompted a number of resignations, notably that of Tom Boya, mayor of the Daveyton township outside Pretoria, who chaired the Urban Municipalities of South Africa, a nationwide body. "I realized there was no room anymore for a person to serve in a structure created by the government," Boya said.

Responding to the boycotts, town councils controlled by the Conservative Party have cut off water and electricity in several areas. Councils dominated by South Africa's ruling National Party generally have opted to negotiate with township residents.

In several of the largest urban centers, white city councils face a further complication. Since the councils were elected, many white voters have moved out of the cities and have been replaced by black residents, whose affairs remain under the control of the white councilors. Late last month, according to the Johannesburg weekly, the New Nation, black city dwellers occupied the Johannesburg city council chambers, demanding and receiving a hearing for their grievances.

Meanwhile, a mounting black death toll from the violence that has erupted between rival groups shows no sign of abating. The conflict has spread from Natal province, where nearly 5,000 people have died, into various parts of the Transvaal region -- which includes Johannesburg and Pretoria -- and to townships around Cape Town.

Much of the fighting has pitted ANC supporters against followers of Mangosutho Gatsha Buthelezi, leader of the Inkatha Freedom Party and chief minister of the KwaZulu homeland. Other clashes have involved the Azanian People's Organization and the Pan Africanist Congress, two black consciousness-oriented movements that are critical of the ANC's pro-negotiations stance.

The government responded toughly to the Transvaal disturbances. Coming on the heels of de Klerk's October decision to lift the state of emergency in Natal, the deployment of special forces and the imposition of curfews in the Transvaal was met with strong criticism by the ANC and other government opponents.

Mandela has charged that a "third force" is promoting the violence. Right-wing white groups and members of government security services have been trying to undermine de Klerk's peace initiative, the ANC says.

Government critics believe one element of this suspected "third force" may be members of two hit squads created by the South African police and military in the 1980s to attack anti-apartheid activists inside the country and in exile abroad. There are charges that members of the Mozambican Resistance Movement (Renamo), as well as other insurgents that Pretoria armed and trained to destabilize neighboring countries, may also be involved.

Gay J. McDougall, author of a critical examination of "death squad" activities recently published by the Washington, D.C.-based Lawyers' Committee for Civil Rights Under Law, warns that the government must act quickly to avert spiraling bloodshed. The study says Pretoria must: extend the investigation now underway; institute criminal procedures against anyone fomenting the violence; and extradite suspected death squad members from neighboring countries. Otherwise, the Lawyers' Committee says, "the assassination bureaus may simply shift to the cover of sympathetic right-wing organizations."

The township tensions are also widely attributed to overcrowded living conditions, rising unemployment and the political uncertainties that have thrust both the ANC and Buthelezi's Inkatha into the new position of vying openly for public support.

Some of the violent outbursts in the Transvaal have involved Buthelezi's followers in the numerous single-sex hostels throughout the area, where residents live far from home, separated from their families for 10 months or more of the year.

The problems confronting male hostel dwellers are shared by the smaller number of women living in hostels. Though they haven't responded with violence, the women have begun to express grievances in other ways. In the following article from the Johannesburg Weekly Mail, Phillipa Garson reports that women from a hostel in Alexandra, outside Johannesburg, have joined a rent boycott -- despite their own vulnerability to eviction.

Africa News


November 12, 1990


UNITA AID MADE CONDITIONAL


LENGTH: 495 words


For the first time in four years, Congress has placed restrictions on lethal assistance to the Angolan rebel movement Unita. Unita has received U.S. arms since 1986, when a decade-old Congressional ban on covert involvement in Angola, known as the Clark amendment, was overturned. The new legislation will suspend Unita aid completely when the Angolan government agrees to a cease-fire, multiparty elections and a suspension of Soviet military aid. 

The restrictions come at a time of renewed efforts to end the 16-year conflict between Unita and the Angolan government, with Portugal acting as the mediator in peace talks.

Congress adopted the Unita aid limits following an unusual open debate on the issue in the House of Representatives on October 17th. The House defeated a proposal by Ronald Dellums and Mervyn Dymally -- California Democrats and Congressional Black Caucus members -- to end all CIA support for Unita.

After rejecting the total aid ban by a vote of 246 to 175, House members approved a complex amendment sponsored by Stephen Solarz (D-NY) linking continuing aid to progress in the peace talks. The measure was adopted by a margin of 213 to 200, but then was almost defeated when Henry Hyde (R-OH), leading the opposition to both amendments, used a parliamentary maneuver to force another vote. The second tally ended in a tie, 206 to 206, which was broken by House Speaker Thomas Foley who cast the deciding "yeah."

A House/Senate conference committee approved a modified version of a bill containing most of the Solarz provisions. The new law caps 1991 spending for "weapons, ammunition or other lethal equipment for Unita" at a level no greater than that of 1990. It also requires suspension of all lethal aid if the Angolan government takes three specific steps. Luanda must agree to a cease-fire and a political settlement of the conflict, set a "reasonable timetable for free and fair multiparty elections," and stop receiving weapons from the Soviet Union.

Half the Unita aid for 1991 must be withheld by the administration until the Congressional intelligence committees agree to disbursement.

Conferees dropped a provision from the Solarz bill that would have permitted certification of Angola's compliance with these conditions by the president, or by the Congress in a joint resolution of both Houses. The compromise leaves certification to the president alone, who must also recertify compliance three months later. At that time, U.S. aid may be resumed if the Angolan government is not adhering to the prescribed conditions. Aid may also be resumed if the Angolan government launches an offensive against Unita or there is "clear and credible evidence" that a military offensive is planned.

Late last month, Angola's ruling MPLA party adopted plans for establishing a multiparty system by the end of March, to be followed by a national census and elections within 36 months of a truce in the fighting.

Africa News


November 12, 1990


BUDGET-CUTTING CONGRESS RAISES AFRICA AID


SECTION: U.S.-AFRICA


LENGTH: 1173 words


American economic assistance to Africa will rise in 1991, after last-minute Congressional budget deliberations that raised Africa's appropriation by 40% over the administration's request.

Congress also voted new guidelines on how the aid must be spent, linked some aid monies for Kenya to progress on human rights and prohibited any military assistance for Liberia, Somalia, Sudan or Zaire. In a separate action, Congress placed conditions on continued covert assistance to the Angolan rebel movement Unita (see page 13).

Egypt, which is grouped with "near east" nations and not Africa, will continue as the leading U.S. aid recipient, along with Israel. A total of $ 2.1 billion was approved for the north African nation -- $ 815 million in economic assistance and $ 1.3 billion in military aid. In addition, Egypt's $6.7 billion military indebtedness to the U.S. was written off by Congress. 

The breakthrough on the sub-Saharan aid increase came during late-hour discussions which preceded adoption of the foreign aid appropriations bill on October 27th, as Congress rushed to adjourn for the year. Following the lead of the House of Representatives, key Senators from both parties agreed to $ 800 million in development aid to sub-Saharan Africa, reversing a recent downward trend.

The Congressional Black Caucus initiated the 1991 increase earlier this year by proposing a $ 1 billion memorial to Rep. Mickey Leland, a Caucus member who died in a plane crash in Ethiopia last year. The House approved $ 800million in June -- $ 240 million more than the Bush administration requested. But there seemed little prospect at the time for the increase to survive the lengthy budget-cutting debate.

However, when the Senate got down to final consideration of the $ 15 billion foreign aid bill in October, the $ 800 million figure was endorsed by two key members of the Appropriations Committee: Patrick J. Leahy (D-VT), who chairs the foreign operations subcommittee, which has jurisdiction over the legislation, and Robert W. Kasten Jr. (R-WI), the subcommittee's ranking Republican.

"Given the level of need, we decided we wanted to increase the Development Fund for Africa (DFA) as much as we could," one Senate aide said. "We made it clear that this was a priority and we were not going to go below the House level."

House Democratic Whip William Gray (D-PA), along with Ronald Dellums (D-CA) and other Caucus members, lobbied their Senate colleagues for the aid increase, Congressional sources say. Among those who supported the larger figure were Nancy Kassebaum (R-KS), Edward Kennedy (D-MA) and Paul Simon (D-IL), who chairs the Senate Africa subcommittee. House Africa subcommittee chairman Howard Wolpe and the subcommittee staff were also key promoters of the aid increase and the new policy language.

The administration was seeking $ 560 million for the DFA budget, plus $ 56 million in Economic Support Funds (ESF), a form of aid generally linked to U.S. strategic concerns. The total administration package of $ 616 represented a$ 20 million increase over 1990 -- when Africa received the least American aid in a decade. But the administration request was still a $ 50 million drop from 1989 levels. The largest previous allocation for Africa was $ 759 million in 1985, when foreign aid expenditures peaked prior to Congressional adoption of the Gramm-Rudman budget-cutting directives.

The Development Fund for Africa was established by Congress in 1988 to emphasize a cost-effective, human-centered and long-term approach to assistance for the continent. But previous attempts by the House to spell out policy language for the fund in legislation have failed to win approval in the Senate.

This year's act states that the purpose of the fund is to guarantee the poor majority a role in economic growth "that is equitable, participatory, environmentally sustainable, and self-reliant." The measure also requires the Agency for International Development (AID), which administers foreign aid, to "take into account the local-level perspectives of the rural and urban poor in sub-Saharan Africa, including women."
 
LINKING AID TO HUMAN RIGHTS

The new foreign aid bill places restrictions on assistance to five sub-Saharan nations. A Senate-sponsored amendment requires the U.S. president to certify that the government of Kenya "is taking steps on the release of political prisoners, stopping physical abuse, restoring independence of the judiciary and restoring freedoms of expression" before committing any Economic Support Funds or military financing to the east African nation. The Bush administration has projected $ 7 million in ESF for Kenya in the coming year, and another $8 million in military financing. A planned $ 26 million in development assistance is unaffected by the amendment.

For Zaire, Congress stipulates that development assistance be dispensed only through non-governmental organizations, and not the government, which is widely regarded as corrupt. The new law also prohibits all military aid to Zaire, as well as for Liberia, Sudan and Somalia.

In other actions affecting Africa, Congress approved $ 13 million for the African Development Foundation, a U.S. institution which supports development projects in Africa with small grants. The administration had requested $ 11.5 million. Another $15 million was appropriated for countries in Africa "which support conservation and biological diversity." The act also authorizes the president to support natural resources and wildlife management activities of "friendly countries, international organizations, and private and voluntary organizations" by providing "excess" materials and by transporting these items "without charge on a space-available basis." In addition, the Defense Department appropriation bill provides $ 15 million for transportation of relief supplies worldwide.
 
SPENDING THE INCREASE

With a few exceptions, Congress is leaving the allocation of the $ 800 million development fund to AID. The exceptions are: $ 50 million for the ten-nation Southern African Development Coordination Conference (SADCC); $ 3.5 million to support participation by disadvantaged South Africans in private enterprise development; and up to five percent of the $ 800 million toward AID's costs of administering the funds.

During Capitol Hill deliberations, administration officials opposed the increase, saying it would exceed AID's capacity. A current hiring freeze prohibits AID from adding personnel. But the argument was rejected by Congressional supporters.

The task of managing disbursement now falls to Scott Spangler, who was sworn in as assistant administrator of AID for Africa on November 2nd. A presidential appointee, Spangler is an Arizona business executive and mechanical engineer who was active in George Bush's 1988 campaign. He served as a Ford Foundation fellow in Africa from 1963 to 1966 and has traveled to 18 African countries, according to his official biography.

Africa News


November 12, 1990


Aid Arrives -- Slowly


SECTION: CONFLICT


LENGTH: 1997 words


A West African peace-keeping force has finally secured the Liberian capital of Monrovia enough to allow the arrival of emergency food aid. But conditions in the city, where an estimated 50-60 people are dying daily from hunger, dehydration and related diseases, remain chaotic.

A Belgian physician from the group Doctors Without Borders has blamed aid agencies and foreign governments for sending evaluation teams to look at the damage rather than to work on the problems. Dr. Pieter Rykelykhuizen told the Associated Press in neighboring Sierra Leone, where he arrived from Monrovia on a U.S. Marine helicopter, that "relief workers and not assessment teams are needed" because "people are dying like flies." Rykelykhuizen said a U.S. shipment of nearly 2000 tons of rice and 40 tons of cooking oil on a chartered French ship is being off loaded much too slowly. He also said Liberians are now so malnourished that rice alone will not save them. Milk, fish and meat are essential, he said.

Aid agencies say security problems are delaying the shipment and hampering the distribution of supplies, but they are hopeful that the five-nation force sent to Liberia from the Economic Community of West African States (Ecowas) will soon have the situation under control. 

The Ecowas force was named to reflect its mission -- the Economic Community Monitoring Group, or Ecomog. But instead of being an observer or peace-keeping force, Ecomog's 7,000 soldiers have become embroiled in the continuing war.

"Eco-bog" is what one West African newsmagazine dubbed the morass in which the force finds itself, as it tries to separate Charles Taylor's National Patriotic Front of Liberia (NPFL) from the breakaway Independent National Patriotic Front of Liberia, led by Taylor's rival, Prince Johnson. And then there are the remnents of the Liberian army that was loyal to the late President Samuel Doe. Critics say that instead of imposing order among the three groups, Ecomog has become a fourth faction in the fighting.

But the worst blow to the West African force was the September kidnapping of Samuel Doe from Ecomog headquarters by Johnson's troops, who tortured and killed the President while recording the event on videotape. Although cynics might say that Doe got as good as he gave -- he was an infamous torturer himself -- Ecomog's failure to protect him during his visit to its headquarters seriously damaged its reputation both inside and outside Liberia. And the debacle widened the rift among Ecowas members, not all of whom support the Liberian intervention.

Nevertheless, this month has brought new hopes that the worst is over. David Nimely, Doe's successor as head of what was left of the Liberian army, has left the country. His forces and Johnson's have agreed to a ceasefire. And although proposed peace talks in Mali were cancelled last week after Taylor appeared unwilling to attend, an NPFL spokesman said the group does want a ceasefire and plans to attend an Ecowas summit on November 27th.

There also were signs that Ecowas may be mending some internal fences. Togo, which had criticized the Liberia action, now appears ready to back it, although it remains the only French-speaking Ecowas member to do so.

Meanwhile, an interim Liberian government chosen at an Ecowas-sponsored meeting of exiles on August 30th was scheduled to arrive in Monrovia this week under Ecomog protection. Led by university professor Amos Sawyer, the group is charged with preparing the country for free elections and beginning the daunting task of rehabilitation. A group of broadcast journalists and technicians from Nigeria, Ghana and Sierra Leone is already in Monrovia, setting up a radio station so the interim government can spread its message throughout the country, most of which is still loosely controlled by Taylor's forces.

However, the reporter who became the voice from Liberia for millions of people around the world this year will not be playing that role again. The British Broadcasting Company's West Africa correspondent, Elizabeth Blunt, is returning to London for a new assignment. Following are excerpts from her Liberia journal, reprinted here with the permission of the BBC quarterly magazine Focus on Africa.
 
Monrovia, June 27-July 14

The day of my arrival was the day of the great peace march, tens of thousands of Liberians led by their priests, Imams and preachers, coming out to ask the President to [resign], and finding themselves in the middle of a riot of gunfire. The last Air Guinea flight brought me in over a deserted city, to colleagues still shaking after spending two hours huddled in an outhouse while bullets whistled around.

That night the water stopped flowing; two days later the electricity, and on Monday, July 2nd the rebels attacked the outskirts of the city.

That was the beginning of the anarchy: suddenly the soldiers, knowing the time left to them was very short, abandoned all discipline. Anyone with a gun could take anything from anyone without a gun. For a week there were no shops, no markets, no offices working. The phones were cut, the radio off the air. After curfew, soldiers looted and settled scores.

But people become used to almost anything. Ten days later, we were learning to live with the shots in the night, the daily search for water and food. Some shops opened and the Ministry of Information pulled itself together enough to revoke my accreditation and send me home.

It seemed bad enough at the time: yet what came later makes it seem a time of ease. This was before fighting engulfed the whole city, before rockets fired from the executive mansion began to land in crowded suburbs, before the massacre [of 600 people] at St. Peter's Church [by Doe's troops].

Finally the fighting got more and more bogged down, with no side able to overcome the other, and diplomatic moves became more important -- the Ecowas summit , and the decision to send the peacekeeping force.

Summarized like that, it sounds as if I have had a very exciting few months. And it's true. For a journalist, it is exciting to have a good story, and to see it develop from the beginning. It's exciting to be right in the middle of events, especially when you know that it is your radio which is the most listened to, the way that most people get their information.

There's no doubt at all that throughout the conflict in Liberia, the BBC has had the nearest thing possible to a saturation audience. Some afternoons it seems as if every man, woman and child in Liberia who understands English and has a radio is listening to the BBC. The BBC was the way Liberians got their news, and anyone who wanted to talk to Liberians was doing it on the BBC.

Which is where we come to the major problem -- the way in which the BBC has been used in this conflict, and used far more skillfully by some parties than others. Charles Taylor, in particular, built his campaign on publicity and used the BBC to do it.

Throughout the war, the National Patriotic Front has used the BBC as its platform, while the government side has treated the media with the utmost suspicion. President Doe has given huge press conferences, he has invited me to come and talk to him in the Executive Mansion, but has only once agreed to be interviewed, and that on a very scratchy radiophone, since the conflict started.

When one side talks freely and the other refuses, what should the BBC do? Should we ration Taylor to the same amount of airtime as Doe?

We compromised by agreeing to talk to Taylor but asking difficult questions, about his past and his financial record and so on. But this is not entirely the answer. Charles Taylor is a wonderful talker. An exasperated interviewer once said, "No one seems to have a good word to say for you, Mr. Taylor". His reply -- "They said the same about Jesus Christ himself when he was on earth"-- got the audience right back on his side.

And even restricting his air time didn't always have the expected results. I was once harangued by a senior Ministry of Information official because the BBC didn't broadcast one of Taylor's press conferences. What were we hiding, he wanted to know. Was Taylor still alive? And how else would the government know what he was up to?

Then there's the problem of the reporter's perspective. As it chanced, I saw the war virtually exclusively from the government side of the lines. In most wars, the government concerned would have been delighted: most governments move heaven and earth to stop reporters consorting with their rebels. But the Liberian war is not most wars, and another of the harangues I got from the Ministry of Information was about why I never reported the nasty things the rebels were doing, only the nasty things on the government side.

And this is true. However conscientiously I report that the rebels are also committing atrocities, they are not atrocities I have seen with my own eyes, and they are never going to be as vivid.

And one should never underestimate the journalist's inbuilt instinct to side with the underdog, to make mischief, to cock a snook at authority. It took a long time before President Doe began to look like the underdog in his war with Charles Taylor.

So where does that leave the BBC? With a big audience, certainly, and a big responsibility. And with the accusation that it was BBC airwaves that let the rebels get as far as they did, and with the fear that this criticism may be justified. But also with the sense that the balance has shifted. In the beginning, President Doe was known by his acts, Charles Taylor only by his words. Now, enough time has gone by for Taylor's acts to begin to speak for themselves. Now armed men on both sides are trying to find BBC reporters and kill them. I suppose I should be happy.
 
To Monrovia with Ecomog, September 3

The Ecomog peacekeeping force arrived in Monrovia on August 25th. I travelled to Liberia by boat from Freetown a week later.

My first drive up to town from the port was a shock. Everything shattered, broken and filthy. Every building lining the road from the port was full of holes -- peppered with bullets and holed by shells.

And the people: what was left of the population of Monrovia wandered to and fro in endless lines, hunting, hoping to find something to eat. In six weeks, prosperous looking friends had slimmed so much I could hardly recognize them. Poorer people were hollow cheeked and gaunt. This wasn't the kind of food shortage where only the rich could afford to eat. No matter how rich you were, there was no rice.

Days fell into a routine: Ecomog briefings -- not very exciting, since Ecomog was making little movement: the hunger was the most important story.
 
Sunday, September 9

Routine visit to Ecomog headquarters. Waiting for an interview when there was a flurry of excitement -- President Doe had arrived unexpectedly.

Then confused shouting -- Johnson's men were there. Soon they were striding up and down stairs, loud and threatening. Voices rose, guns were being cocked. Then a Nigerian officer grabbed me. "In there: lie down. Lie down." Prince Johnson came into the office where I was, shouting and raving, two Ecomog officers trying to calm him. I saw the hands of one of them shaking as he tried to open a can of beer for Johnson. Then I heard Johnson yelling, "Men, open fire. Open fire!" And that was it. For an hour and a half I lay on the floor in the middle of a deafening gunbattle. I could hear Johnson: "Let's get Doe. We'll get him this time." Ecomog officers were begging, pleading for sanity. Every time it stopped and I raised my head, the firing started again.

Finally there was silence, stunned silence. The survivors crept out. The corridor was pooled with blood. Bodies were everywhere. And President Doe was gone.

Africa News


November 12, 1990


"A MATTER OF MONTHS"


LENGTH: 2418 words


VOM: What is the time frame for independence?

Issaias: Precision on a matter of this kind is impossible. What is clear is that compared to the long struggle we have conducted -- over a quarter of a century now -- the road ahead is far shorter. But in this sensitive period, it is difficult to give an exact timetable and assert with full certainty that the task will be accomplished in one, two or three months.

What I can say is that, even with all the uncertainties, it is now a matter of months. Even if something unpredictable happens, we are confident that we will be able to handle it. Our desire is to bring the suffering to an end soon. We have full confidence in our capacity to do so. 
 
Economic Policy

VOM: Do we have blueprints for economic development on this eve of independence? Can we avoid the pitfalls of economic crises in which many Third World countries find themselves?

Issaias: There are lessons we can learn from the economic failures of the Third World. Many countries are grappling with intractable economic problems, even those which possess substantial resources, including oil. The problem is related to the overall political structure, to issues of management.

In many cases, political power is abused to advance the personal wealth of those at the top, at the expense of national interest. Corruption is rife, millions of dollars are embezzled and siphoned off. Big capital often extracts inordinate profits.

There is a rampant brain drain because intolerant systems cannot accommodate educated and skilled manpower and, consequently, drive trained people abroad. There is a tendency to suppress local talent. For these reasons, many viable community projects fail.

We will have to avoid these malaises in tomorrow's Eritrea. For this, the political system must be healthy; free from corruption, nepotism and theft. The proper interaction and balance between foreign and private capital must be rigorously defined and regulated by law.

We have to embark on intensive training programs to upgrade local skills and increase our capacity to absorb and cope with advanced technology. At the same time, we have to avoid the trappings of capital-intensive enterprises in agriculture and manufacturing industry when better results can be produced on a small scale with local skill and resources.

Foreign capital will certainly play a big role in investment. But there must be proper mechanisms to regulate the transactions and harness it to the national interest. State capital can create obstacles to private and foreign capital unless it is kept within reasonable bounds. It must not be permitted to exceed its limits and strangle the economy.

In short, our approach will be based on realism and a prudent evaluation of all these factors.
 
Democracy and Participation

VOM: Do you think that the preconditions exist for the multiparty system which the EPLF says it intends to establish in Eritrea? Will the EPLF allow religious parties? How do you respond to the charge of those who allege that the EPLF will monopolize power in an independent Eritrea?

Issaias: The EPLF has already underlined its commitment to create a multiparty system in Eritrea. This position stems from its strong belief that this is the only system that can guarantee national cohesion, development, peace and stability in Eritrea.

In a future Eritrea, different interest groups will naturally seek to pursue their own interests. Their freedom to do this can only be secure when each individual's or group's basic political rights are guaranteed by law. If any individual, group or section of the society is allowed to be above the law and to politically, economically or culturally dominate the others, there cannot be the peace and stability which will be a key to our progress.

For these reasons, a one-party system will neither enhance national security or stability nor accelerate economic development. In fact, a one-party system could be a major threat to the very existence of our country.

However, the practicalities of forming a multiparty system need to take into account the specifics of our situation. To start with, it has to be accepted that at present our armed struggle for national liberation overrides any political principles that do not contribute to strengthening the objective of the current phase. In practice, there cannot be basic rights while we are under occupation. At present, therefore, the Eritrean people's priority ought to be strengthening the armed struggle to achieve our national liberation.

The EPLF sees its mandate as being limited to the time when independence is achieved. It [the EPLF] might perhaps be charged with provisional responsibilities to run the country until such time as a genuine multiparty system is established. As to what will happen to the EPLF after that, nobody can answer. In general, even though each member might have his/her opinion or principles, it would not be right to seek to impose them on others.

The existing organizations are not the microcosm of the future parties; they do not necessarily reflect the spectrum of political parties that may develop in an independent Eritrea.

A controversial point often raised in relation to political pluralism is whether the model is sustainable in Third World countries, with their heterogeneous societies and in the absence of the industrial and political developments of the West. There is an element of truth in this concern, although it is sometimes deliberately overemphasized in order to curb democratization.

Of course, if there are internal or external forces that seek to foment tribal and ethnic differences -- and if the political parties become the flagships for these tendencies -- the result will be debilitating instability and civil strife. The casualty in this case will not only be pluralism as a model but democracy itself. There is thus a necessity for consistent political work to enlighten the public to these dangers.

Religious parties in Eritrea would, sooner or later, spark civil strife and hence jeopardize national unity. So while the freedom of faith must be respected and religious institutions enabled to proselytize without restrictions of any kind, there must be a ban on religious political parties.

The same rule should apply to regional and ethnic movements. These issues must be sorted out, the provisions and restrictions defined unambiguously by law, before embarking on pluralism. An ethnic or religious group which constitutes a majority may want to form a political party to assert its dominance. At the other end of the scale, a minority group may exploit legitimate grievances, induced by uneven development and denial of opportunities in the past, as a basis for forming a separate political party.

However, these are matters that require the equality, in real terms, of all citizens before the law. They should not be the preserve of a specific religious or ethnic group. Political parties should represent different premises and alternative programs for national political, economic and social development. This is the political climate underpinning a stable and healthy pluralist system.

In the case of Eritrea, there are other factors that must be borne in mind. Small as it is, Eritrea remains a heterogeneous society with diverse linguistic and religious constituencies. The administrative system must therefore be highly decentralized with the different regions -- delineated on geographical and other criteria -- enjoying scope to develop specific institutions that correspond to their cultural particularities. The actual form this takes may vary. But the degree of devolution to the constituent parts needs to be quite high. Political pluralism should take this into account. The whole concept requires careful analysis.

Will the EPLF become intolerant? True, the EPLF has paid heavy sacrifices, carried the burden of struggle alone and surmounted many obstacles. This reality perhaps causes uneasiness in some quarters. But the EPLF is perfectly aware of the limitations of its mandate. It has never entertained the illusion of monopolizing power in Eritrea.

VOM: Why is a referendum [the EPLF calls for a UN-supervised vote to determine Eritrea's future] necessary when the Eritrean people have already affirmed their aspiration for independence through the armed struggle?

Issaias: The issue is a question of principle and international law. Our armed struggle is only a means to an end, not an end itself. It was waged to enable the Eritrean people to exercise their self-determination.

There is no alternative to referendum. The overwhelming majority of Eritreans have demonstrated their desire for self-determination in practical terms through struggle and sacrifice. But the need to legitimize our aspirations in the eyes of the world cannot be ignored. If we simply use the fait accompli of force to achieve our ends, we will be betraying the real objective of our struggle.

It is not a question of emotion, it is a matter of international law. To establish normal relations with other nations based on mutual respect, we must be accepted by the world community of nations.

VOM: A segment of our society does not permit women to play a role in the struggle or have a dignified status in the community. How do you see their role in the coming years?

Issaias: I believe this is an issue long settled. Perhaps one of the hallmarks of our struggle is its achievement in raising the degree and level of women's participation that exceeds, in some areas, even those of men. Obviously, it is impossible to eradicate all inequalities and change attitudes in the society as a whole in a matter of ten to twenty years. But so much has been achieved already, thanks to the struggle of women themselves and the policies of the Front.

We are not interested in token changes. The depth of social transformation -- in relation to the status of women - must not be measured by the ability of a few women to assume high levels of authority. The job involves paving the way for the full participation of women -- their ability to assume the same functions as their male counterparts in the emerging and future Eritrea. Compared to the realities in our region and the experiences in other places, the opportunities for women in Eritrea are unlimited.

There may, of course, still be isolated cases where religious leaders or highly traditional communities attempt to curb the role of women on the basis of the scriptures or communal law. On the whole, we have handled these obstacles with caution and patience. At this stage, there is almost total consensus in the society as a whole. People recognize and have taken for granted the equality and growing role of women in a future Eritrea.
 
External Policies

VOM: Why does the EPLF continue to release Ethiopian POWs while the Dergue [the Ethiopian government] keeps in jail and tortures captured EPLF fighters? How can we exchange our captured fighters if we set free all Ethiopian POWs?

Issaias: We have never expected and do not pursue a policy of exchanging prisoners of war. The Dergue continues to routinely execute captured EPLF members. The few who may be released are emaciated by intense torture and years of imprisonment.

The Dergue also routinely disowns its captured soldiers. So we cannot reasonably entertain the possibility of exchanging prisoners with this regime. We tried to put pressure on the regime through the intermediary of international organizations - such as the International Committee of the Red Cross. But our efforts have not borne fruit.

In these circumstances we cannot lower our moral standards in order to counter the regime. We cannot execute POWs because the Dergue massacres captured fighters. The respect for life and the humane treatment of POWs stem from moral values that cannot and should not be eroded by the brutality of the enemy.

VOM: What is the extent of Israeli involvement against Eritrea? What are the motives?

Issaias: The Ethiopian government has been courting the Arab states in order to isolate the Eritrean cause. But with the recent changes in the Soviet Union, the Dergue began to have serious doubts about the prospects of continued military support from the latter and began to search for alternative sources. To this end, it suddenly renewed diplomatic relations with Israel. The objectives of this measure were clear: to enlist the military assistance of Israel and also extend the alliance for restoring good relations with the U.S.

The Israelis, on their part, perceive considerable benefits in this relationship. From their point of view, there is a threat to their security from Arab control of the Red Sea and they consequently strive to forge alliances with friendly littoral states. This is the general context underpinning Israeli interest in Ethiopia.

The problem with this approach is the durability of the Addis Ababa regime and what it portends for the future. For the moment, the Falasha issue [the attempt by Ethiopian Jews, known as Falashas, to emigrate to Israel] has prompted Israel to give limited assistance to Ethiopia, mainly in the training of pilots and cooperation in security. So far this has not gone beyond tactical assistance. People should not overemphasize the relations between Ethiopia and Israel.

VOM: Access to the sea is an issue of utmost concern to Ethiopia. The EPLF has often hinted that this is a matter that can be amicably resolved between two neighboring countries. What does this involve?

Issaias: The EPLF does not intend to use the Eritrean ports as an instrument to strangle Ethiopia or exacerbate its economic problems. Our vision transcends such issues to encompass wide-ranging and constructive cooperation between the two peoples.

Both sides have to recognize that they are neighbors -- that neither side can benefit at the expense of the other. Mutually beneficial arrangements regulating cooperation in industry, agriculture, communications and port facilities can be worked out once a genuine commitment to work for the common good prevails on both sides.

The EPLF believes that the current climate of hostility and conflict in the region as a whole can gradually be overcome and replaced by a spirit of mutual respect and cooperation.

Africa News


November 12, 1990


EPLF CHIEFGRILLED BY PUBLIC


SECTION: INTERVIEW


LENGTH: 443 words


Wars that range across remote, technically undeveloped areas do not lend themselves to press conferences nor to direct interaction between local people and those who claim to fight on their behalf. So to mark the beginning of the 30th year of the war for Eritrea's independence from Ethiopia, the radio service of the Eritrean People's Liberation Front (EPLF) decided to air a talk-show with EPLF Secretary-General, Issaias Afewerki.

Since a call-in show is impossible in a place where telephones reach only limited areas, the radio service, known as VOM, advertised the plan on the air and solicited questions by mail. By the closing date of mid-August, over 1,100 questions had been received; after weeding out repetitions, VOM editors distilled the number of queries to 540. 

According to the VOM moderator of the interview, many listeners asked for personal details about the EPLF leader, including his age, educational background and experiences in the armed struggle. Ducking the questions, Issaias -- a former engineering student in his early 40s -- said the time for personal reminiscences may come after the war is over.

But the VOM persisted. The moderator said that the EPLF practice of rarely talking about its leadership, although designed to avoid cults of personality, poses a danger "that this routine evasion of personal details imparts an image of a struggle led by anonymous people." The moderator urged Issaias to "make a few comments on these personal details, both in order to break this taboo and in view of the big number of questions asked on the topic."

Still, Issaias demurred. The struggle is rich with heroes and martyrs, he said, and when the history of Eritrea's war is recorded, there should be an attempt to reclaim those experiences for the nation as a whole. "If time and circumstances allow, if I will still be around," Issaias said, "I would be prepared to contribute my share to this collective undertaking. I would like to ask those who have posed the questions to excuse me until such an opportune moment arises."

Although the avoidance of personal questions must have left many Eritrean listeners unsatisfied, Issaias responded in detail to hundreds of other questions, and VOM broadcast the lengthy conversation over a seven-day period.

Parts of the interview appear in the October issue of the EPLF publication Adulis, from which the following questions and answers are excerpted. (For a view from the other side, see excerpts from the interview with Ethiopian President Mengistu Haile Mariam conducted by Charlayne Hunter-Gault for the MacNeil/Lehrer Newshour, Africa News, May 28, 1990.)
