Africa News


November 12, 1990


U.S. Assumes Mediator Role


LENGTH: 630 words


The Bush administration has stepped up contacts with the Ethiopian government and the Eritrean independence movement, amid indications that both sides in the three-decade conflict are seeking an end to the fighting. On October 4th, in Washington, D.C., the U.S. hosted a meeting between Ethiopian Foreign Minister Tesfaye Dinka and Ali Said Abdallah, foreign relations chief for the Eritrean People's Liberation Front (EPLF).

In an attempt to avoid a battlefield defeat, Ethiopian leader Mengistu Haile Mariam has discarded his regime's Marxist trappings, resumed relations with Israel, and allied himself with the international coalition against Iraqi leader Saddam Hussein. 

U.S. officials say the military regime in Addis Ababa continues to receive Soviet military assistance, although the level of Moscow's aid has dropped considerably.

On October 25th, Assistant Secretary of State Herman Cohen raised the issue during a meeting in Rome with Yuri A. Yukalov, head of the Soviet foreign ministry's Africa Directorate. State Department aides say that although Moscow supports the American mediation effort, the Soviets argue that they can best contribute to peace by retaining some ties to Mengistu.

The Ethiopian government proposes to end the war by establishing a federated structure that would allow local autonomy for Eritrea. It argues that until 1886, when Italy invaded Eritrea, the area had been an integral part of the Ethiopian empire. Eritreans counter that the Organzation of African Unity recognizes Africa's colonial boundaries as the basis for modern states. The EPLF insists on a United Nations-supervised referendum that would offer Eritreans the option of choosing independence.

A mediation attempt by former U.S. President Jimmy Carter and former Tanzanian President Julius Nyerere last spring was unsuccessful in bridging the gap between the two positions. The persistence of that gap in the latest talks leaves many observers doubting that a settlement can be achieved without more fighting.

With the Ethiopian Second Army under siege in Eritrea's capital Asmara, Mengistu is also facing a military reversal closer to home. A force led by the Tigray People's Liberation Front, which is seeking the military regime's overthrow, advanced to within 100 miles of Addis Ababa earlier this year.

But in the wake of the Iraqi invasion of Kuwait and the changing strategic situation in the region, the Ethiopian leader may gain a breathing spell. Saudi Arabia, Ethiopia's neighbor across the Red Sea, has made overtures to the Mengistu government.

And in the last year, Mengistu has cultivated relations with Israel, which is believed to have supplied Ethiopia with cluster bombs and other military aid to offset dwindling Soviet supplies. Despite U.S. protests, Israeli shipments continued until July, when the arms flow was cut after Ethiopia reportedly failed to honor a commitment to allow free emigration of the Ethiopian Jews.

On November 1st, Ethiopia announced a new agreement to allow all Ethiopian Jews to leave for Israel. Although Ethiopia denied any linkage between emigration and Israeli arms, the announcement is sure to enhance ties both with Israel and with the U.S., which has made the resumption of ambassadorial relations contingent on the approval for Jewish emigration.

Along with an increasing number of other African governments, Ethiopia has retained two Washington firms to lobby Congress and disseminate information in the U.S. The Pagonis and Donnelly Group has a $ 16,000-a-month contract for "rebuilding the long-term ties" between the two governments, while Henry J. Kaufman and Associates is receiving $ 25,000 a month to polish Ethiopia's image with the American public and news media.

Africa News


November 12, 1990


Eritrean War Grinds Toward End


LENGTH: 964 words


The longest war in modern African history appears to be nearing its final phase. But the prospect of fierce fighting in the coming weeks, combined with a severe drought in the region, is threatening the lives of hundreds of thousands of people.

Twenty-nine years ago last month, the Eritrean People's Liberation Front (EPLF) launched its fight for the independence of Eritrea, a former Italian colony that Ethiopia claims as a province. Now the independence movement is poised to seize Asmara, Eritrea's capital. 

Except for the southern town of Assab, the Asmara area is the only part of Eritrea still controlled by the Ethiopian government. More than a million civilians and an Ethiopian army garrison of 120,000 are trapped in the city and its environs, surrounded by Eritrean forces and dependent on a costly and uncertain airlift for food and other essential supplies.

A September 20th report by the human rights group Africa Watch accuses the Ethiopian government of using starvation as a weapon in the battle for Asmara. The group says that although the EPLF "has shown inflexibility in responding to requests for the safe delivery of humanitarian relief...the primary responsibility for the current disaster lies with the Ethiopian government, which is using the civilian population as a weapon to achieve its military objectives."

Africa Watch condemns the Ethiopian government for refusing to let civilians flee the city. Ethiopia is using Asmara residents as hostages, the group says, to discourage an EPLF attack, even though the captive population has begun to starve. Africa Watch appeals to both sides for a truce that would enable food to get in and people to get out of the besieged area.

The EPLF says it will cooperate with international efforts to transport food overland into Asmara, but the group has refused to stop shelling the Asmara airport, the only current supply line. Out of a dozen daily flights into Asmara, only two or fewer are part of a United Nations-sponsored airlift that provides a fraction of civilian needs. The remainder are Ethiopian military supply flights.

The Ethiopian Catholic Secretariat (ECS), which is coordinating the UN airlift, has appealed to international donors for additional planes to supplement the two Hercules aircraft chartered for the relief effort. The need to build up supplies in the city is urgent, the ECS says, since the EPLF may force the airport to close at any time. So far, there has been no response to the appeal.

Dan Connell, a founder of the Boston-based development aid group Grassroots, is working on a book about the Eritrean conflict. He returned last week from his 14th visit to Eritrea, where he says EPLF-controlled territory, as well as the Asmara area, is threatened by famine. The lack of any rainfall at all in parts of Eritrea, and sparse rains elsewhere, has affected two million of Eritrea's two and a quarter million people, Connell says.

The need to end the war and concentrate on relief and development is pushing EPLF strategists toward a final offensive, Connell believes. But he says the group is weighing several factors before attacking Asmara.

First, there is the question of how the Ethiopian government would respond to an assault on the Eritrean capital. When the EPLF captured the Red Sea port of Massawa last February, Ethiopian bombing raids killed over a hundred people and destroyed 25,000-50,000 tons of food aid. The EPLF may be reluctant to take the capital, only to see it bombarded.

Second, the EPLF is considering how it might provide food and social services in Asmara after it controls the city. Already hard-pressed to get relief to the drought-stricken countryside, the group's resources would be further strained by the responsibility for Asmara.

Third, the EPLF must take into account the international response to an offensive. How would an attack affect the potential political or economic support for Eritrean independence?

Finally, the Eritrean fighters are concerned, Connell says, that the coming battle for Asmara cost as little as possible in lives. Already the siege has continued for over 200 days. And aid officials worry that the city's population -- regarded by the Ethiopians as largely favoring the EPLF -- is increasingly at risk from a jittery soldiery as well as from famine.

Connell says the EPLF is looking for ways to undermine the Ethiopian army's willingness to fight. One approach may be a two-stage strategy whose first step is closing the airport. Without air supplies, soldiers stranded at the Asmara garrison could become demoralized.

Another possibility is an EPLF thrust across the border toward the Ethiopian capital of Addis Ababa, to increase pressures within the Ethiopian government and possibly lead to the downfall of Ethiopian strongman Mengistu Haile Mariam. Eritrean leaders have said repeatedly that only if democracy takes root in Ethiopia can an independent Eritrea be secure. (In the past, the EPLF has indicated that Eritreans might agree to become part of a future democratic Ethiopia.)

There is a widespread feeling in Eritrea that Mengistu -- though buffeted by war -- cannot afford defeat, either on the battlefield or at the negotiating table. "It is rather obvious, says EPLF leader Issaias, "that the defeat of the Ethiopian army would entail the removal of the Dergue," the ruling military council.

Meanwhile, both the Eritreans and the foreign aid workers in the territory worry about what Mengistu will do when faced with further losses. Few believe he is ready to accept what more and more observers regard as inevitable. "Evidently he has gone too far in blood and violence to turn back now," says British journalist David Hirst of the London Guardian

Africa News


January 1, 1990 Monday


Liberia;
Historical Document: NPLF Statement from January 1990


BYLINE: The Perspective


LENGTH: 948 words


I came upon this document today while rummaging through my pile of documents. I thought to share it with your reading public, especially those interested in recent Liberian history. Any editorial or public use of this document, whether now or in the future, as long as is for our common good is herewith authorized.

The document is the first policy statement of Mr. Charles Ghankay Taylor, as leader of the National Patriotic Front of Liberia (unedited). The statement was released on January 1, 1990. It reads:

Since the bloody military coup of April 12, 1980, which brought the regime of Master Sargeant Samuel K. Doe to power in Liberia, the Liberian people have endured 10 years of oppression, summary killings, human rights violations, ethnic genocide, gross economic mismanagement and blatant widespread corruption. 

In October 1985, despite threats and intimidation, the Liberian people turned out in massive numbers to express their will at the polls for a peaceful change of government, only to see the electoral process subverted by the Doe regime which unilaterally declared itself the winner, despite all independent evidence to the contrary.

Following the aborted elections a popular resistence movement under the leadership of the late Commanding General Thomas Quaiwonkpa sought to overturn the Doe regime with minimal force and restore Democracy to Liberia. The Doe regime brutally put down this uprising and took harsh retaliation against innocent civilians in the northern counties, taking hundreds of innocent lives and forcing thousands of Liberians to flee their homes as refugees.

Having exhausted every possible avenue of reason and having seen every effort to peacefully effect a change of governance by constitutional means crushed by the harshest use of force, we the members of the National Patriotic Front under the leadership of Charles Ghankay Taylor feel it is our right and bounded duty to rid the people of Liberia of this cancerous despotism by whatever means at our disposal with the following objectives:

1. The restoration of full constitutional democracy to the Liberian people through free, fair and open elections to be conducted as soon as practically possible following the conclusion of military actions and the restoration of law and order to the country.

2. The rebuilding of the Liberian economy on the basis of our traditional free enterprise system and the protection of private property, without excessive government bureaucracy and public corruption but with concern for health, education housing, employment and food for the vast majority of our populace.

3. The unification of all Liberian people without regard to class, social status, ethnic origin, religion or political philosophy in the common task of nation-building. The National Patriotic Front is a broad-based, popular, non-sectarian nationalist movement which believes in the right of every Liberian to equal protection and opportunity under the law and which does not subscribe to ethnic and social factionalism or recriminations.

4. The National Patriotic Front is not beholden to any foreign group or power and believes in the maintenance of Liberia's traditional relationships with its close friends and allies, particularly the United States, and intends to work closely with these foreign partners in the reconstruction of the country.

5. The National Patriotic Front believes strongly in the implementation of all fundamental human rights guaranteed to every citizen of Liberia by the constitution, particularly the right of life, liberty and security; the right to freedom of the thought, expression, movement and peaceful assembly; and the right to equal protection and due process under the rule of law.

We, therefore, call upon every patriotic Liberian, including the many truly patriotic elements of the Armed Forces of Liberia, and all freedom-loving peoples everywhere to make every sacrifice and join with us in this right and historic struggle to free the Liberian people and nation of the shackles of tyranny and injustice once and for all.

This statement speaks for itself. I hope Mr. Taylor can find the time to read it today in case he needs to be reminded of the wonderful Liberia he envisaged "for all of us" in 1990. ... A Liberia of equal protection and opportunity under the law. A Liberia where the fundamental rights of every citizen would be guaranteed under the constitution, particularly the right to life, liberty, security, freedom of thought, expression and movement and peaceful assembly? I guess all the people who have fallen victim to wanton brutality and outright murder by this regime could not have been Liberians. How else can we explain the deaths of Nowai Flomo, Dogoleah, the Dokies, Chucky's driver (Isaac Gono) who was flogged to death without any credible explanation? Could it also be that James Torh, Commany Wisseh, Sawyer, Bishop Francis, etc., etc., are foreigners? I don't think so. But as Ronald Reagan once said, "people don't start wars, governments do." In as much as some of us detest the current war on our people in Lofa based in the hollowed integrity of its proponents, I believe the "cancer" alluded to by the NPFL in their statement is still gnawing at the freedom, liberty, happiness and peace of Liberia. And again as the NPFL statement said, "it is our right and bounded duty to rid the people of Liberia of this cancerous despotism by whatever means at our disposal." Albert Einstein once said, " The world is a dangerous place not because of the people who are evil, but because of the people who don't do anything about it." That's our challenge.

Thanks for the space.
